2.1. Sequences. The sequence $\{ a _ { n } \} _ { 1 } ^ { \infty }$ has the limit $\pmb { A }$ if to every $\mathfrak { E } > \mathbf { 0 }$ there exists an $\scriptstyle { \pmb { n _ { 0 } } }$ such that $| a _ { n } - A | < \varepsilon$ for $n \geq n _ { 0 }$ A sequence with a finite limit is said to be convergent, and any sequence which does not converge is divergent. If $\operatorname* { l i m } _ { n \to \infty }$ $a _ { n } = \infty$ , the sequence may be said to diverge to infinity.

Only in rare cases can the convergence be proved by exhibiting the limit, so it is extremely important to make use of a method that permits proof of the existence of a limit even when it cannot be determined explicitly. The test that serves this purpose bears the name of Cauchy. A sequence will be called fundamental, or a Cauchy sequence, if it satisfies the following condition: given any $\mathfrak { E } > \mathbf { 0 }$ there exists an $\pmb { n } _ { 0 }$ such that $| a _ { n } - a _ { m } | < \varepsilon$ whenever $n \not \equiv n _ { 0 }$ and $m \geq { \mathfrak { n } } _ { 0 }$ The test reads:

$A$ sequence is convergent if and only if it is $^ { a }$ Cauchy sequence.

The necessity is immediate. If ${ a _ { n } } \to A$ we can find $\pmb { n _ { 0 } }$ such that $| a _ { n } - A | < \varepsilon / 2$ for $n \geq n _ { 0 }$ For $m , n \geq { \mathfrak { n } } _ { 0 }$ it follows by the triangle inequality that $| a _ { n } - a _ { m } | \leq | a _ { n } - A | + | a _ { m } - A | < \mathfrak { e } .$

The sufficiency is closely connected with the definition of real numbers, and one way in which real numbers can be introduced is indeed to postulate the sufficiency of Cauchy's condition. However, we wish to use only the property that every bounded monotone sequence of real numbers has a limit.

The real and imaginary parts of a Cauchy sequence are again Cauchy sequences, and if they converge, so does the original sequence. For this reason we need to prove the sufficiency only for real sequences. We use the opportunity to recall the notions of limes superior and limes inferior. Given a real sequence $\{ \pmb { \alpha _ { n } } \} _ { 1 } ^ { \infty }$ we shall set $a _ { n } = { \mathrm { m a x } }$ $\{ \alpha _ { 1 } , ~ . ~ . ~ . ~ , ~ \alpha _ { n } \}$ , that is, $a _ { n }$ is the greatest of the numbers ${ \pmb { \alpha } } _ { 1 } ,$ . . . , ${ \pmb { \alpha } } _ { n }$ The sequence $\{ a _ { n } \} _ { 1 } ^ { \infty }$ is nondecreasing; hence it has a limit $\pmb { A _ { 1 } }$ which is finite or equal to $+ \infty$ . The number $A _ { 1 }$ is known as the least upper bound or supremum (l.u.b. or sup) of the numbers $\alpha _ { n }$ ; indeed, it is the least number which is $\geqq$ all $\alpha _ { n }$ . Construct in the same way the least upper bound $\scriptstyle A _ { k }$ of the sequence $\{ \alpha _ { n } \} _ { k } ^ { \infty }$ obtained from the original sequence by deleting $\alpha _ { 1 } , ~ \ldots , ~ \alpha _ { k - 1 } .$ . It is clear that $\left\{ A _ { k } \right\}$ is a nonincreasing sequence, and we denote its limit by $A$ . It may be finite, $+ \infty$ , or - ∞. In any case we write

$$
A = \operatorname* { l i m } _ { n \to \infty } \operatorname* { s u p } \alpha _ { n } .
$$

It is easy to characterize the limes superior by its properties. If A is finite and $\mathfrak { e } > \mathbf { 0 }$ there exists an $\pmb { n } _ { 0 }$ such that $A _ { n _ { 0 } } < A + \varepsilon ,$ and it follows that $\alpha _ { n } \equiv A _ { n _ { 0 } } < A + \mathfrak { e }$ for $n \geq n _ { 0 } .$ In the opposite direction, if $\alpha _ { n } \leqq A - \varepsilon$ for $n \not \equiv n _ { 0 } ,$ then $A _ { n _ { 0 } } \cong A - \varepsilon ,$ which is impossible. In other words, there are arbitrarily large $\pmb { n }$ for which $\alpha _ { n } > A - \varepsilon .$ If $\cdot A ^ { - } = + \infty$ there are arbitrarily large $\pmb { \alpha _ { n } }$ ,and $A = - \infty$ if and only if $\pmb { \alpha _ { n } }$ tends to ∞ In all cases there cannot be more than one number A with these properties.

The limes inferior can be defined in the same manner with inequalities reversed. It is quite clear that the limes inferior and limes superior will be equal if and only if the sequence converges to a finite limit or diverges to + ∞ or to - ∞. The notations are frequently simplified to lim and lim. The reader should prove the following relations:

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { \varlimsup \alpha _ { n } + \varliminf \beta _ { n } \leq \varliminf \left( \alpha _ { n } + \beta _ { n } \right) \leq \varliminf \alpha _ { n } + \varlimsup \beta _ { n } } \\ & { \varliminf \alpha _ { n } + \varlimsup \beta _ { n } \leq \varlimsup \left( \alpha _ { n } + \beta _ { n } \right) \leq \varlimsup \alpha _ { n } + \varlimsup \beta _ { n } . } \end{array}
$$

Now we return to the sufficiency of Cauchy's condition. From $| { \alpha } _ { n } - { \alpha } _ { n _ { 0 } } | < \varepsilon { \mathrm { ~ w e ~ } }$ obtain $\vert \alpha _ { n } \vert < \vert \alpha _ { n _ { 0 } } \vert + \varepsilon$ for $n \not \equiv n _ { 0 }$ , and it follows that $A = \widetilde { \mathrm { l i m } } \ \alpha _ { n }$ and $a = \varinjlim \alpha _ { n }$ are both finite. If $a \neq A$ choose

$$
{ \mathfrak { s } } = { \frac { ( A - a ) } { 3 } } \ .
$$

and determine a corresponding $\pmb { n _ { 0 } }$ By definition of $\pmb { a }$ and $A$ there exists an $\alpha _ { n } < a + \varepsilon$ and an $\alpha _ { m } > A - \varepsilon$ with $m , n \geqq n _ { 0 }$ It follows that $A - a = ( A - \alpha _ { m } ) + ( \alpha _ { m } - \alpha _ { n } ) + ( \alpha _ { n } - a ) \leqslant 3 \ 6 ,$ contrary to the choice of e. Hence ${ \pmb a } = { \pmb A }$ , and the sequence converges.

2.2. Series. A very simple application of Cauchy's condition permits us to deduce the convergence of one sequence from that of another. If it is true that $\left| b _ { m } - b _ { n } \right| \leqq \left| a _ { m } - a _ { n } \right|$ for all pairs of subscripts, the sequence $\left\{ b _ { n } \right\}$ may be termed a contraction of the sequence $\left\{ a _ { n } \right\}$ (this is not a standard term). Under this condition, if $\left\{ a _ { n } \right\}$ is a Cauchy sequence, so is $\{ b _ { n } \}$ . Hence convergence of $\left\{ a _ { n } \right\}$ implies convergence of $\left\{ b _ { n } \right\}$ :

An infinite series is a formal infinite sum

$$
a _ { 1 } + a _ { 2 } + \cdots + a _ { n } + \cdots .
$$

Ah   r

$$
s _ { n } = a _ { 1 } + a _ { 2 } + \cdots + a _ { n } .
$$

The ser ssaid o onverge andnly the corrondng equ the series.

Applied to a series Cauchy's convergence test yields the following   
Th $\pmb { n } _ { 0 }$ such tat $| a _ { n } + a _ { n + 1 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + a _ { n + p } | < \mathfrak { e }$ for all $n > n _ { 0 }$ and   
p ≥ 0. For  = 0 we find in particular that |a < 8. Hence the en-

eral term of a convergent series tends to zero. This condition is necessary, but of course not sufficient.

If a finite number of the terms of the series (16) are omitted, the new series converges or diverges together with (16). In the case of convergence, let $\scriptstyle { R _ { n } }$ be the sum of the series which begins with the term $\scriptstyle a _ { n + 1 }$ . Then the sum of the whole series is $S = s _ { n } + R _ { n }$ .

The series (16) can be compared with the series

$$
| a _ { 1 } | + | a _ { 2 } | + \cdot \cdot \cdot + | a _ { n } | + \cdot \cdot \cdot
$$

formed by the absolute values of the terms. The sequence of partial sums of (16) is a contraction of the sequence corresponding to (17), for |an+ $a _ { n + 1 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + a _ { n + p } | \leq | a _ { n } | + | a _ { n + 1 } | + \cdot \cdot \cdot + | a _ { n + p } |$ . Therefore, convergence of (17) implies that the original series (16) is convergent. A series with the property that the series formed by the absolute values of the terms converges is said to be absolutely convergent.

2.3. Uniform Convergence. Consider a sequence of functions ${ f _ { n } } ( x )$ , all defined on the same set $\cal E$ If the sequence of values $\{ f _ { n } ( x ) \}$ converges for every $\pmb { x }$ that belongs to $\boldsymbol { E }$ , then the limit ${ f ( x ) }$ is again a function on $\textit { \textbf { E } }$ By definition, if ${ \mathfrak { s } } > { \mathbf { 0 } }$ and $\pmb { x }$ belongs to $\boldsymbol { E }$ there exists an $\pmb { n } _ { 0 }$ such that $| f _ { n } ( x ) - f ( x ) | < \mathfrak { s }$ for $n \not \equiv n _ { 0 } ,$ but $\pmb { n } _ { 0 }$ is allowed to depend on $\pmb { x }$ . For instance, it is true that

$$
\operatorname* { l i m } _ { n \to \infty } \left( 1 + { \frac { 1 } { n } } \right) x = x
$$

for all $_ { x }$ , but in order to have $| ( 1 + 1 / n ) x - x | = | x | / n < \mathfrak { e }$ for $n \geq n _ { 0 }$ it is necessary that $n _ { 0 } > | x | / \mathfrak { s } .$ Such an $\pmb { n } _ { 0 }$ exists for every fixed $\pmb { x }$ , but the requirement cannot be met simultaneously for all $_ { \pmb { x } }$ .

We say in this situation that the sequence converges pointwise, but not uniformly. In positive formulation: The sequence $\{ f _ { n } ( x ) \}$ converges uniformly to $f ( x )$ on the set $\cal E$ if to every $\bf \varepsilon > 0$ there exists an $\pmb { n _ { 0 } }$ such that $| f _ { n } ( x ) - f ( x ) | < \mathfrak { s }$ for all $n \not \equiv n _ { 0 }$ and all $\pmb { x }$ in $\mathbf { \delta E }$ .

The most important consequence of uniform convergence is the following:

The limit function of $^ { a }$ uniformly convergent sequence of continuous functions is itself continuous.

Suppose that the functions ${ f _ { n } } ( x )$ are continuous and tend uniformly to $\pmb { f } ( \pmb { x } )$ on the set $\boldsymbol { E }$ For any ${ \mathfrak { s } } > { \mathbf { 0 } }$ we are able to find an $\pmb { n }$ such that $| f _ { n } ( x ) - f ( x ) | < \varepsilon / 3$ for all $\pmb { x }$ in $E$ .Let $x _ { 0 }$ be a point in $\boldsymbol { E }$ Because ${ f _ { n } } ( x )$ is continuous at $x _ { 0 }$ we can find $\delta > 0$ such that $| f _ { n } ( x ) - f _ { n } ( x _ { 0 } ) | < \varepsilon / 3$ for all $\pmb { x }$ in $\boldsymbol { E }$ with $| x - x _ { 0 } | < \delta$ Under the same condition on $_ { x }$ it follows that |f(x)- $\cdot f ( x _ { 0 } ) | \ \leq \ | f ( x ) - f _ { n } ( x ) | + | f _ { n } ( x ) - f _ { n } ( x _ { 0 } ) | + | f _ { n } ( x _ { 0 } ) - f ( x _ { 0 } ) | <$ ε, and we have proved that ${ f } ( x )$ is continuous at ${ \boldsymbol { x } } _ { 0 }$ .

In the theory of analytic functions we shall find uniform convergence much more important than pointwise convergence. However, in most cases it will be found that the convergence is uniform only on a part of the set on which the functions are originally defined.

Cauchy's necessary and sufficient condition has a counterpart for uniform convergence. We assert:

The sequence $\{ f _ { n } ( x ) \}$ converges uniformly on $\boldsymbol { E }$ if and only if to every $\mathbf { \varepsilon } _ { \varepsilon } > \mathbf { 0 }$ there exists an $\pmb { n } _ { 0 }$ such that $| f _ { m } ( x ) - f _ { r } ( x ) | < $ for all m ${ \mathcal { n } } \gtrapprox n _ { 0 }$ and all $\pmb { x }$ in $\textit { \textbf { E } }$ .

The necessity is again trivial. For the sufficiency we remark that the limit function ${ f } ( x )$ exists by the ordinary form of Cauchy's test. In the inequality $| f _ { m } ( x ) - f _ { n } ( x ) | < \mathfrak { e }$ we can keep $\pmb { n }$ fixed and let $\mathbf { \nabla } m$ tend to $\infty$ . It follows that $| f ( x ) - f _ { n } ( x ) | \leqq \varepsilon$ for $n \not \equiv n _ { 0 }$ and all $_ { \pmb { x } }$ in $\mathbf { \nabla } _ { E }$ Hence the convergence is uniform.

For practical use the following test is the most applicable: If a sequence of functions $\{ f _ { n } ( x ) \}$ is a contraction of a convergent sequence of constants $\left\{ a _ { n } \right\}$ , then the sequence $\{ f _ { n } ( x ) \}$ is uniformly convergent. The hypothesis means that $| f _ { m } ( x ) - f _ { n } ( x ) | \leq | a _ { m } - a _ { n } |$ on $E _ { i }$ ,and the conclusion follows immediately by Cauchy's condition.

In the case of series this criterion, in a somewhat weaker form, becomes particularly simple. We say that a series with variable terms

$$
f _ { 1 } ( x ) + f _ { 2 } ( x ) + \cdot \cdot \cdot + f _ { n } ( x ) + \cdot \cdot \cdot
$$

has the series with positive terms

$$
a _ { 1 } + a _ { 2 } + \cdots + a _ { n } + \cdots .
$$

for a majorant if it is true that $| f _ { n } ( x ) | \leqq M a _ { n }$ for some constant $M$ and for all sufficiently large $\pmb { n }$ ;ch second. In these circumstances we have

$$
) + f _ { n + 1 } ( x ) + \cdot \cdot \cdot + f _ { n + p } ( x ) | \leq M ( a _ { n } + a _ { n + 1 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + a _ { n + p } )
$$

Therefore, if the majorant converges, the minorant converges uniformly. This condition is frequently referred to as the Weierstrass M est.It has the slight weakness that it applies only to series which are also absolutely convergent. The general principle of contraction is more complicated, but has a wider range of applicability.

2.4. Power Series. A power series is of the form

$$
a _ { 0 } + a _ { 1 } z + a _ { 2 } z ^ { 2 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + a _ { n } z ^ { n } + \cdot \cdot \cdot
$$

where the coefficients ${ a } _ { n }$ and the variable $\pmb { \mathscr { z } }$ are complex. A little more generally we may consider series

$$
\sum _ { n = 0 } ^ { \infty } a _ { n } ( z - z _ { 0 } ) ^ { n }
$$

which are power series with respect to the center ${ \boldsymbol { z } } _ { 0 } ,$ but the difference is so slight that we need not do so in a formal manner.

As an almost trivial example we consider the geometric series

$$
1 + z + z ^ { 2 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + z ^ { n } + \cdot \cdot \cdot
$$

whose partial sums can be written in the form

$$
1 + z + \cdot \cdot \cdot + z ^ { n - 1 } = { \frac { 1 - z ^ { n } } { 1 - z } } \cdot
$$

Since $z ^ { n }  0$ for $| z | < 1$ and $\left| z ^ { n } \right| \gtrapprox 1$ for $| z | \geq 1$ we conclude that the geometric series converges to $1 / ( 1 - z )$ for $| z | < 1$ ,diverges for $| z | \geq 1$ .

It turns out that the behavior of the geometric series is typical. Indeed, we shall find that every power series converges inside a circle and diverges outside the same circle, except that it may happen that the series converges only for ${ \boldsymbol { z } } = \mathbf { 0 }$ , or that it converges for all values of ${ \pmb z }$ .

More precisely, we shall prove the following theorem due to Abel:

Theorem 2. For every power series (18) there exists a number $\pmb { R }$ , $\mathbf { 0 } \le$ $\pmb { R } \leq \infty$ , called the radius of convergence, with the following properties:

(i) The series converges absolutely for every $\pmb { z }$ with $| z | < R$ . If $0 \leqq$ $\rho < R$ the convergence is uniform for $| { \boldsymbol { \mathsf { z } } } | \leq { \boldsymbol { \mathsf { \rho } } }$ .

ii)If $| z | > R$ the terms of the series are unbounded, and the series is consequently divergent.

(iii) $I n \mid z \mid < R$ the sum of the series is an analytic function. The derivative can be obtained by termwise differentiation, and the derived series has the same radius of convergence.

The circle $| z | = R$ is called the circle of convergence; nothing is claimed about the convergence on the circle. We shall show that the assertions in the theorem are true if $\boldsymbol { R }$ is chosen according to the formula

$$
1 / R = \operatorname* { l i m } _ { n \to \infty } \operatorname* { s u p } _ { } \sqrt [ n ] { | a _ { n } | } .
$$

This is known as Hadamard's formula for the radius of convergence.

If $| z | < R$ we can find $\pmb { \rho }$ so that $| z | < \rho < R$ Then $1 / \rho > 1 / R$ ,and by the definition of limes superior there exists an $\scriptstyle n _ { 0 }$ such that $\vert a _ { n } \vert ^ { 1 / n } < 1 / \rho ,$ . $| a _ { n } | < 1 / \rho ^ { n }$ for $n \not \equiv n _ { 0 }$ It follows that $| a _ { n } z ^ { n } | < ( | z | / \rho ) ^ { n }$ for large $^ { \pmb { n } }$ , so that the power series (18) has a convergent geometric series as a majorant, and is consequently convergent. To prove the uniform convergence for $| z | \leq \rho < R$ we choose a $\pmb { \rho } ^ { \prime }$ with $\rho < \rho ^ { \prime } < R$ and find $| a _ { n } z ^ { n } | \leq ( \rho / \rho ^ { \prime } ) ^ { n }$ for $n \geq n _ { 0 }$ . Since the majorant is convergent and has constant terms we conclude by Weierstrass's $M$ test that the power series is uniformly convergent.

If $| z | > R$ we choose $\pmb { \rho }$ so that $R < \rho < | z |$ Since $1 / \rho < 1 / R$ there are arbitrarily large $\pmb { n }$ such that $| a _ { n } | ^ { 1 / n } > 1 / \rho _ { ; }$ b $| a _ { n } | > 1 / \rho ^ { n }$ Thus $\vert a _ { n } z ^ { n } \vert > ( \vert z \vert / \bar { p } ) ^ { n }$ for infinitely many $\pmb { n }$ , and the terms are unbounded.

The derived series $\sum _ { \bf 1 } ^ { \infty } n a _ { n } z ^ { n - 1 }$ has the se aius cv, because $\sqrt [ n ] { n }  1$ Proof: Set $\sqrt [ n ] { n } = 1 + \delta _ { n }$ Then $\delta _ { n } > 0$ ,and by use of the binomial theorem $n = ( 1 + \delta _ { n } ) ^ { n } > 1 + \frac { 1 } { 2 } n ( n - 1 ) \delta _ { n } ^ { 2 }$ This gives δ2 < 2/n, and hence δn → 0.

For $| z | < R$ we shall write

where

$$
s _ { n } ( z ) = a _ { 0 } + a _ { 1 } z + \cdot \cdot \cdot + a _ { n - 1 } z ^ { n - 1 } , R _ { n } ( z ) = \sum _ { k = n } ^ { \infty } a _ { k } z ^ { k } ,
$$

and also

$$
f _ { 1 } ( z ) = \sum _ { 1 } ^ { \infty } { n a _ { n } z ^ { n - 1 } } = \operatorname* { l i m } _ { n \to \infty } { s _ { n } ^ { \prime } ( z ) } .
$$

We have to show that $f ^ { \prime } ( z ) = f _ { 1 } ( z )$ .

Consider the identity

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \frac { z ) - f ( z _ { 0 } ) } { z - z _ { 0 } } - f _ { 1 } ( z _ { 0 } ) = \cfrac { \left( s _ { n } ( z ) - s _ { n } ( z _ { 0 } ) \right. } { z - z _ { 0 } } - s _ { n } ^ { \prime } ( z _ { 0 } ) \cfrac { } { } + ( s _ { n } ^ { \prime } ( z _ { 0 } ) - f _ { 1 } ( z _ { 0 } ) ) } \\ { \cfrac { } { } + \cfrac { \left( R _ { n } ( z ) - R _ { n } ( z _ { 0 } ) \right. } { z - z _ { 0 } } } \end{array}
$$

where we assume that ${ z \neq z _ { 0 } }$ and $| z | , | z _ { 0 } | < \rho < R$ The last term can be rewritten as

$$
\sum _ { k = n } ^ { \infty } a _ { k } ( z ^ { k - 1 } + z ^ { k - 2 } z _ { 0 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + z z _ { 0 } ^ { k - 2 } + z _ { 0 } ^ { k - 1 } ) ,
$$

and we conclude that

$$
\left| \frac { R _ { n } ( z ) - R _ { n } ( z _ { 0 } ) } { z - z _ { 0 } } \right| \leq \sum _ { k = n } ^ { \infty } k | a _ { k } | \rho ^ { k - 1 } .
$$

The expression on the right is the remainder term in a convergent series. Hence we can find $\pmb { n _ { 0 } }$ such that

$$
| \frac { R _ { n } ( z ) - R _ { n } ( z _ { 0 } ) } { z - z _ { 0 } } | < \frac { \varepsilon } { 3 }
$$

for $n \not \equiv n _ { 0 }$

There is also an $\pmb { n _ { 1 } }$ such that $| s _ { n } ^ { \prime } ( z _ { 0 } ) - f _ { 1 } ( z _ { 0 } ) | < \varepsilon / 3$ for $n \not \equiv n _ { 1 }$ . Choose a fixed $n \geq n _ { 0 } , n _ { 1 }$ By the definition of derivative we can find $\delta > 0$ such that $0 < | z - z _ { 0 } | < \delta$ implies

$$
\left| \frac { s _ { n } ( z ) - s _ { n } ( z _ { 0 } ) } { z - z _ { 0 } } - s _ { n } ^ { \prime } ( z _ { 0 } ) \right| < \frac { \varepsilon } { 3 } .
$$

When all these inequalities are combined it follows by (20) that

$$
\left| \frac { f ( z ) - f ( z _ { 0 } ) } { z - z _ { 0 } } - f _ { 1 } ( z _ { 0 } ) \right| < \mathfrak { s }
$$

when $0 < | z - z _ { 0 } | < \delta$ We have proved that $f ^ { \prime } ( z _ { 0 } )$ exists and equals $\pmb { f _ { 1 } } ( z _ { 0 } )$ .

Since the reasoning can be repeated we have in reality proved much more: A power series with positive radius of convergence has derivatives of all orders, and they are given explicitly by

$$
\begin{array} { l } { f ( z ) = a _ { 0 } + a _ { 1 } z + a _ { 2 } z ^ { 2 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot } \\ { f ^ { \prime } ( z ) = a _ { 1 } + 2 a _ { 2 } z + 3 a _ { 3 } z ^ { 2 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot } \\ { f ^ { \prime \prime } ( z ) = 2 a _ { 2 } + 6 a _ { 3 } z + 1 2 a _ { 4 } z ^ { 2 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot \cdot } \\ { \cdot \cdot \cdot \cdot \cdot \cdot \cdot \cdot \cdot \cdot \cdot \cdot \cdot \cdot \cdot \cdot \cdot \cdot \cdot \cdot \cdot \cdot \cdot \cdot \cdot \cdot \cdot \ \cdot \ \cdot \ \ \cdot \ \ \cdot \ \ \cdot \ \ \ \cdot \ \ \ \cdot \ \ \ \cdot \ \ \ \ \cdot \ \ \ \ \cdot \ \ \ \ \cdot \ \ \ \ \cdot \ \ \ \ \ \cdot \ \ \ \ \ \cdot } \\ { f ^ { ( k ) } ( z ) = k ! a _ { k } + \frac { \left( k + 1 \right) ! } { 1 ! } a _ { k + 1 } z + \frac { \left( k + 2 \right) ! } { 2 ! } a _ { k + 2 } z ^ { 2 } + \cdot \cdot \ \cdot \ \cdot \ } \end{array} .
$$

In particular, if we look at the last line we see that $a _ { k } = f ^ { ( k ) } ( 0 ) / k !$ , and the power series becomes

$$
f ( z ) = f ( 0 ) + f ^ { \prime } ( 0 ) z + \frac { f ^ { \prime \prime } ( 0 ) } { 2 \mathrm { ~ l ~ } } z ^ { 2 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + \frac { f ^ { ( n ) } ( 0 ) } { n ! } z ^ { n } + \cdot \cdot \cdot
$$

This is the familiar Taylor-Maclaurin development, but we have proved it only under the assumption that $f ( z )$ has a power series development. We do know that the development is uniquely determined, if it exists, but the main part is still missing, namely that every analytic function has a Taylor development.

2.5. Abel's Limit Theorem. There is $\clubsuit$ second theorem of Abel's which refers to the case where a power series converges at a point of the circle of convergence. We lose no generality by assuming that $R = 1$ and that the convergence takes place at $\pmb { z = 1 }$ .

Theorem 3. If $\sum _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } a _ { n }$ converges, then $f ( z ) = \sum _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } a _ { n } z ^ { n }$ tends to $f ( 1 )$ as 2 approaches 1 in such $^ { a }$ way that $\lfloor 1 - z \rfloor / ( 1 - | z | )$ remains bounded.

Remark. Geometrically, the condition means that $\pmb { z }$ stays in an angle $< 1 8 0 ^ { \circ }$ with vertex 1, symmetrically to the part $( - \infty , 1 )$ of the real axis. It is customary to say that the approach takes place in a Stolz angle.

Proof. We may assume that $\sum _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } a _ { n } = 0 ,$ for this can be attained by adding a constant to ${ \pmb a } _ { \mathbb { 0 } }$ We write $s _ { n } = a _ { 0 } + a _ { 1 } + \cdots + a _ { n } $ and make use of the identity (summation by parts)

$$
\begin{array} { l } { { a _ { 0 } + a _ { 1 } z + \cdot \cdot \cdot + a _ { n } z ^ { n } = s _ { 0 } + ( s _ { 1 } - s _ { 0 } ) z + \cdot \cdot \cdot + ( s _ { n } - s _ { n - 1 } } } \\ { { - z ) + s _ { 1 } ( z - z ^ { 2 } ) + \cdot \cdot \cdot \cdot + s _ { n - 1 } ( z ^ { n - 1 } - z ^ { n } ) + s _ { n } z ^ { n } } } \\ { { \cdot z ) ( s _ { 0 } + s _ { 1 } z + \cdot \cdot \cdot \cdot + s _ { n - 1 } z ^ { n - 1 } ) + s _ { n } z ^ { n } . } } \end{array}
$$

But $\pmb { s } _ { n } \pmb { \tilde { z } } ^ { n }  \mathbf { 0 }$ , so we obtain the representation

$$
f ( z ) = ( 1 - z ) \sum _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } s _ { n } z ^ { n } .
$$

We are assuming that $| 1 - z | \leq K ( 1 - | z | )$ , say, and that $\mathbf { \delta } ^ { s } \mathbf { \hat { \eta } } \xrightarrow { } \mathbf { 0 }$ . Choose $\pmb { m }$ so large that $| s _ { n } | < \varepsilon$ for $n \geq m$ " The remainder of the series $\Sigma s _ { n } z ^ { n } $ ,from $\mathscr { n } = \mathscr { m }$ on, is then dominated by the geometric series 8 $\varepsilon \sum _ { m } | z | ^ { n } = \mathfrak { e } | z | ^ { m } / ( 1 - | z | ) < \mathfrak { e } / ( 1 - | z | ) ,$ It follows that

$$
| f ( z ) | \leq | 1 - z | { \Big \vert } \sum _ { 0 } ^ { m - 1 } s _ { k } z ^ { k } { \Big \vert } + K \varepsilon .
$$

The first term on the right can be made arbitrarily small by choosing $\pmb { z }$ sufficiently close to 1, and we conclude that $f ( z ) \to 0$ when $z  1$ subject to the stated restriction.

# THE EXPONENTIAL AND TRIGONOMETRIC FUNCTIONS

The person who approaches calculus exclusively from the point of view of real numbers will not expect any relationship between the exponential function e\* and the trigonometric functions cos x and sin x. Indeed, these functions seem to be derived from completely different sources and with different purposes in mind. He will notice, no doubt, a similarity between the Taylor developments of these functions, and if willing to use imaginary arguments he will be able to derive Euler'sformula ei = cos x + i sin x as a formal identity. But it took the genius of a Gauss to analyze its full depth.

With the preparation given in the preceding section it will be easy to define $e ^ { z }$ ,cos $\pmb { z }$ and sin $\pmb { z }$ for complex ${ \pmb z } .$ , and to derive the relations between these functions. At the same time we can define the logarithm as the inverse function of the exponential, and the logarithm leads in turn to the correct definition of the argument of a complex number, and hence to the nongeometric definition of angle.

3.1. The Exponential. We may begin by defining the exponential function as the solution of the differential equation

$$
f ^ { \prime } ( z ) = f ( z )
$$

with the initial value $f ( { \bf 0 } ) = { \bf 1 }$ We solve it by setting

$$
\begin{array} { c } { { f ( z ) = a _ { 0 } + a _ { 1 } z + \cdot \cdot \cdot + a _ { n } z ^ { n } + \cdot \cdot \cdot } } \\ { { f ^ { \prime } ( z ) = a _ { 1 } + 2 a _ { 2 } z + \cdot \cdot \cdot \cdot + n a _ { \cdot } z ^ { n - 1 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot . } } \end{array}
$$

If (21) is to be satisfied, we must have $\begin{array} { r } { { a } _ { n - 1 } = n { a } _ { n } . } \end{array}$ and the initial condition gives ${ a } _ { 0 } = 1$ It follows by induction that $a _ { n } = 1 / n !$ .

The solution is denoted by $e ^ { z }$ or exp $\pmb { z } _ { \bot }$ ,depending on purely typographical considerations. We must show of course that the series

$$
e ^ { z } = 1 + \frac { z } { 1 ! } + \frac { z ^ { 2 } } { 2 ! } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + \frac { z ^ { n } } { n ! } + \cdot \cdot \cdot
$$

converges. It does so in the whole plane, for $\sqrt [ n ] { n ! }  \infty$ (proof by the reader).

It is a consequence of the differential equation that $e ^ { z }$ satisfies the addition theorem

$$
e ^ { { \mathfrak { a } } + { \mathfrak { b } } } = e ^ { { \mathfrak { a } } } \cdot e ^ { { \mathfrak { b } } } .
$$

indeed, we find that D(e² ·ec-z) = e² ·ec-z + ex.(-ec-z) = 0. Hence is a constant.The value of the constant is found by settingz = 0. We conclude that  = e, and (23follows for  = a, c = a+.

Remark: We have used that $f ( z )$ is constant if $f ^ { \prime } ( z )$ is identically zero. This is certainly so if $\pmb { f }$ is defined in the whole plane. For if $\begin{array} { r } { f = u + i v \mathbf { w } \mathbf { e } } \end{array}$ obtain ${ \frac { \partial u } { \partial x } } = { \frac { \partial u } { \partial y } } = { \frac { \partial v } { \partial x } } = { \frac { \partial v } { \partial y } } = 0 _ { z }$ , and the real version of the theorem shows that $f$ is constant on every horizontal and every vertical line.

As a particular case of the addition theorem $e ^ { z } \cdot e ^ { - z } = 1$ This shows that $e ^ { z }$ is never zero. For real $\pmb { x }$ the series development (22) shows that $e ^ { x } > 1$ for $\mathbf { \nabla } _ { \mathbf { \mathcal { X } } } > \mathbf { 0 }$ , and since $e ^ { \pmb { x } }$ and $e ^ { - { \boldsymbol { x } } }$ are reciprocals, $0 < e ^ { x } < 1$ for $x < 0$ . The fact that the series has real coefficients shows that exp $\pmb { \bar { z } }$ is the complex conjugate of exp z. Hence $| e ^ { i y } | ^ { 2 } = e ^ { i y } \cdot e ^ { - i y } = 1$ ,and $| e ^ { x + i y } | = e ^ { x }$ .

3.2. The Trigonometric Functions. The trigonometric functions are defined by

$$
\cos z = { \frac { e ^ { i z } + e ^ { - i z } } { 2 } } , \sin z = { \frac { e ^ { i z } - e ^ { - i z } } { 2 i } } \cdotp
$$

Substitution in (22) shows that they have the series developments

$$
\begin{array} { c } { { \cos z = 1 - \frac { z ^ { 2 } } { 2 ! } + \frac { z ^ { 4 } } { 4 ! } - \cdot \cdot \cdot } } \\ { { \sin z = z - \frac { z ^ { 3 } } { 3 ! } + \frac { z ^ { 5 } } { 5 ! } - \cdot \cdot \cdot } } \end{array}
$$

For real $z$ they reduce to the familiar Taylor developments of cos $_ { \pmb { x } }$ and sin $\pmb { x }$ , with the significant difference that we have now redefined these functions without use of geometry.

From (24) we obtain further Euler's formula

$$
e ^ { i z } = \cos z + i \sin z
$$

as well as the identity

$$
\cos ^ { 2 } z + \sin ^ { 2 } z = 1 .
$$

It follows likewise that

$$
D \cos z = - \sin z , ~ D \sin z = \cos z .
$$

The addition formulas

$$
\begin{array} { c } { { ( a + b ) = \cos a \cos b - \sin a \sin b } } \\ { { ( a + b ) = \cos a \sin b + \sin a \cos b } } \end{array}
$$

are direct consequences of (24) and the addition theorem for the exponential function.

The other trigonometric functions tan $\pmb { z }$ , cot z, sec z, cosec $\pmb { \ z }$ are of secondary importance. They are defined in terms of cos $\pmb { \mathcal { z } }$ and sin $\pmb { \mathscr { z } }$ in the

customary manner. We find for instance

$$
\tan z = - i { \frac { e ^ { i z } - e ^ { - \pi z } } { e ^ { i z } + e ^ { - i z } } } \cdot
$$

Observe that all the trigonometric functions are rational functions of $e ^ { i z }$ .

Derive the addition formulas, and formulas for cosh $2 z$ , sinh $\mathbf { 2 } z$ .

$\texttt { \textbf { 3 } }$ Use the addition formulas to separate cos $( x + i y )$ , sin $( x + i y )$ in real and imaginary parts.

4. Show that

$$
z | ^ { 2 } = \sinh ^ { 2 } y + \cos ^ { 2 } x = \cosh ^ { 2 } y - \sin ^ { 2 } x = { \frac { 1 } { 2 } } ( \cosh 2 y + \cos
$$

and

$$
\mathrm { i n ~ } z | ^ { 2 } = \sinh ^ { 2 } y + \sin ^ { 2 } x = \cosh ^ { 2 } y - \cos ^ { 2 } x = { \frac { 1 } { 2 } } \left( \cosh 2 y - \cos y \right)
$$

3.3. The Periodicity. We say that $f ( z )$ has the period $\pmb { c }$ if $f ( z + c ) = f ( z )$ for all ${ \pmb z } .$ Thus a period of $e ^ { z }$ satisfies $e ^ { z + c } = e ^ { z }$ , or $e ^ { \pmb { c } } = { \pmb { 1 } }$ It follows that $c = i \omega$ with real $\pmb { \omega }$ ; we prefer to say that $\pmb { \omega }$ is a period of $e ^ { i z }$ We shall show that there are periods, and that they are all integral multiples of a positive period $\omega _ { 0 }$ .

Of the many ways to prove the existence of a period we choose the following: From $\pmb { D }$ sin $y = \cos y \leq 1$ and sin $\mathbf { 0 } \approx \mathbf { 0 }$ we obtain sin $y < y$ for $y > 0$ er byintegration or by use  the mean-value theorem.In the same way $\pmb { D }$ cos $y = - \sin y > - y$ and cos $\mathbf { 0 } = \mathbf { 1 }$ gives cos $y > 1 -$ $y ^ { 2 } / 2$ which in turn leads to sin $y > y - y ^ { 3 } / 6$ and finally to cos $y < 1 -$ $y ^ { 2 } / 2 + y ^ { 4 } / 2 4$ This inequality shows that cos $\sqrt { 3 } < 0$ ,and therefore there is a ${ \pmb y } _ { \ r _ { 0 } }$ between 0 and $\sqrt { 3 }$ with cos $y _ { 0 } = 0$ Because

$$
\cos ^ { 2 } y _ { 0 } + \sin ^ { 2 } y _ { 0 } = 1
$$

we have sin $y _ { 0 } = \pm 1$ , that is, $e ^ { i y _ { 0 } } = \pm \ i ,$ and hence $e ^ { 4 i y _ { 0 } } = 1$ We have shown that 4yo is a period.

Actually, it is the smallest positive period. To see this, take $\mathbf { 0 } < y$ $< y _ { 0 } .$ Then sin $y > y ( 1 - y ^ { 2 } / 6 ) > y / 2 > 0 ,$ which shows that cos $\pmb { y }$ is stictly sig $_ y$ is positive and $\cos ^ { 2 } y + \sin ^ { 2 } y = 1$ it follows that sin y is strictly increasing, and hence sin y < sin yo = 1.

The double inequality $0 < \sin y < 1$ guarantees that $e ^ { i \pmb { y } }$ is neither $\pm 1$ nor $\pm i$ Therefore $e ^ { 4 i y } \neq 1$ , and $4 y _ { 0 }$ is indeed the smallest positive period. We denote it by $\omega _ { 0 }$ .

Consider now an arbitrary period $\pmb { \omega }$ There exists an integer $\pmb { n }$ such that $n \omega _ { 0 } \leq \omega < ( n + 1 ) \omega _ { 0 } ,$ If $\pmb { \omega }$ were not equal to ${ n } \omega _ { \mathbf { 0 } } ,$ then $\omega - n \omega _ { 0 }$ would be a positive period $< . \omega _ { 0 }$ Since this is not possible, every period must be an integral multiple of $\omega _ { 0 }$ .

The smallest positive period of $e ^ { i z }$ is denoted by $2 \pi$

In the course of the proof we have shown that

$$
e ^ { \pi i / 2 } = i , \qquad e ^ { \pi i } = - 1 , \qquad e ^ { 2 \pi i } = 1 .
$$

These equations demonstrate the intimate relationship between the numbers $e$ and $\pmb { \pi }$ .

When $y$ increases from 0 to $2 \pi$ , the point $w = e ^ { i y }$ describes the unit circle $| w | = 1$ in the positive sense, namely from 1 over $_ i$ to $^ { - 1 }$ and back over $- i$ to l. For every $\pmb { w }$ with $| w | = 1$ there is one and only one $y$ from the half-open interval $0 \leq y < 2 \pi$ such that $w = e ^ { i y }$ All this follows readily from the established fact that cos $y$ is strictly decreasing in the "first quadrant," that is, between O and $\pi / 2$ .

From an algebraic point of view the mapping $w = e ^ { i y }$ establishes a homomorphism between the additive group of real numbers and the multiplicative group of complex numbers with absolute value 1. The kernel of the homomorphism is the subgroup formed by all integral multiples $2 \pi n$ .

3.4. The Logarithm. Together with the exponential function we must also study its inverse function, the logarithm. By definition, $z = \log { w }$ is a root of the equation $e ^ { z } = w$ . First of all, since $e ^ { z }$ is always $\neq 0$ ,the number 0 has no logarithm. For $w \neq 0$ the equation $e ^ { x + i y } = w$ is equivalent to

$$
e ^ { x } = | w | , \qquad e ^ { i y } = w / | w | .
$$

The first equation has a unique solution $x = \log | w |$ , the real logarithm of the positive number $| w |$ . The right-hand member of the second equation (29) is a complex number of absolute value 1. Therefore, as we have just seen, it has one and only one solution in the interval $0 \leq y < 2 \pi$ In addition, it is also satisfied by all $y$ that differ from this solution by an integral multiple of $2 \pi .$ We see that every complex number other than 0 has infinitely many logarithms which differ from each other by multiples of $\scriptstyle 2 { \overrightarrow { \pi } } i$ .

The imaginary part of log w is also called the argument of $\pmb { w }$ , arg $\pmb { w }$ ,and it is interpreted geometrically as the angle, measured in radians, between

$\pmb { w }$ hAcord this definition the argument has infinitely many values which differ ultiples of 2π, and

$$
{ \mathrm { l o g ~ } } w = { \mathrm { l o g ~ } } | w | + i { \mathrm { ~ a r g ~ } } w .
$$

The addition theorem of the exponential function clearly implies

A function ${ \pmb w } = { \pmb f } ( { \pmb z } )$ may be viewed as a mapping which represents a point $\pmb { z }$ by its image w. The purpose of this chapter is to study, in a preliminary way, the special properties of a mapping that is defined by an analytic function.

In order to carry out this program it is desirable to develop the underlying concepts with sufficient generality, for otherwise we would soon be forced to introduce a great number of ad hoc definitions whose mutual relationship would be far from clear. Since present-day students are exposed to abstraction and generality at quite an early stage, no apologies are needed. It is perhaps more appropriate to sound a warning that greatest possible generality should not become a purpose.

In the first section we develop the fundamentals of point set topology and metric spaces. # 1. ELEMENTARY POINT SET TOPOLOGY

The branch of mathematics which goes under the name of topology is concerned with all questions directly or indirectly related to continuity. The term is traditionally used in a very wide sense and without strict limits. Topological considerations are extremely important for the foundation of the study of analytic functions, and the first systematic study of topology was motivated by this need.

The logical foundations of set theory belong to another discipline. Our approach will be quite naive, in keeping with the fact that all our applications will be to very familiar objects. In this limited framework no logical paradoxes can occur.

1.1. Sets and Elements. In our language a set will be a collection of identifiable objects, its elements. The reader is familiar with the notation ${ \pmb x } \in { \pmb X }$ which expresses that $\pmb { x }$ is an element of $X$ (as a rule we denote sets by capital letters and elements by small letters). Two sets are equal if and only if they have the same elements. $X$ is a subset of $\pmb { Y }$ if every element of $\pmb { X }$ is also an element of $\pmb { Y }$ , and this relationship is indicated by $X \subset Y$ or $Y \supset X$ (we do not exclude the possibility that $X = Y ,$ . The empty set is denoted by 0 (the possibility of confusion with the number 0 is very remote).

A set can also be referred to as a space, and an element as a point. Subsets of a given space are usually called point sets. This lends a geometric flavor to the language, but should not be taken too literally. For instance, we shall have occasion to consider spaces whose elements are functions; in that case a "point" is a function.

The intersection of two sets $X$ and $Y$ , denoted by $\textstyle X \cap Y$ , is formed by all points which are elements of both $X$ and $Y$ The union $X \cup Y$ consists of all points which are elements of either $X$ or $Y$ ,including those which are elements of both. $^ \dagger$ One can of course form the intersection and union of arbitrary collections of sets, whether finite or infinite in number.

The complement of a set $\boldsymbol { x }$ consists of all points which are not in $\pmb { X }$ ; it will be denoted by ${ \sim } X$ We note that the complement depends on the totality of points under consideration. For instance, a set of real numbers has one complement with respect to the real line and another with respect to the complex plane. More generally, if $X \subset Y$ we can consider the relative complement $Y \sim X$ which consists of all points that are in $Y$ but not in $X$ (we find it clearer to use this notation only when $X \subset Y )$

It is helpful to keep in mind the distributive laws

$$
\begin{array} { l c r } { { X \cup ( Y \cap Z ) = ( X \cup Y ) \cap ( X \cup Z ) } } \\ { { X \cap ( Y \cup Z ) = ( X \cap Y ) \cup ( X \cap Z ) } } \end{array}
$$

and the De Morgan laws

$$
\begin{array} { c } { { { \sim } ( X \cup Y ) = { \sim } X \cap { \sim } Y } } \\ { { { \sim } ( X \cap Y ) = { \sim } X \cup { \sim } Y . } } \end{array}
$$

These are purely logical identities, and they have obvious generalizations to arbitrary collections of sets.

1.2. Metric Spaces. For all considerations of limits and continuity it is essential to give a precise meaning to the terms "sufficiently near" and arbitrarily near." In the spaces $\mathbf { R }$ and $\mathbf { c }$ of real and complex numbers, respectively, such nearness can be expressed by a quantitative condition $| x - y | < \varepsilon$ For instance, to say that a set $\pmb { X }$ contains all $\pmb { x }$ sufficiently near to $\pmb { y }$ means that there exists an $\smash { \varepsilon < 0 }$ such that ${ \pmb x } \in { \pmb X }$ whenever $| x - y | < \varepsilon$ Similarly, $\pmb { X }$ contains points arbitrarily near to $\pmb { y }$ if to every $\mathfrak { E } > \mathbf { 0 }$ there exists an ${ \pmb x } \in { \pmb X }$ such that $| x - y | < \varepsilon .$ .

What we need to describe nearness in quantitative terms is obviously a distance $d ( x , y )$ between any two points. We say that a set $\pmb { S }$ is a metric space if there is defined, for every pair $x \in S , y \in S ,$ , a nonnegative real number $d ( x , y )$ in such a way that the following conditions are fulfilled:

1. $d ( x , y ) = \mathbf { 0 }$ if and only if $x = y$   
2. $d ( y , x ) = d ( x , y )$ .   
3. $d ( x , z ) \leq d ( x , y ) + d ( y , z )$ .

The last condition is the triangle inequaly

For instance, $\mathbf { R }$ and $\mathbf { C }$ are metric spaces with $d ( x , y ) = | x - y |$ . The $\pmb { n }$ -dimensional euclidean space $\mathbf { R } ^ { n }$ is the set of real $\pmb { \mathscr { n } }$ tuples

$$
\pmb { x } = ( \pmb { x } _ { 1 } , \ \dots \ , \pmb { x } _ { n } )
$$

with a distance defined by $d ( x , y ) ^ { 2 } = \sum _ { 1 } ^ { n } ( x _ { i } - y _ { \cdot } ) ^ { 2 } $ We recall that we have defined a distance in the extended complex plane by

$$
d ( z , z ^ { \prime } ) = \frac { 2 | z - z ^ { \prime } | } { \sqrt { ( 1 + | z | ^ { 2 } ) ( 1 + | z ^ { \prime } | ^ { 2 } ) } }
$$

(Cap.S.2.; sihiheae h oviusly ulfle. A example  uncion space  ven y C[a,.

the set of all continuous functions defined on the interval $a \leq x \leq b .$ .It becomes a metric space if we define distance by $d ( f , g ) = \operatorname* { m a x } | f ( x ) - g ( x ) | .$

In terms of distance we introduce the following terminology: The set $N _ { \mathfrak { z } } ( y )$ of all points $x \epsilon S$ with ${ d ( x , y ) < \delta }$ is called the $\delta$ neighborhood of $\pmb { y }$ . More essential still is the definition of an arbitrary neighborhood:

Definition 1. $A$ set $N \subset S$ is called a neighborhood of y if it contains $\pmb { a }$ δ-neighborhood $N _ { \mathfrak { z } } ( y )$ .

In other words, a neighborhood of $\pmb { y }$ is a set which contains all points sufficiently near to $\pmb { y }$ We use the notion of neighborhood to define open set:

Definition 2. A set is an open set if it is a neighborhood of each of its elements.

The definition is interpreted to mean that the empty set is open (the condition is fulfilled because the set has no elements). The following is an immediate consequence of the triangle inequality:

Every $\pmb { \delta }$ neighborhood is an open set.

Indeed, if z $N _ { \mathfrak { z } } ( y )$ , then $\delta ^ { \prime } = \delta - d ( y , z ) > 0$ The triangle inequality shows that $N _ { \delta ^ { \prime } } ( z ) \subset N _ { \delta } ( y )$ , for $d ( x , z ) < \delta ^ { \prime }$ gives

$$
d ( x , y ) < \delta ^ { \prime } + d ( y , z ) = \delta .
$$

Hence $N _ { \mathfrak { z } } ( y )$ is a neighborhood of ${ \pmb z } .$ ,and since $\pmb { z }$ was any point in $N _ { \mathfrak { s } } ( y )$ , we conclude that $N _ { \mathfrak { s } } ( y )$ is an open set.

For instance, in the complex plane a $\delta$ neighborhood $N _ { \mathfrak { z } } ( z _ { 0 } )$ is an open disk with center ${ \pmb z _ { 0 } }$ and radius $\pmb { \delta }$ ; it consists of all complex numbers $\pmb { z }$ which satisfy the inequality $| z - z _ { 0 } | < \delta . \dagger$ We have just proved that it is an open set, and the reader is urged to interpret the proof in geometric terms.

It is convenient to consider not only open sets, but also closed sets. From a logical point of view this contributes nothing new, for by definition a set is closed if its complement is open. In positive formulation, $X$ is closed if it contains all points $\pmb { x }$ with the property that every $N _ { \vartheta } ( \pmb { x } )$ intersects $X$ .

The reader should beware of a false impression that the terminology could easily convey: the terms "open" and "closed" are not contradictory.

$^ \dagger$ The standard notation is to write $N _ { \delta } ( z _ { 0 } ) = \{ z ; | z - z _ { 0 } | < \delta \}$ . We shall use this notation very sparingly. Even if the practice is slightly objectionable, we shall not hesitate to speak of "the disk $| z - z _ { 0 } | < \delta$ " It is to be clearly distinguished from "the circle $\vert z - z _ { 0 } \vert = \delta$ "

The empty set 0 and the whole space $\pmb { S }$ are at the same time open and closed, and there may be other sets with the same property.

The following properties of open and closed sets are fundamental:

The intersection of a finite number of open sets is open.

The union of any collection of open sets is open.

The union of a finite number of closed sets is closed.

The intersection of any collection of closed sets is closed.

The proofs are so obvious that they can be left to the reader. It should be noted that the last two statements follow from the first two by use of the De Morgan laws.

There are many terms in common usage which are directly related to the idea of open sets. A complete list would be more confusing than helpful, and we shall limit ourselves to the following: interior, closure, boundary, exterior.

The interior of a set $X$ is the largest open set contained in $\boldsymbol { X }$ .It exists, for it may be characterized as the union of all open sets $\subset X$ .It can also be described as the set of all points of which $\pmb { X }$ is a neighborhood. We denote it by Int $\pmb { X }$ .

iiThe closure of $\boldsymbol { X }$ is the smallest closed set which contains $X$ ,or the intersection of all closed sets $\supset X$ A point belongs to the closure of $\pmb { X }$ if and only if allits neighborhoods intersect $\boldsymbol { X }$ The closure is usually denoted by $X ^ { - }$ , infrequently by Cl $X$ .

(iii) The boundary of $X$ is the closure minus the interior. A point belongs to the boundary if and only if all its neighborhoods intersect both $X$ and ${ \sim } X$ Notation: Bd $X$ or $\pmb { \partial X }$ .

(iv) The exterior of $X$ is the interior of ${ \sim } X$ It is also the complement of the closure. As sucb it can be denoted by ${ \sim } X ^ { - }$ .

Observe that Int $X \subset X \subset X ^ { - }$ and that $\pmb { X }$ is open if Int $X = X$ , closed if $X ^ { - } = X$ Also, $x \subset Y$ implies Int X  Int $Y , X ^ { - } \subset Y ^ { - }$ .For added convenience we shall also introduce the notions of isolated point and accumulation point. We say that $x \in X$ is an isolated point of $\pmb { X }$ if $\pmb { x }$ has a neighborhood whose intersection with $X$ reduces to the point $\pmb { x } .$ . An accumulation point is a point of $X ^ { - }$ which is not an isolated point. It is clear that $\pmb { x }$ is an accumulation point of $\pmb { X }$ if and only if every neighborhood of $_ x$ contains infinitely many points from $X$ .

1.3. Connectedness. If $\pmb { \mathscr { E } }$ is any nonempty subset of a metric space $\pmb { S }$ we may consider $\pmb { { \cal E } }$ as a metric space in its own right with the same distance function $\boldsymbol { d } ( \boldsymbol { x } , \boldsymbol { y } )$ as on all of $s$ Neighborhoods and open sets on $\mathbf { \nabla } \mathbf { E }$ are defined as on any metric space, but an open set on $\mathbf { \nabla } \mathbf { E }$ need not be open when regarded as a subset of $\pmb { S }$ To avoid confusion neighborhoods and open sets are often referred to as relative neighborhoods and relatively open sets. As an example, if we regard the closed interval $\mathbf { 0 } \le x \le \mathbf { 1 }$ as a subspace of $\pmb { R }$ , then the semiclosed interval $0 \leq x < 1$ is relatively open, but not open in $\scriptstyle { R }$ Henceforth, when we say that a subset $\mathbf { \delta } _ { E }$ has some specific topological property, we shall always mean that it has this property as a subspace, and its subspace topology is called the relative topology.

Intuitively speaking, a space is connected if it consists of a single piece. This is meaningness unless we define the statement in terms of nearness. The easiest way is to give a negative characterization: $s$ is not connected if there exists $\pmb { a }$ partition $S = A \cup B$ into open subsets $A$ and $B$ . It is understood that $A$ and $\pmb { B }$ are disjoint and nonempty. The connectedness of a space is often used in the following manner: Suppose that we are able to construct two complementary open subsets $A$ and $\boldsymbol { B }$ of $s$ ; if $\pmb { S }$ is connected, we may conclude that either $A$ or $B$ is empty.

A subset $\pmb { { \cal E } } \subset \pmb { { \cal S } }$ is said to be connected if it is connected in the relative topology. At the risk of being pedantic we repeat:

Definition 3. A subset of a metric space is connected if it cannot be represented as the union of two disjoint relatively open sets none of which is empty.

If $\pmb { \cal E }$ is open, a subset of $\pmb { { \cal E } }$ is relatively open if and only if it is open. Similarly, if $\pmb { \mathcal { E } }$ is closed, relatively closed means the same as closed. We can therefore state: ${ \pmb A } { \pmb n }$ open set is connected if it cannot be decomposed into two open sets, and a closed set is connected if it cannot be decomposed into two closed sets. Again; none of the sets is allowed to be empty.

Trivial examples of connected sets are the empty set and any set that consists of a single point.

In the case of the real line it is possible to name all connected sets. The most important result is that the whole line is connected, and this is indeed one of the fundamental properties of the real-number system.

An interval is defined by an inequality of one of the four types: $a < x < b _ { \mathrm { : } }$ b $a \leq x < b$ b $a < x \leq b$ b $a \\leqq x \leqq b . \dagger$ For $a = - \infty$ or $b = + \infty$ this includes the semi-infinite intervals and the whole line.

Theorem l. The nonempty connected subsets of the real line are the intervals.

We reproduce one of the classical proofs, based on the fact that any monotone sequence has a finite or infinite limit.

Suppose that the real line $\mathbf { R }$ is represented as the union $\mathbf { R } = A \cup B$ of two disjoint closed sets. If neither is empty we can find ${ \pmb a } _ { 1 } \in { \pmb A }$ and $\boldsymbol { b } _ { 1 } \in \mathcal { B }$ ; we may assume that $a _ { 1 } < b _ { 1 }$ We bisect the interval $( a _ { 1 } , b _ { 1 } )$ and note that one of the two halves has its left end point in A and its right end point in $B$ We denote this interval by $( a _ { 2 } , b _ { 2 } )$ and continue the process indefinitely. In this way we obtain a sequence of nested intervals $( a _ { n } , b _ { n } )$ with ${ \pmb a } _ { n } \in { \pmb A }$ , $\smash { b _ { n } \epsilon B }$ The sequences $\left\{ a _ { n } \right\}$ and $\left\{ b _ { n } \right\}$ have a common limit c. Since $A$ and $\pmb { B }$ are closed $\pmb { c }$ would have to be a common point of $A$ and $\pmb { B }$ . This contradiction shows that either $A$ or $\pmb { B }$ is empty, and hence $\mathbf { R }$ is connected.

With minor modifications the same proof applies to any interval.

Before proving the converse we make an important remark. Let $\pmb { \mathscr { E } }$ be an arbitrary subset of $\mathbf { R }$ and call $\pmb { \alpha }$ a lower bound of $\pmb { \cal E }$ if $\alpha \leq x$ for all ${ \pmb x } \in { \pmb E }$ Consider the set $A$ of all lower bounds. It is evident that the complement of $A$ is open. As to $A$ itself it is easily seen that $\pmb { A }$ is open whenever it does not contain any largest number. Because the line is connected, $A$ and its complement cannot both be open unless one of them yThee r thus thee pois: $\pmb { A }$ is empty, $A$ contains a largest number, or $A$ is the whole line. The largest number $\pmb { a }$ of $\pmb { A }$ $\pmb { \cal E }$ ; it is commonly denoted as g.l.b. x or inf x for x e E. If A is empty, we agree to set ${ \pmb a } = - \infty$ , and if $A$ is the whole line we set $a = + \infty$ With this convenon very   bers h niquely i lower bound;it is clear that a=and only if the t E is pty. $\mathbf { b y } \ [ a , b ] .$ $^ { ( a , b ) }$ and the closed interval

The least upper bound, denoted as I.u.b. $\pmb { x }$ or sup $\pmb { x }$ for ${ \boldsymbol { x } } \in { \boldsymbol { E } }$ is defined in a corresponding manner. $^ \dagger$

Returning to the proof we assume that $\pmb { \mathcal { E } }$ is a connected set with the greatest lower bound $\pmb { a }$ and the least upper bound $^ b$ All points of $\pmb { \mathcal { E } }$ lie between $\pmb { a }$ and $^ { b }$ , limits included. Suppose that a point $\pmb { \xi }$ from the interval $a < \xi < b$ did not belong to $\pmb { \cal E }$ Then the open sets $x < \xi { \mathrm { ~ a n d ~ } } x > \xi$ would cover $\mathbf { \nabla } \mathbf { E }$ , and because $E$ is connected one of them must fail to intersect $\mathbfit { \Delta } E$ Suppose, for instance, that no point of $\mathbfit { \Delta } E$ lies to the left of $\pmb { \xi }$ In this case ${ \pmb \xi }$ would be a lower bound in contradiction with the fact that $^ { a }$ was the greatest lower bound. The opposite assumption would lead to a similar contradiction, and we conclude that $\pmb { \xi }$ must belong to $\pmb { { \cal E } }$ Hence $\mathbf { \nabla } \mathbf { E }$ is identical with one of the four intervals $^ { ( a , b ) }$ , and the proof is completed.

In the course of the proof we have introduced the notions of greatest lower bound and least upper bound. If the set is closed and if the bounds are finite, they must belong to the set, in which case they are called the minimum and the maximum. In order to be sure that the bounds are finite we must know that the set is not empty and that there is some finite lower bound and some finite upper bound. In other words, the set must lie in a finite interval; such a set is said to be bounded. We have proved:

Theorem 2. Any closed and bounded nonempty set of real numbers has a minimum and a maximum.

The structure of connected sets in the plane is not nearly so simple as in the case of the line, but the following characterization of open connected sets contains essentially all the information we shall need.

Theorem 3. A nonempty open set in the plane is connected if and only if any two of its points can be joined by a polygon which lies in the set.

The notion of a joining polygon is so simple that we need not give a formal definition.

We prove first that the condition is necessary. Let $A$ be an open connected set, and choose a point ${ \pmb { a } } \in A$ We denote by $A _ { 1 }$ the subset of $A$ whose points can be joined to $\pmb { a }$ by polygons in $A$ , and by $\pmb { A _ { 2 } }$ the subset whose points cannot be so joined. Let us prove that $A _ { 1 }$ and $A _ { 2 }$ are both open. First, if $\pmb { a _ { 1 } } \in \varLambda _ { 1 }$ there exists a neighborhood $| z - a _ { 1 } | < \varepsilon$ contained in $A$ . All points in this neighborhood can be joined to ${ \pmb a } _ { \bf 1 }$ by a line segment, and from there to $\pmb { a }$ by a polygon. Hence the whole neighborhood † The supremum of a sequence was introduced already in Chap. 2, Sec. 2.1.

is contained in $A _ { 1 } ,$ and $\scriptstyle A _ { 1 }$ is open. Secondly, if $\displaystyle a _ { 2 } \epsilon A _ { 2 } ,$ ,let $| z - a _ { 2 } | < \varepsilon$ be a neighborhood contained in $A$ . If a point in this neighborhood could be joined to $\pmb { a }$ by a polygon, then ${ \pmb a } _ { \mathfrak { z } }$ could be joined to this point by a line segment, and from there to $\pmb { a }$ This is contrary to the definition of $A _ { 2 } ,$ and we conclude that $A _ { 2 }$ is open. Since $A$ was connected either $A _ { 1 }$ or $A _ { 2 }$ must be empty. But $A _ { 1 }$ contains the point $\pmb { a }$ ; hence $A _ { 2 }$ is empty, and all points can be joined to $\pmb { a }$ Finally, any two points in $A$ can be joined bv way of ${ \pmb a } _ { \imath }$ and we have proved that the condition is necessary.

For future use we remark that it is even possible to join any two points by a polygon whose sides are parallel to the coordinate axes. The proof is the same.

In order to prove the sufficiency we assume that $A$ has a representation $A = A _ { 1 } \cup A _ { 2 }$ as the union of two disjoint open sets. Choose $\alpha _ { 1 } \epsilon A _ { 1 , }$ . $\mathbf { \sigma } _ { a _ { 2 } \in A _ { 2 } } .$ and suppose that these points can be joined by a polygon in $A$ . One of the sides of the polygon must then join a point in $A _ { 1 }$ to a point in $\scriptstyle A _ { 2 } ,$ and for this reason it is sufficient to consider the case where ${ \pmb a } _ { \mathbf { 1 } }$ and $\mathbf { \delta } \mathbf { a } \mathbf { \cdot }$ are joined by a line segment. This segment has a parametric representation $\textit { z } = a _ { 1 } + t ( a _ { 2 } - a _ { 1 } )$ where $\pmb { t }$ runs through the interval $0 \leq t \leq 1$ . The subsets of the interval $0 < t < 1$ which correspond to points in $A _ { 1 }$ and $A _ { 2 } ,$ ,respectively, are evidently open, disjoint, and nonvoid. This contradicts the connectedness of the interval, and we have proved that the condition of the theorem is sufficient.

The theorem generalizes easily to $\mathbf { R } ^ { n }$ and $\mathbf { C } ^ { n }$ .

# Definition 4. A nonempty connected open set is called a region.

By Theorem 3 the whole plane, an open disk $| z - a | < \rho _ { ; }$ ,and a half plane are regions. The same is true of any δ-neighborhood in $\mathbf { R } ^ { n }$ . A region is the more-dimensional analogue of an open interval. The closure of a region is called a closed region. It should be observed that different regions may have the same closure.

It happens frequently that we have to analyze the structure of sets which are defined very implicitly, for instance in the course of a proof. In    - nected components. As the name indicates, a component of a set is a connected subset which is not contained in any larger connected subset.

Th .

If $\mathcal { E }$ is the given set, consider a point ${ \pmb a } \in { \pmb E }$ and let $C ( { a } )$ denote the union of all connected subsets of $E$ that contain $\pmb { a }$ Then $C ( { \pmb a } )$ is sure to contain ${ \pmb a } _ { \pmb { \imath } }$ $\pmb { a }$ is connected. If we can show that $\pmb { C } ( \pmb { a } )$ is connected, then it is a maximal connected set, in other words a component. It would follow, moreover, that any two components are either disjoint or identical, which is precisely what we want to prove. Indeed, if $c \in C ( a ) \cap C ( b )$ ,then $C ( a ) \subset C ( c )$ by the definition of $C ( \pmb { c } )$ and the connectedness of $C ( { \pmb a } )$ Hence $a \in C ( c )$ , and by the same reasoning $C ( c ) \subset C ( a )$ , so that in fact $C ( \boldsymbol { a } ) = C ( \boldsymbol { c } )$ Similarly $C ( b ) = C ( c )$ ,and consequently $C ( { \boldsymbol a } ) = C ( { \boldsymbol b } )$ We call $C ( { \boldsymbol { a } } )$ the component of $\pmb { a }$

Suppose that $C ( \pmb { a } )$ were not connected. Then we could find relatively open sets $A$ , $B \neq 0$ such that $C ( d ) = A \cup B$ b $A \cap B = 0$ We may assume that ${ \pmb { a } } \in { \pmb { A } }$ while $\pmb { B }$ contains a point $^ { b }$ Since $b \in C ( a )$ there is a connected set $\mathbfcal { E } _ { 0 } \subset \mathbb { E }$ which contains $\pmb { a }$ and $^ { b }$ The representation $E _ { 0 } = ( E _ { 0 } \cap A ) \cup ( E _ { 0 } \cap B )$ would be a decomposition into relatively open subsets, and since $a \in E _ { 0 } \cap A$ , $b \in E _ { 0 } \cap B$ neither part would be empty. This is a contradiction, and we conclude that $\mathbf { } C ( \pmb { a } )$ is connected.

Theorem 5. ${ \pmb { I } } { \pmb { n } } \pmb { \mathbb { R } } ^ { n }$ the components of any open set are open.

This is a consequence of the fact that the $\pmb { \delta }$ -neighborhoods in $\mathbf { R } ^ { n }$ are connected. Consider $a \in C ( a ) \subset E$ If $\pmb { \cal E }$ is open it contains an ${ \cal N } _ { \mathfrak { d } } ( a )$ , and because ${ \cal N } _ { \delta } ( a )$ is connected $N _ { \mathfrak { s } } ( a ) \subset C ( a )$ Hence $\mathbf { } C ( \pmb { a } )$ is open. A little more generally the assertion is true for any space $\pmb { S }$ which is locally connected. By this we mean that any neighborhood of a point $\pmb { a }$ contains a connected neighborhood of $\pmb { a }$ The proof is left to the reader.

In the case of $\mathbf { R } ^ { n }$ we can conclude, furthermore, that the number of components is countable. To see this we observe that every open set must contain a point with rational coordinates. The set of points with rational coordinates is countable, and may thus be expressed as a sequence $\left\{ p _ { k } \right\}$ . For each component $C ( \pmb { a } )$ , determine the smallest $\pmb { k }$ such that $p _ { k } \in { \cal { C } } ( a )$ . To different components correspond different $\pmb { k }$ We conclude that the components are in one-to-one correspondence with a subset of the natural numbers, and consequently the set of components is countable.

For instance, every open subset of $\mathbf { R }$ is $\pmb { a }$ countable union of disjoint open intervals.

Again, it is possible to analyze the proof and thereby arrive at a more general result. We shall say that a set $\pmb { \cal E }$ is dense in $\pmb { S }$ if $\mathbf { \delta E ^ { - } } = \mathbf { \delta S } ,$ and we shall say that a metric space is separable if there exists a countable subset which is dense in S. We are led to the following result:

In a locally connected separable space every open set is $\pmb { a }$ countable union of disjoint regions.

1.4. Compactness. The notions of convergent sequences and Cauchy sequences are obviously meaningful in any metric space. Indeed, we would say that $x _ { n } \to x$ if $d ( x _ { n } , x ) \to 0 ,$ ,and we would say that $\{ x _ { n } \}$ is a Cauchy sequence if $d ( x _ { n } , x _ { m } ) \to 0$ as $\pmb { n }$ and m tend to $\infty$ . It is clear that every convergent sequence is a Cauchy sequence. For $\mathbf { R }$ and $\mathbf { C }$ we have proved the converse, namely that every Cauchy sequence is convergent (Chap. 2, Sec. 2.1), and it is not hard to see that this property carries over to any $\mathbf { R } ^ { n }$ . In view of its importance the property deserves a special name.

Definiton 5. A metric space is said to be complete if every Cauchy sequence is convergent.

A subset is complete if it is complete when regarded as a subspace. The reaeiy h $\pmb { a }$ m

We shal now introduce the stronger concept of compactness. It is strongr than copleteness i the sese that evy copact spac i p the copct subsets  R n r the clos buned s. In this result it would be possible to dispense with the notion o compactness and closed sets of real or complex numbers. The outcome would be that we would have to repeat essentially the same proof in many different connections.

There are several equivalent characterizations of compactness, and it is a matter of taste which one to choose as definition. Whatever we do the uninitiated reader will feel somewhat bewildered, for he will not be able to discern the purpose of the definition. This is not surprising, for it took a whole generation of mathematicians to agree on the best approach. The consensus of present opinion is that it is best to focus the attention on the different ways in which a given set can be covered by open sets.

Let us say that a collection of open sets is an open covering of a set $X$ if $\pmb { X }$ is contained in the union of the open sets. A subcovering is a subcollection with the same property, and a finite covering is one that consists of a finite number of sets. The definition of compactness reads:

Definition 6. $A$ set $\pmb { X }$ is compact if and only if every open covering of $\pmb { X }$ contains $\pmb { a }$ finite subcovering.

In this context we are thinking of $X$ as a subset of a metric space S, and the covering is by open sets of $s$ But if $U$ is an open set in $\pmb { S }$ , then $U \cap X$ is an open subset of $X$ (a relatively open set), and conversely every open subset of $X$ can be expressed in this form (Sec. 1.3, Ex. 1). For this reason it makes no difference whether we formulate the definition for a full space or for a subset.

The property in the definition is frequently referred to as the Heine-Borel property. Its importance lies in the fact that many proofs become particularly simple when formulated in terms of open coverings.

We prove first that every compact space is complete. Suppose that $X$ is compact, and let $\{ x _ { n } \}$ be a Cauchy sequence in $\boldsymbol { X }$ .If $_ y$ is not the limit of $\{ x _ { n } \}$ there exists an $\mathfrak { E } > \mathbf { 0 }$ such that $d ( x _ { n } , y ) > 2 \varepsilon$ for infinitely many $\pmb { n }$ Determine $\scriptstyle n _ { 0 }$ such that $d ( x _ { m } , x _ { n } ) < \mathfrak { e }$ for m $, n \geq n _ { 0 }$ We chpose a fixed $n \not \equiv n _ { 0 }$ for which $d ( x _ { n } , y ) > 2 \varepsilon$ Then $d ( x _ { m } , y ) \geq d ( x _ { n } , y ) - d ( x _ { m } , x _ { n } )$ $> \ E$ for all $m \geq n _ { 0 }$ It follows that the e-neighborhood $N _ { \mathfrak { e } } ( y )$ contains only finitely many $x _ { n }$ (better: contains $x _ { n }$ only for finitely many $\pmb { n }$ ).

Consider now the collection of all open sets $U$ which contain only finitely many ${ \pmb x } _ { \pmb { \mathscr { n } } }$ .If $\{ x _ { n } \}$ is not convergent, it follows by the preceding reasoning that this collection is an open covering of $X$ Therefore it must contain a finite subcovering, formed by $U _ { 1 , }$ . . . , $U _ { N }$ But that is clearly impossible, for since each $U _ { i }$ contains only finitely many ${ \pmb x } _ { \pmb n }$ it would follow that the given sequence is finite.

Secondly, a compact set is necessarily bounded (a metric space is bounded if all distances lie under ${ \mathfrak { a } }$ finite bound). To see this, choose a point $\pmb { x _ { 0 } }$ and consider all neighborhoods $N _ { \rho } ( x _ { 0 } ) \dag$ This is an open covering, and if $\pmb { X }$ is compact it contains a finite subcovering; in other words, $\overrightarrow { X } \subset N _ { \rho _ { 1 } } ( x _ { 0 } ) \cup \cdot \cdot \cdot \cup N _ { \rho _ { m } } ( x _ { 0 } )$ which means the same as $\because \Sigma \subset N _ { \rho } ( x _ { 0 } )$ with ${ \pmb \rho } = \mathrm { { \ m a x } }$ $( \rho _ { 1 } , \ldots , \rho _ { m } )$ . For any $x , y \in X$ it follows that $d ( x , y ) \leqq$ $d ( x , x _ { 0 } ) + d ( y , x _ { 0 } ) < 2 \rho _ { ; }$ ,and we have proved that $\pmb { X }$ is bounded.

But boundedness is not all we can prove. It is convenient to define a stronger property, called total boundedness:

Definition 7. $A$ set $\boldsymbol { \cal X }$ is totally bounded if, for every $\varepsilon > 0$ $X$ can be covered by finitely many e-neighborhoods.

This is certainly true of any compact set. For the collection of all e-neighborhoods is an open covering, and the compactness implies that we can select finitely many that cover $X$ We observe that a totally bounded set is necessarily bounded, for if $X \subset N _ { e } ( x _ { 1 } ) \cup \cdot \cdot \cdot \cup N _ { e } ( x _ { m } )$ then any two points of $\boldsymbol { X }$ have a distance $< 2 \tt { e } + \operatorname* { m a x } { d ( x _ { i } , x _ { j } ) }$ . (The preceding proof that any compact set is bounded becomes redundant; it was included because of its elementary nature.)

We have now proved one part of the following theorem:

Theorem 6. A set is compact if and only if it is complete and totally bounded.

To prove the other part, assume that the metric space $\pmb { S }$ is complete and totally bounded. Suppose that there exists an open covering which does not contain any finite subcovering. Set $\mathfrak { e } _ { n } = 2 ^ { - n }$ We know that $\boldsymbol { s }$ can be covered by finitely many ${ \tt e r }$ -neighborhoods. If each had a finite subcovering the same would be true of $\pmb { S }$ ; hence there exists an $\smash { N _ { \varepsilon _ { 1 } } ( x _ { 1 } ) }$ which does not admit a finite subcovering. Because $\smash { N _ { \mathrm { e } _ { 1 } } ( x _ { 1 } ) }$ is itself totally bounded we can find an $x _ { 2 } \in N _ { \mathrm { e _ { 1 } } } ( x _ { 1 } )$ such that $N _ { \mathfrak { e } _ { 2 } } ( x _ { 2 } )$ has no finite subcovering. $^ \ddagger$ It is clear how to continue the construction: we obtain a sequence $\{ x _ { n } \}$ with the property that ${ \cal N } _ { \sf e _ { n } } ( x _ { n } )$ has no finite subcovering and $\boldsymbol { x } _ { n + 1 } \in N _ { e _ { n } } ( \boldsymbol { x } _ { n } )$ The second property means that $d ( x _ { n } , x _ { n + 1 } ) < \varepsilon _ { n } ,$ and hennce $d ( x _ { n } , x _ { n + p } ) < \mathfrak { e } _ { n } + \mathfrak { e } _ { n + 1 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + \mathfrak { e } _ { n + p - 1 } < 2 ^ { - n + 1 }$ It folows that $\{ x _ { n } \}$ is Cuch eq.Itnrges t $_ y$ and this $_ y$ belongs to one of the open sets $U$ in the given covering. Becus $U$ is open it contains beighborhoods oth ae We are used to consider only small neighborhoods, but here we must include the $\pmb { \rho }$ $^ { \prime \prime } { \pmb \rho }$ spheroid'' would

$\ddagger$ Here we are using the fact that any subset of a totally bounded set is totally bounded. The reader should prove this.

an $ { N _ { \delta } } ( y )$ . Choose $\pmb { n }$ so large that $d ( x _ { n } , y ) < \delta / 2$ and $\varepsilon _ { n } < \delta / 2$ Then $N _ { e _ { n } } ( x _ { n } ) \subset N _ { \delta } ( y )$ , for $d ( x , x _ { n } ) < \mathsf { e } _ { n }$ implies $d ( x , y ) \leq d ( x , x _ { n } ) + d ( x _ { n } , y ) < \delta .$ . Therefore $N _ { \mathfrak { e } _ { n } } ( x _ { n } )$ admits a finite subcovering, namely by the set $U$ This is a contradiction, and we conclude that $s$ has the Heine-Borel property.

Corollary. A subset of $\mathbf { R }$ or $\mathbf { C }$ is compact if and only if it is closed and bounded.

We have already mentioned this particular consequence. In one direction the conclusion is immediate: We know that a compact set is bounded and complete; but $\mathbf { R }$ and $\mathbf { C }$ are complete, and complete subsets of a complete space are closed. For the opposite conclusion we need to show that every bounded set in $\mathbf { R }$ or $\mathbf { C }$ is totally bounded. Let us take the case of $\mathbf { c }$ If $X$ is bounded it is contained in a disk, and hence in a square. The square can be subdivided into a finite number of squares with arbitrarily small side, and the squares can in turn be covered by disks with arbitrarily small radius. This proves that $X$ is totally bounded, except for a small point that should not be glossed over. When Definition 7 is applied to a subset $x \subset s$ it is slightly ambiguous, for it is not clear whether the e-neighborhoods should be with respect to $X$ or with respect to S; that is, it is not clear whether we require their centers to lie on $X$ . It happens that this is of no avail. In fact, suppose that we have covered $X$ by e-neighborhoods whose centers do not necessarily lie on $X$ If such a neighborhood does not meet $X$ it is superfluous, and can be dropped. If it does contain a point from $X _ { i }$ , then we can replace it by a $\mathbf { 2 } \varepsilon .$ neighborhood around that point, and we obtain a finite covering by $\mathbf { 2 } \varepsilon .$ -neighborhoods with centers on $\pmb { X }$ For this reason the ambiguity is only apparent, and our proof that bounded subsets of $c$ are totally bounded is valid.

There is a third characterization of compact sets. It deals with the notion of limit point (sometimes called cluster value): We say that $_ y$ is a limit point of the sequence $\{ x _ { n } \}$ if there exists a subsequence $\{ x _ { n _ { k } } \}$ that converges to $_ y$ A limit point is almost the same as an accumulation point of the set formed by the points $x _ { n }$ , except that a sequence permits repetitions of the same point. If $_ y$ is a limit point, every neighborhood of $\pmb { y }$ contains infinitely many ${ \pmb x } _ { n }$ . The converse is also true. Indeed, suppose that $\Xi _ { k } \to 0$ If every $\smash { N _ { \varepsilon _ { k } } ( y ) }$ contains infinitely many $x _ { n }$ we can choose subscripts $\pmb { n } _ { k }$ , by induction, in such a way that $x _ { n _ { k } } \in N _ { \mathfrak { e } _ { k } } ( y )$ and $n _ { k + 1 } > n _ { k }$ . It is clear that $\{ x _ { n _ { k } } \}$ converges to $\pmb { y }$ .

Theorem 7. A metric space is compact if and only if every infinite sequence has $\pmb { a }$ limit point.

This theorem is usually referred to as the Bolzano-Weierstrass theorem. The original formulation was that every bounded sequence of complex numbers has a convergent subsequence. It came to be recognized as an important theorem precisely because of the role it plays in the theory of analytic functions.

The first part of the proof is a repetition of an earlier argument. If $\pmb { y }$ is not a limit point of $\{ x _ { n } \}$ it has a neighborhood which contains only finitely many $x _ { n }$ (abbreviated version of the correct phrase). If there were no limit points the open sets containing only finitely many $x _ { n }$ would form an open covering. In the compact case we could select a finite subcovering, and it would follow that the sequence is finite. The previous time we used this reasoning was to prove that a compact space is complete. We showed in essence that every sequence has a limit point, and then we observed that a Cauchy sequence with a limit point is necessarily convergent. For strict economy of thought it would thus have been better to prove Theorem 7 before Theorem 6, but we preferred to emphasize the importance of total boundedness as early as possible.

It remains to prove the converse. In the first place it is clear that the Bolzano-Weierstrass property implies completeness. Indeed, we just pointed out that a Cauchy sequence with a limit point must be convergent. Suppose now that the space is not totally bounded. Then there exists an $\mathfrak { E } > \mathbf { 0 }$ such that the space cannot be covered by finitely many e-neighborhoods. We construct a sequence $\{ x _ { n } \}$ as follows: $x _ { 1 }$ is arbitrary, and when $x _ { 1 } , \ldots , x _ { i }$ have been selected we choose $x _ { n + 1 }$ so that it does not lie in $N _ { e } ( x _ { 1 } ) \cup \cdots \cup N _ { e } ( x _ { n } )$ This is always possible because these neighborhoods do not cover the whole space. But it is clear that $\{ x _ { n } \}$ has no convergent subsequence, for $d ( x _ { m } , x _ { n } ) > \varepsilon$ for all $\textbf { \textit { m } }$ and $\pmb { \mathscr { n } }$ We conclude that the Bolzano-Weierstrass property implies total boundedness. In view of Theorem 6 that is what we had to prove.

The reader should reflect on the fact that we have exhibited three characterizations of compactness whose logical equivalence is not at all trivial. It should be clear that results of this kind are particularly valuabl ore ur eti pro
