1.5. Continuous Functions. We shall consider functions $\pmb { f }$ which are defined on a metric space $\boldsymbol { s }$ and have values in another metric space $S ^ { \prime }$ . Functions are also referred to as mappings: we say that $f$ maps $\boldsymbol { s }$ into $S ^ { \prime }$ , and we write $f : S  S ^ { \prime }$ . Naturally, we shall be mainly concerned with real- or complex-valued functions; occasionally the latter are allowed to take values in the extended complex plane, ordinary distance being replaced by distance on the Riemann sphere.

The space $\pmb { \ S }$ is the domain of the function. We are of course free to consider functions $\pmb { f }$ whose domain is only a subset of $s$ , in which case the domain is regarded as a subspace. In most cases it is safe to slur over the distinction: a function on $\pmb { S }$ and its restriction to a subset are usually denoted by the same symbol. If $x \subset s$ the set of all values $f ( x )$ for ${ \pmb x } \in { \pmb S }$ is called the image of $\pmb { X }$ under $f _ { i }$ and it is denoted by $f ( X )$ The inverse image f $^ 1 ( X ^ { \prime } )$ of $X ^ { \prime } \subset S ^ { \prime }$ consists of all $x \in S$ such that $f ( x ) \in X ^ { \prime }$ Observe that $f ( f ^ { - 1 } ( X ^ { \prime } ) ) \subset X ^ { \prime }$ , and $f ^ { - 1 } ( f ( X ) ) \supset X .$ .

The definition of a continuous function needs practically no modification: $f$ is continuous at $\pmb { a }$ if to every ${ \mathfrak { E } } > { \mathbf { 0 } }$ there exists $\delta > 0$ such that $d ( x , a ) < \delta$ implies $d ^ { \prime } ( f ( x ) , f ( a ) ) < \varepsilon .$ We are mainly concerned with functions that are continuous at all points in the domain of definition. The following characterizations are immediate consequences of the definition:

A function is continuous if and only if the inverse image of every open set is open.

A function is continuous if and only if the inverse image of every closed set is closed.

If $\pmb { f }$ is not defined on all of $\pmb { S }$ , the words "open" and "closed," when referring to the inverse image, should of course be interpreted relatively to the domain of $f$ It is very important to observe that these properties hold only for the inverse image, not for the direct image. For instance the mapping $f ( x ) = x ^ { 2 } / ( 1 + x ^ { 2 } )$ of $\mathbf { R }$ into R has the image $f ( \mathbf { R } ) =$ $\{ y ; 0 \le y < 1 \}$ which is neither open nor closed. In this example $f ( \mathbf { R } )$

fails to be closed because R is not compact. In fact, the following is true:

Theorem 8. Under a continuous mapping the image of every compact set is compact, and consequently closed.

Suppose that $\pmb { f }$ is defined and continuous on the compact set $X$ . Consider a covering of $f ( X )$ by open sets $U$ The inverse images $f ^ { - 1 } ( U )$ are open and form a covering of $\pmb { X }$ Because $\ b X$ is compact we can select a finite subcovering: $X \subset f ^ { - 1 } ( U _ { 1 } ) \cup \cdot \cdot \cdot \cup f ^ { - 1 } ( U _ { m } )$ . It follows that $f ( X ) \subset U _ { 1 } \cup \cdot \cdot \cdot \cup U _ { m } ,$ and we have proved that $f ( X )$ is compact.

Corollary. A continuous real-valued function on a compact set has a maximum and a minimum.

The image is a closed bounded subset of $\mathbf { R }$ The existence of a maximum and a minimum follows by Theorem 2.

Theorem 9. Under a continuous mapping the image of any connected set is connected.

We may assume that $\pmb { f }$ is defined and continuous on the whole space $s ,$ and that $f ( S )$ is all of ${ \mathbf { } } ^ { g \prime }$ Suppose that $S ^ { \prime } = A \cup B$ where $A$ and $\pmb { B }$ are open and disjoint. Then $S = f ^ { - 1 } ( A ) \cup f ^ { - 1 } ( B )$ is a representation of $\pmb { S }$ as a union of disjoint open sets. If $\pmb { S }$ is connected either $f ^ { - 1 } ( A ) = 0$ or $f ^ { - 1 } ( B ) = 0 ,$ and hence $\boldsymbol { A } = \mathbf { 0 }$ or $\boldsymbol { B } = \mathbf { 0 }$ We conclude that $s ^ { \prime }$ is connected.

A typical application is the assertion that a real-valued function which is continuous and never zero on a connected set is either always positive or always negative. In fact, the image is connected, and hence an interval. But an interval which contains positive and negative numbers also contains zero.

A mapping $f : S \to S ^ { \prime }$ is said to be one to one if $f ( x ) = f ( y )$ only for $x = y$ ; it is said to be onto if $f ( S ) = S ^ { \prime } . \dag$ A mapping with both these properties has an inverse $f ^ { - 1 }$ ,defined on $s ^ { \prime }$ ; it satisfies $f ^ { - 1 } ( f ( x ) ) = x$ and $f ( f ^ { - 1 } ( x ^ { \prime } ) ) = x ^ { \prime }$ In this situation, if $\pmb { f }$ and $f ^ { - 1 }$ are both continuous we say that $f$ is a topological mapping or a homeomorphism. A property of a set which is shared by all topological images is called a topological property. For instance, we have proved that compactness and connectedness are topological properties (Theorems 8 and 9). In this connection it is per-

† These linguistically clumsy terms can be replaced by injective (for one to one) and surjective (for onto). A mapping with both properties is called bijective.

haps useful to point out that the property of being an open subset is not topological. If $\boldsymbol { x } \subset \boldsymbol { s }$ and $\pmb { Y } \subset \pmb { S ^ { \prime } }$ and if $X$ is homeomorphic to $Y$ there is no reason why $\pmb { X }$ and $\pmb { Y }$ should be simultaneously open. It happens to be true if $S = S ^ { \prime } = \mathbf { R } ^ { n }$ (invariance of the region), but this is a deep theorem that we shall not need.

The notion of uniform continuity will be in constant use. Quite generally, a condition is said to hold uniformly with respect to a parameter if it can be expressed by inequalities which do not involve the parameter. Accordingly, a function $\pmb { f }$ is said to be uniformly continuous on $\boldsymbol { \cal X }$ if, to every ${ \mathfrak { e } } > { \mathbf { 0 } }$ ,there exists a $\delta > 0$ such that $d ^ { \prime } ( f ( x _ { 1 } ) , f ( x _ { 2 } ) ) < \mathfrak { s }$ for all pairs $( x _ { 1 } , x _ { 2 } )$ with $d ( x _ { 1 } , x _ { 2 } ) < \delta$ The emphasis is on the fact that $\pmb { \delta }$ is not allowed to depend on $\pmb { x _ { 1 } }$ .

Theorem 10. On a compact set every continuous function is uniformly continuous.

The proof is typical of the way the Heine-Borel property can be used. Suppose that $\pmb { f }$ is continuous on a compact set $\pmb { X }$ . Every $y \in X$ has a neighborhood $N _ { \rho } ( y )$ such that $d ^ { \prime } ( f ( x ) , f ( y ) ) < \mathfrak { e } / 2$ for $x \in N _ { \rho } ( y )$ ; here $\pmb { \rho }$ may depend on $_ y$ Consider the open covering of $\pmb { X }$ by the smaller neighborhoods $N _ { \rho / 2 } ( y )$ . We can find a finite subcovering: $X \subset N _ { \rho _ { 1 } / 2 } ( y _ { 1 } ) \cup$ $\cdots \cup N _ { \rho _ { m } / 2 } ( y _ { m } )$ Let $\pmb { \delta }$ be the smallest of the numbers $\rho _ { 1 } / 2$ , . . . , $\pmb { \rho _ { m } } / 2$ Consider a pair $( x _ { 1 } , x _ { 2 } )$ with $d ( x _ { 1 } , x _ { 2 } ) < \delta$ There exists a $_ { y _ { k } }$ with $d ( x _ { 1 } , y _ { k } ) < \rho _ { k } / 2 $ and we obtain $d ( x _ { 2 } , y _ { k } ) < \rho _ { k } / 2 + \delta \le \rho _ { k } .$ Hence $d ^ { \prime } ( f ( x _ { 1 } ) , f ( y _ { k } ) ) < \mathfrak { e } / 2$ and $d ^ { \prime } ( f ( x _ { 2 } ) , f ( y _ { k } ) ) < \mathfrak { e } / 2$ so that $d ^ { \prime } ( f ( x _ { 1 } ) , f ( x _ { 2 } ) ) < \mathfrak { e }$ as desired.

On sets which are not compact some continuous functions are uniformly continuous and others are not. For instance, the function $\pmb { z }$ is uniformly continuous on the whole complex plane, but the function $z ^ { 2 }$ is not.

# EXERCISES

1. Construct a topological mapping of the open disk $| z | < 1$ onto the whole plane. 2. Prove that a subset of the real line which is topologically equivalent to an open interval is an open interval. (Consider the effect of removing a point.) 3. Prove that every continuous one-to-one mapping of a compact space is topological. (Show that closed sets are mapped on closed sets.) 4. Let $\pmb { X }$ and $Y$ be closed sets in a complete metric space, at least one of them compact. Prove that there exist $x \in X _ { i }$ $y \in Y$ such that $\scriptstyle d ( x , y )$ is a minimum.

5. Which of the following functions are uniformly continuous on the whole real line: sin $x , x$ sin $x , x$ sin $( x ^ { 2 } )$ , $| x | ^ { \frac { 3 } { 2 } }$ sin $x ?$

1.6. Topological Spaces. It is not necessary, and not always convenient, to express nearness in terms of distance. The observant reader will have noticed that most results in the preceding sections were formulated in terms of open sets. True enough, we used distances to define open sets, but there is really no strong reason to do this. If we decide to consider the open sets as the primary objects we must postulate axioms that they have to satisfy. The following axioms lead to the commonly accepted definition of a topological space:

Definition 8. A topological space is $\pmb { a }$ set $\pmb { T }$ together with a collection of its subsets, called open sets. The following conditions have to be fulfilled:

(i) The empty set 0 and the whole space $\pmb { T }$ are open sets.   
(ii) The intersection of any two open sets is an open set.   
(ii) The union of an arbitrary collection of open sets is an open set.

We recognize at once that this terminology is consistent with our earlier definition of an open subset of a metric space. Indeed, properties (ii) and (iii) were strongly emphasized, and (i) is trivial.

Closed sets are the complements of open sets, and it is immediately clear how to define interior, closure, boundary, and so on. Neighborhoods could be avoided, but they are rather convenient: $N$ is a neighborhood of $_ { x }$ if there exists an open set $U$ such that $x \in U$ and $U \subset N$ .

Connectedness was defined purely by means of open sets. Hence the definition carries over to topological spaces, and the theorems remain true. The Heine-Borel property is also one that deals only with open sets. Therefore it makes perfect sense to speak of a compact topological space. However, Theorem 6 becomes meaningless, and Theorem 7 becomes false.

As a matter of fact, the first serious difficulty we encounter is with convergent sequences. The definition is clear: we say that $x _ { n } \to x$ if every neighborhood of $\pmb { x }$ contains all but a finite number of the ${ \boldsymbol { x } } _ { n }$ . But if $x _ { n } \to x$ and $x _ { n } \to y$ we are not able to prove that $x = y$ This awkward situation is remedied by introducing a new axiom which characterizes the topological space as a Hausdorf space:

Definition 9. A topological space is called a Hausdorff space if any two distinct points are contained in disjoint open sets.

In other words, if $x \not = y$ we require the existence of open sets $U , V$ such that $x \in U , y \in V$ and $U \cap V = \mathbf { 0 } .$ In the presence of this condition it is obvious that the limit of a convergent sequence is unique. We shall never in this book have occasion to consider a space that is not a Hausdorff space.

This is not the place to give examples of topologies that cannot be derived from a distance function. Such examples would necessarily be very complicated and would not further the purposes of this book. The point is that it may be unnatural to introduce a distance in situations when one is not really needed. The reason for including this section has been to alert the reader that distances are dispensable.

# 2. CONFORMALITY

We now return to our original setting where all functions and variables are restricted to real or complex numbers. The role of metric spaces will seem disproportionately small: all we actually need are some simple applications of connectedness and compactness.

The whole section is mainly descriptive. It centers on the geometric consequences of the existence of a derivative.

2.1. Arcs and Closed Curves. The equation of an arc $\pmb { \gamma }$ in the plane is most conveniently given in parametric form $x = x ( t )$ , $y = y ( t )$ where $t$ runs through an interval $\alpha \leq t \leq \beta$ and $x ( t ) , y ( t )$ are continuous functions. We can also use the complex notation $z = z ( t ) = x ( t ) + i y ( t )$ which has several advantages.

Considered as a point set an arc is thus the image of a closed finite interval under a continuous mapping. As such it is compact and connected. However, an arc is not merely a set of points, but very essentially also a succession of points, ordered by increasing values of the parameter. If a nondecreasing function $t = \varphi ( \tau )$ maps an interval $\alpha ^ { \prime } \leq \tau \leq \beta ^ { \prime }$ onto $\alpha \leq t \leq \beta _ { \mathrm { { i } } }$ ,then $z = z ( \varphi ( \tau ) )$ defines the same succession of points as $z = z ( t )$ . We say that the first equation arises from the second by a change of parameter. The change is reversible if and only if $\varphi ( \tau )$ is strictly increasing. For instance, the equation $z = t ^ { 2 } + i t ^ { 4 }$ , $0 \leq t \leq 1$ arises by a reversible change of parameter from the equation $z = t + i t ^ { 2 }$ , $0 \leq t \leq 1$ A change of the parametric interval $( \alpha , \beta )$ can always be brought about by a linear change of parameter, which is one of the form $\mathbf { \nabla } t = \mathbf { \nabla } a \tau + \mathbf { \nabla } b$ , ${ \pmb a } > { \bf 0 }$ .

Logically, the simplest course is to consider two arcs as different as soon as they are given by different equations, regardless of whether one equation may arise from the other by a change of parameter. In following this course, as we will, it is important to show that certain properties of arcs are invariant under a change of parameter. For instance, the initial and terminal point of an arc remain the same after a change of parameter.

If the derivative $z ^ { \prime } ( t ) = x ^ { \prime } ( t ) + i y ^ { \prime } ( t )$ exists and is $\bf { \neq 0 }$ , the arc $\gamma$ has a tangent whose direction is determined by arg ${ \boldsymbol { z } } ^ { \prime } ( t )$ . We shall say that the arc is differentiable if ${ \pmb z } ^ { \prime } ( t )$ exists and is continuous (the term continuously differentiable is too unwieldy); if, in addition, $z ^ { \prime } ( t ) \neq 0$ the arc is said to be regular. An arc is piecewise differentiable or piecewise regular if the same conditions hold except for a finite number of values $\pmb { t }$ ; at these points ${ \boldsymbol z } ( t )$ shall still be continuous with left and right derivatives which are equal to the left and right limits of $z ^ { \prime } ( t )$ and, in the case of a piecewise regular arc, $\neq \mathbf { 0 }$ .

The differentiable or regular character of an arc is invariant under the change of parameter $t = \varphi ( \tau )$ provided that $\varphi ^ { \prime } ( \tau )$ is continuous and, for regularity, $\bf { \neq 0 }$ When this is the case, we speak of a differentiable or regular change of parameter.

An arc is simple, or a Jordan arc, if $z ( t _ { 1 } ) = z ( t _ { 2 } )$ only for $t _ { 1 } = t _ { 2 }$ An arc is a closed curve if the end points coincide: $z ( \alpha ) = z ( \beta )$ For closed curves a shift of the parameter is defined as follows: If the original equation is $z = z ( t )$ , $\alpha \leq t \leq \beta _ { \mathrm { { i } } }$ we choose a point $t _ { 0 }$ from the interval $( \alpha , \beta )$ and define a new closed curve whose equation is $z = z ( t )$ for $t _ { 0 } \leq t \leq \beta$ and $z = z ( t - \beta + \alpha )$ for $\beta \le t \le t _ { 0 } + \beta - \alpha .$ The purpose of the shift is to get rid of the distinguished position of the initial point. The correct definitions of a differentiable or regular closed curve and of a simple closed curve (or Jordan curve) are obvious.

The opposite arc of $z = z ( t )$ , $\alpha \leq t \leq \beta$ , is the arc $z = z ( - t ) , - \beta \leq$ $t \leq - \alpha$ Opposite arcs are sometimes denoted by $\pmb { \gamma }$ and $- \gamma _ { 1 }$ sometimes by $\gamma$ and $\gamma ^ { - 1 }$ ,depending on the connection. A constant function ${ \boldsymbol { z } } ( t )$ defines a point curve.

A circle $c$ , originally defined as a locus $| z - a | = r _ { ; }$ , can be considered as a closed curve with the equation ${ z = a + r e ^ { i t } }$ , $0 \leq t \leq 2 \pi$ We will use this standard parametrization whenever a finite circle is introduced. This convention saves us from writing down the equation each time it is needed; also, and this is its most important purpose, it serves as a definite rule 'to distinguish between $c$ and $- c$ .

2.2. Analytic Functions in Regions. When we consider the derivative

$$
f ^ { \prime } ( z ) = \operatorname* { l i m } _ { h \to 0 } { \frac { f ( z + h ) - f ( z ) } { h } }
$$

of a complex-valued function, defined on a set $A$ in the complex plane, it is of course understood that $z \in A$ and that the limit is with respect to values h such that $z + h \in A$ . The existence of the derivative will therefore have different meaning depending on whether $\pmb { z }$ is an interior point or a boundary point of $\pmb { A }$ . The only simple way to avoid this is to insist that all analytic functions be defined on open sets. We shall find that further advantages ensue if every analytic function is defined in a region.

We give a formal statement of the definition:

Definition 10. A complex-valued function $f ( z )$ is said to be analytic in the region $\pmb { \Omega }$ if it is defined and has a derivative at each point of $\pmb { \Omega }$ .

According to this definition an analytic function in $\pmb { \Omega }$ is always singlevalued. It is very important that the definition is localized to a fixed region $\Omega _ { s }$ , and it is not permissible to speak of an analytic function without specifying the region in which it is considered. Sometimes the region is clearly implied by the context, and in such cases the explicit reference may be omitted.

For greater flexibility of the language it is desirable to introduce the following complement to Definition 11:

Definition 1l. A function $f ( z )$ is analytic on an arbitrary point set A if it is analytic in some region which contains $\pmb { A }$

It is clear that the last definition is merely an agreement to use a convenient terminology. This is a case in which the region $\pmb { \Omega }$ need not be explicitly mentioned, for it will be found that the specific choice of $\pmb { \Omega }$ is immaterial as long as it contains $A$ . A typical application is the use of the phrase: "Let $f ( z )$ be analytic at ${ \mathfrak { z } } _ { 0 }$ " It means that a function $f ( z )$ is defined and has a derivative in some neighborhood of ${ \mathfrak { z } } _ { 0 }$ which need not be specified.

Although our definition requires all analytic functions to be singlevalued, it is possible to consider such multiple-valued functions as ${ \sqrt { z } } ,$ log $\pmb { z } _ { \pmb { \imath } }$ ,or arc cos $\pmb { z }$ , provided that they are restricted to a definite region in which it is possible to select a single-valued and analytic branch of the function.

For instance, we may choose for $\pmb { \Omega }$ the complement of the negative real axis ${ \boldsymbol { z } } \leq \mathbf { 0 }$ ; this set is indeed open and connected. In $\pmb { \Omega }$ one and only one of the values of $\sqrt { z }$ has a positive real part. With this choice ${ \pmb w } = \sqrt { \tilde { z } }$ becomes a single-valued function in $\pmb { \Omega }$ ; let us prove that it is continuous. Choose two points ${ \pmb z _ { 1 } }$ , $z _ { 2 } \epsilon \Omega$ and denote the corresponding values of w by $\boldsymbol { w } _ { 1 } = u _ { 1 } + i \ v { v } _ { 1 } ,$ . ${ w _ { 2 } = u _ { 2 } + i v _ { 2 } }$ with $u _ { 1 } , u _ { 2 } > 0$ Then

$$
\left| z _ { 1 } - z _ { 2 } \right| = \left| w _ { 1 } ^ { 2 } - w _ { 2 } ^ { 2 } \right| = \left| w _ { 1 } - w _ { 2 } \right| \cdot \left| w _ { 1 } + w _ { 2 } \right|
$$

and $| w _ { 1 } + w _ { 2 } | \geq u _ { 1 } + u _ { 2 } > u _ { 1 } .$ Hence

$$
| w _ { 1 } - w _ { 2 } | < \frac { | z _ { 1 } - z _ { 2 } | } { u _ { 1 } }
$$

and it follows that $\pmb { w } = \pmb { \ V } _ { \pmb { z } } ^ { - }$ is continuous at ${ \pmb z _ { 1 } }$ Once the continuity is established the analyticity follows by derivation of the inverse function $z = w ^ { 2 }$ . Indeed, with the notations used in calculus $\pmb { \Delta } z  \mathbf { 0 }$ implies $\Delta w \to 0$ Therefore,

$$
\operatorname* { l i m } _ { \Delta z \to 0 } { \frac { \Delta w } { \Delta z } } = \operatorname* { l i m } _ { \Delta w \to 0 } { \frac { \Delta w } { \Delta z } }
$$

and we obtain

$$
{ \frac { d w } { d z } } = { \frac { 1 } { \frac { d z } { d w } } } = { \frac { 1 } { 2 w } } = { \frac { 1 } { 2 \sqrt { z } } }
$$

with the same branch of ${ \sqrt { z } } .$

In the case of log $\pmb { z }$ we can use the same region $\mathfrak { a }$ obtained by excluding the negative real axis, and define the principal branch of the logarithm by the condition $\left| \operatorname { I m } \log z \right| < \pi$ Again, the continuity must be proved, but this time we have no algebraic identity at our disposal, and we are forced to use a more general reasoning. Denote the principal branch by $w = u + i v = \log \ z .$ For a given point $w _ { 1 } = u _ { 1 } + i v _ { 1 } , \ | v _ { 1 } | < \pi _ { : }$ and a given ${ \mathfrak { e } } > { \mathbf { 0 } }$ , consider the set $\pmb { A }$ in the $\pmb { w }$ -plane which is defined by the inequalities $\begin{array} { r } { | w - w _ { 1 } | \ge \varepsilon , | v | \le \pi , | u - u _ { 1 } | \le \log 2 . } \end{array}$ This set is closed and bounded, and for sufficiently small e it is not empty. The continuous function $| e ^ { w } - e ^ { w 1 } |$ has consequently a minimum $\pmb { \rho }$ on $\pmb { A }$ (Theorem 8, Corollary). This minimum is positive, for $A$ does not contain any point $w _ { 1 } + n \cdot 2 \pi i$ Choose $\delta = \operatorname* { m i n } \ \left( \rho , { \frac { 1 } { 2 } } e ^ { u _ { 1 } } \right)$ , and assume that

$$
| z _ { 1 } - z _ { 2 } | = | e ^ { w _ { 1 } } - e ^ { w _ { 2 } } | < \delta .
$$

Then $\mathbf { \boldsymbol { w } _ { 2 } }$ cannot lie in $A$ , for this would make $\left| e ^ { w _ { 1 } } - e ^ { w _ { 2 } } \right| \geq \rho \geq \delta .$ . Neither is it possible that $u _ { 2 } < u _ { 1 } - \log { \ 2 }$ or $u _ { 2 } > u _ { 1 } + \log { \ 2 }$ in the former case we would obtain $| e ^ { w _ { 1 } } - e ^ { w _ { 2 } } | \geq e ^ { u _ { 1 } } - e ^ { u _ { 2 } } > { \frac { 1 } { 2 } } e ^ { u _ { 1 } } \geq \delta$ , and in the latter case $| e ^ { w _ { 1 } } - e ^ { w _ { 2 } } | \ge e ^ { u _ { 2 } } - e ^ { u _ { 1 } } > e ^ { u _ { 1 } } > \delta$ Hence ${ \pmb w } _ { 2 }$ must lie in the disk $\lvert w - w _ { 1 } \rvert < \varepsilon$ , and we have proved that $\pmb { w }$ is a continuous function of $\pmb { z }$ From the continuity we conclude as above that the derivative exists and equals $\mathbf { 1 } / z$ .

The infinitely many values of arc cos $\pmb { z }$ are the same as the values of $i \log ( z + { \sqrt { z ^ { 2 } - 1 } } )$ . In this case we restrict $\pmb { \mathscr { z } }$ to the complement $\pmb { \Omega } ^ { \prime }$ of the half-lines ${ \pmb x } \le { \bf 0 }$ , $y = 0$ and $x \geq 1$ , $y = 0$ Since ${ \bf 1 } - z ^ { 2 }$ is never real and $\leq 0$ in $\pmb { \Omega } ^ { \prime }$ , we can define $\sqrt { 1 - z ^ { 2 } }$ as in the first example and then set ${ \sqrt { z ^ { 2 } - 1 } } = i { \sqrt { 1 - z ^ { 2 } } }$ Moreover, $\pmb { \mathscr { z } }$ + $\sqrt { z ^ { 2 } - 1 }$ cannot be negative or zero in $\pmb { \Omega } ^ { \prime }$ ; indeed, since $z + { \sqrt { z ^ { 2 } - 1 } } { \mathrm { ~ a n d } } z - { \sqrt { z ^ { 2 } - 1 } }$ are reciprocal. $z + \sqrt { z ^ { 2 } - 1 } < 0$ would imply $z - \sqrt { z ^ { 2 } - 1 } < 0$ and hence, $2 z < 0$ . We can thus define an analytic branch of log $( z + \sqrt { z ^ { 2 } - 1 } )$ whose imaginary part lies between $- \pmb { \pi }$ and $\pmb { \pi } .$ In this way we obtain a singlevalued analytic function

$$
\operatorname { a r c } \cos z = i \log ( z + { \sqrt { z ^ { 2 } - 1 } } )
$$

in $\pmb { \Omega } ^ { \prime }$ whose derivative is

$$
D { \mathrm { ~ a r c ~ c o s ~ } } z = i { \frac { 1 } { z + { \sqrt { z ^ { 2 } - 1 } } } } { \bigg ( } 1 + { \frac { z } { \sqrt { z ^ { 2 } - 1 } } } { \bigg ) } = { \frac { 1 } { \sqrt { 1 - z ^ { 2 } } } }
$$

where $\sqrt { 1 - z ^ { 2 } }$ has a positive real part.

There is nothing unique about the way in which the region and the single-valued branches have been chosen in these examples. Therefore, each time we consider a function such as log $\pmb { \mathscr { z } }$ the choice of the branch has to be specified. It is a fundamental fact that it is impossible to define a single-valued and analytic branch of log $\pmb { \mathscr { z } }$ in certain regions. This will be proved in the chapter on integration.

All the results of Chap. II, Sec. 1.2 remain valid for functions which are analytic in a region. In particular, the real and imaginary parts of an analytic function in $\pmb { \Omega }$ satisfy the Cauchy-Riemann equations

$$
{ \frac { \partial u } { \partial x } } = { \frac { \partial v } { \partial y } } , \qquad { \frac { \partial u } { \partial y } } = - { \frac { \partial v } { \partial x } } \cdot
$$

Conversely, if $\pmb { u }$ and $v$ satisfy these equations in $\mathfrak { a } _ { \mathrm { : } }$ , and if the partial derivatives are continuous, then $u + i v$ is an analytic function in $\Omega$ .

An analytic function in $\Omega$ degenerates if it reduces to a constant. In the following theorem we shall list some simple conditions which have this consequence:

Theorem 11. An analytic function in a region Ω whose derivative vanishes identically must reduce to a constant.  The same is true if either the real part, the imaginary part, the modulus, or the argument is constant.

The vanishing of the derivative implies that $\partial u / \partial x , \ \partial u / \partial y , \ \partial v / \partial x ,$ ∂v/∂y are all zero. It follows that $\textbf { \em u }$ and $v$ are constant on any line segment in $\pmb { \Omega }$ which is parallel to one of the coordinate axes. In Sec. 1.3 we remarked, in connection with Theorem 3, that any two points in a region can be joined within the region by a polygon whose sides are parallel to the axes. We conclude that $u + i v$ is constant.

If $\pmb { u }$ or $\pmb { v }$ is constant,

$$
f ^ { \prime } ( z ) = { \frac { \partial u } { \partial x } } - i { \frac { \partial u } { \partial y } } = { \frac { \partial v } { \partial y } } + i { \frac { \partial v } { \partial x } } = 0 ,
$$

and hence $f ( z )$ must be constant. If $u ^ { 2 } + v ^ { 2 }$ is constant, we obtain

$$
u { \frac { \partial u } { \partial x } } + v { \frac { \partial v } { \partial x } } = 0
$$

and

$$
u { \frac { \partial u } { \partial y } } + v { \frac { \partial v } { \partial y } } = - u { \frac { \partial v } { \partial x } } + v { \frac { \partial u } { \partial x } } = 0 .
$$

These equations permit the conclusion $\partial u / \partial x = \partial v / \partial x = 0$ unless the determinant $u ^ { 2 } + v ^ { 2 }$ vanishes. But if $u ^ { 2 } + v ^ { 2 } = 0$ at a single point it is constantly zero and $f ( z )$ vanishes identically. Hence $f ( z )$ is in any case a constant.

Finally, if arg $f ( z )$ is constant, we can set $u = k v$ with constant $k$ (unless $\pmb { v }$ is identically zero). But $\boldsymbol { u } - \boldsymbol { k } \boldsymbol { v }$ is the real part of $( 1 + i k ) f$ and we conclude again that $\pmb { f }$ must reduce to a constant.

# EXERCISES

1. Give a precise definition of a single-valued branch of $\sqrt { 1 + z } +$ $\sqrt { 1 - z }$ in a suitable region, and prove that it is analytic. 2. Same problem for log log $\pmb { \tilde { \varepsilon } }$ . 3. Suppose that $f ( z ) = u + i v$ is analytic in a region $\pmb { \Omega }$ and that $F ( u , v ) = 0$ where $\pmb { F }$ is a real-valued function with continuous first derivatives. Under what condition can one conclude that $f ( z )$ is constant? 4 Suppose that $f ( z )$ is analytic and satisfies the condition $| f ( z ) ^ { 2 } - 1 |$ $< 1$ in a region $\pmb { \Omega }$ Show that either Re $f ( z ) > 0$ or $\mathrm { R e } f ( z ) < 0$ throughout $\pmb { \Omega }$ .

2.3. Conformal Mapping. Suppose that an arc $\pmb { \gamma }$ with the equation $z = z ( t )$ , $\alpha \leq t \leq \beta$ ,is contained in a region $\pmb { \Omega }$ , and let $f ( z )$ be defined and continuous in $\pmb { \Omega }$ Then the equation $w = w ( t ) = f ( z ( t ) )$ defines an arc $\gamma ^ { \prime }$ in the $\pmb { w }$ -plane which may be called the image of $\pmb { \gamma }$ .

Consider the case of an $f ( z )$ which is analytic in $\Omega$ If ${ \boldsymbol { z } } ^ { \prime } ( t )$ exists, we find that ${ \pmb w } ^ { \prime } ( t )$ also exists and is determined by

$$
w ^ { \prime } ( t ) = f ^ { \prime } ( z ( t ) ) z ^ { \prime } ( t ) .
$$

We will investigate the meaning of this equation at a point $z _ { 0 } = z ( t _ { 0 } )$ with $z ^ { \prime } ( t _ { 0 } ) \neq 0$ and $f ^ { \prime } ( z _ { 0 } ) \neq 0$ .

The first conclusion is that $w ^ { \prime } ( t _ { 0 } ) \neq 0$ Hence $\gamma ^ { \prime }$ has a tangent at

${ \pmb w } _ { 0 } = { \pmb f } ( { \pmb z } _ { 0 } )$ , and its direction is determined by

$$
\arg w ^ { \prime } ( t _ { 0 } ) = \arg f ^ { \prime } ( z _ { 0 } ) + \arg z ^ { \prime } ( t _ { 0 } ) .
$$

This relation asserts that the angle between the directed tangents to $\gamma$ at ${ \mathfrak { z } } _ { 0 }$ and to $\gamma ^ { \prime }$ at ${ \pmb w } _ { \pmb 0 }$ is equal to arg $f ^ { \prime } ( z _ { 0 } )$ . It is hence independent of the curve $\pmb { \gamma }$ . For this reason curves through ${ \pmb z } _ { 0 }$ which are tangent to each other are mapped onto curves with a common tangent at ${ \pmb w } _ { \pmb 0 } .$ . Moreover, two curves which form an angle at ${ \mathfrak { z } } _ { 0 }$ are mapped upon curves forming the same angle, in sense as well as in size. In view of this property the mapping by ${ \pmb w } = { \pmb f } ( { \pmb z } )$ is said to be conformal at all points with $f ^ { \prime } ( z ) \neq 0$ .

A related property of the mapping is derived by consideration of the modulus $\vert f ^ { \prime } ( z _ { 0 } ) \vert$ We have

$$
\operatorname* { l i m } _ { z \to z _ { 0 } } { \frac { | f ( z ) - f ( z _ { 0 } ) | } { | z - z _ { 0 } | } } = | f ^ { \prime } ( z _ { 0 } ) | ,
$$

and this means that any small line segment with one end point at ${ \pmb z _ { 0 } }$ is, in the limit, contracted or expanded in the ratio $\vert f ^ { \prime } ( z _ { 0 } ) \vert$ In other words, the linear change of scale at ${ \pmb z _ { 0 } } ,$ effected by the transformation $w = f ( z ) { \mathrm { , } }$ , is independent of the direction. In general this change of scale will vary from point to point.

Conversely, it is clear that both kinds of conformality together imply the existence of $f ^ { \prime } ( z _ { 0 } )$ . It is less obvious that each kind will separately imply the same result, at least under additional regularity assumptions.

To be more precise, let us assume that the partial derivatives $\partial f / \partial x$ and af/∂y are continuous. Under this condition the derivative of $w ( t ) = f ( \pmb { z } ( t ) )$ can be expressed in the form

$$
w ^ { \prime } ( t _ { 0 } ) = \frac { \partial f } { \partial x } x ^ { \prime } ( t _ { 0 } ) + \frac { \partial f } { \partial y } y ^ { \prime } ( t _ { 0 } )
$$

where the partial derivatives are taken at ${ \pmb z _ { 0 } }$ In terms of ${ z } ^ { \prime } ( t _ { 0 } )$ this can be rewritten as

$$
w ^ { \prime } ( t _ { 0 } ) = \frac { 1 } { 2 } \left( \frac { \partial f } { \partial x } - i \frac { \partial f } { \partial y } \right) z ^ { \prime } ( t _ { 0 } ) + \frac { 1 } { 2 } \left( \frac { \partial f } { \partial x } + i \frac { \partial f } { \partial y } \right) \overline { { z ^ { \prime } ( t _ { 0 } ) } } .
$$

If angles are preserved, arg $[ w ^ { \prime } ( t _ { 0 } ) / z ^ { \prime } ( t _ { 0 } ) ]$ must be independent of arg $z ^ { \prime } ( t _ { 0 } )$ The expression

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \frac { 1 } { 2 } \Big ( \frac { \partial f } { \partial x } - i \frac { \partial f } { \partial y } \Big ) + \frac { 1 } { 2 } \Big ( \frac { \partial f } { \partial x } + i \frac { \partial f } { \partial y } \Big ) \frac { \overline { { z ^ { \prime } ( t _ { 0 } ) } } } { \overline { { z ^ { \prime } ( t _ { 0 } ) } } } } \end{array}
$$

must therefore have a constant argument. As arg $z ^ { \prime } ( t _ { 0 } )$ is allowed to vary, the point represented by (3) describes $\mathfrak { a }$ circle having the radius

$\gimel { \left| \left( \partial f / \partial x \right) + i \left( \partial f / \partial y \right) \right| }$ . The argument cannot be constant on this circle unless its radius vanishes, and hence we must have

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \frac { \partial f } { \partial x } = - i \frac { \partial f } { \partial y } } \end{array}
$$

which is the complex form of the Cauchy-Riemann equations.

Quite similarly, the condition that the change of scale shall be the same in all directions implies that the expression (3) has a constant modulus. On a circle the modulus is constant only if the radius vanishes or if the center lies at the origin. In the first case we obtain (4), and in the second case

$$
{ \frac { \partial f } { \partial x } } = i { \frac { \partial f } { \partial y } } .
$$

The last equation expresses the fact that $\overline { { f ( z ) } }$ is analytic. A mapping by the conjugate of an analytic function with a nonvanishing derivative is said to be indirectly conformal. It evidently preserves the size but reverses the sense of angles.

If the mapping of $\Omega$ by $w = f ( z )$ is topological, then the inverse function $z = f ^ { - 1 } ( w )$ is also analytic. This follows easily if $f ^ { \prime } ( z ) \neq 0 ,$ ,for then the derivative of the inverse function must be equal to $1 / f ^ { \prime } ( z )$ at the point $z = f ^ { - 1 } ( w )$ . We shall prove later that $f ^ { \prime } ( z )$ can never vanish in the case of a topological mapping by an analytic function.

The knowledge that $f ^ { \prime } ( z _ { 0 } ) \neq 0$ is sufficient to conclude that the mapping is topological if it is restricted to a sufficiently small neighborhood of ${ \boldsymbol { \gtrsim } } _ { 0 }$ This follows by the theorem on implicit functions known from the calculus, for the Jacobian of the functions $u = u ( x , y ) , v = v ( x , y )$ at the point ${ \boldsymbol { \hat { z } } } _ { 0 }$ is $| f ^ { \prime } ( z _ { 0 } ) | ^ { 2 }$ and hence $\yen 0$ Later we shall present a simpler proof of this important theorem.

But even if $f ^ { \prime } ( z ) \neq 0$ throughout the region $\Omega$ , we cannot assert that the mapping of the whole region is necessarily topological. To illustrate what may happen we refer to Fig. 4. Here the mappings of the subregions $\pmb { \Omega _ { 1 } }$ and $\Omega _ { 2 }$ are one to one, but the images overlap. It is helpful to think of the image of the whole region as a transparent film which partly covers itself. This is the simple and fruitful idea used by Riemann when he introduced the generalized regions now known as Riemann surfaces.

![](images/e4d6d37e6678cddb185072c037d15e233945f6a2d4ff8cb8e2bb69dc357c961f.jpg)

![](images/bf1b8d3e5f2168a494752eb148c11ede08d42e3109d354316d46166ceabcfb72.jpg)

# 3. LINEAR TRANSFORMATIONS

Of all analytic functions the first-order rational functions have the simplest mapping properties, for they define mappings of the extended plane onto itself which are at the same time conformal and topological. The linear transformations have also very remarkable geometric properties, and for that reason their importance goes far beyond serving as simple examples of conformal mappings. The reader will do well to pay particular attention to this geometric aspect, for it will equip him with simple but very valuable techniques.

3.1. The Linear Group. We have already remarked in Chap. 2, Sec.   
1.4 that a linear fractional transformation

$$
w = S ( z ) = { \frac { a z + b } { c z + d } }
$$

with $a d - b c \neq 0$ has an inverse

$$
z = S ^ { - 1 } ( w ) = { \frac { d w - b } { - c w + a } } \cdotp
$$

The special values $S ( \infty ) = a / c$ and $s ( - d / c ) = \infty$ can be introduced either by convention or as limits for $z  \infty$ and $z  - d / c$ With the latter interpretation it becomes obvious that $s$ is a topological mapping of the extended plane onto itself, the topology being defined by distances on the Riemann sphere.

For linear transformations we shall usually replace the notation $S ( z )$ by ${ \pmb S } { \pmb z }$ The representation (5) is said to be normalized if $a d - b c = 1$ . It is clear that every linear transformation has two normalized representations, obtained from each other by changing the signs of the coefficients.

A convenient way to express a linear transformation is by use of homogeneous coordinates. If we write $z = z _ { 1 } / z _ { 2 } , w = w _ { 1 } / w _ { 2 }$ we find that $\boldsymbol { w } = \boldsymbol { S } \boldsymbol { z }$ if

$$
\begin{array} { l } { { w _ { 1 } = a z _ { 1 } + b z _ { 2 } } } \\ { { w _ { 2 } = c z _ { 1 } + d z _ { 2 } } } \end{array}
$$

or, in matrix notation,

$$
{ \binom { w _ { 1 } } { w _ { 2 } } } = { \binom { a } { c } } \left( { \begin{array} { l l } { z _ { 1 } } \\ { z } & { d } \end{array} } \right) { \binom { z _ { 1 } } { z _ { 2 } } } .
$$

The main advantage of this notation is that it leads to a simple determina-

tion of a composite transformation $w = S _ { 1 } S _ { 2 } z .$ If we use subscripts to distinguish between the matrices that correspond to $\displaystyle { \cal S } _ { 1 } , { \cal S } _ { 2 }$ it is immediate that S1S2 belongs to the matrix product

$$
{ \binom { a _ { 1 } } { c _ { 1 } } } { \binom { b _ { 1 } } { d _ { 1 } } } { \binom { a _ { 2 } } { c _ { 2 } } } b _ { 2 } ) = { \binom { a _ { 1 } a _ { 2 } + b _ { 1 } c _ { 2 } } { c _ { 1 } a _ { 2 } + d _ { 1 } c _ { 2 } } } a _ { 1 } b _ { 2 } + b _ { 1 } d _ { 2 } ) .
$$

All linear transformations form a group. Indeed, the associative law $( S _ { 1 } S _ { 2 } ) S _ { 3 } = S _ { 1 } ( S _ { 2 } S _ { 3 } )$ holds for arbitrary transformations, the identity $w = z$ is a linear transformation, and the inverse of a linear transformation is linear. The ratios $z _ { 1 } : z _ { 2 } \neq 0 : 0$ are the points of the complex projective line, and (6) identifies the group of linear transformations with the onedimensional projective group over the complex numbers, usually denoted by $P ( 1 , \mathbf { C } )$ . If we use only normalized representations, we can also identify it with the group of two-by-two matrices with determinant 1 (denoted ${ \cal S } { \cal L } ( 2 , { \bf C } ) )$ , except that there are two opposite matrices corresponding to the same linear transformation.

We shall make no further use of the matrix notation, except for remarking that the simplest linear transformations belong to matrices of the form

$$
{ \binom { 1 } { 0 } } \alpha \alpha \rangle , { \binom { k } { 0 } } \beta \binom { 0 } { 1 } \beta \langle 0 \rangle .
$$

The first of these, $w = \varepsilon + \alpha _ { : }$ , is called a parallel translation. The second, $w = k z ,$ , is a rotation if $| k | = 1$ and a homothetic transformation if $k > 0$ . For arbitrary complex $k \neq 0$ we can set $k = | \boldsymbol { k } | \cdot \boldsymbol { k } / | \boldsymbol { k } |$ , and hence $w = k z$ can be represented as the result of a homothetic transformation followed by a rotation. The third transformation, $w = 1 / z$ , is called an inversion.

If $c \neq 0$ we can write

$$
\frac { a z + b } { c z + d } = \frac { b c - a d } { c ^ { 2 } ( z + d / c ) } + \frac { a } { c } ,
$$

and this decomposition shows that the most general linear transformaion is composed by a translation, an inversion, a rotation, and a homothetic transformation followed by another translation. If $\pmb { c } = \mathbf { 0 } ;$ ,the inversion falls out and the last translation is not needed.

2. If

$$
T _ { 1 } z = \frac { z + 2 } { z + 3 } , T _ { 2 } z = \frac { z } { z + 1 } ,
$$

find $\pmb { T } _ { 1 } \pmb { T } _ { 2 } \pmb { z } _ { 3 }$ $\pmb { T _ { 2 } T _ { 1 } z }$ and $T _ { 1 } ^ { - 1 } T _ { 2 } z .$

3. Prove that the most general transformation which leaves the origin fixed and preserves all distances is either a rotation or a rotation followed by reflexion in the real axis.

4. Show that any linear transformation which transforms the real axis into itself can be written with real coefficients.

3.2. The Cross Ratio. Given three distinct points $z _ { 2 } , \ z _ { 3 } , \ z _ { 4 }$ in the extended plane, there exists a linear transformation $\pmb { S }$ which carries them into $1 , 0 , \infty$ in this order. If none of the points is $\infty , s$ will be given by

$$
S z = \frac { z - z _ { 3 } } { z - z _ { 4 } } { : \frac { z _ { 2 } - z _ { 3 } } { z _ { 2 } - z _ { 4 } } } .
$$

If ${ \hat { z } } _ { 2 } , { \hat { z } } _ { 3 }$ or $z _ { 4 } = \infty$ the transformation reduces to

$$
\frac { z - z _ { 3 } } { z - z _ { 4 } } , \frac { z _ { 2 } - z _ { 4 } } { z - z _ { 4 } } , \frac { z - z _ { 3 } } { z _ { 2 } - z _ { 3 } }
$$

respectively.

If $\pmb { T }$ were another linear transformation with the same property, then $\$ 123$ would leave ${ \bf 1 } , 0 , \infty$ invariant. Direct calculation shows that this is true only for the identity transformation, and we would have $s = T$ We conclude that $s$ is uniquely determined.

Definition 12. The cross ratio $( z _ { 1 } , z _ { 2 } , z _ { 3 } , z _ { 4 } )$ is the image of ${ \pmb z } _ { \bf 1 }$ under the linear transformation which carries $z _ { 2 } , z _ { 3 } , z _ { 4 }$ into 1, 0, ∞.

The definition is meaningful only if $\mathbf { \hat { z } _ { 2 } , \hat { z } _ { 3 } , \hat { z } _ { 4 } }$ are distinct. A conventional value can be introduced as soon as any three of the points are distinct, but this is unimportant.

The cross ratio is invariant under linear transformations. In more precise formulation:

Theorem 12. $H f z _ { 1 } , z _ { 2 } , z _ { 3 } , z$ ${ z _ { 4 } }$ are distinct points in the extended plane and $\pmb { T }$ any linear transformation, then $( T z _ { 1 } , T z _ { 2 } , T z _ { 3 } , T z _ { 4 } ) = ( z _ { 1 } , z _ { 2 } , z _ { 3 } , z _ { 4 } )$ .

The proof is immediate, for if $S z = ( z , z _ { 2 } , z _ { 3 } , z _ { 4 } )$ , then $\pmb { S T } ^ { - 1 }$ carries $T z _ { 2 } , T z _ { 3 } , T z _ { 4 }$ into $1 , 0 , \infty$ . By definition we have hence

$$
( T z _ { 1 } , T z _ { 2 } , T z _ { 3 } , T z _ { 4 } ) = { \cal S } T ^ { - 1 } ( T z _ { 1 } ) = { \cal S } z _ { 1 } = ( z _ { 1 } , z _ { 2 } , z _ { 3 } , z _ { 4 } ) .
$$

With the help of this property we can immediately write down the linear transformation which carries three given points $z _ { 1 } , z _ { 2 } , z _ { 3 }$ to pre-

scribed positions $w _ { 1 } , w _ { 2 } , w _ { 3 } .$ The correspondence must indeed be given by

$$
( w , w _ { 1 } , w _ { 2 } , w _ { 3 } ) = ( z , z _ { 1 } , z _ { 2 } , z _ { 3 } ) .
$$

In general it is of course necessary to solve this equation with respect to w.

Theorem 13. The cross ratio $( z _ { 1 } , z _ { 2 } , z _ { 3 } , z _ { 4 } )$ is real if and only if the four points lie on a circle or on $\pmb { a }$ straight line.

This is evident by elementary geometry, for we obtain

$$
\arg { ( z _ { 1 } , z _ { 2 } , z _ { 3 } , z _ { 4 } ) } = \arg \frac { z _ { 1 } - z _ { 3 } } { z _ { 1 } - z _ { 4 } } - \arg \frac { z _ { 2 } - z _ { 3 } } { z _ { 2 } - z _ { 4 } } ,
$$

and if the points lie on a circle this dfference of angles is either 0 or depending on the relative location.

For an analytic proof we need only show that the image of the real axis under any linear transformation is either a circle or a straight line. Indeed, $T z = ( z , z _ { 2 } , z _ { 3 } , z _ { 4 } )$ is real on the image of the real axis under the transformation $\pmb { T } ^ { \ll 1 }$ and nowhere else.

The values of $w = \dot { T } ^ { - 1 } z$ for real $\pmb { z }$ satisfy the equation $T w = \overline { { T w } }$ . Explicitly, this condition is of the form

$$
{ \frac { a w + b } { c w + d } } = { \frac { { \bar { a } } { \bar { w } } + { \bar { b } } } { { \bar { c } } { \bar { w } } + { \bar { d } } } } \cdotp
$$

By cross multiplication we obtain

$$
( a \bar { c } - c \bar { a } ) | w ^ { 2 } | + ( a \bar { d } - c \bar { b } ) w + ( b \bar { c } - d \bar { a } ) \bar { w } + b \bar { d } - d \bar { b } = 0 .
$$

If $a { \bar { c } } - c { \bar { a } } = 0$ this is the equation of a straight line, for under this condition the coefficient $a { \bar { d } } - c { \bar { b } }$ cannot also vanish. If $a \bar { c } - c \bar { a } \neq 0$ we can divide by this coefficient and complete the square. After a simple computation we obtain

$$
\left| \ w + { \frac { { \bar { a } } d - { \bar { c } } b } { { \bar { a } } c - { \bar { c } } a } } \right| = \left| { \frac { a d - b c } { { \bar { a } } c - { \bar { c } } a } } \right|
$$

which is the equation of a circle.

The last result makes it clear that we should not, in the theory of linear transformations, distinguish between circles and straight lines. A further justification was found in the fact that both correspond to circles on the Riemann sphere. Accordingly we shall agree to use the word circle in this wider sense.†

This agreement will be in force only when dealing with linear transformations.

The following is an immediate corollary of Theorems 12 and 13:

Theorem 14. A linear transformation carries circles into circles.

$$
| z _ { 1 } - z _ { 3 } | \cdot | z _ { 2 } - z _ { 4 } | = | z _ { 1 } - z _ { 2 } | \cdot | z _ { 3 } - z _ { 4 } | + | z _ { 2 } - z _ { 3 } | \cdot | z _ { 1 } - z _ { 4 } |
$$

and interpret the result geometrically.

3.3. Symmetry. The points $\pmb { \mathscr { z } }$ and $\bar { z }$ are symmetric with respect to the real axis. A linear transformation with real coefficients carries the real axis into itself and $z , \ \bar { z }$ into points which are again symmetric. More generally, if a linear transformation $\pmb { T }$ carries the real axis into a circle $c$ , we shall say that the points $w = T z$ and $w ^ { \ast } = T \bar { z }$ are symmetric with respect to $c$ This is a relation between w, $w ^ { * }$ and $c$ which does not depend on $\pmb { T }$ For if $s$ is another transformation which carries the real axis into $c$ , then $S ^ { - 1 } T$ is a real transformation, and hence $S ^ { - 1 } w = S ^ { - 1 } T z$ and $S ^ { - 1 } w ^ { * } = S ^ { - 1 } T \bar { z }$ are also conjugate. Symmetry can thus be defined in the following terms:

Definition 13. The points $\pmb { z }$ and $z ^ { * }$ are said to be symmetric with respect to the circle $c$ through $z _ { 1 } , z _ { 2 } , z _ { 3 }$ if and only if $( z ^ { \ast } , z _ { 1 } , \widetilde { z } _ { 2 } , \widetilde { z } _ { 3 } ) = ( \widetilde { z } , \widetilde { z } _ { 1 , } \widetilde { z } _ { 2 , } \widetilde { z } _ { 3 } )$ .

The points on $c$ , and only those, are symmetric to themselves. The mapping which carries $\boldsymbol { z }$ into $z ^ { * }$ is a one-to-one correspondence and is called reflection with respect to $c$ . Two reflections will evidently result in a linear transformation.

We wish to investigate the geometric significance of symmetry. Suppose first that $c$ is a straight line. Then we can choose $z _ { 3 } = \infty$ and the condition for symmetry becomes

$$
\frac { z ^ { * } - z _ { 2 } } { z _ { 1 } - z _ { 2 } } = \frac { \bar { z } - \bar { z } _ { 2 } } { \bar { z } _ { 1 } - \bar { z } _ { 2 } }
$$

Taking absolute values we obtain $| z ^ { * } - z _ { 2 } | = | z - z _ { 2 } |$ Here ${ z } _ { 2 }$ can be any finite point on. $c$ , and we conclude that $\pmb { z }$ and $z ^ { * }$ are equidistant from all points on $c$ By (9) we have further

$$
\mathrm { I m } { \frac { z ^ { \ast } - z _ { 2 } } { z _ { 1 } - z _ { 2 } } } = - \mathrm { I m } { \frac { z - z _ { 2 } } { z _ { 1 } - z _ { 2 } } } ,
$$

and hence $\pmb { z }$ and $z ^ { * }$ are in different half planes determined by C.t We leave to the reader to prove that $\pmb { c }$ is the bisecting normal of the segment between $\pmb { z }$ and $z ^ { * }$

Consider now the case of $\pmb { \mathrm { a } }$ finite circle $c$ of center $\pmb { a }$ and radius $\pmb { R }$ . Systematic use of the invariance of the cross ratio allows us to conclude as follows:

$$
\begin{array} { r l r } {  { \overline { { \hat { z } ^ { 2 } \hat { z } _ { 3 } } } = ( \overline { { z - a , z _ { 1 } - a , z _ { 2 } - a , z _ { 3 } - a } } ) } } \\ & { } & { ( \bar { z } - \bar { a } , \frac { R ^ { 2 } } { z _ { 1 } - a } , \frac { R ^ { 2 } } { z _ { 2 } - a } , \frac { R ^ { 2 } } { z _ { 3 } - a } ) = ( \frac { R ^ { 2 } } { \bar { z } - \bar { a } } , z _ { 1 } - a , z _ { 2 } - a , z _ { 3 } - a  } \\ & { } & {  = ( \frac { R ^ { 2 } } { \bar { z } - \bar { a } } + a , z _ { 1 } , z _ { 3 }   } \end{array}
$$

This equation shows that the symmetric point of $\pmb { \mathscr { z } }$ . $\mathrm { i s } z ^ { * } = R ^ { 2 } / ( \bar { z } - \bar { a } ) + a$ or that $\pmb { \mathscr { z } }$ and $z ^ { * }$ satisfy the relation

$$
( z ^ { * } - a ) ( \bar { z } - \bar { a } ) = R ^ { 2 } .
$$

The product $| z ^ { * } - a | \cdot | z - a |$ of the distances to the center is hence $R ^ { 2 }$ . Further, the ratio $( z ^ { * } - a ) / ( z - a )$ is positive, which means that $\pmb { \mathscr { z } }$ and $z ^ { * }$ are situated on the same half line from $\pmb { a }$ There is a simple geometric construction for the symmetric point of $\pmb { \mathscr { z } }$ (Fig. 5). We note that the symmetric point of $\pmb { a }$ is $\infty$ :

Unless they coincide and lie on $\pmb { c }$ .

![](images/fcab778d0b0e501e54577cbf169e8f855c9607b12659bd140a0fce1c43070712.jpg)

Theorem 15. (The symmetry principle.) If $\pmb { a }$ linear transformation carries $\pmb { a }$ circle $c _ { 1 }$ into $^ { a }$ circle ${ \cal C } _ { 2 } ,$ then it transforms any pair of symmetric points with respect to $c _ { 1 }$ into $\pmb { a }$ pair of symmetric points with respect to $c _ { 2 } .$ .

Briefly, linear transformations preserve symmetry. If $c _ { 1 }$ or $C _ { 2 }$ is the real axis, the principle follows from the definition of symmetry. In the general case the assertion follows by use of an intermediate transformation which carries $c _ { 1 }$ into the real axis.

There are two ways in which the principle of symmetry can be used. If the images of $\pmb { z }$ and $\pmb { c }$ under a certain linear transformation are known, then the principle allows us to find the image of $z ^ { * }$ On the other hand, if the images of $\pmb { z }$ and $z ^ { * }$ are known, we conclude that the image of $\pmb { c }$ must be a line of symmetry of these images. While this is not enough to determine the image of $\pmb { c }$ , the information we gain is nevertheless valuable.

The principle of symmetry is put to practical use in the problem of finding the linear transformations which carry a circle $\pmb { c }$ into a circle $c ^ { \prime }$ . We can always determine the transformation by requiring that three points $z _ { 1 } , z _ { 2 } , z _ { 3 }$ on $c$ go over into three points $w _ { 1 } , w _ { 2 } , w _ { 3 }$ on $c ^ { \prime }$ ; the transformation is then $( w , w _ { 1 } , w _ { 2 } , w _ { 3 } ) = ( z , z _ { 1 } , z _ { 2 } , z _ { 3 } )$ But the transformation is also determined if we prescribe that a point ${ \pmb z _ { 1 } }$ on $c$ shall correspond to a point $\mathbf { \pmb { w _ { 1 } } }$ on $c ^ { \prime }$ and that a point ${ \pmb z } _ { 2 }$ not on $c$ shall be carried into a pomt ${ \pmb w } _ { \pmb 2 }$ not on $c ^ { \prime }$ We know then that ${ \boldsymbol { z } } _ { 2 } ^ { * }$ (the symmetric point of ${ \pmb z } _ { 2 }$ with respect to $c$ must correspond to $\boldsymbol { w _ { 2 } ^ { * } }$ (the symmetric point of ${ \pmb w } _ { \mathbf { 2 } }$ with respect to $c ^ { \prime }$ ). Hence the transformation will be obtained from the relation $( w , w _ { 1 } , w _ { 2 } , w _ { 2 } ^ { * } ) = ( z , z _ { 1 } , z _ { 2 } , z _ { 2 } ^ { * } )$ .

Carry out the reflections in the preceding exercise by geometric construction. $\clubsuit _ { \bullet }$ Find the linear transformation which carries the circle $| z | = 2$ into $| z + 1 | = 1$ , the point $- 2$ into the origin, and the origin into $\mathbf { \delta } _ { i }$ . 5. Find the most general linear transformation of the circle $| z | = R$ into itself. 6. A linear transformation carries a pair of concentric circles into another pair of concentric circles. Prove that the ratios of the radii must be the same.

3.4. Oriented Circles. Because $ { \boldsymbol { S } } ( z )$ is analytic and

$$
S ^ { \prime } ( z ) = \frac { a d - b c } { ( c z + d ) ^ { 2 } } \ne 0
$$

the mapping $w = \mathcal { S } ( z )$ is conformal for $z \neq - d / c$ and $\infty$ . It follows that a pair of intersecting circles are mapped on circles that include the same angle. In addition, the sense of an angle is preserved. From an intuitive point of view this means that right and left are preserved, but a more precise formulation is desirable.

An orientation of a circle $\pmb { c }$ is determined by an ordered triple of points $z _ { 1 , 2 , 2 , 3 }$ on $c$ With respect to this orientation a point $\pmb { \mathscr { z } }$ not on $c$ is said to lie to the right of $c$ if In $\mathbf { \varepsilon } _ { 1 } \left( z , z _ { 1 } , z _ { 2 } , z _ { 3 } \right) > 0$ and to the left of $c$ is $\mathrm { I m } \ ( z , z _ { 1 } , z _ { 2 } , z _ { 3 } ) < 0$ (this checks with everyday use because $( i , 1 , 0 , \infty ) = i )$ . It is essential to show that there are only two different orientations. By this we mean that the distinction between left and right is the same for all triples, while the meaning may be reversed. Since the cross ratio is invariant, it is sufficient to consider the case where $c$ is the real axis. Then

$$
( z , z _ { 1 } , z _ { 2 } , z _ { 3 } ) = { \frac { a z + b } { c z + d } }
$$

can be written with real coefficients, and a simple calculation gives

$$
\mathrm { I m } ( z , z _ { 1 , } z _ { 2 , } z _ { 3 } ) = { \frac { a d - b c } { | c z + d | ^ { 2 } } } \mathrm { I m } z .
$$

We recognize that the distinction between right and left is the same as the distinction between the upper and lower half plane. Which is which depends on the sign of the determinant $a d - b c ,$ .

A linear transformation $s$ carries the oriented circle $c$ into a circle which we orient through the triple $S z _ { 1 } , S z _ { 2 } , S z _ { 3 } .$ From the invariance of the cross ratio it follows that the left and right of $c$ will be mapped on the left and right of the image circle.

If two circles are tangent to each other, their orientations can be compared. Indeed, we can use a linear transformation which throws their common point to ∞. The circles become parallel straight lines, and we know how to compare the directions of parall lines.

In the geometric representation the orientation $z _ { 1 } , z _ { 2 } , z _ { 3 }$ can be indicated by an arrow which points from ${ \pmb z _ { 1 } }$ over ${ \pmb z } _ { 2 }$ to ${ \pmb { \imath } } _ { \pmb { \imath } }$ With the usual choice of the coordinate system left and right wll have their customary meaning with respect to this arrow.

When the finite plane is considered as part of the extended plane, the point at infinity is distinguished. We can therefore define an absolute positive orientation of all finite circles by the requirement that $\infty$ should lie to the right of the oriented circles. The points to the left are said to form the inside of the circle and the points to the right form its outside.
