1. Compute

Compute

$$
\int _ { | z | = 1 } { \frac { e ^ { z } } { z } } d z .
$$

$$
\int _ { | z | = 2 } { \frac { d z } { z ^ { 2 } + 1 } }
$$

by decomposition of the integrand in partial fractions.

3. Compute

$$
\int \limits _ { | z | = p } ^ { \infty } | \frac { | d z | } { | z - a | ^ { 2 } }
$$

under the condition $| a | \neq \rho$ Hint: make use of the equations $z { \bar { z } } = \rho ^ { 2 }$ and

$$
| d z | = - i \rho \frac { d z } { z } .
$$

2.3. Higher Derivatives. The representation formula (22) gives us an ideal tool for the study of the local properties of analytic functions. In particular we can now show that an analytic function has derivatives of all orders, which are then also analytic.

We consider a function $f ( z )$ which is analytic in an arbitrary region $\Omega$ . To a point $a \in \Omega$ we determine a $\pmb { \delta }$ -neighborhood $\Delta$ contained in $\Omega$ , and in $\Delta$ a circle $c$ about $\pmb { a }$ Theorem 6 can be applied to $f ( z )$ in $\Delta$ Since ${ \bf \nabla } _ { n } ( C , { \bf a } ) = 1$ we have ${ \pmb n } ( C , z ) = { \bf 1 }$ for all points $\pmb { z }$ inside of $c$ For such $\pmb { z }$ we obtain by (22)

$$
f ( z ) = { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } } \int _ { C } { \frac { f ( \zeta ) \ d \zeta } { \zeta \ - \ z } } \cdotp
$$

Provided that the integral can be differentiated under the sign of integration we find

$$
f ^ { \prime } ( z ) ~ = { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } } \int _ { C } { \frac { f ( \xi ) ~ d \xi } { ( \xi ~ - ~ z ) ^ { 2 } } }
$$

and

$$
f ^ { \left( n \right) } { \left( z \right) } = \frac { n ! } { 2 \pi i } \int _ { C } \frac { f ( \xi ) \ d \xi } { ( \xi \ - \ z ) ^ { n + 1 } } \cdot
$$

If the differentiations can be justified, we shall have proved the existence of all derivatives at the points inside of $c$ . Since every point in $\Omega$ lies inside of some such circle, the existence will be proved in the whole region $\pmb { \Omega }$ At the same time we shall have obtained a convenient representation formula for the derivatives.

For the justification we could either refer to corresponding theorems in the real case, or we could prove a general theorem concerning line integrals whose integrand depends analytically on a parameter. Actually, we shall prove only the following lemma which is all we need in the present case:

Lemma 3. Suppose that $\varphi ( \zeta )$ is continuous on the arc $\pmb { \gamma }$ Then the function

$$
F _ { n } ( z ) = \int _ { \gamma } { \frac { \varphi ( \zeta ) \ d \xi } { ( \zeta \ - \ z ) ^ { n } } }
$$

is analytic in each of the regions determined by $\gamma$ , and its derivative is $F _ { n } ^ { \prime } ( z ) \ : = \ : n F _ { n + 1 } ( z )$ .

We prove first that $F _ { 1 } ( z )$ is continuous. Let ${ \pmb z _ { 0 } }$ be a point not on $\pmb { \gamma }$ , and choose the neighborhood $| z - z _ { 0 } | < \delta$ so that it does not meet $\pmb { \gamma }$ By restricting $\pmb { z }$ to the smaller neighborhood $| z - z _ { 0 } | < \delta / 2$ we attain that $| \xi - z | > \delta / 2$ for all $\zeta \in \gamma$ From

$$
F _ { 1 } ( z ) \ : - \ : F _ { 1 } ( z _ { 0 } ) = ( z - z _ { 0 } ) \ : \int _ { \gamma } \frac { \varphi ( \xi ) \ : d \xi } { ( \xi - z ) ( \xi - z _ { 0 } ) }
$$

we obtain at once

$$
| F _ { 1 } ( z ) - F _ { 1 } ( z _ { 0 } ) | < | z - z _ { 0 } | \cdot \frac { 2 } { \delta ^ { 2 } } \int _ { \gamma } | \varphi | | d \xi | ,
$$

and this inequality proves the continuity of $F _ { 1 } ( z )$ at ${ \boldsymbol { z } } _ { \mathbf { 0 } }$ .

From this part of the lemma, applied to the function $\varphi ( \zeta ) / ( \zeta - z _ { 0 } )$ , we conclude that the difference quotient

$$
\frac { F _ { 1 } ( z ) - F _ { 1 } ( z _ { 0 } ) } { z - z _ { 0 } } = \int _ { \gamma } \frac { \varphi ( \xi ) \ : d \xi } { ( \xi - z ) ( \xi - z _ { 0 } ) }
$$

tends to the limit $F _ { 2 } ( z _ { 0 } )$ as $z  z _ { 0 }$ Hence it is proved that $F _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } ( z ) = F _ { 2 } ( z )$

The general case is proved by induction. Suppose we have shown that $F _ { n - 1 } ^ { \prime } ( z ) \ : = \ : ( n - 1 ) F _ { n } ( z )$ From the identity

$$
{ \frac { \varphi d \xi } { ( \xi - z ) ^ { n - 1 } ( \xi - z _ { 0 } ) } } - \int _ { \gamma } { \frac { \varphi d \xi } { ( \xi - z _ { 0 } ) ^ { n } } } ] + ( z - z _ { 0 } ) \int _ { \gamma } { \frac { \varphi d \xi } { ( \xi - z ) ^ { n } ( \xi - z _ { 0 } ) } }
$$

we can conclude that $F _ { n } ( z )$ is continuous. Indeed, by the induction hypothesis, applied to $\varphi ( \zeta ) / ( \zeta - z _ { 0 } )$ , the first term tends to zero for ${ z  z _ { 0 } } ,$ and in the second term the factor of ${ z - z _ { 0 } }$ is bounded in a neighborhood of ${ \mathfrak { z } } _ { 0 }$ Now, if we divide the identity by ${ z - z _ { 0 } }$ and let $\pmb { z }$ tend to ${ \pmb z _ { 0 } } ,$ , the quotient in the first term tends to a derivative which by the induction hypothesis equals $( n - 1 ) F _ { n + 1 } ( z _ { 0 } )$ . The remaining factor in the second term is continuous, by what we have already proved, and has the limit $F _ { n + 1 } ( z _ { 0 } )$ Hence $F _ { n } ^ { \prime } ( z _ { 0 } )$ exists and equals $n F _ { n + 1 } ( z _ { 0 } )$ .

It is clear that Lemma 3 is just what is needed in order to deduce (23) and (24) in a rigorous way. We have thus proved that an analytic function has derivatives of all orders which are analytic and can be represented by the formula (24).

Among the consequences of this result we like to single out two classical theorems. The first is known as Morera's theorem, and it can be stated as follows:

If $f ( z )$ is defined and continuous in a region $\Omega$ , and if $\int _ { \gamma } f d z = 0$ for all closed curves $\gamma$ in $\Omega _ { i }$ , then $f ( z )$ is analytic in $\Omega$ .

The hypothesis implies, as we have already remarked in Sec. 1.3, that $f ( z )$ is the derivative of an analytic function $F ( z )$ . We know now that $f ( z )$ is then itself analytic.

A second classical result goes under the name of Liouvill's theorem:

A function which is analytic and bounded in the whole plane must reduce to a constant.

For the proof we make use of a simple estimate derived from (24). Let the radius of $c$ be $r _ { : }$ , and assume that $| f ( \zeta ) | \leqq M$ on $c$ If we apply (24) with ${ \mathfrak { z } } = { \mathfrak { a } }$ , we obtain at once

$$
\left| f ^ { ( n ) } ( a ) \right| \leq M n ! r ^ { - n } .
$$

For Liouville's theorem we need only the case $\pmb { n } = 1$ The hypothesis means that $| f ( \zeta ) | \leqq M$ on all circles. Hence we can let $\pmb { r }$ tend to $\infty$ , and (25) leads to $f ^ { \prime } ( a ) = 0$ for all $\pmb { a }$ We conclude that the function is constant.

Liouville's theorem leads to an almost trivial proof of the fundamental theorem of algebra. Suppose that $P ( z )$ is a polynomial of degree $> 0$ If $P ( z )$ were never zero, the function $\mathbf { 1 } / P ( z )$ would be analytic in the whole plane. We know that $P ( z )  \infty$ for ${ z \to \infty }$ , and therefore $1 / P ( z )$ tends to zero. This implies boundedness (the absolute value is continuous on the Riemann sphere and has thus a finite maximum), and by Liouville's theorem $1 / P ( z )$ would be constant. Since this is not so, the equation $P ( z ) = 0$ must have a root.

The inequality (25) is known as Cauchy's estimate. It shows above all that the successive derivatives of an analytic function cannot be arbitrary; there must always exist an $M$ and an $\pmb { r }$ so that (25) is fulfilled. In order to make the best use of the inequality it is important that $\pmb { r }$ be judiciously chosen, the object being to minimize the function $M ( r ) r ^ { - n }$ , where $M ( r )$ is the maximum of $| f |$ on $| \zeta - a | = r$ .

# EXERCISES

# 1. Compute

$$
\int _ { | z | = 1 } e ^ { z } z ^ { - n } d z ,
$$

$$
\int _ { | z | = 2 } z ^ { n } ( 1 - z ) ^ { m } d z , \qquad \int _ { | z | = \rho } | z - a | ^ { - 4 } | d z | ( | a | \neq \rho ) .
$$

2. Prove that a function which is analytic in the whole plane and satisfies an inequality $| f ( z ) | < | z | ^ { n }$ for some $\pmb { n }$ and all sufficiently large $| z |$ reduces to a polynomial.

${ \mathfrak { z } } _ { * }$ If $f ( z )$ is analytic and $| f ( z ) | \leqeq M$ for $| z | \leq R$ , find an upper bound for $\left| f ^ { ( n ) } ( z ) \right|$ in $| z | \leq \rho < R$ .

4. If $f ( z )$ is analytic for $| z | < 1$ and $| f ( z ) | \leq 1 / ( 1 - | z | )$ , find the best estimate of $\left| f ^ { ( n ) } ( 0 ) \right|$ that Cauchy's inequality will yield.

5. Show that the successive derivatives of an analytic function at a point can never satisfy $| f ^ { ( n ) } ( z ) | > n ! n ^ { n }$ Formulate a sharper theorem of the same kind.

$\pm \infty$ A more general form of Lemma 3 reads as follows:

Let the function $\varphi ( z , t )$ be continuous as a function of both variables when $_ z$ lies in a region $\Omega$ and $\alpha \leq t \leq \beta$ Suppose further that $\varphi ( { \boldsymbol { z } } , t )$ is analytic as a function of $z \in \Omega$ for any fixed $t$ Then

$$
F ( z ) = \int _ { \alpha } ^ { \beta } \varphi ( z , t ) \ d t
$$

is analytic in $\pmb { z }$ and

$$
F ^ { \prime } ( z ) = \int _ { \alpha } ^ { \beta } { \frac { \partial \varphi ( z , t ) } { \partial z } } d t .
$$

To prove this represent $\varphi ( { \boldsymbol { z } } , t )$ as a Cauchy integral

$$
\varphi ( z , t ) = { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } } \int _ { C } { \frac { \varphi ( \xi , t ) } { \xi - z } } d \xi .
$$

Fill in the necessary details to obtain

$$
F ( z ) = \int _ { C } \left( { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } } \int _ { \alpha } ^ { \beta } \varphi ( \zeta , t ) \ d t \right) { \frac { d \zeta } { \zeta \ - \ z } }
$$

and use Lemma 3 to prove (26).

Remark: It is interesting to note that the powerful Cauchy theorem makes it unnecessary to assume that ${ \pmb { \partial } } F / { \pmb { \partial } } z$ is a uniform limit of the difference quotient.

# 3. LOCAL PROPERTIES OF ANALYTIC FUNCTIONS

We have already proved that an analytic function has derivatives of all orders. In this section we will make a closer study of the local properties. It will include a classification of the isolated singularities of analytic functions.

3.1. Removable Singularities. Taylor's Theorem. In Theorem 3 we introduced a weaker condition which could be substituted for analyticity at a finite number of points without affecting the end result. We showed moreover, in Theorem 5, that Cauchy's theorem in a circular disk remains true under these weaker conditions. This was an essential point in our derivation of Cauchy's integral formula, for we were required to apply Cauchy's theorem to a function of the form $( f ( z ) - f ( a ) ) / ( z - a )$ .

Finally, it was pointed out that Cauchy's integral formula remains valid in the presence of a finite number of exceptional points, all satisfying the fundamental condition of Theorem 3, provided that none of them coincides with $\pmb { a }$ This remark is more important than it may seem on the surface. Indeed, Cauchy's formula provides us with a representation of $f ( z )$ through an integral which in its dependence on $\pmb { z }$ has the same character at the exceptional points as everywhere else. It follows that the exceptional points are such only by lack of information, and not by their intrinsic nature. Points with this character are called removable singularities. We shall prove the following precise theorem:

Theorem 7. Suppose that $f ( z )$ is analytic in the region $\pmb { \Omega } ^ { \prime }$ obtained by omitting a point $\pmb { a }$ from a region $\Omega$ $A$ necessary and sufficient condition that there exist an analytic function in $\Omega$ which coincides with $f ( z )$ in $\pmb { \Omega } ^ { \prime }$ is that lim $( z - a ) f ( z ) = 0$ . The extended function is uniquely determined. $_ { z  \pmb { \alpha } }$

The necessity and the uniqueness are trivial since the extended function must be continuous at $^ { a }$ To prove the sufficiency we draw a circle $c$ about $\pmb { a }$ so that $c$ and its inside are contained in $\Omega$ Cauchy's formula is valid, and we can write

$$
f ( z ) = { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } } \int _ { C } { \frac { f ( \zeta ) ~ d \zeta } { \zeta ~ - ~ z } }
$$

for all $z \neq a$ inside of $C$ . But the integral in the right-hand member

represents an analytic function of $\pmb { z }$ throughout the inside of $\pmb { c }$ Consequently, the function which is equal to $f ( z )$ for $z \neq a$ and which has the value

$$
\frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } \int _ { c } \frac { f ( \zeta ) ~ d \xi } { \zeta ~ - ~ a }
$$

for ${ \textit { z } } = { \textit { a } }$ is analytic in $\pmb { \Omega }$ It is natural to denote the extended function by $f ( z )$ and the value (27) by $f ( a )$ .

We apply this result to the function

$$
F ( z ) = { \frac { f ( z ) - f ( a ) } { z - a } }
$$

used in the proof of Cauchy's formula. It is not defined for ${ \pmb z } = { \pmb a }$ , but it satisfies the condition lim $( z - a ) F ( z ) = 0 $ The limit of $F ( z )$ as $\pmb { z }$ z→a   
tends to $\pmb { a }$ is ${ f ^ { \prime } } ( a )$ . Hence there exists an analytic function which is equal to $F ( z )$ for ${ \pmb z } \neq { \pmb a }$ and equal to $f ^ { \prime } ( a )$ for ${ z = a }$ Let us denote this function by $f _ { 1 } ( z )$ . Repeating the process we can define an analytic function $f _ { 2 } ( z )$ which equals $( f _ { 1 } ( z ) - f _ { 1 } ( a ) ) / ( z - a )$ for ${ \boldsymbol { z } } \neq { \boldsymbol { a } }$ and $f _ { \mathbf { 1 } } ^ { \prime } ( a )$ for ${ z } = { a } _ { \mathrm { { } { } { } } }$ ,and so on.

The recursive scheme by which $f _ { n } ( z )$ is defined can be written in the form

$$
\begin{array} { l } { f ( z ) = f ( a ) + ( z - a ) f _ { 1 } ( z ) } \\ { f _ { 1 } ( z ) = f _ { 1 } ( a ) + ( z - a ) f _ { 2 } ( z ) } \\ { \cdot \ \cdot \ \cdot \ \cdot \ \ \cdot \ \ \cdot \ \ \cdot \ \ \cdot \ \ \cdot \ \ \cdot \ \ \cdot \ \ \cdot \ \ \cdot \ \ \cdot \ . } \\ { f _ { n - 1 } ( z ) = f _ { n - 1 } ( a ) + ( z - a ) f _ { n } ( z ) . } \end{array}
$$

From these equations which are trivially valid also for ${ \textit { \textbf { z } } } = { \textit { \textbf { a } } }$ we obtain

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \begin{array} { r } { = f ( a ) + ( z - a ) f _ { 1 } ( a ) + ( z - a ) ^ { 2 } f _ { 2 } ( a ) + \cdot \cdot \cdot + ( z - a ) ^ { n - 1 } f _ { n - 1 } ( a ) } \\ { + ( z - a ) ^ { n } f _ { n } ( z } \end{array} } \end{array}
$$

Differentiating $\pmb { n }$ times and setting ${ \textit { \textbf { z } } } = { \textit { \textbf { a } } }$ we find

$$
f ^ { ( n ) } ( a ) \ = \ n ! f _ { n } ( a ) .
$$

This determines the coefficients $f _ { n } ( a )$ , and we obtain the following form of Taylor's theorem:

Theorem 8. If $f ( z )$ is analytic in a region $\Omega$ , containing $^ { a }$ , it is possible to write

$$
\begin{array} { c } { { f ( z ) = f ( a ) + \displaystyle \frac { f ^ { \prime } ( a ) } { 1 ! } ( z - a ) + \displaystyle \frac { f ^ { \prime \prime } ( a ) } { 2 ! } ( z - a ) ^ { 2 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot } } \\ { { + \displaystyle \frac { f ^ { ( n - 1 ) } ( a ) } { ( n - 1 ) ! } ( z - a ) ^ { n - 1 } + f _ { n } ( z ) ( z - a ) } } \end{array}
$$

where $f _ { n } ( z )$ is analytic in $\Omega$ .

This finite development must be well distinguished from the infinite Taylor series which we will study later. It is, however, the finite development (28) which is the most useful for the study of the local properties of $\pmb { f } ( \pmb { z } )$ . Its usefulness is enhanced by the fact that $f _ { n } ( z )$ has a simple explicit expression as a line integral.

Using the same circle $c$ as before we have first

$$
f _ { n } ( z ) = { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } } \int _ { \cal C } { \frac { f _ { n } ( \xi ) \ d \xi } { \xi - z } } .
$$

For $f _ { n } ( \xi )$ we substitute the expression obtained from (26). There will be one main term containing $f ( \zeta )$ . The remaining terms are, except for constant factors, of the form

$$
F _ { \nu } ( a ) = \int _ { C } \frac { d \xi } { ( \xi - a ) ^ { \nu } ( \xi - z ) } , \qquad \nu \geq 1 .
$$

But

$$
F _ { 1 } ( a ) = \frac { 1 } { z - a } \int _ { C } \left( \frac { 1 } { \xi - z } - \frac { 1 } { \xi - a } \right) d \xi = 0 ,
$$

identically for all $^ { a }$ inside of $c$ By Lemma 3 we have $F _ { \nu + 1 } ( a ) = F _ { 1 } ^ { ( \nu ) } ( a ) / \nu !$ and thus $F _ { \nu } ( a ) = 0$ for all $\nu \geq 1$ Hence the expression for $f _ { n } ( z )$ reduces to

$$
f _ { n } ( z ) = \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } \int _ { C } \frac { f ( \xi ) ~ d \xi } { ( \xi - a ) ^ { n } ( \xi - z ) } \cdot
$$

The representation is valid inside of $c$ .

3.2. Zeros and Poles. If $f ( a )$ and all derivatives $f ^ { ( \nu ) } ( a )$ vanish, we can write by (28)

$$
f ( z ) = f _ { n } ( z ) ( z - a ) ^ { n }
$$

for any $\pmb { n }$ An estimate for $f _ { n } ( z )$ can be obtained by (29). The disk with the circumference $c$ has to be contained in the region $\mathfrak { a }$ in which $f ( z )$ is defined and analytic. The absolute value $| f ( z ) |$ has a maximum $M$ on $c$ ; if the radius of $c$ is denoted by $\pmb { R }$ , we find

$$
| f _ { n } ( z ) | \leq \frac { M } { R ^ { n - 1 } ( R - | z - a | ) }
$$

for $| z - a | < R$ By (28) we have thus

$$
| f ( z ) | \leq \left( { \frac { | z - a | } { R } } \right) ^ { n } \cdot { \frac { M R } { R - | z - a | } } \cdot
$$

But $( | z - a | / R ) ^ { n } \to 0$ for $n \to \infty$ , since $| z - a | < R$ Hence $f ( z ) = 0$ inside of $c$ .

We show now that $f ( z )$ is identically zero in all of $\Omega$ Let ${ \pmb E _ { 1 } }$ be the set on which $f ( z )$ and all derivatives vanish and ${ \bf { { E } } } _ { 2 }$ the set on which the function or one of the derivatives is different from zero. ${ \pmb E _ { 1 } }$ is open by the above reasoning, and $\mathbf { { E _ { 2 } } }$ is open because the function and all derivatives are continuous. Therefore either ${ \pmb E _ { 1 } }$ or $\mathbf { { { E } _ { 2 } } }$ must be empty. If ${ \bf E _ { 2 } }$ is empty, the function is identically zero. If $\mathbf { { E _ { 1 } } }$ is empty, $\pmb { f } ( \pmb { z } )$ can never vanish together with all its derivatives.

Assume that $f ( z )$ is not identically zero. Then, if $\pmb { f } ( \pmb { a } ) = \mathbf { 0 }$ ,there exists a first derivative $f ^ { ( h ) } ( a )$ which is different from zero. We say then that $^ { a }$ is a zero of order $h _ { \mathrm { i } }$ , and the result that we have just proved expresses that there are no zeros of infinite order. In this respect an analytic function has the same local behavior as a polynomial, and just as in the case of polynomials we find that it is possible to write $f ( z ) = ( z - a ) ^ { h } f _ { h } ( z )$ where $f _ { h } ( z )$ is analytic and $f _ { h } ( a ) \neq 0$ .

In the same situation, since $f _ { h } ( z )$ is continuous, $f _ { h } ( z ) \neq 0$ in a neighborhood of $^ { a }$ and ${ \pmb z } = { \pmb a }$ is the only zero of $f ( z )$ in this neighborhood. In other words, the zeros of an analytic function which does not vanish identically are isolated. This property can also be formulated as a uniqueness theorem: If $f ( z )$ and $g ( z )$ are analytic in $\pmb { \Omega }$ , and if $f ( z ) = g ( z )$ on $^ { a }$ set which has an accumulation point in $\mathfrak { a }$ , then $f ( z )$ is identically equal to $g ( z )$ . The conclusion follows by consideration of the difference $f ( z ) - g ( z )$ :

Particular instances of this result which deserve to be quoted are the following: If $f ( z )$ is identically zero in a subregion of $\Omega ,$ then it is identically zero in $\mathfrak { a }$ , and the same is true if $f ( z )$ vanishes on an arc which does not reduce to a point. We can also say that an analytic function is uniquely determined by its values on any set with an accumulation point in the region of analyticity. This does not mean that we know of any way in which the values of the function can be computed.

We consider now a function $f ( z )$ which is analytic in a neighborhood of $^ { a }$ , except perhaps at $^ { a }$ itself. In other words, $f ( z )$ shall be analytic in a region $0 < | z - a | < \delta$ The point $^ { a }$ is called an isolated singularity of $f ( z )$ . We have already treated the case of a removable singularity. Since we can then define $f ( a )$ so that $f ( z )$ becomes analytic in the disk $| z - a | < \delta _ { \cdot }$ , it needs no further consideration. $^ \dagger$

If $\operatorname* { l i m } _ { z \to a } f ( z ) = \infty$ , the point $^ { a }$ is said to be a pole of $f ( z )$ , and we set $f ( a ) = \infty$ . There exists a $\delta ^ { \prime } \leq \delta$ such that $f ( z ) \neq 0$ for $0 < | z - a | < \delta ^ { \prime }$ . In this region the function $g ( z ) = 1 / f ( z )$ is defined and analytic. But the singularity of $g ( z )$ at $\pmb { a }$ is removable, and $g ( z )$ has an analytic exten-

$^ \dag$ If $\pmb { a }$ is a removable singularity, $\pmb { f } ( \pmb { z } )$ is frequently said to be regular at $\pmb { a }$ ; this term is sometimes used as a synonym for analytic.

sion with ${ \pmb g } ( { \pmb a } ) = { \pmb 0 }$ Since $g ( z )$ does not vanish identically, the zero at $\pmb { a }$ has a finite order, and we can write $g ( z ) = ( z - a ) ^ { h } g _ { h } ( z )$ with $g _ { h } ( a ) \neq 0$ . The number $\pmb { h }$ is the order of the pole, and $f ( z )$ has the representation $f ( z ) = ( z - a ) ^ { - h } f _ { h } ( z )$ where $f _ { h } ( z ) = 1 / g _ { h } ( z )$ is analytic and different from zero in a neighborhood of $\pmb { a }$ The nature of a pole is thus exactly the same as in the case of a rational function.

A function $f ( z )$ which is analytic in a region $\Omega$ , except for poles, is said to be meromorphic in $\pmb { \Omega }$ More precisely, to every ${ \pmb a } \in \Omega$ there shall exist a neighborhood $| z - a | < \delta$ ,contained in $\Omega$ , such that either $f ( z )$ is analytic in the whole neighborhood, or else $f ( z )$ is analytic for $0 < | z - a | < \delta ,$ and the isolated singularity is a pole. Observe that the poles of a meromorphic function are isolated by definition. The quotient $f ( z ) / g ( z )$ of two analytic functions in $\pmb { \Omega }$ is a meromorphic function in $\Omega _ { i }$ provided that ${ \pmb g } ( z )$ is not identically zero. The only possible poles are the zeros of $g ( z )$ , but a common zero of $\pmb { f } ( z )$ and ${ \pmb g } ( { \pmb z } )$ can also be a removable singularity. If this is the case, the value of the quotient must be determined by continuity. More generally, the sum, the product, and the quotient of two meromorphic functions are meromorphic. The case of an identically vanishing denominator must be excluded, unless we wish to consider the constant $\infty$ as a meromorphic function.

For a more detailed discussion of isolated singularities, we consider the conditions (1) $\operatorname* { l i m } _ { z \to a } | z - a | ^ { \alpha } | f ( z ) | = 0 ,$ (2) $\operatorname* { l i m } _ { z \to a } | z - a | ^ { \alpha } | f ( z ) | = \infty$ , for real values of $\pmb { \alpha }$ If (1) holds for a certain $\pmb { \alpha } ,$ then it holds for all larger $\pmb { \alpha }$ . and hence for some integer m. Then $( z \mathrm { ~ - ~ } a ) ^ { { m } f ( z ) }$ has a removable singularity and vanishes for $z = a$ Either $f ( z )$ is identically zero, in which case (1) holds for all $\pmb { \alpha }$ ,or $( z \mathrm { ~ - ~ } a ) ^ { { m } } f ( z )$ has a zero of finite order $\boldsymbol { k }$ In the latter case it follows at once that (1) holds for all $\alpha > h = - m - k _ { \mathrm { { r } } }$ while (2) holds for all $\alpha < h$ . Assume now that (2) holds for some $\pmb { \alpha }$ ; then it holds for all smaller $\pmb { \alpha }$ , and hence for some integer $\pmb { n }$ The function $( z - a ) ^ { n } f ( z )$ has a pole of finite order $l ,$ and setting $h = n + l$ we find again that (1) holds for $\alpha > h$ and for $\alpha < h$ The discussion shows that there are three possibilities: (i) condition (1) holds for all $\pmb { \alpha }$ , and $f ( z )$ vanishes identically; (ii) there exists an integer $\pmb { h }$ such that (1) holds for $\alpha > h$ and (2) for $\alpha < h$ ; (iii) neither (1) nor (2) holds for any $\pmb { \alpha }$ .

Case (i) is uninteresting. In case (ii) $h$ may be called the algebraic order of $f ( z )$ at ${ \pmb a } .$ It is positive in case of a pole, negative in case of a zero, and zero if $f ( z )$ is analytic but $\yen 0$ at ${ \pmb a }$ The remarkable thing is that the order is always an integer; there is no single-valued analytic function which tends to 0 or $\infty$ like a fractional power of $| z - a |$ .

In the case of a pole of order $\boldsymbol { h }$ , let us apply Theorem 8 to the analytic function $( z \mathrm { ~ - ~ } a ) ^ { h } f ( z )$ We obtain a development of the form

$$
~ ) = B _ { h } + B _ { h - 1 } ( z - a ) + \cdot ~ \cdot ~ \cdot ~ + B _ { 1 } ( z - a ) ^ { h - 1 } + \varphi ( z ) ( z - a ) ^ { h }
$$

where $\varphi ( z )$ is analytic at $z = a$ For $z \neq a$ we can divide by $( z - a ) ^ { h }$ and find

$$
\mathbf { \varepsilon } = B _ { h } ( z \cdot \mathbf { \varepsilon } - a ) ^ { - h } + B _ { h - 1 } ( z \cdot \mathbf { \varepsilon } - a ) ^ { - h + 1 } + \mathbf { \varepsilon } \cdot \mathbf { \varepsilon } \cdot \mathbf { \varepsilon } + B _ { 1 } ( z \cdot \mathbf { \varepsilon } - a ) ^ { - 1 } + \varphi ( z
$$

The part of this development which precedes $\varphi ( z )$ is called the singular part of $f ( z )$ at ${ \pmb z } = { \pmb a }$ A pole has thus not only an order, but also a welldefined singular part. The difference of two functions with the same singular part is analytic at $\pmb { a }$ .

In case (iii) the point $\pmb { a }$ is an essential isolated singularity. In the neighborhood of an essential singularity $f ( z )$ is at the same time unbounded and comes arbitrarily close to zero. As a characterization of the complicated behavior of a function in the neighborhood of an essential singularity, we prove the following classical theorem of Weierstrass:

Theorem 9. An analytic function comes arbitrarily close to any complex value in every neighborhood of an essential singularity.

If the assertion were not true, we could find a complex number $A$ and a $\delta > 0$ such that $| f ( z ) - A | > \delta$ in a neighborhood of $\pmb { a }$ (except for $z = a )$ . For any $\alpha < 0$ we have then $\operatorname* { l i m } _ { z \to a } | z - a | ^ { \alpha } | f ( z ) - A | = \infty$ . Hence ${ \pmb a }$ would not be an essential singularity of $f ( z ) - A$ Accordingly, there exists a $\beta$ with $\operatorname* { i m } _ { z \to a } | z - a | ^ { \beta } | f ( z ) - A | = 0$ ,and we are free . to choose ${ \boldsymbol { \beta } } > \mathbf { 0 }$ Since in that case $\operatorname* { l i m } _ { z \to a } \left| z - a \right| ^ { \beta } \left| A \right| = 0$ it would follow that $\operatorname* { l i m } _ { z \to a } | z - a | ^ { \beta } | f ( z ) | = 0 ,$ and $\pmb { a }$ would not be an essential singularity of $f ( z )$ . The contradiction proves the theorem.

The notion of isolated singularity applies also to functions which are analytic in a neighborhood $| z | > R$ of $\infty$ . Since $f ( \infty )$ is not defined, we treat $\infty$ as an isolated singularity, and by convention it has the same character of removable singularity, pole, or essential singularity as the singularity of $g ( z ) = f ( 1 / z )$ at ${ z } = 0$ If the singularity is nonessential, $f ( z )$ has an algebraic order $\pmb { h }$ such that $\operatorname* { l i m } _ { z \to \infty } z ^ { - h } f ( z )$ is neither zero nor infinity, and for a pole the singular part is a polynomial in $\pmb { z }$ If $\infty$ is an essential singularity, the function has the property expressed by Theorem 9 in every neighborhood of infinity.

1. If $f ( z )$ and $\pmb { g } ( \pmb { z } )$ have the algebraic orders $\pmb { h }$ and $\pmb { k }$ at ${ \boldsymbol { z } } = { \boldsymbol { a } } ,$ show that fg has the order $h + k , f / g$ the order $h - k ,$ and $f + g$ an order which does not exceed max (h,k).

2. Show that a function which is analytic in the whole plane and has a nonessential singularity at $\infty$ reduces to a polynomial.

Show that the functions $e ^ { z }$ , sin $z$ and cos $z$ have essential singularities at $\infty$ .

4. Show that any function which is meromorphic in the extended plane is rational.

5. Show that an isolated singularity of $f ( z )$ cannot be a pole of exp $f ( z )$ . Deduce that an isolated singularity is removable as soon as Re $f ( z )$ is bounded above or below.

3.3. The Local Mapping. We begin with the proof of a general formula which enables us to determine the number of zeros of an analytic function. We are considering a function $f ( z )$ which is analytic in an open disk $\Delta$ Let $\pmb { \gamma }$ be a closed curve in $\Delta$ such that $f ( z ) \neq 0$ on $\pmb { \gamma }$ For the sake of simplicity we suppose first that $f ( z )$ has only a finite number of zeros in $\Delta$ , and we agree to denote them by $z _ { 1 } , z _ { 2 } ,$ . . . , $z _ { n }$ where each zero is repeated as many times as its order indicates.

By repeated applications of Theorem 8, or rather its consequence (30), it is clear that we can write $f ( z ) = ( z - z _ { 1 } ) ( z - z _ { 2 } ) \cdot \cdot \cdot ( z - z _ { n } ) g ( z )$ where $g ( z )$ is analytic and $\yen 0$ in $\Delta$ Forming the logarithmic derivative we obtain

$$
{ \frac { f ^ { \prime } ( z ) } { f ( z ) } } = { \frac { 1 } { z - z _ { 1 } } } + { \frac { 1 } { z - z _ { 2 } } } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + { \frac { 1 } { z - z _ { n } } } + { \frac { g ^ { \prime } ( z ) } { g ( z ) } }
$$

for $z \neq z _ { j } ,$ and particularly on $\pmb { \gamma }$ Since $g ( z ) \neq 0$ in $\Delta$ , Cauchy's theorem yields

$$
\int _ { \gamma } { \frac { g ^ { \prime } ( z ) } { g ( z ) } } d z = 0 .
$$

Recalling the definition of $n ( \gamma , z _ { j } )$ we find

$$
n ( \gamma , z _ { 1 } ) + n ( \gamma , z _ { 2 } ) + \cdot \cdot \cdot + n ( \gamma , z _ { n } ) = \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } \int _ { \gamma } \frac { f ^ { \prime } ( z ) } { f ( z ) } d z .
$$

This is still true if $f ( z )$ has infinitely many zeros in $\Delta$ It is clear that $\pmb { \gamma }$ is contained in a concentric disk $\Delta ^ { \prime }$ smaller than $\Delta .$ Unless $f ( z )$ is identically zero, a case which must obviously be excluded, it has only a finite number of zeros in $\Delta ^ { \prime }$ This is an obvious consequence of the Bolzano-Weierstrass theorem, for if there were infinitely many zeros they would have an accumulation point in the closure of $\Delta ^ { \prime }$ , and this is impossible. We can now apply (31) to the disk $\Delta ^ { \prime }$ The zeros outside of $\Delta ^ { \prime }$ satisfy $n ( \gamma , z _ { j } ) = 0 \mathrm { ~ }$ and hence do not contribute to the sum in (31). We have thus proved:

Theorem 10. Let $\textit { \textbf { z } } _ { \textit { j } }$ be the zeros of $\pmb { a }$ function $f ( z )$ which is analytic in a circular disk $\Delta$ and does not vanish identically, each zero being counted as many times as its order indicates. For every closed curve $\pmb { \gamma }$ in $\Delta$ which does not pass through $\pmb { a }$ zero

$$
\sum _ { j } n ( \gamma , z _ { j } ) = \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } \int _ { \gamma } \frac { f ^ { \prime } ( z ) } { f ( z ) } \ : d z ,
$$

where the sum has only $\pmb { a }$ finite number of terms $\yen 0$ .

The function ${ \pmb w } = { \pmb f } ( { \pmb z } )$ maps $\pmb { \gamma }$ onto a closed curve $\mathbf { r }$ in the w-plane, and we find

$$
\int _ { \Gamma } { \frac { d w } { w } } = \int _ { \gamma } { \frac { f ^ { \prime } ( z ) } { f ( z ) } } d z .
$$

The formula (32) has thus the following interpretation:

$$
n ( \Gamma , 0 ) = \sum _ { j } n ( \gamma , z _ { j } ) .
$$

The simplest and most useful application is to the case where it is known beforehand that each $n ( \gamma , z _ { \ j } )$ must be either 0 or 1. Then (32) yields a formula for the total number of zeros enclosed by $\pmb { \gamma }$ This is evidently the case when $\pmb { \gamma }$ is a circle.

Let $\pmb { a }$ be an arbitrary complex value, and apply Theorem 10 to $f ( z ) -$ $\pmb { a }$ The zeros of $f ( z ) - a$ are the roots of the equation $f ( z ) = a _ { \mathrm { ; } }$ ,and we denote them by $z _ { \jmath } ( a )$ . In the place of (32) we obtain the formula

$$
\sum _ { j } n ( \gamma , z _ { j } ( a ) ) = { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } } \int _ { \gamma } { \frac { f ^ { \prime } ( z ) } { f ( z ) - a } } d z
$$

and (33) takes the form

$$
n ( \Gamma , a ) = \sum _ { j } n ( \gamma , z _ { j } ( a ) ) .
$$

It is necessary to assume that $f ( z ) \neq a$ on $\pmb { \gamma }$

If ${ \pmb a }$ and $^ { b }$ are in the same region determined by $\Gamma$ , we know that $n ( \Gamma , a ) = n ( \Gamma , b )$ , and hence we have also $\sum _ { j } n ( \gamma , z _ { j } ( a ) ) = \sum _ { j } n ( \gamma , z _ { j } ( b ) ) .$ . If $\pmb { \gamma }$ is a circle, it follows that $f ( z )$ takes the values $\pmb { a }$ and $^ { b }$ equally many times inside of $\pmb { \gamma }$ The following theorem on local correspondence is an immediate consequence of this result.

Theorem 11. Suppose that $f ( z )$ is analytic at ${ \tilde { \mathbf { \varepsilon } } } _ { \mathbf { 0 } } ,$ $f ( z _ { 0 } ) = w _ { 0 } ,$ and that $f ( z ) \mathrm { ~ - ~ } w _ { \mathrm { c } }$ has $\pmb { a }$ zero of order $\mathscr { n }$ at ${ \boldsymbol { z } } _ { \mathbf { 0 } }$ . If $\mathfrak { s } > 0$ is sufficiently small, there exists $\pmb { a }$ corresponding $\delta > 0$ such that for all $^ a$ with $| a - w _ { 0 } | < \delta$ the equation $f ( z ) = a$ has exactly $\mathscr { n }$ roots in the disk $| z - z _ { 0 } | < \varepsilon .$

We can choose & so that $f ( z )$ is defined and analytic for $| z - z _ { 0 } | \leq \varepsilon$ and so that ${ \pmb z _ { 0 } }$ is the only zero of $f ( z ) - w _ { 0 }$ in this disk. Let $\pmb { \gamma }$ be the circle $| z - z _ { 0 } | = \varepsilon$ and $\mathbf { r }$ its image under the mapping ${ \pmb w } = { \pmb f } ( { \pmb z } )$ Since ${ \pmb w } _ { \pmb 0 }$ belongs to the complement of the closed set $\mathbf { r }$ ,there exists a neighborhood $| w - w _ { 0 } | < \delta$ which does not intersect $\mathbf { r }$ (Fig. 21). It follows immediately that all values $\pmb { a }$ in this neighborhood are taken the same number of times inside of $\pmb { \gamma }$ The equation $f ( z ) = w _ { 0 }$ has exactly $\pmb { n }$ coinciding roots inside of $\pmb { \gamma } _ { \mathrm { : } }$ , and hence every value $\pmb { a }$ is taken $\pmb { n }$ times. It is understood that multiple roots are counted according to their multiplicity, but if e is sufficiently small we can assert that all roots of the equation $f ( z ) = a$ are simple for $a \neq w _ { 0 }$ Indeed, it is sufficient to choose e so that $f ^ { \prime } ( z )$ does not vanish for $0 < | z - z _ { 0 } | < \varepsilon .$ .

Corollary l. A nonconstant analytic function maps open sets onto open sets.

This is merely another way of saying that the image of every sufficiently small disk $| z - z _ { 0 } | < \varepsilon$ contains a neighborhood $\vert w - w _ { 0 } \vert < \delta$ .

In the case $\pmb { n } = \pmb { 1 }$ there is one-to-one correspondence between the disk $| w - w _ { 0 } | < \delta$ and an open subset $\Delta$ of $| z - z _ { 0 } | < \varepsilon .$ Since open sets in the $\pmb { z }$ -plane correspond to open sets in the w-plane the inverse function of $f ( z )$ is continuous, and the mapping is topological. The mapping can be restricted to a neighborhood of ${ \boldsymbol { z } } _ { 0 }$ contained in $\Delta$ , and we are able to state:

Corollary 2. If $f ( z )$ is analytic at ${ \mathfrak { z } } _ { \mathfrak { V } }$ with $f ^ { \prime } ( z _ { 0 } ) \neq 0 ;$ , it maps a neighborhood of ${ \pmb z _ { 0 } }$ conformally and topologically onto $\pmb { a }$ region.

From the continuity of the inverse function it follows in the usual way that the inverse function is analytic, and hence the inverse mapping is likewise conformal. Conversely, if the local mapping is one to one, Theorem 11 can hold only with $n = 1$ , and hence $\hat { f } ^ { \prime } ( z _ { 0 } )$ must be different from zero.

![](images/0f6d7b368d75f2507f87e541ff9bd5b98dded61c5202ab73ec8207c1b20d120e.jpg)

![](images/3fea0dc6341cfd906111d0e99467633e89885937d568fac61a8c0889669b2a06.jpg)  
FIG. 22. Branch point: $\pmb { n } = 3$ .

For $n > 1$ the local correspondence can still be described in very precise terms. Under the assumption of Theorem 11 we can write

$$
f ( z ) - w _ { 0 } = ( z - z _ { 0 } ) ^ { n } g ( z )
$$

where $\pmb { g } ( z )$ is analytic at ${ \pmb z _ { 0 } }$ and ${ \pmb g } ( z _ { 0 } ) \neq { \pmb 0 }$ Choose $\mathfrak { E } > \mathbf { 0 }$ so that $| g ( z ) - g ( z _ { 0 } ) | < | g ( z _ { 0 } ) |$ for $| z - z _ { 0 } | < \varepsilon$ In this neighborhood it is possible to define a single-valued analytic branch of $\sqrt [ n ] { g ( z ) }$ , which we denote by $h ( z )$ . We have thus

$$
\begin{array} { c } { { f ( z ) - w _ { 0 } = \xi ( z ) ^ { n } } } \\ { { \xi ( z ) = ( z - z _ { 0 } ) h ( z ) . } } \end{array}
$$

Since $\begin{array} { r } { \xi ^ { \prime } ( z _ { 0 } ) \ = \ h ( z _ { 0 } ) \neq 0 } \end{array}$ the mapping $\boldsymbol { \zeta } = \boldsymbol { \zeta } ( \boldsymbol { z } )$ is topological in a neighborhood of ${ \pmb z _ { 0 } }$ On the other hand, the mapping $w = w _ { 0 } + \xi ^ { n }$ is of an elementary character and determines $\pmb { n }$ equally spaced values $\boldsymbol { \xi }$ for each value of $\pmb { w }$ . By performing the mapping in two steps we obtain a very illuminating picture of the local correspondence. Figure 22 shows the inverse image of a small circular disk and the $\pmb { n }$ arcs which are mapped onto the positive radius.

Determine $\xi ( z )$ explicitly. 4. If $f ( z )$ is analytic at the origin and $f ^ { \prime } ( 0 ) \neq 0$ , prove the existence of

3.4. The Maximum Principle. Corol ary 1 oi Theorem 11 has a very important analytical consequence known as the maximum principle for analytic functions. Because of its simple and explicit formulation it is one of the most useful general theorems in the theory of functions. As a rule all proofs based on the maximum principle are very straightforward, and preference is quite justly given to proofs of this kind.

Theorem 12. (The maximum principle.) If $f ( z )$ is analytic and nonconstant in a region $\pmb { \Omega }$ , then its absolute value $\vert f ( z ) \vert$ has no maximum in $\pmb { \Omega }$ .

The proof is clear. If $\boldsymbol { w } _ { 0 } = f ( \boldsymbol { z } _ { 0 } )$ is any value taken in $\pmb { \Omega }$ ,there exists a neighborhood $\vert w - w _ { 0 } \vert < \mathfrak { s }$ contained in the image of $\pmb { \Omega }$ In this neighborhood there are points of modulus $> \vert w _ { 0 } \vert$ ,and hence $| f ( z _ { 0 } ) |$ is not the maximum of $\vert f ( z ) \vert$ .

In a positive formulation essentially the same theorem can be stated in the form:

Theorem ${ \bf { l 2 ^ { \prime } } }$ If $f ( z )$ is analytic on a closed bounded set $\mathbfit { \Delta } E$ , then the maximum of $| f ( z ) |$ is taken on the boundary of $\mathscr { E }$ .

Since $\pmb { \cal E }$ is compact $| f ( z ) |$ has a maximum on $\mathscr { E }$ If $f ( z )$ is constant, the assertion of the theorem is trivially true, for the boundary of $\mathbfit { \Delta } E$ is not empty. Suppose that the maximum were taken at an interior point ${ \boldsymbol { z } } _ { \mathbf { 0 } }$ Then $\vert f ( z _ { 0 } ) \vert$ would also be the maximum of $| f ( z ) |$ in a neighborhood $| z - z _ { 0 } | < \delta$ contained in $\pmb { \cal E }$ But this is impossible unless $f ( z )$ is constant in this neighborhood, and then $f ( z )$ is constant throughout its region of definition (we recall that $f ( z )$ is analytic on $\pmb { \cal E }$ if it is defined and analytic in a region which contains $\pmb { \cal E }$ ). Thus the maximum is always taken at a boundary point.

The maximum principle can also be proved analytically, as a consequence of Cauchy's integral formula. If the formula (22) is specialized to the case where $\pmb { \gamma }$ is a circle of center ${ \mathfrak { z } } _ { \mathfrak { d } }$ and radius $\boldsymbol { r }$ ,we can write $\zeta = z _ { 0 } + r e ^ { i \theta }$ , $d \xi = i r e ^ { i \theta } d \theta$ on $\pmb { \gamma }$ and obtain for $z = z _ { 0 }$

$$
f ( z _ { 0 } ) = \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi } \int _ { 0 } ^ { 2 \pi } f ( z _ { 0 } + r e ^ { i \theta } ) d \theta .
$$

This formula shows that the value of an analytic function at the center of a circle is equal to the arithmetic mean of its values on the circle, subject to the condition that the closed disk $| z - z _ { 0 } | \leq r$ is contained in the region of analyticity.

From (34) we derive the inequality

$$
\left| f ( z _ { 0 } ) \right| \leq { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi } } \int _ { 0 } ^ { 2 \pi } \left| f ( z _ { 0 } + r e ^ { i \theta } ) \right| d \theta .
$$

Suppose that $| f ( z _ { 0 } ) |$ were a maximum. Then we would have $| f ( z _ { 0 } + r e ^ { i \theta } ) |$ $\leq \vert f ( z _ { 0 } ) \vert$ , and if the strict inequality held for a single value of $\pmb \theta$ it would hold, by continuity, on a whole arc. But then the mean value of $| f ( z _ { \complement } + r e ^ { i \theta } ) |$ would be strictly less than $| f ( z _ { 0 } ) |$ , and (35) would lead to the contradiction $\lvert f ( z _ { 0 } ) \rvert < \lvert f ( z _ { 0 } ) \rvert$ Thus $| f ( z ) |$ must be constantly equal to $| f ( z _ { 0 } ) |$ on all sufficiently small circles $| z - z _ { 0 } | = r$ and, hence, in a neighborhood of ${ \boldsymbol { z } } _ { 0 }$ It follows easily that $f ( z )$ must reduce to a constant. This reasoning provides a second proof of the maximum principle. We have given preference to the first proof because it shows that the maximum principle is a consequence of the topological properties of the mapping by an analytic function.

Returning to the formulation given in Theorem $\mathbf { 1 2 ^ { \prime } }$ we observe that the most natural application is to the case where $\boldsymbol { \mathbf { \mathit { E } } }$ is a closed region. We find that a function which is analytic on a closed region takes its maximum on the boundary of the region. For some applications it is important to notice that the hypothesis of the theorem can be weakened. It is indeed sufficient to suppose that $f ( z )$ is continuous in the closed region and analytic in the open region. The continuity on the closed and bounded region ensures the existence of a maximum, and the analyticity in the open region implies that the maximum cannot be attained at an interior point unless the function reduces to a constant.

Consider now the case of a function $f ( z )$ which is analytic in the open disk $| z | < R$ and continuous on the closed disk $| z | \leq R$ If it is known that $| f ( z ) | \leq M$ on $| z | = R _ { \mathrm { i } }$ ,then $| f ( z ) | \leq M$ in the whole disk, by the preceding remark. The equality can hold only if $f ( z )$ is a constant of absolute value $M$ Therefore, if it is known that $f ( z )$ takes some value of modulus $< M$ , it may be expected that a better estimate can be given. Theorems to this effect are very useful. The following particular result is known as the lemma of Schwarz:

Theorem 13. If $f ( z )$ is analytic for $| z | < 1$ and satisfies the conditions $| f ( z ) | \leq 1 , f ( 0 ) = 0 ;$ then $| f ( z ) | \leq | z |$ and $\left| f ^ { \prime } ( 0 ) \right| \leq 1$ Equality holds only if $f ( z ) = c z$ with $\pmb { a }$ constant $\pmb { c }$ of absolute value 1.

We apply the maximum principle to the function $f _ { 1 } ( z )$ which is equal to $f ( z ) / z$ for $z \neq 0$ and to $f ^ { \prime } ( 0 )$ for $z = 0$ On the circle $| z | = r < 1$ it is of absolute value $\leq 1 / r$ ,and hence $\left| f _ { 1 } ( z ) \right| \le 1 / r$ for $| z | \leq r$ Letting $\pmb { r }$ tend to 1 we find that $\left| f _ { 1 } ( z ) \right| \le 1$ for all $z$ , and this is the assertion of the theom. If the equality holds at a sigle point, it means that $\vert f _ { 1 } ( z ) \vert$ attains its maximum and, hence, that $f _ { 1 } ( z )$ must reduce to a constant.

The rather specialized assumptions of Theorem 13 are not essential, but should be looked upon as the result f a noralizaion.Forinse, if $f ( z )$ is known to satisfy the conditions of the theorem in a disk of radius $R ,$ the original form of the theorem can be applied to the function $f ( R z )$ . As a result we obtain $\left| f ( R z ) \right| \leqq | z |$ , which can be rewritten as $| f ( z ) | \leq | z | / R$ . Similarly, if the upper bound of the modulus is $M$ instead of 1, we apply the theorem to $f ( z ) / M$ or, in the more general case, to $f ( R z ) { \mathrm { \cdot } } / M$ The resulting inequality is $| f ( z ) | \leq M | z | / R$ .

Still more generally, we may replace the condition $f ( { \bf 0 } ) = { \bf 0 }$ by an arbitrary condition $f ( z _ { 0 } ) = w _ { 0 }$ where $\vert z _ { 0 } \vert < R$ and $| w _ { 0 } | < M$ Let $\xi = T z$ be a linear transformation which maps $| z | < R$ onto $| \zeta | < 1$ with ${ \mathfrak { z } } _ { 0 }$ going into the origin, and let $\ b { S w }$ be a linear transformation with $\mathbf { \nabla } S w _ { 0 } = 0$ which maps $| w | < M$ onto $| S w | < 1$ It is clear that the function $S f ( T ^ { - 1 } \zeta )$ satisfies the hypothesis of the original theorem. Hence we obtain $| S f ( T ^ { - 1 } \{ \} ) | \leq | \zeta |$ ,or $| S f ( z ) | \leqq | T z |$ . Explicitly, this inequality can be written in the form

$$
\left| \frac { M ( f ( z ) - w _ { 0 } ) } { M ^ { 2 } - \bar { w } _ { 0 } f ( z ) } \right| \leq \left| \frac { R ( z - z _ { 0 } ) } { R ^ { 2 } - \bar { z } _ { 0 } z } \right| .
$$

$$

$$

$$

and

{ \frac { | f ^ { \prime } ( z ) | } { \operatorname { I m } f ( z ) } } \leq { \frac { 1 } { y } } \qquad ( z = x + i y ) .

$\ast _ { 6 }$ If $\pmb { \gamma }$ is a piecewise differentiable arc contained in $| z | < 1$ the integral

\int _ { \gamma } \frac { | d z | } { 1 - | z | ^ { 2 } }

is called the noneuclidean length (or hyperbolic length) of $\pmb { \gamma }$ Show that an analytic function $f ( z )$ with $| f ( z ) | < 1$ for $| z | < 1$ maps every $\pmb { \gamma }$ on an arc with smaller or equal noneuclidean length.

The shortest noneuclidean length is called the noneuclidean distance between the end points. Derive a formula for the noneuclidean distance between ${ \pmb z _ { 1 } }$ and ${ \pmb z } _ { 2 }$ Answer:

$$
\frac { 1 } { 2 } \log \frac { 1 + \left| \frac { z _ { 1 } - z _ { 2 } } { 1 - \bar { z } _ { 1 } z _ { 2 } } \right| } { 1 - \left| \frac { z _ { 1 } - z _ { 2 } } { 1 - \bar { z } _ { 1 } z _ { 2 } } \right| } .

# 4. THE GENERAL FORM OF CAUCHY'S THEOREM

In our preliminary treatment of Cauchy's theorem and the integral formula we considered only the case of a circular region. For the purpose of studying the local properties of analytic functions this was quite adequate, but from a more general point of view we cannot be satisfied with a result which is so obviously incomplete. The generalization can proceed in two directions. For one thing we can seek to characterize the regions in which Cauchy's theorem has universal validity. Secondly, we can consider an arbitrary region and look for the curves $\pmb { \gamma }$ for which the assertion of Cauchy's theorem is true.

4.1. Chains and Cycles. In the first place we must generalize the notion of line integral. To this end we examine the equation

$$
\int _ { \gamma _ { 1 } + \gamma _ { 2 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + \gamma _ { n } } f d z = \int _ { \gamma _ { 1 } } f d z + \int _ { \gamma _ { 2 } } f d z + \cdot \cdot \cdot + \int _ { \gamma _ { n } } f d z
$$

which is valid when γ1, , . . . , Yn form a subdivision of the arc γ. Since the right-hand member of (37) has a meaning for any finite collectio n t  en  y $\gamma _ { 1 } + \gamma _ { 2 } + \cdots + \gamma _ { n _ { 1 } }$ wh need not be n  and wedene e responding integral by means of equation (37). Such formal sums of arcs are called chains. It is clear that nothing is lost and much may be gained by considering line integrals over arbitrary chains.

Just as there is nothing unique about the way in which an arc can be subdivided, it is clear that different formal sums can represent the same chain. The guiding principle is that two chains should be considered identical if they yield the same line integrals for all functions $f .$ If this principle is analyzed, we find that the following operations do not change the identity of a chain: (1) permutation of two arcs, (2) subdivision of an arc, (3) fusion of subarcs to a single arc, (4) reparametrization of an arc, (5) cancellation of opposite arcs. On this basis it would be easy to formulate a logical equivalence relation which defines the identity of chains in a formal manner. Inasmuch as the situation does not involve any logical pitfalls, we shall dispense with this formalization.

The sum of two chains is defined in the obvious way by juxtaposition. It is clear that the additive property (37) of line integrals remains valid for arbitrary chains. When identical chains are added, it is convenient to denote the sum as a multiple. With this notation every chain can be written in the form

$$
\gamma = a _ { 1 } \gamma _ { 1 } + a _ { 2 } \gamma _ { 2 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + a _ { n } \gamma _ { n }
$$

where the ${ \pmb a } _ { j }$ are positive integers and the $\gamma _ { j }$ are all different. For opposite arcs we are allowed to write $a ( - \gamma ) = - a \gamma$ and continue the reduction of (38) until no two $\pmb { \gamma } _ { j }$ are opposite. The coefficients will be arbitrary integers, and terms with zero coefficients can be added at will. The last device enables us to express any two chains in terms of the same arcs, and their sum is obtained by adding corresponding coefficients. The zero chain is either an empty sum or a sum with all coefficients equal to zero.

A chain is a cycle if it can be represented as a sum of closed curves. Very simple combinatorial considerations show that a chain is a cycle if and only if in any representation the initial and end points of the individual arcs are identical in pairs. Thus it is immediately possible to tell whether a chain is a cycle or not.

In the applications we shall consider chains which are contained in a given region $\pmb { \Omega }$ By this we mean that the chains have a representation by arcs in $\Omega$ and that only such representations will be considered. It is clear that all theorems which we have heretofore formulated only for closed curves in a region are in fact valid for arbitrary cycles in a region. In particular, the integral of an exact differential over any cycle is zero.

The index of a point with respect to a cycle is defined in exactly the same way as in the case of a single closed curve. It has the same properties, and in addition we can formulate the obvious but important additive law expressed by the equation $n ( \gamma _ { 1 } + \gamma _ { 2 } , a ) = n ( \gamma _ { 1 } , a ) + n ( \gamma _ { 2 } , a )$ .

4.2. Simple Connectivity. There is little doubt that all readers will know what we mean if we speak about a region without holes. Such regions are said to be simply connected, and it is for simply connected regions that Cauchy's theorem is universally valid. The suggestive language we have used cannot take the place of a mathematical cefinition, but fortunately very little is needed to make the term precise. Indeed, a region without holes is obviously one whose complement consists of a single piece. We are thus led to the following definition:

Definition l. A region is simply connected if its complement with respect to the extended plane is connected.

At this point we warn the reader that this definition is not the one that is commonly accepted, the main reason being that our definition cannot be used in more than two real dimensions. In the course of our work we shall find, however, that the property expressed by Definition 1 is equivalent to a number of other properties, more or less equally important. One of these states that any closed curve can be contracted to a point, and this condition is usually chosen as definition. Our choice has the advantage of leading very quickly to the essential results in complex integration theory.

It is easy to see that a circular disk, a half plane, and a parallel strip are simply connected. The last example shows the importance of taking the complement with respect to the extended plane, for the complement of the strip in the finite plane is evidently not connected. The definition can be applied to regions on the Riemann sphere, and this is evidently the most symmetric situation. For our purposes it is nevertheless better to agree that all regions lie in the finite plane unless the contrary is explicitly stated. According to this convention the outside of a circle is not simply connected, for its complement consists of a closed disk and the point at infinity.

Theorem 14. A region Ω is simply connected if and only if $\boldsymbol { n } ( \boldsymbol { \gamma } , \boldsymbol { a } ) = \mathbf { 0 }$ for all cycles $\pmb { \gamma }$ in $\pmb { \Omega }$ and all points $\pmb { a }$ which do not belong to $\Omega _ { \ast }$

This alternative condition is also very suggestive. It states that a closed curve in a simply connected region cannot wind around any point which does not belong to the region. It seems quite evident that this condition is not fulflled in the case of a region with a hole.

The necessity of the condition is almost trivial. Let $\pmb { \gamma }$ be any cycle in If the complement of $\pmb { \Omega }$ is connected, it must be contained in one of the regions determined by $\pmb { \gamma } ,$ and inasmuch as $\infty$ belongs to the complement this must be the unbounded region. Consequently $\pmb { n } ( \pmb { \gamma } , \pmb { a } ) = \mathbf { 0 }$ for all finite points in the complement.

![](images/25ec8ada19e7b5ebafc452525e02ca07be6985ec10d5c12f032cb23383e3953d.jpg)  
FIG. 23. Curve with index 1.

For the precise proof of the sufficiency an explicit construction is needed. We assume that the complement of $\pmb { \Omega }$ can be represented as the union $A \cup B$ of two disjoint closed sets. One of these sets contains $\infty$ , and the other is consequently bounded; let $A$ be the bounded set. The sets $A$ and $B$ have a shortest distance $\delta > 0$ Cover the whole plane with a net of squares $Q$ of side $< \delta / { \sqrt { 2 } }$ We are free to choose the net so that a certain point ${ \pmb { a } } \in A$ lies at the center of a square. The boundary curve of $Q$ is denoted by $\partial Q$ ; we assume explicitly that the squares $Q$ are closed and that the interior of $Q$ lies to the left of the directed line segments which make up $\partial Q$ .

Consider now the cycle

$$
\gamma = \sum _ { j } \partial Q _ { j }
$$

where the sum ranges over all squares $Q _ { j }$ in the net which have a point in common with $A$ (Fig. 23). Because $\pmb { a }$ is contained in one and only one of these squares, it is evident that $n ( \gamma , a ) = 1$ Furthermore, it is clear that $\pmb { \gamma }$ does not meet $\pmb { B }$ But if the cancellations are carried out, it is equally clear that $\pmb { \gamma }$ does not meet $\pmb { A }$ . Indeed, any side which meets $A$ is a common side of two squares included in the sum (39), and since the directions are opposite the side does not appear in the reduced expression of $\pmb { \gamma }$ Hence $\pmb { \gamma }$ is contained in $\Omega ,$ and our theorem is proved.

We remark now that Cauchy's theorem is certainly not valid for regions which are not simply connected. In fact, if there is a cycle $\pmb { \gamma }$ in $\pmb { \Omega }$ such that ${ \pmb n } ( { \pmb \gamma } , { \pmb a } ) \neq { \pmb 0 }$ for some $\pmb { a }$ outside of $\pmb { \Omega }$ , then $1 / ( z - a )$ is analytic in $\pmb { \Omega }$ while its integral

$$
\int _ { \gamma } { \frac { d z } { z - a } } = 2 \pi i n ( \gamma , a ) \not \simeq 0 .
$$

4.3. Exact Differentials in Simply Connected Regions. We will now prove that Cauchy's theorem holds in an arbitrary simply connected region. In view of Theorem 1 we need only prove that $f ( z ) ~ d z$ is an exact differential. This is known to be so when attention is restricted to a circular disk contained in $\pmb { \Omega }$ Therefore, our task is to prove that a differential which is exact in a neighborhood of each point is also exact in the whole region $\Omega _ { i }$ provided that $\pmb { \Omega }$ is simply connected. In this form the statement is not limited to differentials of the form $f d z ,$ and we prefer to prove the following proposition:

Theorem 15. The differential $p \ d { \boldsymbol { x } } + q$ dy whose coefficients are defined and continuous in $\pmb { a }$ simply connected region $\pmb { \Omega }$ is exact in $\pmb { \Omega }$ if and only if

$$
\int _ { \partial R } p d x + q d y = 0
$$

for every rectangle $\boldsymbol { R }$ contained in $\pmb { \Omega }$ .

We choose a point $z _ { 0 } \in \Omega$ and define $U ( z )$ by

$$
U ( z ) = \int _ { \sigma } p d x + q d y
$$

where $\pmb { \sigma }$ is a polygon from ${ \mathfrak { z } } _ { \mathfrak { d } }$ to ${ \pmb z } _ { 1 }$ contained in $\Omega _ { i }$ , with sides parallel to the axes. If we can show that $U ( z )$ is independent of the choice of $\pmb { \sigma }$ , it follows in the same way as in the proof of Theorem 1 that $\partial U / \partial x = p ,$ $\partial U / \partial y = q$ The difference $\gamma = \sigma _ { 1 } - \sigma _ { 2 }$ of two polygons from ${ \pmb z _ { 0 } }$ to $\pmb { z }$ is a closed polygon in $\Omega$ with vertical and horizontal sides, and we have to show that the integral of $p d x + q d y$ over $\pmb { \gamma }$ is zero.

For the proof we use a rectangular net obtained by drawing lines parallel to both axes through all the vertices of $\pmb { \gamma }$ (Fig. 24). There will be some finite rectangles $R _ { i }$ and some unbounded regions $R _ { j } ^ { \prime }$ which may be considered as infinite rectangles. Inasmuch as we need not consider the trivial case in which $\pmb { \gamma }$ lies on a vertical or horizontal line, we may assume that there is at least one finite rectangle $R _ { i }$ .

![](images/624d3619a4c3b3165565311a0dae16181c981533d87bce4a08f37585d170c316.jpg)  
FIG. 24

Choose a point $a _ { i }$ from the interior of each $R _ { i } ,$ and form the cycle

$$
\gamma _ { 0 } = \sum _ { i } n ( \gamma , a _ { i } ) \ \partial R _ { i }
$$

where the sum ranges over all finite rectangles; the coefficients $n ( \gamma , a _ { i } )$ are well determined, for no ${ \pmb a } _ { i }$ can lie on $\pmb { \gamma }$ In the discussion that follows we shall also make use of points $\pmb { a } _ { j } ^ { \prime }$ chosen from the interior of each $R _ { j } ^ { \prime } { \mathrm { : } }$ , although they are not needed for the construction of $\pmb { \gamma _ { 0 } }$ .

The choice of $\gamma _ { 0 } ,$ defined by (40), is dictated by a definite purpose. It is clear that $n ( \partial R _ { i } , a _ { k } ) = 1$ if $k = i$ and 0 if $k \neq i$ ; similarly, $n ( \partial R _ { i } , a _ { j } ^ { \prime } ) = 0$ for all $j$ With this in mind we obtain from (40) $n ( \gamma _ { 0 } , a _ { i } ) = n ( \gamma , a _ { i } )$ and $n ( \gamma _ { 0 } , a _ { j } ^ { \prime } ) = 0 .$ It is also true that ${ \pmb n } ( \gamma , a _ { j } ^ { \prime } ) = { \pmb 0 }$ , for evidently the interior of $R _ { j } ^ { \prime }$ must belong to the unbounded region determined by $\pmb { \gamma }$ We have thus shown that $n ( \gamma - \gamma _ { 0 } , a ) = 0$ for all $a = a _ { i }$ and $\mathbf { \nabla } \mathbf { a } = \mathbf { a } _ { j } ^ { \prime } ,$ .

From this property of $\gamma - \gamma _ { 0 }$ we wish to conclude that $\pmb { \gamma _ { 0 } }$ is identical with $\pmb { \gamma }$ Let $\sigma _ { i k }$ be the common side of two adjacent rectangles $R _ { i } , \ R _ { k }$ ; we choose the orientation so that $R _ { i }$ lies to the left of $\sigma _ { i k }$ Suppose that the reduced expression of $\gamma - \gamma _ { 0 }$ contains the multiple $\cos \alpha$ Then the cycle $\gamma - \gamma _ { 0 } - c \partial R _ { i }$ does not contain $\pmb { \sigma } _ { i k }$ , and it follows that ${ a } _ { i }$ and $\pmb { a } _ { k }$ must have the same index with respect to this cycle. On the other hand, the respective indices are evidently $- \pmb { c }$ and 0; we conclude that $\pmb { c } = \pmb { 0 }$ The same reasoning applies if $\pmb { \sigma } _ { i j }$ is the common side of a finite rectangle $\pmb { R _ { i } }$ and an infinite rectangle $\mathcal { R } _ { j } ^ { \prime }$ . In the presence of at least one finite rectangie it is clear that no two infinite rectangles can have a finite side in common, and we have proved conclusively that $\gamma - \gamma _ { 0 }$ must be identically zero. This means that $\pmb { \gamma }$ can be represented in the form

$$
\pmb { \gamma } = \sum _ { i } n ( \gamma , \pmb { a } _ { i } ) \ \partial R _ { i } .
$$

We prove now that the representation (41) is a representation in $\pmb { \Omega }$ ; more precisely, we show that the rectangles $R _ { i }$ whose corresponding coefficient ${ \pmb n } ( { \pmb \gamma } , { \pmb a } _ { i } )$ is different from 0 are contained in $\Omega$ This is a consequence of the simple connectivity of $\pmb { \Omega }$ Indeed, suppose that a point $^ { a }$ in the closed rectangle $R _ { i }$ were not in $\pmb { \Omega } ,$ Then $\boldsymbol { n } ( \gamma , a ) = \mathbf { 0 } ,$ , by Theorem 14. On the other hand, the line segment between $\pmb { a }$ and ${ \pmb a } _ { i }$ does not intersect $\pmb { \gamma } _ { : }$ , and hence $n ( \gamma , a _ { i } ) = n ( \gamma , a )$ , contradicting the hypothesis $n ( \gamma , a _ { i } ) \neq 0$ .

It follows that the hypothesis of Theorem 15 applies to each $\partial ( R _ { i } )$ which occurs effectively in (41). Consequently,

$$
\int _ { \gamma } p d x + q d y = 0 ,
$$

and the theorem is proved.

The most important consequence is Cauchy's theorem for simply connected regions:

Theorem 16. If $f ( z )$ is analytic in $\pmb { a }$ simply connected region Ω, then

$$
\int _ { \gamma } f ( z ) d z = 0
$$

for every cycle $\pmb { \gamma }$ in S2.

The following corollary is of frequent use:

Corollary. If $f ( z )$ is analytic and $\yen 0$ in $\pmb { a }$ simply connected region $\pmb { \Omega }$ $\pmb { a }$ single-valued analytic branch of log $f ( z )$ can be defined in $\Omega$ .

By the preceding theorem the function $f ^ { \prime } ( z ) / f ( z )$ has an indefinite integral $F ( z )$ in $\pmb { \Omega }$ The function $f ( z ) e ^ { - F ( z ) }$ has then the derivative 0 and must reduce to a constant. Choosing a point $z _ { 0 } \epsilon \Omega$ and an arbitrary value of log $\scriptstyle f ( z _ { 0 } )$ we find that

$$
e ^ { F ( z ) - F ( z _ { 0 } ) + \log f ( z _ { 0 } ) } = f ( z ) ,
$$

and consequently we can set log $f ( z ) = F ( z ) - F ( z _ { 0 } ) + \log f ( z _ { 0 } ) .$

Under the same circumstances we can also determine a single-valued branch of an arbitrary power $f ( z ) ^ { \mu } = e ^ { \mu \log f ( z ) }$ .

4.4. Multiply Connected Regions. The proof of Theorem 15 includes more than we have stated. As a matter of fact the hypothesis of simple connectivity was used merely to conclude that $\boldsymbol { n } ( \gamma , a ) = \mathbf { 0 }$ for all points $\pmb { a }$ not in $\pmb { \Omega }$ For particular polygons this condition may well be fulflled even when the region $\pmb { \Omega }$ is not simply connected. In an arbitrary region we have in effect proved that

$$
\int _ { \gamma } \mathscr { p } d x + \mathscr { q } d y = 0
$$

for any closed polygon $\pmb { \gamma }$ which does not wind around any outside points, and it is reasonable to expect that this result is not restricted to polygons. However, the transition from a polygon with horizontal and vertical sides to an arbitrary cycle must be based on a different argument, for we can no longer be sure that the differential is exact.

Before we proceed with this proof it is convenient to introduce a name for the type of cycles with which we shall be concerned.

Definition 2. A cycle $\pmb { \gamma }$ in $\pmb { \Omega }$ is said to be homologous to zero with respect to $\pmb { \Omega }$ if and only if ${ \pmb n } ( { \pmb \gamma } , { \pmb a } ) = { \pmb 0 }$ for all points $\pmb { a }$ not in $\pmb { \Omega }$ .

In symbols we write $\gamma \sim 0 \ ( \mathrm { m o d } \ \Omega )$ . When it is clear to what region we are referring, $\pmb { \Omega }$ need not be mentioned explicitly. The notation $\gamma _ { 1 } \sim \gamma _ { 2 }$ shall be equivalent to $\gamma _ { 1 } - \gamma _ { 2 } \sim 0 .$ It is clear that homologies can be added and subtracted. Moreover, $\gamma \sim 0$ (mod $\Omega$ implies $\gamma \sim 0$ (mod $\pmb { \Omega } ^ { \prime }$ )for every $\Omega ^ { \prime } \supset \Omega .$ .

Again, our terminology does not quite agree with standard practice. It was E. Artin who discovered that the characterization of homology by vanishing winding numbers ties in precisely with what is needed for Cauchy's theorem. This idea has led to a remarkable simplification of earlier proofs.

Theorem 17. If $p d x + q$ dy is locally exact in $\pmb { \Omega }$ ,i.e., if

$$
\int _ { \gamma } p d x + q d y = 0
$$

for $\gamma = \partial R$ , $\pmb { R }$ being any rectangle contained in $\Omega ,$ , then (42) holds for every cycle $\pmb { \gamma }$ which is homologous to zero in $\pmb { \Omega }$ .

For the proof it is sufficient to show that $\pmb { \gamma }$ can be replaced by a polygon $\pmb { \sigma }$ with horizontal and vertical sides such that every locally exact differential has the same integral over $\pmb { \sigma }$ as over $\pmb { \gamma }$ This property implies, in particular, $\boldsymbol { n } ( \sigma , a ) = \boldsymbol { n } ( \gamma , a )$ , and hence $\sigma \sim 0$ The proof of Theorem 15 applies to $\pmb { \sigma }$ , and we may conclude that $\pmb { \gamma }$ satisfies condition (42).

We construct $\pmb { \sigma }$ as an approximation of $\pmb { \gamma }$ Let the distance from $\pmb { \gamma }$ to the complement of $\pmb { \Omega }$ be $\geq \rho > 0$ If the equation of $\pmb { \gamma }$ is $z = z ( t )$ , $a \leq t \leq b$ , the function ${ \boldsymbol z } ( t )$ is uniformly continuous on the closed interval $^ { ( a , b ) }$ . We determine $\delta < 0$ so that $| z ( t ) - z ( t ^ { \prime } ) | < \rho$ for $| t - t ^ { \prime } | < \delta$ and divide $^ { ( a , b ) }$ in subintervals of length $< \delta$ The corresponding subarcs of $\pmb { \gamma } _ { : }$ ,which we denote by $\gamma _ { \mathrm { i } , \mathrm { \ell } }$ have the property that each $\gamma _ { i }$ is contained in a disk of radius $\pmb { \rho }$ which lies entirely in $\Omega$ The end points of $\gamma _ { i }$ can be joined within the same disk by a polygon $\pmb { \sigma } _ { i }$ consisting of a horizontal and a vertical segment. Since our differential is exact in the disk,

$$
\int _ { \sigma _ { i } } p d x + q d y = \int _ { x i } p d x + a d y ,
$$

and setting $\boldsymbol { \sigma } = \boldsymbol { \Sigma } \sigma _ { i }$ we obtain

$$
\int _ { \sigma } p d x + q d y = \int _ { \gamma } p d x + q d y ;
$$

this completes the proof.

Theorem 17 characterizes the cycles which are homologous to zero. We have proved, in effect, that if the integral over $\pmb { \gamma }$ vanishes for all differentials of the special form $d z / ( z - a )$ with $\mathbf { \Omega } _ { a }$ outside $\Omega _ { s }$ then it vanishes for all locally exact differentials. $^ \dagger$ In particular, it vanishes for the differentials of the form $f ( z ) ~ d z$ where $f ( z )$ is analytic in $\pmb { \Omega }$ This is the final and most complete form of Cauchy's theorem.

Theorem 18. If $f ( z )$ is analytic in $\pmb { \Omega }$ , then

$$
\int _ { \gamma } f ( z ) \ d z = 0
$$

for every cycle $\gamma$ which is homologous to zero in $\Omega$

A region which is not simply connected is called multiply connected. More precisely, $\Omega$ is said to have the finite connectivity $\pmb { n }$ if the complement of $\Omega$ has exactly $\pmb { n }$ components and infinite connectivity if the complement has infinitely many components. In a less precise but more suggestive language, a region of connectivity $\pmb { n }$ arises by punching $\pmb { \mathscr { n } }$ holes in the Riemann sphere.

In the case of finite connectivity, let $A _ { 1 } , A _ { 2 } ,$ . . . , $A _ { n }$ be the components of the complement of $\Omega _ { i }$ , and assume that $\infty$ belongs to $\pmb { A } _ { n }$ . If

$^ \dagger$ As in the hypothesis of Theorem 17 a differential is said to be locally exact in $\clubsuit$ region $\mathfrak { a }$ if it is exact in some neighborhood of each point in $\Omega _ { \ast }$

$\pmb { \gamma }$ is an arbitrary cycle in $\Omega _ { \mathfrak { a } }$ , we can prove, just as in Theorem 14, that ${ \pmb n } ( \pmb { \gamma } , { \pmb a } )$ is constant when $\pmb { a }$ varies over any one of the components $A _ { \imath }$ and that ${ \pmb n } ( { \pmb \gamma } , { \pmb a } ) = { \pmb 0 }$ in $\pmb { A } _ { n }$ Moreover, duplicating the construction used in the proof of the same theorem we can find cycle: $\mathrm { ~ s ~ } \gamma _ { i } , i = 1 , \mathrm { ~ . ~ . ~ . ~ } , n - 1 ,$ such that $\mathbf { \nabla } n ( \gamma _ { i } , a ) = 1$ for ${ \pmb { a } } \in A _ { i }$ and ${ \pmb n } ( \gamma _ { i } , { \pmb a } ) = { \pmb 0 }$ for all other points outside of $\pmb { \Omega }$ .

For a given cycle $\pmb { \gamma }$ in $\Omega _ { i }$ ,let $c _ { i }$ be the constant value of ${ \pmb n } ( { \pmb \gamma } , { \pmb a } )$ for ${ \pmb { a } } \in A _ { \imath }$ . We find that any point outside of $\pmb { \Omega }$ has the index zero with respect to the cycle $\gamma - c _ { 1 } \gamma _ { 1 } - c _ { 2 } \gamma _ { 2 } - \cdot \cdot \cdot - c _ { n - 1 } \gamma _ { n - 1 } .$ In other words,

$$
\gamma \sim c _ { 1 } \gamma _ { 1 } + c _ { 2 } \gamma _ { 2 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + c _ { n - 1 } \gamma _ { n - 1 } .
$$

Every cycle is thus homologous to a linear combination of the cycles $\gamma _ { 1 } , \ \gamma _ { 2 } , \ \dots \ , \ \gamma _ { n - 1 } ,$ This linear combination is uniquely determined, for if two linear combinations were homologous to the same cycle their difference would be a linear combination which is homologous to zero. But it is clear that the cycle $c _ { 1 } \gamma _ { 1 } + c _ { 2 } \gamma _ { 2 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + c _ { n - 1 } \gamma _ { n - 1 }$ winds $\pmb { c _ { i } }$ times around the points in $A _ { i }$ ; hence it cannot be homologous to zero unless all the $\pmb { c _ { i } }$ vanish.

In view of these circumstances the cycles $\gamma _ { 1 } , \gamma _ { 2 } , . . . , \gamma _ { n - 1 }$ are said to form a homology basis for the region $\pmb { \Omega }$ It is not the only homology basis, but by an elementary theorem in linear algebra we may conclude that every homology basis has the same number of elements. We find that every region with a finite homology basis has finite connectivity, and the number of basis elements is one less than the connectivity.

By Theorem 18 we obtain, for any analytic function $f ( z )$ in $\Omega ,$

$$
\int _ { \gamma } f d z = c _ { 1 } \int _ { \gamma _ { 1 } } f d z + c _ { 2 } \int _ { \gamma _ { 2 } } f d z + \cdot \cdot \cdot + c _ { n - 1 } \int _ { \gamma _ { n - 1 } } f d z .
$$

The numbers

$$
P _ { i } = \int _ { \gamma _ { i } } f d z
$$

depend only on the function, and not on $\pmb { \gamma }$ They are called modules of periodicity of the differential $f d z _ { 1 }$ , or, with less accuracy, the periods of the indefinite integral. We have found that the integral of $f ( z )$ over any cycle is a linear combination of the periods with integers as coefficients, and the integral along an arc from ${ \mathfrak { z } } _ { 0 }$ to $z$ is determined up to additive multiples of the periods. The vanishing of the periods is a necessary and sufficient condition for the existence of a single-valued indefinite integral.

In order to illustrate, let us consider the extremely simple case of an annulus, defined by $r _ { 1 } < | z | < r _ { 2 }$ The complement has the components $| z | \leq r _ { 1 }$ and $| z | \geq r _ { 2 }$ ; we include the degenerate cases $\pmb { r _ { 1 } = 0 }$ and $r _ { 2 } = \infty$ The annulus is doubly connected, and a homology basis is formed by any circle $| z | = r$ $r _ { 1 } < r < r _ { 2 }$ If this circle is denoted by $c _ { i }$ , any cycle in the annulus satisfies $\gamma \sim n C$ where ${ \pmb n } = { \pmb n } ( \gamma , { \pmb 0 } )$ The integral of an analytic function over a cycle is a multiple of the single period

$$
P = \int _ { \cal C } f d z
$$

whose value is of course independent of the radius $\pmb { r }$

# EXERCISES

1. Prove that the region obtained from a simply connected region by removing m points has the connectivity $m + 1$ , and find a homology basis. 2. Show that the bounded regions determined by a closed curve are simply connected, while the unbounded region is doubly connected. $\mathfrak { s } _ { \mathfrak { s } }$ Show that single-valued analytic branches of $\log z , z ^ { \alpha }$ and $z ^ { z }$ can be defined in any simply connected region which does not contain the origin. 4. Show that a single-valued analytic branch of $\sqrt { 1 - z ^ { 2 } }$ can be defined in any region such that the points $\pm 1$ are in the same component of the complement. What are the possible values of

$$
\int { \frac { \ d z } { \sqrt { 1 - z ^ { 2 } } } }
$$

over a closed curve in the region?

# 5. THE CALCULUS OF RESIDUES

The results of the preceding section have shown that the determination of line integrals of analytic functions over closed curves can be reduced to the determination of periods. Under certain circumstances it turns out that the periods can be found without or with very little computation. We are thus in possession of a method which in many cases permits us to evaluate integrals without resorting to explicit calculation. This is of great value for practical purposes as well as for the further development of the theory.

In order to make this method more systematic a simple formalism, known as the calculus of residues, was introduced by Cauchy, the founder of complex integration theory. From the point of view adopted in this book the use of residues amounts essentially to an application of the results proved in Sec. 4 under particularly simple circumstances.

5.1. The Residue Theorem. Our first task is to review earlier results in the light of the more general theorems of Sec. 4. Clearly, all results which were derived as consequences of Cauchy's theorem for a disk remain valid in arbitrary regions for all cycles which are homologous t zeroor instance, andthis ppication s typical, Cuchy's il formula can now be expressed in the following form:

If $f ( z )$ is analytic in a region $\pmb { \Omega }$ ,then

$$
n ( \gamma , a ) f ( a ) = { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } } \int _ { \gamma } { \frac { f ( z ) ~ d z } { z - a } }
$$

for every cycle $\pmb { \gamma }$ which is homologous to zero in $\pmb { \Omega }$ .

The proof is a repetition of the proof of Theorem 6. In this connection we point out that there is of course no longer any need to give a separate proof of Theorem 16 in the presence of removable singularities. Indeed, our discussion of the local behavior has already shown that all removable singularities can simply be ignored.

We turn now to the discussion of a function $f ( z )$ which is analytic in a region $\pmb { \Omega }$ except for isolated singularities. For a first orientation, let us assume that there are only a finite number of singular points, denoted by ${ \pmb a _ { 1 } } , { \pmb a _ { 2 } } ,$ . . . , ${ \pmb a } _ { n }$ . The region obtained by excluding the points ${ \pmb a } _ { j }$ will be denoted by $\pmb { \Omega } ^ { \prime }$

To each ${ \pmb a } _ { j }$ there exists a $\delta _ { j } > 0$ such that the doubly connected region $0 < | z - a _ { j } | < \delta _ { j }$ is contained in $\pmb { \Omega } ^ { \prime }$ Draw a circle $C _ { j }$ about $\pmb { a } _ { j }$ of radius $< \delta _ { j } ,$ and let

$$
P _ { j } = \int _ { C _ { j } } f ( z ) \ d z
$$

be the corresponding period of $f ( z )$ . The particular function $1 / ( z - a _ { j } )$ has the period $\scriptstyle 2 \pi i .$ Therefore, if we set $R _ { j } = { P _ { j } } / { 2 \pi i }$ ,the combination

$$
f ( z ) \mathrm { ~ - ~ } \frac { R _ { j } } { z \mathrm { ~ - ~ } a _ { j } }
$$

has a vanishing period. The constant $R _ { j }$ which produces this result is called the residue of $f ( z )$ at the point ${ a } _ { j }$ We repeat the definition in the following form:

Definition 3. The residue of $f ( z )$ at an isolated singularity $\pmb { a }$ is the unique complex number $\boldsymbol { R }$ which makes $f ( z ) - R / ( z - a )$ the derivative of ${ \pmb a }$ singlevalued analytic function in an annulus $0 < | z - a | < \delta .$ .

It is helpful to use such self-explanatory notations as $R = \mathrm { R e s } _ { z = a } f ( z )$

Let $\gamma$ be a cycle in $\pmb { \Omega } ^ { \prime }$ which is homologous to zero with respect to $\pmb { \Omega }$ . Then $\pmb { \gamma }$ satisfies the homology

$$
\gamma \sim \sum _ { j } h ( \gamma , a _ { j } ) C _ { j }
$$

with respect to $\pmb { \Omega } ^ { \prime }$ ; indeed, we can easily verify that the points ${ \pmb q } _ { j }$ as well as all points outside of $\pmb { \Omega }$ have the same order with respect to both cycles.

By virtue of the homology we obtain, with the notation (43),

$$
\int _ { \gamma } f d z = \sum _ { j } n ( \gamma , a _ { j } ) P _ { j } ,
$$

and since $P _ { j } = 2 \pi i \cdot R _ { j }$ finally

$$
\frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } \int _ { \gamma } f d z = \sum _ { j } n ( \gamma , a _ { j } ) R _ { j } .
$$

This is the residue theorem, except for the restrictive assumption that there are only a finite number of singularities. In the general case we need only prove that $n ( \gamma , a _ { j } ) = 0$ except for a finite number of points ${ \pmb { a } } _ { 3 } { \bmod { \frac { } { 2 } } }$ for then the same proof can be applied. The assertion follows by routine reasoning. The set of all points $\pmb { a }$ with $n ( \gamma , a ) = 0$ is open and contains all points outside of a large circle. The complement is consequently a compact set, and as such it cannot contain more than a finite number of the isolated points $\pmb { a } _ { \mathcal { I } }$ Therefore $n ( \gamma , a _ { j } ) \neq 0$ only for a finite number of the singularities, and we have proved:

Theorem 19. Let $f ( z )$ be analytic except for isolated singularities $\pmb { a } _ { j }$ in ${ \pmb a }$ region $\pmb { \Omega }$ Then

$$
{ \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } } \int _ { \gamma } f ( z ) \ d z = \sum _ { j } { n ( \gamma , a _ { j } ) \ \mathrm { R e s } _ { z = a _ { j } } f ( z ) }
$$
