for any cycle $\gamma$ which is homologous to zero in $\Omega$ and does not pass through any of the points ${ \pmb { a } } _ { j } .$

In the applications it is frequently the case that each $n ( \gamma , a _ { \ j } )$ is either 0 or 1. Then we have simply

$$
{ \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } } \int _ { \gamma } f ( z ) \ d z = \sum _ { j } \operatorname { R e s } _ { z = a _ { j } } f ( z )
$$

where the sum is extended over all singularities enclosed by $\gamma$

The residue theorem is of little value unless we have at our disposal a simple procedure to determine the residues. For essential singularities there is no such procedure of any practical value, and thus it is not surprising that the residue theorem is comparatively seldom used in the presence of essential singularities. With respect to poles the situation is entirely different. We need only look at the expansion

$$
f ( z ) = B _ { h } ( z - a ) ^ { - h } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + B _ { 1 } ( z - a ) ^ { - 1 } + \varphi ( z )
$$

to recognize that the residue equals the coefficient $\scriptstyle B _ { 1 }$ Indeed, when the term $B _ { 1 } ( z - a ) ^ { - 1 }$ is omitted, the remainder is evidertly a derivative.

Since the principal part at a pole is always either given or can be easily found, we have thus a very simple method for finding the residues.

For simple poles the method is even more immediate, for then the residue equals the value of the function $( z - \alpha ) f ( z )$ for ${ z } = { a }$ For instance, let it be required to find the residues of the function

$$
\frac { e ^ { z } } { ( z - a ) ( z - b ) }
$$

at the poles $\pmb { a }$ and $b \neq \pmb { a }$ The residue at $\pmb { a }$ is obviously $e ^ { a } / ( a - b )$ ,and the residue at $^ { b }$ is $e ^ { b } / ( b - a )$ . If $b = \pmb { a } _ { \mathrm { { i } } }$ ,the situation is slightly more complicated. We must then expand $e ^ { z }$ by Taylor's theorem in the form $e ^ { z } = e ^ { a } + e ^ { a } ( z - a ) + f _ { 2 } ( z ) ( z - a ) ^ { 2 }$ Dividing by $( z - a ) ^ { 2 }$ we find that the residue of $e ^ { z } / ( z - a ) ^ { 2 }$ at ${ \textit { z } } = { \textit { a } }$ is $e ^ { a }$ .

Remark. In presentations of Cauchy's theorem, the integral formula and the residue theorem which follow more classical lines, there is no mention of homology, nor is the notion of index used explicitly. Instead, the curve $\pmb { \gamma }$ to which the theorems are applied is supposed to form the complete boundary of a subregion of $\Omega$ , and the orientation is chosen so that the subregion lies to the left of $\pmb { \Omega }$ In rigorous texts considerable effort is spent on proving that these intuitive notions have a precise meaning. The main objection to this procedure is the necessity to allot time and attention to rather delicate questions which are peripheral in comparison with the main issues.

With the general point of view that we have adopted it is still possible, and indeed quite easy, to isolate the classical case. All that is needed is to accept the following definition:

Definition 4. A cycle $\gamma$ is said to bound the region $\Omega$ if and only if ${ \mathfrak { n } } ( \gamma , a )$ is defined and equal to 1 for all points ${ \pmb a } \in \Omega$ and either undefined or equal to zero for all points a not in $\pmb { \Omega }$ .

If $\pmb { \gamma }$ bounds $\pmb { \Omega }$ ,and if $\Omega + \gamma$ is contained in a larger region $\pmb { \Omega } ^ { \prime }$ , then it is clear that $\gamma$ is homologous to zero with respect to $\Omega ^ { \prime }$ The following statements are therefore trivial consequences of Theorems 18 and 19:

If $\pmb { \gamma }$ bounds $\Omega$ and $f ( z )$ is analytic on the set $\Omega + \gamma ,$ then

$$
\int _ { \gamma } f ( z ) \ d z = 0
$$

and

$$
f ( z ) = { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } } \int _ { \gamma } { \frac { f ( \zeta ) ~ d \xi } { \xi - z } }
$$

for all z  Ω.

If $f ( z )$ is analytic on $\Omega + \gamma$ except for isolated singularities in $\pmb { \Omega }$ , then

$$
{ \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } } \int _ { \gamma } f ( z ) \ d z = \sum _ { j } \operatorname { R e s } _ { z = a _ { j } } f ( z )
$$

where the sum ranges over the singularities $a _ { j } \in \Omega$ .

We observe that a cycle $\pmb { \gamma }$ which bounds $\pmb { \Omega }$ must contain the set theoretic boundary of $\pmb { \Omega }$ Indeed, if ${ \mathit { z } } _ { 0 }$ lies on the boundary of $\pmb { \Omega }$ ,then every neighborhood of ${ \mathit { z } } _ { 0 }$ contains points from $\pmb { \Omega }$ and points not in $\pmb { \Omega } .$ . If such a neighborhood were free from points of $\gamma , n ( \gamma , z )$ would be defined and constant in the neighborhood. This contradicts the definition, and hence every neighborhood of ${ \pmb z _ { 0 } }$ must meet $\pmb { \gamma }$ ; since $\pmb { \gamma }$ is closed, ${ \mathfrak { z } } _ { 0 }$ must lie on $\pmb { \gamma }$ .

The converse of the preceding statement is not true, for a point on $\pmb { \gamma }$ may well have a neighborhood which does not meet $\pmb { \Omega }$ Normally, one would try to choose $\gamma$ so that it is identical with the boundary of $\pmb { \Omega }$ , but for Cauchy's theorem and related considerations this assumption is not needed.

5.2. The Argument Principle. Cauchy's integral formula can be considered as a special case of the residue theorem. Indeed, the function $f ( z ) / ( z - a )$ has a simple pole at ${ \pmb z } = { \pmb a }$ with the residue ${ f } ( \pmb { a } )$ , and when we apply (44), the integral formula results.

Another application of the residue theorem occurred in the proof of Theorem 10 which served to determine the number of zeros of an analytic function, For a zero of order $\pmb { h }$ we can write $f ( z ) = ( z - a ) ^ { h } f _ { h } ( z )$ , with $f _ { h } ( a ) \neq 0$ and obtain $f ^ { \prime } ( z ) = h ( z - a ) ^ { h - 1 } f _ { h } ( z ) + ( z - a ) ^ { h } f _ { h } ^ { \prime } ( z )$ Consequently $f ^ { \prime } ( z ) / f ( z ) = h / ( z - a ) + f _ { h } ^ { \prime } ( z ) / f _ { h } ( z )$ , and we see that $f ^ { \prime } / f$ has a simple pole with the residue $h$ In the formula (32) this residue is accounted for by a corresponding repetition of terms.

We can now generalize Theorem 10 to the case of a meromorphic function. If $\pmb { f }$ has a pole of order $\pmb { h }$ , we find by the same calculation as above, with $- h$ replacing $\pmb { h }$ ,that $f ^ { \prime } / f$ has the residue $- h ,$ The following theorem results:

Theorem 20. If $f ( z )$ is meromorphic in $\Omega$ with the zeros ${ \pmb a } _ { j }$ and the poles $\begin{array} { r } { b _ { k } , } \end{array}$ then

$$
{ \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } } \int _ { \gamma } { \frac { f ^ { \prime } ( z ) } { f ( z ) } } d z = \sum _ { j } n ( \gamma , a _ { j } ) - \sum _ { k } n ( \gamma , b _ { k } )
$$

for every cycle $\pmb { \gamma }$ which is homologous to zero in $\Omega$ and does not pass through any of the zeros or poles.

It is understood that multiple zeros and poles have to be repeated as many times as their order indicates; the sums in (45) are finite.

Theorem 20 is usually referred to as the argument principle. The name refers to the interpretation of the left-hand member of (5) as ${ \boldsymbol { n } } ( \mathbf { { T } } , \mathbf { 0 } )$ where $\mathbf { r }$ is the image cycle of $\gamma$ If $\mathbf { \Gamma } ^ { \mathbf { \Gamma } }$ lies in a disk which does not contain the origin, then $n ( \Gamma , 0 ) = 0$ This observation is the basis for the following corollary, known as Rouché's theorem:

Corollary. Let $\gamma$ be homologous to zero in $\Omega$ and such that $n ( \gamma , z )$ is either 0 or 1 for any point z not on $\pmb { \gamma }$ Suppose that $f ( z )$ and $g ( z )$ are analytic in $\Omega$ and satisfy the inequality $| f ( z ) - g ( z ) | < | f ( z ) | \ o n \ \gamma .$ Then $f ( z )$ and $g ( z )$ have the same number of zeros enclosed by $\gamma$ .

The assumption implies that $f ( z )$ and $g ( z )$ are zero-free on $\gamma$ Moreover, they satisfy the inequality

$$
\left| \frac { g ( z ) } { f ( z ) } - 1 \right| < 1
$$

on $\pmb { \gamma }$ The values of $F ( z ) = g ( z ) / f ( z )$ on $\pmb { \gamma }$ are thus contained in the open disk of center 1 and radius 1. When Theorem 20 is applied to $F ( z )$ , we have thus $n ( \Gamma , 0 ) = 0$ ,and the assertion follows.

A typical application of Rouché's theorem would be the following. Suppose that we wish to find the number of zeros of $\mathbf { a }$ function $f ( z )$ in the disk $| z | \leq R$ Using Taylor's theorem we can write

$$
f ( z ) = P _ { n - 1 } ( z ) + z ^ { n } f _ { n } ( z )
$$

where $P _ { n - 1 }$ is a polynomial of degree $n - 1$ For a suitably chosen $\pmb { n }$ it may happen that we can prove the inequality $R ^ { n } | f _ { n } ( z ) | < | P _ { n - 1 } ( z ) |$ on $| z | = R$ Then $f ( z )$ has the same number of zeros in $| z | \leq R$ as $P _ { n - 1 } ( z )$ , and this number can be determined by approximate solution of the polynomial equation $P _ { n - 1 } ( z ) = 0$ .

Theorem 20 can be generalized in the following manner. If $g ( z )$ is analytic in $\Omega$ , then $g ( z ) \ : \frac { f ^ { \prime } ( z ) } { f ( z ) }$ has the residue $\hbar g ( a )$ at a zero $^ { a }$ of order $\pmb { h }$ and the residue $- h g ( \pmb { a } )$ at a pole. We obtain thus the formula

$$
\frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } \int _ { \gamma } g ( z ) \frac { f ^ { \prime } ( z ) } { f ( z ) } d z = \sum _ { j } n ( \gamma , a _ { j } ) g ( a _ { j } ) - \sum _ { k } n ( \gamma , b _ { k } ) g ( b _ { k } ) .
$$

This result is important for the study of the inverse function. With the notations of Theorem 11 we know that the equation $\begin{array} { r } { f ( z ) = w , } \end{array}$ $\vert w - w _ { 0 } \vert < \delta$ has $\pmb { n }$ roots $z _ { j } ( w )$ in the disk $| z - z _ { 0 } | < \varepsilon .$ If we apply

46) with $g ( z ) = z ,$ we obtain

$$
\sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { n } z _ { j } ( w ) = { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } } \int _ { | z - z _ { 0 } | = \epsilon } { \frac { f ^ { \prime } ( z ) } { f ( z ) - w } } z d z .
$$

For $n = 1$ the inverse function $f ^ { - 1 } ( w )$ can thus be represented explicitly by

$$
f ^ { - 1 } ( w ) = { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } } \int _ { | z - z _ { 0 } | = \epsilon } { \frac { f ^ { \prime } ( z ) } { f ( z ) - w } } z d z .
$$

If (46) is applied with $g ( z ) = z ^ { m }$ equation (47) is replaced by

$$
\sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { n } z _ { j } ( w ) ^ { m } = { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } } \int _ { | z - z _ { 0 } | = \epsilon } { \frac { f ^ { \prime } ( z ) } { f ( z ) - w } } z ^ { m } d z .
$$

It is not difficult to show that the right-hand member represents an analytic function of $\pmb { w }$ for $\vert w \mathrm { ~ - ~ } w _ { 0 } \vert < \delta$ Thus the power sums of the roots $z _ { j } ( w )$ are single-valued analytic functions of $\pmb { w }$ But it is well known that the elementary symmetric functions can be expressed as polynomials in the power sums. Hence they are also analytic, and we find that the $z _ { j } ( w )$ are the roots of a polynomial equation

$$
z ^ { n } + a _ { 1 } ( w ) z ^ { n - 1 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + a _ { n - 1 } ( w ) z + a _ { n } ( w ) = 0
$$

whose coefficients are analytic functions of $\pmb { w }$ in $\vert w - w _ { 0 } \vert < \delta .$ .

5.3. Evaluation ${ \pmb { o } } _ { \mathbf { \sigma } } ^ { \pmb { \mathscr { f } } }$ Definite Integrals. The calculus of residues provides a very efficient tool for the evaluation of definite integrals. It is particularly important when it is impossible to find the indefinite integral explicitly, but even if the ordinary methods of calculus can be applied the use of residues is frequently a laborsaving device. The fact that the calculus of residues yields complex rather than real integrals is no disadvantage, for clearly the evaluation of a complex integral is equivalent to the evaluation of two definite integrals.

There are, however, some serious limitations, and the method is far from infallible. In the first place, the integrand must be closely connected with some analytic function. This is not very serious, for usually we are only required to integrate elementary functions, and they can all be extended to the complex domain. It is much more serious that the technique of complex integration applies only to closed curves, while a real integral is always extended over an interval. A special device must be used in order to reduce the problem to one which concerns integration over a closed curve. There are a number of ways in which this can be accomplished, but they all apply under rather special circumstances.

The technique can be learned at the hand of typical examples, but even complete mastery does not guarantee success.

1. All integrals of the form

$$
\int _ { 0 } ^ { 2 \pi } R ( \cos \theta , \sin \theta ) \ d \theta
$$

where the integrand is a rational function of cos $\pmb \theta$ and sin $\pmb \theta$ can be easily evaluated by means of residues. Of course these integrals can also be computed by explicit integration, but this technique is usually very laborious. It is very natural to make the substitution $z = e ^ { i \theta }$ which immediately transforms (48) into the line integral

$$
- i \int _ { | z | = 1 } R \left[ { \frac { 1 } { 2 } } { \bigg ( } z + { \frac { 1 } { z } } { \bigg ) } , { \frac { 1 } { 2 i } } { \bigg ( } z - { \frac { 1 } { z } } { \bigg ) } \right] { \frac { d z } { z } } .
$$

It remains only to determine the residues which correspond to the poles of the integrand inside the unit circle.

As an example, let us compute

$$
\int _ { 0 } ^ { \pi } \frac { d \theta } { a + \cos \theta } , \qquad a > 1 .
$$

This integral is not extended over $^ { ( 0 , 2 \pi ) }$ , but since cos $\pmb \theta$ takes the same values in the intervals $( 0 , \pi )$ and $( \pi , 2 \pi )$ is is clear that the integral from 0 to $\pmb { \pi }$ is one-half of the integral from 0 to $2 \pi$ Taking this into account we find that the integral equals

$$
- i \int _ { | z | = 1 } { \frac { d z } { z ^ { 2 } + 2 a z + 1 } }
$$

The denominator can be factored into $( z - \alpha ) ( z - \beta )$ with

$$
\alpha = - a + \sqrt { a ^ { 2 } - 1 } , ~ \beta = - a - \sqrt { a ^ { 2 } - 1 } .
$$

Evidently $| \alpha | < 1 , | \beta | > 1$ , and the residue at $_ { \pmb { \alpha } }$ is $1 / ( \alpha - \beta )$ The value of the integral is found to be $\pi / \sqrt { a ^ { 2 } - 1 }$ .

2. An integral of the form

$$
\int _ { - \infty } ^ { \infty } R ( x ) \ d x .
$$

converges if and only if in the rational function $\mathcal { R } ( x )$ the degree of the denominator is at least two units higher than the degree of the numerator, and if no pole lies on the real axis. The standard procedure is to integrate the complex function $R ( z )$ over a closed curve consisting of a line segment $\scriptstyle ( - \pmb \rho , \pmb \rho )$ and the semicircle from $\pmb { \rho }$ to $- \pmb { \rho }$ in the upper half plane.

If $\pmb { \rho }$ is large enough this curve encloses all poles in the upper half plane, and the corresponding integral is equal to $2 \pi i$ times the sum of the residues in the upper half plane. As $\pmb { \rho }  \infty$ obvious estimates show that the integral over the semicircle tends to 0, and we obtain

$$
\int _ { - \infty } ^ { \infty } R ( x ) \ : d x = 2 \pi i \sum _ { y > 0 } { \mathrm { R e s ~ } } R ( z ) .
$$

3. The same method can be applied to an integral of the form

$$
\int _ { - \infty } ^ { \infty } R ( x ) e ^ { i x } d x
$$

whose real and imaginary parts determine the important integrals

$$
\int _ { - \infty } ^ { \infty } R ( x ) \cos x d x , \qquad \int _ { - \infty } ^ { \infty } R ( x ) \sin x d x .
$$

Since $| e ^ { i z } | = e ^ { - y }$ is bounded in the upper half plane, we can again conclude that the integral over the semicircle tends to zero, provided that the rational function $R ( z )$ has a zero of at least order 2 at infinity. We obtain

$$
\int _ { - \infty } ^ { \infty } R ( x ) e ^ { i x } d x = 2 \pi i \sum _ { y > 0 } { \mathrm { R e s ~ } } R ( z ) e ^ { i z } .
$$

It is less obvious that the same result holds when $R ( z )$ has only a simple zero at infinity. In this case it is not convenient to use semicircles. For one thing, it is not so easy to estimate the integral over the semicircle, and secondly, even if we were successful we would only have proved that the integral

$$
\int _ { - \rho } ^ { \rho } R ( x ) e ^ { i x } d x
$$

over a symmetric interval has the desired limit for $\pmb { \rho }  \infty$ . In reality we are of course required to prove that

$$
\int _ { - X _ { 1 } } ^ { X _ { 2 } } R ( x ) e ^ { i x } d x
$$

has a limit when $X _ { 1 }$ and $X _ { 2 }$ tend independently to $\infty$ . In the earlier examples this question did not arise because the convergence of the integral was assured beforehand.

For the proof we integrate over the perimeter of a rectangle with the vertices $X _ { 2 }$ $\zeta _ { 2 } , X _ { 2 } + i Y , - X _ { 1 } + i Y , - X _ { 1 }$ where $Y > 0$ As soon as $X _ { 1 , }$ $X _ { 2 }$ and $Y$ are sufficiently large, this rectangle contains all the poles in the upper half plane. Under the hypothesis $\vert z R ( z ) \vert$ is bounded. Hence the integral over the right vertical side is, except for a constant factor,

less than

$$
\int _ { 0 } ^ { y } e ^ { - y } { \frac { d y } { | z | } } < { \frac { 1 } { X _ { 2 } } } \int _ { 0 } ^ { y } e ^ { - y } d y .
$$

The last integral can be evaluated explicitly and is found to be $< 1$ . Hence the integral over the right vertical side is less than a constant times $1 / X _ { 2 }$ , and a corresponding result is found for the left vertical side. The integral over the upper horizontal side is evidently less than $e ^ { - \mathbf { Y } } ( X _ { 1 } + X _ { 2 } ) / Y$ multiplied with a constant. For fixed $X _ { 1 } , X _ { 2 }$ it tends to 0 as $Y  \infty$ , and we conclude that

$$
\bigg | \int _ { - X _ { 1 } } ^ { X _ { 2 } } R ( x ) e ^ { i x } d x - 2 \pi i \sum _ { y > 0 } \mathrm { R e s } R ( z ) e ^ { i z } \bigg | < A \left( \frac { 1 } { X _ { 1 } } + \frac { 1 } { X _ { 2 } } \right)
$$

where $A$ denotes a constant. This inequality proves that

$$
\int _ { - \infty } ^ { \infty } R ( x ) e ^ { i x } d x = 2 \pi i \sum _ { y > 0 } { \mathrm { R e s ~ } } R ( z ) e ^ { i z }
$$

under the sole condition that $R ( \infty ) = \mathbf { 0 }$ .

In the discussion we have assumed, tacitly, that $\boldsymbol { R } ( \boldsymbol { z } )$ has no poles on the real axis since otherwise the integral (49) has no meaning. However, one of the integrals (50) may well exist, namely, if $\boldsymbol { R } ( \boldsymbol { z } )$ has simple poles which coincide with zeros of sin $_ { x }$ or cos $_ { x }$ Let us suppose, for instance, that $R ( z )$ has a simple pole at ${ z } = \mathbf { 0 }$ Then the second integral (50) has a meaning and calls for evaluation.

We use the same method as before, but we use a path which avoids the origin by following a small semicircle of radius $\pmb { \delta }$ in the lower half plane (Fig. 25). It is easy to see that this closed curve encloses the poles in the upper half plane, the pole at the origin, and no others, as soon as $X _ { 1 } , X _ { 2 } ,$ . $Y$ are sufficiently large and $\delta$ is sufficiently small. Suppose that the residue at 0 is $B$ , so that we can write $R ( z ) e ^ { i z } = B / z + R _ { 0 } ( z )$ where $\scriptstyle { \mathcal { R } } _ { 0 } ( z )$ is analytic at the origin. The integral of the first term over the semicircle is $\pi i B$ , while the integral of the second term tends to 0 with δ. It is clear that we are led to the result

![](images/42d5df8642928f4212944215beb0b7c63b7837b96e5cfa2ccdb89d3b811066af.jpg)

$$
\operatorname* { l i m } _ { \delta \to 0 } \int _ { - \infty } ^ { - \delta } + \int _ { \delta } ^ { \infty } R ( x ) e ^ { i x } d x = 2 \pi i \Big [ \sum _ { y > 0 } \mathrm { R e s } R ( z ) e ^ { i z } + { \textstyle \frac { 1 } { 2 } } B \Big ] .
$$

The limit on the left is called the Cauchy principal value of the integral; it exists although the integral itself has no meaning. On the right-hand side we observe that one-half of the residue at O has been included; this is as if one-half of the pole were counted as lying in the upper half plane.

In the general case where several poles lie on the real axis we obtain

$$
\operatorname { \mathrm { ~ \operatorname { ~ \mu ~ } } } _ { \operatorname { \mathrm { ~ - ~ } } \operatorname { \mathrm { ~ e ~ } } } \int _ { - \infty } ^ { \infty } R ( x ) e ^ { i x } d x = 2 \pi i \sum _ { y > 0 } \operatorname { \mathrm { ~ R e s ~ } } R ( z ) e ^ { i z } + \pi i \sum _ { y = 0 } \operatorname { \mathrm { ~ R e s ~ } } R ( z ) e ^ { i z }
$$

where the notations are self-explanatory. It is an essential hypothesis that all the poles on the real axis be simple, and as before we must assume that $R ( \infty ) = \mathbf { 0 }$ .

As the simplest example we have

$$
\operatorname { p r . v } \int _ { - \infty } ^ { \infty } { \frac { e ^ { i x } } { x } } d x = \pi i .
$$

Separating the real and imaginary part we observe that the real part of the equation is trivial by the fact that the integrand is odd. In the imaginary part it is not necessary to take the principal value, and since the integrand is even we find

$$
\int _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } { \frac { \sin { x } } { x } } d x = { \frac { \pi } { 2 } } .
$$

We remark that integrals containing a factor $\cos ^ { n } \ x$ or $\sin ^ { \pmb { x } } \pmb { x }$ can be evaluated by the same technique. Indeed, these factors can be written as linear combinations of terms cos mx and sin mx, and the corresponding integrals can be reduced to the form (49) by a change of variable:

$$
\int _ { - \infty } ^ { \infty } R ( x ) e ^ { i m x } d x = { \frac { 1 } { m } } \int _ { - \infty } ^ { \infty } R \left( { \frac { x } { m } } \right) e ^ { i x } d x .
$$

4. The next category of integrals have the form

$$
\int _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } x ^ { \alpha } R ( x ) d x
$$

where the exponent $_ { \pmb { \alpha } }$ is real and may be supposed to lie in the interval $0 < \alpha < 1$ For convergence $\textstyle R ( z )$ must have a zero of at least order two at $\infty$ and at most a simple pole at the origin.

The new feature is the fact that $\scriptstyle { \mathcal { R } } ( z ) z ^ { \alpha }$ is not single-valued. This, however, is just the circumstance which makes it possible to find the integral from 0 to $\infty$ .

The simplest procedure is to start with the substitution $x = t ^ { 2 }$ which transforms the integral into

$$
2 \int _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } t ^ { 2 \alpha + 1 } R ( t ^ { 2 } ) d t .
$$

For the function $z ^ { 2 \alpha }$ we may choose the branch whose argument lies between $- \pi \alpha$ and $\scriptstyle 3 \pi \alpha$ ; it is well defined and analytic in the region obtained by omitting the negative imaginary axis. As long as we avoid the negative imaginary axis, we can apply the residue theorem to the function $z ^ { 2 \alpha + 1 } R ( z ^ { 2 } )$ .We use a closed curve consisting of two line segments along the positive and negative axis and two semicircles in the upper half plane, one very large and one very small (Fig. 26). Under our assumptions it is easy to show that the integrals over the semicircles tend to zero. Hence the residue theorem yields the value of the integral

$$
\int _ { - \infty } ^ { \infty } z ^ { 2 \alpha + 1 } R ( z ^ { 2 } ) \ d z = \int _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } { \bigl ( } z ^ { 2 \alpha + 1 } + ( - z ) ^ { 2 \alpha + 1 } { \bigr ) } R ( z ^ { 2 } ) \ d z .
$$

However, $( - z ) ^ { 2 \alpha } = e ^ { 2 \pi i \alpha } z ^ { 2 \alpha } ;$ and the integral equals

$$
( 1 - e ^ { 2 \pi i \alpha } ) \int _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } z ^ { 2 \alpha + 1 } R ( z ^ { 2 } ) \ : d z .
$$

Since the factor in front is $\yen 0$ , we are finally able to determine the value of the desired integral.

The evaluation calls for determination of the residues of $z ^ { 2 \alpha + 1 } R ( z ^ { 2 } )$ in the upper half plane. These are the same as the residues of $\scriptstyle z ^ { \alpha } R ( z )$ in the whole plane. For practical purposes it may be preferable not to use any preliminary substitution and integrate the function $\scriptstyle { \mathfrak { z } } ^ { \alpha } R ( z )$ over the closed curve shown in Fig. 27. We bave then to use the branch of $z ^ { \alpha }$ whose argument lies between 0 and $2 \pi \alpha$ This method needs some justification, for it does not conform to the hypotheses of the residue theorem. The justification is trivial.

![](images/29ecfaed862fcea79ce83a5f3f94f2b8693ccb6557608dee91554a15fea3386b.jpg)  
FIG. 26

![](images/1c93ac10cab3d162382bdcabe09e9594dd3c4bca6fd10a5bced1f7c9156519e4.jpg)  
FJG. 27

5. As a final example we compute the special integral

$$
\int _ { 0 } ^ { \pi } \log \sin \theta d \theta .
$$

Consider the function $1 - e ^ { 2 i z } = - 2 i e ^ { i z }$ sin $\boldsymbol { z }$ From the representation $1 - e ^ { 2 i z } = 1 - e ^ { - z _ { U } } ( \cos 2 x + i \sin 2 x )$ , we find that this function is real and negative only for $x = n \pi , y \notin { \mathbf { 0 } }$ In the region obtained by omitting these half lines the principal branch of log $\left( 1 - e ^ { 2 i z } \right)$ is hence singlevalued and analytic. We apply Cauchy's theorem to the rectangle whose vertices are $0 , \pi , \pi + i Y$ , and $_ { i Y }$ ; however, the points 0 and $\pi$ have to be avoided, and we do this by following small circular quadrants of radius δ.

Because of the periodicity the integrals over the vertical sides cancel against each other. The integral over the upper horizontal side tends to 0 as $Y  \infty$ . Finally, the integrals over the quadrants can also be seen to approach zero as $\delta \to 0$ . Indeed, since the imaginary part of the logarithm is bounded we need consider only the real part. Since $e ^ { 2 i z }$ has the derivative ${ \bf 2 } i$ at the origin, we find that $| 1 - e ^ { 2 i z } | / | z | \to 2$ for $z  0$ . Hence we need only show that $\delta \log \delta$ tends to O with δ. For the sake of completeness we include a proof of this elementary fact. By studying the derivative we find that the function $t \log { \left( \varepsilon / t \right) }$ is increasing for $t \leq \varepsilon / e$ . For $\delta < \mathfrak { e } / e$ we have thus $\delta \log { ( \mathfrak { e } / \delta ) } < \mathfrak { e } / e$ If we choose e $< 1$ and let $\pmb { \delta }$ satisfy the additional condition $\delta < \mathfrak { e } / [ e \log { ( 1 / \mathfrak { e } ) } ] _ { : }$ we find that $\delta \log { ( 1 / \delta ) }$ $< 2 \varepsilon / e$ , and this proves that $\operatorname* { l i m } _ { \delta  0 } \delta \log ( 1 / \delta ) = 0 .$ .

The same proof applies near the vertex $\pi$ , and we obtain

$$
\int _ { 0 } ^ { \pi } \log \left( - 2 i e ^ { i x } \sin x \right) d x = 0
$$

If we choose l $\ y e ^ { i x } = i x$ , the imaginary part lies between 0 and $\pi$ Therefore, in order to obtain the principal branch with an imaginary part between $- \pi$ and $\pi _ { ; }$ ,we must choose $\log { ( - i ) } = - \pi i / 2$ The equation can now be written in the form

$$
\pi \log 2 - \left( { \frac { \pi ^ { 2 } } { 2 } } \right) i + \int _ { 0 } ^ { \pi } \log \sin { x } d x + \left( { \frac { \pi ^ { 2 } } { 2 } } \right) i = 0 ,
$$

and we find

$$
\int _ { 0 } ^ { \pi } \log \sin x d x = - \pi \log 2 .
$$

# EXERCISES

Evaluate the following integrals by the method of residues:

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { \mathbb { 1 } \int _ { 0 } ^ { \infty / 2 } \frac { d z } { \alpha + \alpha ^ { 3 / 2 } } , \quad \mathrm { ~ \alpha ~ > 0 . } } \\ & { \geq \int _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } \frac { d z } { \alpha + \alpha ^ { 3 / 2 } + 1 8 } } \\ & { \geq \int _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } \frac { d z } { \alpha + \alpha ^ { 3 / 2 } + 1 8 } } \\ & { \qquad \alpha \int _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } \frac { d z } { \alpha + \alpha ^ { 3 / 2 } + 9 } d \alpha \mathbb { 1 } . } \\ & { \leq \int _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } \frac { d z } { \alpha + \alpha ^ { 3 / 2 } } } \\ & { \leq \int _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } - \frac { d z \sin z } { \alpha + \alpha ^ { 3 / 2 } } d \alpha , \quad \mathrm { ~ \alpha ~ \in \mathbb { Z } ^ { 3 } \setminus \left\{ \alpha \right\} ~ } } \\ & { \in \int _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } \frac { d z \sin z d z } { \alpha + \alpha ^ { 3 / 2 } } , \quad \mathrm { ~ \alpha ~ \in \mathbb { Z } ^ { 3 } \setminus \left\{ \alpha \right\} ~ } } \\ & { \geq \int _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } \frac { d z } { \alpha + \alpha ^ { 3 / 2 } + \alpha ^ { 2 } } , \quad \mathrm { ~ \alpha ~ \in \mathbb { Z } ^ { 3 } \setminus \left\{ \alpha \right\} ~ } } \\ & { \leq \int _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } \frac { d z } { \alpha + \alpha ^ { 3 / 2 } + 1 8 } } \\ & { \leq \int _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } \frac { ( 1 + \alpha ^ { 3 / 2 } ) \log z d z } { \alpha + \alpha ^ { 3 / 2 } + 1 8 } , } \\ & { \leq \int _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } \frac { d z } { \alpha + \alpha ^ { 3 / 2 } + \alpha ^ { 3 / 2 } + 1 8 } . \quad \mathrm { ~ ( 0 < \alpha < 2 ) } . } \end{array}
$$

# 6. HARMONIC FUNCTIONS

The real and imaginary parts of an analytic function are conjugate harmonic functions. Therefore, all theorems on analytic functions are also theorems on pairs of conjugate harmonic functions. However, harmonic functions are important in their own right, and their treatment is not always simplified by the use of complex methods. This is particularly true when the conjugate harmonic function is not single-valued.

We assemble in this section some facts about harmonic functions that are intimately connected with Cauchy's theorem. The more delicate properties of harmonic functions are postponed to a later chapter.

6.1. Definition and Basic Properties. A real-valued function $u ( z )$ or $u ( x , y )$ , defined and single-valued in a region $\Omega$ , is said to be harmonic in $\Omega _ { i }$ , or a potential function, if it is continuous together with its partial derivatives of the first two orders and satisfies Laplace's equation

$$
\Delta u = \frac { \partial ^ { 2 } u } { \partial x ^ { 2 } } + \frac { \partial ^ { 2 } u } { \partial y ^ { 2 } } = 0 .
$$

We shall see later that the regularity conditions can be weakened, but this is a point of relatively minor importance.

The sum of two harmonic functions and a constant multiple of a harmonic function are again harmonic; this is due to the linear character of Laplace's equation. The simplest harmonic functions are the linear functions $a x + b y$ , In polar coordinates $( r , \theta )$ equation (51) takes the form

$$
r \frac { \partial } { \partial r } \left( r \frac { \partial u } { \partial r } \right) + \frac { \partial ^ { 2 } u } { \partial \theta ^ { 2 } } = 0 . \dag
$$

This shows that log $\pmb { r }$ is a harmonic function and that any harmonic function which depends only on $\pmb { r }$ must be of the form $a \log r + b$ The argument $\pmb \theta$ is harmonic whenever it can be uniquely defined.

If $\mathbf { \Delta } _ { \mathbf { \mathcal { U } } }$ is harmonic in $\pmb { \Omega }$ , then

$$
f ( z ) = { \frac { \partial u } { \partial x } } - i { \frac { \partial u } { \partial y } }
$$

is analytic, for writing $\quad U = { \frac { \partial u } { \partial x } } , V = - { \frac { \partial u } { \partial y } }$ we have

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { \frac { \partial U } { \partial x } = \frac { \partial ^ { 2 } u } { \partial x ^ { 2 } } = - \frac { \partial ^ { 2 } u } { \partial y ^ { 2 } } = \frac { \partial V } { \partial y } } \\ & { \frac { \partial U } { \partial y } = \frac { \partial ^ { 2 } u } { \partial x \partial y } = - \frac { \partial V } { \partial x } \cdot } \end{array}
$$

This, it should be remembered, is the most natural way of passing from harmonic to analytic functions.

From (52) we pass to the differential

$$
f d z = { \biggl ( } { \frac { \partial u } { \partial x } } d x + { \frac { \partial u } { \partial y } } d y { \biggr ) } + i { \biggl ( } - { \frac { \partial u } { \partial y } } d x + { \frac { \partial u } { \partial x } } d y { \biggr ) } .
$$

In this expression the real part is the differential of $\textbf { \em u }$ ,

$$
d u = { \frac { \partial u } { \partial x } } d x + { \frac { \partial u } { \partial y } } d y .
$$

If $\textbf { \em u }$ has a conjugate harmonic function $v$ , then the imaginary part can be written as

$$
d v = { \frac { \partial v } { \partial x } } d x + { \frac { \partial v } { \partial y } } d y = - { \frac { \partial u } { \partial y } } d x + { \frac { \partial u } { \partial x } } d y .
$$

In general, however, there is no single-valued conjugate function, and in these circumstances it is better not to use the notation $\pmb { d v }$ Instead we write

$$
\mathbf { \nabla } ^ { * } d u = \mathbf { \nabla } - \frac { \partial u } { \partial y } d x + \frac { \partial u } { \partial x } d y
$$

† This form cannot be used for $\pmb { r } = \mathbf { 0 }$

and call ${ { \bf \Pi } ^ { * } } d u$ the conjugate diffrential of du. We have by (53)

$$
f d z = d u + i \ ^ { * } d u .
$$

By Cauchy's theorem the integral of $f d z$ vanishes along any cycle which is homologous to zero in $\Omega$ On the other hand, the integral of the exact differential du vanishes along all cycles. It follows by (54) that

$$
\int _ { \gamma } { ^ { * } d u } = \int _ { \gamma } { - \frac { \partial u } { \partial y } d x } + \frac { \partial u } { \partial x } d y = 0
$$

for all cycles $\pmb { \gamma }$ which are homologous to zero in $\Omega .$

The integral in (55) has an important interpretation which cannot be left unmentioned. If $\pmb { \gamma }$ is a regular curve with the equation $z = z ( t )$ the direction of the tangent is determined by the angle $\alpha = \arg \ z ^ { \prime } ( t ) ,$ and we can write $d x = | d z |$ cos $\pmb { \alpha }$ $\iota , d y = | d z |$ sin $_ \alpha$ The normal which points to the right of the tangent has the direction $\beta = \alpha - \pi / 2$ ,and thus cos $\alpha = - \sin \beta ,$ sin $\alpha = \cos { \beta }$ The expression

$$
{ \frac { \partial u } { \partial n } } = { \frac { \partial u } { \partial x } } \cos \beta + { \frac { \partial u } { \partial y } } \sin \beta
$$

is a directional derivative of $\mathbf { \Delta } _ { \mathbf { \mathcal { U } } }$ , the right-hand normal derivative with respect to the curve $\pmb { \gamma }$ We obtain $\ast d u = ( \partial u / \partial n ) | d z |$ , and (55) can be written in the form

$$
\int _ { \gamma } \frac { \partial u } { \partial n } \left| d z \right| = 0 .
$$

This is the classical notation. Its main advantage is that $\partial u / \partial n$ actually represents a rate of change in the direction perpendicular to $\gamma$ . For instance, if $\gamma$ is the circle $| z | = r$ , described in the positive sense, $\partial u / \partial n$ can be replaced by the partial derivative $\partial u / \partial r$ It has the disadvantage that (56) is not expressed as an ordinary line integral, but as an integral with respect to arc length. For this reason the classical notation is less natural in connection with homology theory, and we prefer to use the notation ${ } ^ { * } d u$ .

In a simply connected region the integral of $* d u$ vanishes over all cycles, and $\textbf { \em u }$ has a single-valued conjugate function $v$ which is determined up to an additive constant. In the multiply connected case the conjugate function has periods

$$
\int _ { \gamma _ { 1 } } \ast d u = \int _ { \gamma _ { 1 } } \frac { \partial u } { \partial n } \left| d z \right|
$$

corresponding to the cycles in a homology basis.

There is an important generalization of (55) which deals with a pair of

harmonic functions. If ${ \pmb u } _ { \mathbf { 1 } }$ and $\pmb { u _ { 2 } }$ are harmonic in $\mathfrak { a }$ , we claim that

$$
\int _ { \gamma } u _ { 1 } \ast d u _ { 2 } - u _ { 2 } \ast d u _ { 1 } = 0
$$

for every cycle $\pmb { \gamma }$ which is homologous to zero in $\pmb { \Omega } .$ According to Theorem 17, Sec. 4.4, it is sufficient to prove (57) for $\gamma = \partial R$ ,where $\pmb { R }$ is a rectangle contained in $\Omega$ In $\pmb { R }$ $\boldsymbol { u } _ { 1 }$ and $u _ { 2 }$ have single-valued conjugate functions $v _ { 1 } , v _ { 2 }$ and we can write

$$
u _ { 1 } { ^ \ast } d u _ { 2 } - u _ { 2 } { ^ \ast } d u _ { 1 } = u _ { 1 } d v _ { 2 } - u _ { 2 } d v _ { 1 } = u _ { 1 } d v _ { 2 } + v _ { 1 } d u _ { 2 } - d ( u _ { 2 } v _ { 1 } ) .
$$

Here $\scriptstyle d ( u _ { 2 } v _ { 1 } )$ is an exact differential, and $u _ { 1 } d v _ { 2 } + v _ { 1 } d u _ { 2 }$ is the imaginary part of

$$
( u _ { 1 } + i v _ { 1 } ) ( d u _ { 2 } + i d v _ { 2 } ) .
$$

The last differential can be written in the form $\pmb { F } _ { 1 } \pmb { f } _ { 2 } d z$ where $F _ { 1 } ( z )$ and $f _ { 2 } ( z )$ are analytic on $\scriptstyle { \pmb R }$ The integral of $\pmb { F } _ { 1 } \pmb { f } _ { 2 } d z$ vanishes by Cauchy's theorem, and so does therefore the integral of its imaginary part. We conclude that (57) holds for $\gamma = \partial R$ , and we have proved:

Theorem 21. If ${ \pmb u } _ { 1 }$ and $\pmb { u } _ { 2 }$ are harmonic in a region $\pmb { \Omega }$ , then

$$
\int _ { \gamma } u _ { 1 } \ : { ^ * d u _ { 2 } } - u _ { 2 } \ : { ^ * d u _ { 1 } } = 0
$$

for every cycle $\pmb { \gamma }$ which is homologous to zero in $\Omega ,$ .

For $u _ { 1 } = 1 , u _ { 2 } = u$ the formula reduces to (55). In the classical notation (57) would be written as

$$
\int _ { \gamma } \left( u _ { 1 } \frac { \partial u _ { 2 } } { \partial n } - u _ { 2 } \frac { \partial u _ { 1 } } { \partial n } \right) | d z | = 0 .
$$

6.2. The Mean-value Property. Let us apply Theorem 21 with $u _ { 1 } = \log r$ and ${ \boldsymbol { u } } _ { 2 }$ equal to a function $\mathbf { \mathscr { u } } _ { : }$ ,harmonic in $| z | < \rho$ For $\pmb { \Omega }$ we must choose the punctured disk $0 < | z | < \rho _ { ; }$ , and for $\pmb { \gamma }$ we take the cycle $C _ { 1 } - C _ { 2 }$ where $c _ { i }$ is a circle $| z | = r _ { i } < \rho$ described in the positive sense. On a circle $| z | = r$ we have $^ { * } d u = r ( \partial u / \partial r ) \ d \theta$ and hence (57) yields

$$
\log r _ { 1 } \int _ { C _ { 1 } } r _ { 1 } { \frac { \partial u } { \partial r } } d \theta - \int _ { C _ { 1 } } u d \theta = \log r _ { 2 } \int _ { C _ { 2 } } r _ { 2 } { \frac { \partial u } { \partial r } } d \theta - \int _ { C _ { 2 } } u d \theta .
$$

In other words, the expression

$$
\int _ { | z | = r } u d \theta - \log r \int _ { | z | = r } r { \frac { \partial u } { \partial r } } d \theta
$$

is constant, and this is true even if $\textbf { \em u }$ is only known to be harmonic in an annulus. By (55) we find in the same way that

$$
\int _ { | z | = r } \mathop { r } \frac { \partial { u } } { \partial r } \mathop { d \theta }
$$

is constant in the case of an annulus and zero if $\pmb { u }$ is harmonic in the whole disk. Combining these results we obtain:

Theorem 22. The arithmetic mean of a harmonic function over concentric circles $| z | = r$ is a linear function of $\log r _ { i }$

$$
\frac { 1 } { 2 \pi } \int _ { | z | = r } u d \theta = \alpha \log r + \beta ,
$$

and if $\mathbf { \nabla } ^ { \mathbf { \mathfrak { u } } }$ is harmonic in a disk $\alpha = 0$ and the arithmetic mean is constant.

In the latter case $\beta = u ( 0 )$ , by continuity, and changing to a new origin we find

$$
u ( z _ { 0 } ) = \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi } \int _ { 0 } ^ { 2 \pi } u ( z _ { 0 } + r e ^ { i \theta } ) \ : d \theta .
$$

It is clear that (59) could also have been derived from the corresponding formula for analytic functions, Sec. 3.4, (34). It leads directly to the maximum principle for harmonic functions:

Theorem 23. A nonconstant harmonic function has neither a maximum nor a minimum in its region of definition. Consequently, the maximum and the minimum on a closed bounded set $\displaystyle E$ are taken on the boundary of $E _ { \ast }$ .

The proof is the same as for the maximum principle of analytic functions and will not be repeated. It applies also to the minimum for the reason that ${ } - u$ is harmonic together with $_ { \ b { u } }$ In the case of analytic functions the corresponding procedure would have been to apply the maximum principle to $\mathbf { 1 } / f ( z )$ which is illegitimate unless $f ( z ) \neq 0$ . Observe that the maximum principle for analytic functions follows from the maximum principle for harmonic functions by applying the latter to $\log | f ( z ) |$ which is harmonic when $f ( z ) \neq 0$ .

# EXERCISES

1. If $\mathbf { \boldsymbol { u } }$ is harmonic and bounded in $0 < | z | < \rho ,$ show that the origin is a removable singularity in the sense that $\textbf { \em u }$ becomes harmonic in $| z | < \rho$ when ${ \pmb u } ( { \bf 0 } )$ is properly defined.

2. If $\boldsymbol { u } ( z )$ is harmonic for $0 < | z | < \rho$ and $\begin{array} { r } { \operatorname* { l i m } _ { z \to 0 } z u ( z ) = 0 } \end{array}$ , prove that ${ \pmb u }$ can be written in the form $u ( z ) = \alpha \log | z | + u _ { 0 } ( z )$ where $_ { \alpha }$ is a constant and $\pmb { u _ { 0 } }$ is harmonic in $| z | < \rho$ .

3. Suppose that $f ( z )$ is analytic for $r _ { 1 } \leq | z | \leq r _ { 2 } ,$ and set

$$
M ( r ) = \operatorname* { m a x } | f ( z ) |
$$

for $\left| z \right| = r ( r _ { 1 } \leq r \leq r _ { 2 } )$ Show that

$$
M ( r ) \leq M ( r _ { 1 } ) ^ { \alpha } M ( r _ { 2 } ) ^ { 1 - \alpha }
$$

where $\alpha = \log { ( r _ { 2 } / r ) } { : } \log { ( r _ { 2 } / r _ { 1 } ) }$ (Hadamard's three-circle theorem). Discuss cases of equality. Hint: Apply the maximum principle to a linear combination of $\log \mid f ( z ) \mid$ and $\log | z |$ .

6.3. Poisson's Formula. The maximum principle has the following important consequence: If $u ( z )$ is harmonic on a closed bounded set $\pmb { { \cal E } }$ that is, if it is defined and harmonic in a region containing $E _ { i }$ , then it is uniquely determined by its values on the boundary of $\mathbf { \nabla } \mathbf { E }$ Indeed, if ${ \pmb u } _ { 1 }$ and $\mathbf { \lambda } _ { u _ { 2 } }$ are two harmonic functions with the same boundary values, then $u _ { 1 } - u _ { 2 }$ is harmonic with the boundary values O. Using the maximum and minimum principle we find that $u _ { 1 } \sim u _ { 2 }$ must be identically zero on $\mathbf { \nabla } \mathbf { E }$ .

There arises the problem of finding $\mathbf { \Delta } _ { u }$ when its boundary values are given. At this point we shall solve the problem only in the simplest case, namely for a closed disk.

Formula (59) determines the value of $\mathbf { \Delta } _ { u }$ at the center of the disk. But this is all we need, for there exists a linear transformation which carries any point to the center. To be explicit, suppose that $u ( z )$ is harmonic in the closed disk $| z | \leq R$ , The linear transformation

$$
z = S ( \xi ) = \frac { R ( R \xi + a ) } { R + \bar { a } \xi }
$$

maps $| \boldsymbol { \underline { { \hat { \imath } } } } | \leq \mathbf { 1 }$ onto $| z | \leq R$ with $\boldsymbol { \xi } = \mathbf { 0 }$ corresponding to $z = a$ The function $u ( S ( \xi ) )$ is harmonic in $| \boldsymbol { \{ \widetilde { \varsigma } | \le 1 } $ , and by (59) we obtain

$$
u ( a ) = { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi } } \int _ { | \xi | = 1 } u ( S ( \xi ) ) \ d \arg \xi .
$$

From

$$
\xi = \frac { R ( z - a ) } { R ^ { 2 } - \bar { a } z }
$$

we compute

$$
d \arg \xi = - { \underline { { i } } } { \frac { d \xi } { \xi } } = - { \underline { { i } } } \left( { \frac { 1 } { z - a } } + { \frac { \bar { a } } { R ^ { 2 } - \bar { a } z } } \right) d z = \left( { \frac { z } { z - a } } + { \frac { \bar { a } z } { R ^ { 2 } - \bar { a } z } } \right) d \theta .
$$

On substituting $R ^ { 2 } = z { \bar { z } }$ the coefficient of $\pmb { d \theta }$ in the last expression can be rewritten as

$$
{ \frac { z } { z - a } } + { \frac { \bar { a } } { \bar { z } - \bar { a } } } = { \frac { R ^ { 2 } } { | z - a | ^ { 2 } } } 
$$

or, equivalently, as

$$
{ \frac { 1 } { 2 } } \left( { \frac { z + a } { z - a } } + { \frac { { \bar { z } } + { \bar { a } } } { { \bar { z } } - { \bar { a } } } } \right) = \operatorname { R e } { \frac { z + a } { z - a } } \cdot
$$

We obtain the two forms

$$
u ( a ) = { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi } } \int _ { | z | = R } { \frac { R ^ { 2 } - | a | ^ { 2 } } { | z - a | ^ { 2 } } } u ( z ) d \theta = { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi } } \int _ { | z | = R } \operatorname { R e } { \frac { z + a } { z - a } } u ( z ) d \theta
$$

o Poisson's formula. In polar coordinates,

$$
u ( r e ^ { i \varphi } ) = { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi } } \int _ { 0 } ^ { 2 \pi } { \frac { R ^ { 2 } - r ^ { 2 } } { R ^ { 2 } - 2 r R \cos \left( \theta - \varphi \right) + r ^ { 2 } } } u ( R e ^ { i \theta } ) d \theta .
$$

In the derivation we have assumed that $u ( z )$ is harmonic in the closed disk. However, the result remains true under the weaker condition that $u ( z )$ is harmonic in the open disk and continuous in the closed disk. Indeed, if $0 < r < 1$ , then $u ( r z )$ is harmonic in the closed disk, and we obtain

$$
u ( r a ) = { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi } } \int _ { | z | = R } { \frac { R ^ { 2 } - | a | ^ { 2 } } { | z - a | ^ { 2 } } } u ( r z ) d \theta .
$$

Now all we need to do is to let $\boldsymbol { r }$ tend to 1. Because $u ( z )$ is uniformiy continuous on $| z | \leq R$ it is true that $u ( r z ) \to u ( z )$ uniformly for $| z | = R ,$ . and we conclude that (60) remains valid.

We shall formulate the result as a theorem:

Theorem 24. Suppose that $u ( z )$ is harmonic for $| z | < R$ , continuous for $| z | \leq R$ . Then

$$
u ( a ) = { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi } } \int _ { | z | = R } { \frac { R ^ { 2 } - | a | ^ { 2 } } { | z - a | ^ { 2 } } } u ( z ) d \theta
$$

for all $| a | < R$ .

The theorem leads at once to an explicit expression for the conjugate function of $\mathbf { \Delta } ^ { u }$ Indeed, formula (60) gives

$$
u ( z ) = \mathrm { R e } \left[ \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } \mathop { { \int } } _ { | \xi _ { i } = R } \frac { \xi + z } { \xi - z } u ( \xi ) \frac { d \xi } { \xi } \right] .
$$

The bracketed expression is an analytic function of $\pmb { z }$ for $| z | < R$ . It follows that $\pmb { u } ( z )$ is the real part of

$$
f ( z ) = \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } \mathop { { } \int } _ { | \xi | = R } \frac { \xi + z } { \xi - z } u ( \xi ) \frac { d \xi } { \xi } + i C
$$

where $c$ is an arbitrary real constant. This formula is known as Schwarz's formula.

As a special case of (61), note that $u = 1$ yields

$$
\int _ { | z | = R } { \frac { R ^ { 2 } - | z | ^ { 2 } } { | z - a | ^ { 2 } } } d \theta = 2 \pi
$$

for all $| a | < R$ .

6.4. Schwarz's Theorem. Theorem 24 serves to express a given harmonic function through its values on a circle. But the right-hand side of formula (61) has a meaning as soon as $\textbf { \em u }$ is defined on $| z | = R$ provided it is sufficiently regular, for instance piecewise continuous. As in (62) the integral can again be written as the real part of an analytic function, and consequently it is a harmonic function. The question is, does it have the boundary values $u ( z )$ on $| z | = R ?$

There is reason to clarify the notations. Choosing $\mathcal { R } \simeq \bf { 1 }$ we define, for any piecewise continuous function $U ( \theta )$ in $0 \leq \theta \leqq 2 \pi$ ,

$$
P _ { U } ( z ) = { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi } } \int _ { 0 } ^ { 2 \pi } \operatorname { R e } { \frac { e ^ { i \theta } + z } { e ^ { i \theta } - z } } U ( \theta ) d \theta
$$

and call this the Poisson integral of $U$ Observe that $P _ { \cal U } ( z )$ is not only a function of $\boldsymbol { z }$ , but also a function of the function $\boldsymbol { U }$ ; as such it is called a functional. The functional is linear inasmuch as

and

$$
\begin{array} { c } { { P _ { U + V } = P _ { U } + P _ { V } } } \\ { { \ } } \\ { { P _ { c U } = c P _ { U } } } \end{array}
$$

for constant c. Moreover, $U \not \equiv { \bf 0 }$ implies $P _ { U } ( z ) \geq 0$ ; because of this property $P _ { U }$ is said to be a positive linear functional.

We deduce from (64) that $P _ { c } = \pmb { c }$ From this property, together with the linear and positive character of the functional, it follows that any inequality $m \leq U \leq M$ implies $m \leq P v \leq M$ .

The question of boundary values is settled by the following fundamental theorem that was first proved by H. A. Schwarz:

Theorem 25. The function $P _ { U } ( z )$ is harmonic for $| z | < 1$ ,and

$$
\operatorname* { l i m } _ { z \to e ^ { i \theta _ { 0 } } } P _ { U } ( z ) = U ( \theta _ { 0 } )
$$

provided that $\boldsymbol { \mathbf { \mathit { U } } }$ is continuous at $\pmb { \theta } _ { 0 }$

We have already remarked that $P _ { U }$ is harmonic. To study the boundary behavior, let $c _ { \mathbf { i } }$ and $C _ { 2 }$ be complementary arcs of the unit circle, and denote by $U _ { \mathbf { I } }$ the function which coincides with $U$ on $c _ { \mathbf { r } }$ and vanishes on $C _ { 2 } ,$ by $U _ { 2 }$ the corresponding function for $C _ { 2 }$ Clearly, $P { \boldsymbol { v } } = P _ { \boldsymbol { v _ { 1 } } } + P _ { \boldsymbol { v _ { 2 } } }$ .

Since $P _ { U _ { 1 } }$ can be regarded as a line integral over $c _ { \mathbf { r } }$ it is, by the same reasoning as before, harmonic everywhere except on the closed arc $c _ { \mathbf { 1 } } .$ The expression

$$
\operatorname { R e } { \frac { e ^ { i \theta } + z } { e ^ { i \theta } - z } } = { \frac { 1 - | z | ^ { 2 } } { | e ^ { i \theta } - z | ^ { 2 } } }
$$

vanishes on $| z | = 1$ for $z \neq e ^ { i \theta }$ It follows that $P _ { \boldsymbol { v _ { 1 } } }$ is zero on the open arc $C _ { 2 } ,$ and since it is continuous $\begin{array} { r } { \operatorname* { l i m } _ { z  e } { } ^ { i \theta } P _ { U _ { 1 } } ( z ) = 0 } \end{array}$ for $e ^ { i \theta } \epsilon C _ { 2 }$ .

In proving (65) we may suppose that $U ( \theta _ { 0 } ) = \mathbf { 0 }$ , for if this is not the case we need only replace $\boldsymbol { \tau }$ by $U - U ( \theta _ { 0 } )$ Given $\mathfrak { e } > \mathbf { 0 }$ we can find $c _ { \mathbf { i } }$ and $C _ { 2 }$ such that $e ^ { i \theta _ { 0 } }$ is an interior point of $C _ { 2 }$ and $| U ( \theta ) | < \mathfrak { e } / 2$ for $e ^ { i \theta } \in C _ { 2 }$ . Under this condition $| U _ { 2 } ( \theta ) | < \mathfrak { e } / 2$ for all $\pmb \theta$ ,and hence $| P _ { U _ { 2 } } ( z ) | < \mathfrak { e } / 2$ for all $| z | < 1$ On the other hand, since $U _ { 1 }$ is continuous and vanishes at $e ^ { i \theta _ { 0 } }$ there exists a δ such that $| P _ { U _ { 1 } } ( z ) | < \varepsilon / 2$ for $| z - e ^ { i \theta _ { 0 } } | < \delta$ It follows that $| P _ { U } ( z ) | \ \le \ | P _ { U _ { 1 } } | + | P _ { U _ { 2 } } | < \mathfrak { e }$ as soon as $| z | < 1$ and $| z - e ^ { i \theta _ { 0 } } |$ $< \delta$ , which is precisely what we had to prove.

There is an interesting geometric interpretation of Poisson's formula, also due to Schwarz. Given a fixed $z$ inside the unit circle we determine for each $e ^ { i \theta }$ the point $e ^ { i \theta ^ { * } }$ which is such that $e ^ { i \theta }$ , $\boldsymbol { \mathscr { z } }$ and $e ^ { i \theta ^ { * } }$ are in a straight

![](images/2c471d8e44149485568472713a4c7be9195ab242fd748b4b84078e7f435332dd.jpg)

FIG. 28

line (Fig. 28). It is clear geometrically, or by simple calculation, that

$$
1 - | z | ^ { 2 } = | e ^ { i \theta } - z | | e ^ { i \theta ^ { \ast } } - z | .
$$

But the ratio $( e ^ { i \theta } - z ) / ( e ^ { i \theta ^ { \ast } } - z )$ is negative, so we must have

$$
1 - | z | ^ { 2 } = - ( e ^ { i \theta } - z ) ( e ^ { - i \theta ^ { * } } - \bar { z } ) .
$$

We regard $\pmb { \theta } ^ { * }$ as a function of $\pmb \theta$ and differentiate. Since $z$ is constant we obtain

$$
\frac { e ^ { i \theta } d \theta } { e ^ { i \theta } - z } = \frac { e ^ { - i \theta ^ { * } } d \theta ^ { * } } { e ^ { - i \theta ^ { * } } - \bar { z } }
$$

and, on taking absolute values,

$$
\frac { d \theta ^ { * } } { d \theta } = \bigg | \frac { e ^ { i \theta ^ { * } } - z } { e ^ { i \theta } - z } \bigg | .
$$

It follows by (66) and (67) that

$$
{ \frac { 1 - | z | ^ { 2 } } { | e ^ { i \theta } - z | ^ { 2 } } } = { \frac { d \theta ^ { \ast } } { d \theta } }
$$

and hence

$$
P _ { U } ( z ) = { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi } } \int _ { 0 } ^ { 2 \pi } U ( \theta ) d \theta ^ { * } = { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi } } \int _ { 0 } ^ { 2 \pi } U ( \theta ^ { * } ) d \theta .
$$

In other words, to find $P _ { \cal { t } } \left( z \right)$ , replace each value of $U ( \theta )$ by the value at the point opposite to $\pmb { z }$ , and take the average over the circle.

1 Assume that $U ( \xi )$ is piecewise continuous and bounded for all real Show that

P _ { U } ( z ) = \frac { 1 } { \pi } \int _ { - \infty } ^ { \infty } \frac { y } { ( x - \xi ) ^ { 2 } + y ^ { 2 } } U ( \xi ) \ d \xi
$$

represents a harmonic function in the upper half plane with boundary values $U ( \xi )$ at points of continuity (Poisson's integral for the half plane).

Prove that a function which is harmonic and bounded in the upper half plane, continuous on the real axis, can be represented as a Poisson integral (Ex. 1).

Remark. The point at $\infty$ presents an added difficulty, for we cannot immediately apply the maximum and minimum principle to $u - P _ { \ast }$ . A good try would be to apply the maximum principle to $u - P _ { u } - \varepsilon y$ for $\mathfrak { z } > \mathbf { 0 }$ , with the idea of letting e tend to 0. This almost works, for the function tend to 0 for $y  0$ and to $- \infty$ for $y  \infty$ , but we lack control when $| x |  \infty$ . Show that the reasoning can be carried out successfully by application to $u \mathrm { ~ - ~ } P _ { u } \mathrm { ~ - ~ } \mathfrak { e } \mathrm { ~ I m ~ } ( \sqrt { i z } )$

In Ex. 1, assume that $U$ has a jump at 0, for instance $U ( + 0 ) = \mathbf { 0 } _ { ; }$ $U ( - { \bf 0 } ) = { \bf 1 }$ Show that $P _ { U } ( z ) \ - \ { \frac { 1 } { \pi } } \arg \ z$ tends to 0 as ${ z \to 0 }$ Generalize to arbitrary jumps and to the case of the circle.

$^ { 4 . }$ If $C _ { 1 }$ and $C _ { 2 }$ are complementary arcs on the unit circle, set $U = 1$ on $C _ { 1 } ,$ $U = { \bf 0 }$ on $C _ { 2 }$ Find $P _ { U } ( z )$ explicitly and show that ${ 2 } \pi P _ { U } ( z )$ equals the length of the arc, opposite to $C _ { 1 , }$ cut off by the straight lines through $\pmb { z }$ and the end points of $C _ { 1 } .$ .

$\blacktriangle$ Show that the mean-value formula (59) remains valid for $u = \log { \left| 1 + z \right| }$ . ${ \mathfrak { z } } _ { 0 } = 0$ , $\pmb { r } = \pmb { 1 }$ , and use this fact to compute

$$
\int _ { 0 } ^ { \pi } \log \sin \theta d \theta .
$$

6.5. The Reflection Principle. An elementary aspect of the symmetry principle, or reflection principle, has been discussed already in connection with linear transformations (Chap. 3, Sec. 3.3). There are many more gencral variants first formulated by H. A. Schwarz.

The principle of reflection is based on the observation that if $u ( z )$ is a harmonic function, then $u ( \breve { z } )$ is likewise harmonic, and if $f ( z )$ is an analytic function, then $\overline { { f ( \overline { { z } } ) } }$ is also analytic. More precisely, if $u ( z )$ is harmonic and $f ( z )$ analytic in a region then $u ( \bar { z } )$ is harmonic and $\overline { { f ( \bar { z } ) } }$ analytic as functions of $\pmb { \mathscr { z } }$ in the region $\Omega ^ { * }$ obtained by reflection $\Omega$ in the real axis; that is, $z \in \Omega ^ { * }$ if and only if $\Xi \in \Omega$ , The proofs of these statements consist in trivial verifications.

Consider the case of a symmetric region: $\Omega ^ { * } = \Omega$ Because $\Omega$ is connected it must intersect the real axis along at least one open interval. Assume now that $f ( z )$ is analytic in $\Omega$ and real on at least one interval of the real axis. Since $f ( z ) - { \overline { { f ( { \overline { { z } } } ) } } }$ is analytic and vanishes on an interval it must be identically zero, and we conclude that $f ( z ) = { \overline { { f ( { \bar { z } } ) } } }$ in $\Omega ,$ With the notation $f = u + i v$ we have thus $u ( z ) = u ( \bar { z } ) , v ( z ) = - v ( \bar { z } )$ .

This is important, but it is a rather weak result, for we are assuming that $f ( z )$ is already known to be analytic in all of $\pmb { \Omega }$ Let us denote the intersection of $\Omega$ with the upper half plane by $\Omega ^ { + }$ , and the intersection of $\Omega$ with the real axis by $\pmb { \sigma }$ Suppose that $f ( z )$ is defined on $\Omega ^ { + } \cup \sigma$ ,analytic in $\Omega ^ { + }$ , continuous and real on $\pmb { \sigma }$ . Under these conditions we want to show that $f ( z )$ is the restriction to $\Omega ^ { + }$ of a function which is analytic in all of $\Omega$ and satisfies the symmetry condition ${ f ( z ) = \widetilde { f ( { \bar { z } } ) } }$ . In other words, part of our theorem asserts that $f ( z )$ has an analytic continuation to $\Omega$ .

Even in this formulation the assumptions are too strong. Indeed, the main thing is that the imaginary part $v ( z )$ vanishes on $\pmb { \sigma } _ { . }$ and nothing at all need to be assumed about the real part. In the definitive statement of the reflection principle the emphasis should therefore be on harmonic functions.

Theorem 26. Let $\Omega ^ { + }$ be the part in the upper half plane of a symmetric region $\Omega$ , and let $\pmb { \sigma }$ be the part of the real axis in $\Omega$ Suppose that $v ( x )$ is continuous in $\Omega ^ { + } \cup \sigma$ , harmonic in $\Omega ^ { + }$ , and zero on $\pmb { \sigma }$ , Then v has a harmonic extension to $\Omega$ which satisfies the symmetry relation $v ( \Xi ) = - v ( z )$ . In the same situation, if v is the imaginary part of an analytic function $f ( z )$ in $\Omega ^ { + }$ , then $f ( z )$ has an analytic extension which satisfies $f ( z ) = { \overline { { f ( { \bar { z } } ) } } }$ .

For the proof we construct the function $V ( z )$ which is equal to $v ( z )$ in $\Omega ^ { + }$ , 0 on $\pmb { \sigma }$ , and equal to $- v ( { \bar { z } } )$ in the mirror image of $\Omega ^ { + }$ We have to show that $V$ is harmonic on $\pmb { \sigma }$ . For a point ${ \boldsymbol { \mathscr { x } } } _ { 0 } \in { \boldsymbol { \sigma } }$ consider a disk with center $x _ { 0 }$ contained in $\Omega$ and let $P _ { \pmb { \nu } }$ denote the Poisson integral with respect to this disk formed with the boundary values $V$ The difference $V \mathrm { ~ - ~ } P _ { \mathbf { \nu } }$ is harmonic in the upper half of the disk. It vanishes on the half circle, by Theorem 25, and also on the diameter, because $V$ tends to zero by definition and $P _ { V }$ vanishes by obvious symmetry. The maximum and minimum principle implies that $V = P _ { \pmb { v } }$ in the upper half disk, and the same proof can be repeated for the lower half. We conclude that $V$ is harmonic in the whole disk, and in particular at $\pmb { x _ { 0 } }$ .

For the remaining part of the theorem, let us again consider a disk with center on $\pmb { \sigma }$ . We have already extended $v$ to the whole disk, and $v$ has a conjugate harmonic function $- \boldsymbol { u } _ { \boldsymbol { \mathbf { 0 } } }$ in the same disk which we may normalize so that $u _ { 0 } = \operatorname { R e } f ( z )$ in the upper half. Consider

$$
U _ { 0 } ( z ) = u _ { 0 } ( z ) - u _ { 0 } ( \bar { z } ) .
$$

On the real diameter it is clear that $\partial U _ { 0 } / \partial x = 0$ and also

$$
\frac { \partial U _ { 0 } } { \partial y } = 2 \frac { \partial u _ { 0 } } { \partial y } = - 2 \frac { \partial v } { \partial x } = 0 .
$$

It follows that the analytic function $\partial U _ { 0 } / \partial x - i \partial U _ { 0 } / \partial y$ vanishes on the real axis, and hence identically. Therefore $U _ { \mathfrak { g } }$ is a constant, and this constant is evidently zero. We have proved that $u _ { 0 } ( z ) = u _ { \mathrm { c } } ( \bar { z } )$ .

The construction can be repeated for arbitrary disks. It is clear that the $\boldsymbol { u } _ { 0 }$ coincide in overlapping disks. The definition can be extended to all of $\mathfrak { a }$ , and the theorem follows.

The theorem has obvious generalizations. The domain $\Omega$ can be taken to be symmetric with respect to a circle $c$ rather than with respect to a straight line, and when $\pmb { z }$ tends to $c$ it may be assumed that $f ( z )$ approaches another circle $c ^ { \prime }$ Under such conditions $f ( z )$ has an analytic continuation which maps symmetric points with respect to $c$ onto symmetric points with respect to $c ^ { \prime }$ .

# 5 SERIES AND PRODUCT DEVELOPMENTS

Very general theorems have their natural place in the theory of analytic functions, but it must also be kept in mind that the whole theory originated from a desire to be able to manipulate explicit analytic expressions. Such expressions take the form of infinite series, infinite products, and other limits. In this chapter we deal partly with the rules that govern such limits, partly with quite explicit representations of elementary transcendental functions and other specific functions.

# 1. POWER SERIES EXPANSIONS

In a preliminary way we have considered power series in Chap. 2, mainly for the purpose of defining the exponential and trigonometric functions. Without use of integration we were not able to prove that every analytic function has a power series expansion. This question will now be resolved in the affirmative, essentially as an application of Cauchy's theorem.

The first subsection deals with more general properties of sequences of analytic functions.

1.1. Weierstrass's Theorem. The central theorem concerning the convergence of analytic functions asserts that the limit of a uniformly convergent sequence of analytic functions is an analytic function. The precise assumptions must be carefully stated, and they should not be too restrictive.

We are considering a sequence $\{ f _ { \mathfrak { n } } ( z ) \}$ where each $f _ { \pmb { \mathscr { n } } } ( \pmb { \mathscr { z } } )$ is defined and analytic in a region $\Omega _ { n }$ The limit function $f ( z )$ must also be considered in some region $\mathfrak { Q } _ { ; }$ , and clearly, if $f ( z )$ is to be defined in $\Omega$ , each point of $\mathfrak { a }$ must belong to all $\Omega _ { n }$ for $_ { \pmb { n } }$ greater than a certain $\scriptstyle n _ { 0 }$ In the general case $\scriptstyle \mathbf { \mathfrak { n } } _ { 0 }$ will not be the same for all points of $\Omega _ { i }$ , and for this reason it would not make sense to require that the convergence be uniform in $\Omega .$ In fact, in the most typical case the regions $\Omega _ { n }$ form an increasing sequence, $\Omega _ { \mathbf { I } } \subset$ $\Omega _ { 2 } \subset \cdots \subset \Omega _ { n } \subset \cdots$ , and $\Omega$ is the union of the $\Omega _ { n }$ In these circumstances no single function $f _ { n } ( z )$ is defined in all of $\Omega$ s yet the limit $f ( z )$ may exist at all points of $\Omega$ , although the convergence cannot be uniform.

As a very simple example take $f _ { n } ( z ) = z / ( 2 z ^ { n } + 1 )$ and let $\Omega _ { n }$ be the disk $| z | < 2 ^ { - 1 / n }$ . It is practically evident that $\operatorname* { l i m } _ { n \to \infty } f _ { n } ( z ) = z$ in the disk $| z | < 1$ which we choose as our region $\pmb { \Omega } _ { * }$ In order to study the uniformity of the convergence we form the difference

$$
f _ { n } ( z ) \ : - \ : z = \ : - 2 z ^ { n + 1 } / ( 2 z ^ { n } + 1 ) .
$$

For any given value of $\pmb { z }$ we can make $| z ^ { n } | < \varepsilon / 4$ by taking $\textit { \textbf { n } } >$ $\log { ( 4 / \varepsilon ) } / \log { ( 1 / | z | ) }$ If $\mathfrak { e } < 1$ we have then $2 | z | ^ { n + 1 } < \mathfrak { e } / 2$ and $\left| 1 + 2 z ^ { n } \right| > { \frac { 1 } { 2 } }$ so that $| f _ { n } ( z ) - z | < \varepsilon$ . It follows that the convergence is uniform in any closed disk $| z | \leq r < 1$ , or on any subset of such a closed disk.

With another formulation, in the preceding example the sequence $\{ f _ { n } ( z ) \}$ tends to the limit function $f ( z )$ uniformly on every compact subset of the region $\Omega$ , In fact, on a compact set $| z |$ has a maximum $r < 1$ and the set is thus contained in the closed disk $| z | \leq r$ ,This is the typical situation. We shall find that we can frequently prove uniform convergence on every compact subset of $\Omega$ ; on the other hand, this is the natural condition in the theorem that we are going to prove.

Theorem 1. Suppose that $f _ { n } ( z )$ is analytic in the region $\Omega _ { n } ,$ and that the sequence $\{ f _ { n } ( z ) \}$ converges to a limit function $f ( z )$ in a region $\Omega$ , uniformly on every compact subset of $\Omega$ Then $f ( z )$ is analytic in $\Omega$ Moreover, $f _ { n } ^ { \prime } ( z )$ converges uniformly to $f ^ { \prime } ( z )$ on every compact subset of $\Omega$ .

The analyticity of $f ( z )$ follows most easily by use of Morera's theorem (Chap. 4, Sec. 2.3). Let $| z - a | \leq r$ be a closed disk contained in $\mathfrak { a }$ ; the assumption implies that this disk lies in $\Omega _ { n }$ for all $\mathbfit { \Delta } n$ greater than a certain $n _ { 0 } \bar { . } \bar { 1 }$ If $\gamma$ is any closed curve contained in $| z - a | < r$ , we have

$$
\int _ { \gamma } f _ { n } ( z ) \ d z = 0
$$

$^ \dagger$ In fact, the regions $\Omega _ { n }$ form an open covering of $| z - \boldsymbol { a } | \le \tau$ The disk is comp e  Th   i $\Omega _ { n _ { 0 } }$ .

for $n > n _ { 0 } ,$ by Cauchy's theorem. Because of the uniform convergence on $\pmb { \gamma }$ we obtain

$$
\int _ { \gamma } f ( z ) \ d z = \operatorname* { l i m } _ { n \to \infty } \int _ { \gamma } f _ { n } ( z ) \ d z = 0 ,
$$

and by Morera's theorem it follows that $f ( z )$ is analytic in $| z - a | < r$ Consequently $f ( z )$ is analytic in the whole region $\Omega$ .

An alternative and more explicit proof is based on the integral formula

$$
f _ { n } ( z ) = { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } } \int _ { \cal C } { \frac { f _ { n } ( \xi ) \ d \xi } { \xi - z } } ,
$$

where $c$ is the circle $| \zeta - a | = r$ and $| z - a | < r$ Letting $\pmb { n }$ tend to $\infty$ we obtain by uniform convergence

$$
f ( z ) = { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } } \int _ { \cal C } { \frac { f ( \xi ) d \xi } { \xi - z } } \cdotp
$$

and this formula shows that $f ( z )$ is analytic in the disk. Starting from the formula

$$
f _ { n } ^ { \prime } ( z ) = { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } } \int _ { \cal C } { \frac { f _ { n } ( \xi ) \ d \xi } { ( \xi - \ z ) ^ { 2 } } }
$$

the same reasoning yields

$$
\operatorname* { l i m } _ { n \to \infty } f _ { n } ^ { \prime } ( z ) = { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } } \int _ { C } { \frac { f ( \xi ) ~ d \xi } { ( \xi - z ) ^ { 2 } } } = f ^ { \prime } ( z ) ,
$$

and simple estimates show that the convergence is uniform for $| z - a |$ $\leq \rho < r$ Any compact subset of $\Omega$ can be covered by a finite number of such closed disks, and therefore the convergence is uniform on every compact subset. The theorem is proved, and by repeated applications it follows that $f _ { \textit { n } } ^ { ( k ) } ( z )$ converges uniformly to $f ^ { ( k ) } ( z )$ on every compact subset of $\Omega$ .

Theorem 1 is due to Weierstrass, in an equivalent formulation. Its application to series whose terms are analytic functions is particularly important. The theorem can then be expressed as follows:

If a series with analytic terms,

$$
f ( z ) = f _ { 1 } ( z ) + f _ { 2 } ( z ) + \cdot \cdot \cdot + f _ { n } ( z ) + \cdot \cdot \cdot ,
$$

converges uniformly on every compact subset of a region $\Omega$ , then the sum $f ( z )$ is analytic in $\Omega$ , and the series can be differentiated term by term.

The task of proving uniform convergence on a compact point set A can be facilitated by use of the maximum principle. In fact, with the notations of Theorem 1, the difference $\left| f _ { m } ( z ) - f _ { n } ( z ) \right|$ attains its maxi-

mum in A on the boundary of A. For this reason uniform convergence on the boundary of $\pmb { A }$ implies uniform convergence on $\pmb { A }$ For instance, if the functions $f _ { \pi } ( z )$ are analytic in the disk $| z | < 1$ , and if it can be shown that the sequence converges uniformly on each circle $| z | = r _ { m }$ where lim $r _ { m } = 1$ , then Weierstrass's theorem applies and we can conm→ ∞   
clude that the limit function is analytic.

The following theorem is due to A. Hurwitz:

Theorem 2. If the functions $f _ { n } ( z )$ are analytic and $\yen 0$ in a region $\mathfrak { a }$ . and if $f _ { n } ( z )$ converges to $f ( z )$ , uniformly on every compact subset of $\Omega _ { i }$ , then $f ( z )$ is either identically zero or never equal to zero in $\Omega ,$

Suppose that $f ( z )$ is not identically zero. The zeros of $f ( z )$ are in any case isolated. For any point $z _ { 0 } \in \Omega$ there is therefore a number $r > 0$ such that $f ( z )$ is defined and $\yen 0$ for $0 < | z - z _ { 0 } | \leq r$ In particular, $\vert f ( z ) \vert$ has a positive minimum on the circle $| z - z _ { 0 } | = r$ , which we denote by $c$ . It follows that $\pmb { 1 } / f _ { n } ( z )$ converges uniformly to $\mathbf { 1 } / f ( z )$ on $c$ Since it is also true that $f _ { n } ^ { \prime } ( z ) \to f ^ { \prime } ( z )$ , uniformly on $c$ , we may conclude that

$$
\operatorname* { l i m } _ { n \to \infty } { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } } \int _ { C } { \frac { f _ { n } ^ { \prime } ( z ) } { f _ { n } ( z ) } } d z = { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } } \int _ { C } { \frac { f ^ { \prime } ( z ) } { f ( z ) } } d z .
$$

But the integrals on the left are all zero, for they give the number of roots of the equation $\pmb { f } _ { n } ( z ) = \mathbf { 0 }$ inside of $c$ The integral on the right is therefore zero, and consequently $f ( z _ { 0 } ) \neq 0$ by the same interpretation of the integral. Since ${ \mathfrak { z } } _ { 0 }$ was arbitrary, the theorem follows.

$$

uniformly on all compact sets.

Show that the series

$$
\xi ( z ) = \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { \infty } n ^ { - s }
$$

converges for $\mathbf { R e } z > 1$ , and represent its derivative in series form.

$$
( 1 - 2 ^ { 1 - z } ) \xi ( z ) = 1 ^ { - z } - 2 ^ { - \varepsilon } + 3 ^ { - \varepsilon } - \cdot \cdot \cdot

$\pmb { 5 }$ Prove that

$$
\sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { \infty } { \frac { n z ^ { n } } { 1 - z ^ { n } } } = \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { \infty } { \frac { z ^ { n } } { ( 1 - z ^ { n } ) ^ { 2 } } }
