for $| z | < 1$ . (Develop in a double series and reverse the order of summation.)

1.2. The Taylor Series. We show now that every analytic function can be developed in a convergent Taylor series. This is an almost immediate consequence of the finite Taylor development given in Chap. 4, Sec. 3.1, Theorem 8, together with the corresponding representation of the remainder term. According to this theorem, if $f ( z )$ is analytic in a region $\Omega$ containing $z _ { 0 } ,$ we can write

$$
f ( z ) = f ( z _ { 0 } ) + \frac { f ^ { \prime } ( z _ { 0 } ) } { 1 1 } ( z - z _ { 0 } ) + \cdot \cdot \cdot + \frac { f ^ { ( n ) } ( z _ { 0 } ) } { n ! } ( z - z _ { 0 } ) ^ { n } \nonumber \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ :  \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ :  \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ : \ :
$$

with

$$
f _ { n + 1 } ( z ) - { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } } \int _ { C } { \frac { f ( \xi ) d \xi } { ( \xi - z _ { 0 } ) ^ { n + 1 } ( \xi - z ) } } \cdotp
$$

In the last formula $c$ is any circle $| z - z _ { 0 } | = \rho$ such that the closed disk $| z - z _ { 0 } | \leq \rho$ is contained in $\Omega _ { * }$

If $M$ denotes the maximum of $| f ( z ) |$ on $c$ , we obtain at once the estimate

$$
| f _ { n + 1 } ( z ) ( z - z _ { 0 } ) ^ { n + 1 } | \leq { \frac { M | z - z _ { 0 } | ^ { n + 1 } } { \rho ^ { n } ( \rho - | z - z _ { 0 } | ) } }
$$

We conclude that the remainder term tends uniformly to zero in every disk $| z - z _ { 0 } | \leq r < \rho$ On the other hand, $\pmb { \rho }$ can be chosen arbitrarily close to the shortest distance from ${ \mathfrak { z } } _ { 0 }$ to the boundary of $\Omega$ We have proved:

Theorem 3. If $f ( z )$ is analytrc in the region $\Omega ,$ containing ${ \mathfrak { z } } _ { 0 } ,$ then the representation

$$
f ( z ) = f ( z _ { 0 } ) + \frac { f ^ { \prime } ( z _ { 0 } ) } { 1 ! } ( z - z _ { 0 } ) + \cdot \cdot \cdot + \frac { f ( ^ { ( n ) } ( z _ { 0 } ) } { n ! } ( z - z _ { 0 } ) ^ { n } + \cdot \cdot \cdot
$$

is valid in the largest open disk of center ${ \mathit { z } } _ { 0 }$ contained in $\Omega$

The radius of convergence of the Taylor series is thus at least equal to the shortest distance from ${ z _ { 0 } }$ to the boundary of $\pmb { \Omega }$ It may well be larger, but if it is there is no guarantee that the series still represents $f ( z )$ at all points which are simultaneously in $\Omega$ and in the circle of convergence.

We recall that the developments

$$
\begin{array} { l } { { e ^ { \varepsilon } = 1 + z + \displaystyle \frac { z ^ { 2 } } { 2 ! } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + \displaystyle \frac { z ^ { n } } { n ! } + \cdot \cdot \cdot } } \\ { { \cos z = 1 - \displaystyle \frac { z ^ { 2 } } { 2 ! } + \frac { z ^ { 4 } } { 4 ! } - \frac { z ^ { 6 } } { 6 ! } + \cdot \cdot \cdot } } \\ { { \sin z = z - \displaystyle \frac { z ^ { 3 } } { 3 ! } + \frac { z ^ { 5 } } { 5 ! } - \displaystyle \frac { z ^ { 7 } } { 7 ! } + \cdot \cdot \cdot } } \end{array}
$$

served as definitions of the functions they represent. Of course, as we have remarked before, every convergent power series is its own Taylor series. We gave earlier a direct proof that power series can be differentiated term by term. This is also a direct consequence of Weierstrass's theorem.

If we want to represent a fractional power of $\pmb { z }$ or $\log z$ through a power series, we must first of all choose a well-defined branch, and secondly we have to choose a center $z _ { 0 } \neq 0$ . It amounts to the same thing if we develop the function $( 1 + z ) ^ { \mu }$ or log $( 1 + z )$ about the origin, choosing the branch which is respectively equal to 1 or 0 at the origin. Since this branch is single-valued and analytic in $| z | < 1$ , the radius of convergence is at least 1. It is elementary to compute the coefficients, and we obtain

$$
\begin{array} { c } { { ( 1 + z ) ^ { \mu } = 1 + \mu z + { \binom { \mu } { 2 } } z ^ { 2 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + { \binom { \mu } { n } } z ^ { n } + \cdot \cdot \cdot } } \\ { { \log \left( 1 + z \right) = z - \displaystyle \frac { z ^ { 2 } } { 2 } + \frac { z ^ { 3 } } { 3 } - \frac { z ^ { 4 } } { 4 } + \frac { z ^ { 5 } } { 5 } - \cdot \cdot \cdot } } \end{array}
$$

where the binomial coefficients are defined by

$$
{ \binom { \mu } { n } } = { \frac { \mu ( \mu - 1 ) \ \cdot \ \cdot \ ( \mu - n + 1 ) } { 1 \cdot 2 \ \cdot \ \cdot \ n } } .
$$

If the logarithmic series had a radius of convergence greater than 1, then log $( 1 + z )$ would be bounded for $| z | < 1$ . Since this is not the case, the radius of convergence must be exactly 1. Similarly, if the binomial series were convergent in a circle of radius ${ > } 1$ , the function $( 1 + z ) ^ { \mu }$ and all its derivatives would be bounded in $| z | < 1$ . Unless $\pmb { \mu }$ is a positive integer, one of the derivatives will be a negative power of $1 + z _ { i }$ and hence unbounded. Thus the radius of convergence is precisely 1 except in the trivial case in which the binomial series reduces to a polynomial.

The series developments of the cyclometric functions arc tan $\pmb { z }$ and arc sin $\pmb { z }$ are most easily obtained by consideration of the derived series. From the expansion

$$
\frac { 1 } { 1 + z ^ { 2 } } = 1 - z ^ { 2 } + z ^ { 4 } - z ^ { 6 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot
$$

we obtain by integration

$$
\operatorname { a r c } \tan z = z - { \frac { z ^ { 3 } } { 3 } } + { \frac { z ^ { 5 } } { 5 } } - { \frac { z ^ { 7 } } { 7 } } + \cdot \cdot \cdot
$$

where the branch is uniquely determined as

$$
\operatorname { a r c } \tan z = \int _ { 0 } ^ { z } { \frac { d z } { 1 + z ^ { 2 } } }
$$

for any path inside the unit circle. For justification we can either rely on uniform convergence or apply Theorem 1. The radius of convergence cannot be greater than that of the derived series, and hence it is exactly 1.

If $\sqrt { 1 - z ^ { 2 } }$ is the branch with a positive real part, we have

$$
{ \frac { 1 } { \sqrt { 1 - z ^ { 2 } } } } = 1 + { \frac { 1 } { 2 } } z ^ { 2 } + { \frac { 1 \cdot 3 } { 2 \cdot 4 } } z ^ { 4 } + { \frac { 1 \cdot 3 \cdot 5 } { 2 \cdot 4 \cdot 6 } } z ^ { 6 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot \cdot
$$

for $| z | < 1$ , and through integration we obtain

$$
\arcsin z = z + { \frac { 1 z ^ { 3 } } { 2 } } + { \frac { 1 \cdot 3 \ z ^ { 5 } } { 2 \cdot 4 \ 5 } } + { \frac { 1 \cdot 3 \cdot 5 z ^ { 2 } } { 2 \cdot 4 \cdot 6 \ 7 } } + \cdot \cdot \cdot \cdot
$$

The series represents the principal branch of arc sin $\boldsymbol { \mathscr { z } }$ with a real part between $- \pi / 2$ and $\pi / 2$ .

For combinations of elementary functions it is mostly not possible to find a general law for the coefficients. In order to find the first few coefficients we need not, however, calculate the successive derivatives. There are simple techniques which allow us to compute, with a reasonable amount of labor, all the coefficients that we are likely to need.

It is convenient to introduce the notation $[ z ^ { n } ]$ for any function which is analytic and has a zero of at least order $\mathbfit { \Delta } n$ at the origin; less precisely, $\left\{ z ^ { n } \right\}$ denotes a function which "contains the factor $z ^ { n }$ " With this notation any function which is analytic at the origin can be written in the form

$$
f ( z ) = a _ { 0 } + a _ { 1 } z + \cdot \cdot \cdot + a _ { n } z ^ { n } + [ z ^ { n + 1 } ] ,
$$

where the coefficients are uniquely determined and equal to the Taylor coefficients of $f ( z )$ . Thus, in order to find the first $\mathbfit { \Delta } n$ coefficients of the Taylor expansion, it is sufficient to determine a polynomial $P _ { \mathfrak { n } } ( z )$ such

that $f ( z ) \mathrm { ~ - ~ } P _ { n } ( z )$ has a zero of at least order $n + 1$ at the origin. The degree of $P _ { n } ( z )$ does not matter; it is true in any case that the coefficients of $z ^ { m }$ $m \leq n$ , are the Taylor coefficients of $f ( z )$ .

For instance, suppose that

$$
\begin{array} { l } { { f ( z ) = a _ { 0 } + a _ { 1 } z + a _ { 2 } z ^ { 2 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + a _ { n } z ^ { n } + \cdot \cdot \cdot . } } \\ { { g ( z ) = b _ { 0 } + b _ { 1 } z + b _ { 2 } z ^ { 2 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + b _ { n } z ^ { n } + \cdot \cdot \cdot . } } \end{array}
$$

With an abbreviated notation we write

$$
f ( z ) = P _ { n } ( z ) + [ z ^ { n + 1 } ] ; g ( z ) = Q _ { n } ( z ) + [ z ^ { n + 1 } ] .
$$

It is then clear that $f ( z ) g ( z ) = P _ { n } ( z ) Q _ { n } ( z ) + [ z ^ { n + 1 } ]$ , and the coefficients of the terms of degree $\lneq n$ in $P _ { n } Q _ { n }$ are the Taylor coefficients of the product $f ( z ) g ( z )$ Explicitly we obtain

$$
\begin{array} { r l } { f ( z ) g ( z ) = a _ { 0 } b _ { 0 } + ( a _ { 0 } b _ { 1 } + a _ { 1 } b _ { 0 } ) z + \cdot \cdot \cdot } & { { } } \\ { + \ ( a _ { 0 } b _ { n } + a _ { 1 } b _ { n - 1 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + a _ { n } b _ { 0 } ) z ^ { n } + } & { { } \cdot \cdot \cdot . } \end{array}
$$

In deriving this expansion we have not even mentioned the question of convergence, but since the development is identical with the Taylor development of $f ( z ) g ( z )$ , it follows by Theorem 3 that the radius of convergence is at least equal to the smaller of the radii of convergence of the given series $f ( z )$ and $g ( z )$ . In the practical computation of $P _ { n } Q _ { n }$ it is of course not necessary to determine the terms of degree higher than n.

In the case of a quotient $f ( z ) / g ( z )$ the same method can be applied, provided that $g ( 0 ) = b _ { 0 } \neq 0$ By use of ordinary long division, continued until the remainder contains the factor $z ^ { n + 1 }$ , we can determine a polynomial $R _ { \mathfrak { n } }$ such that $P _ { n } = Q _ { n } R _ { n } + \left\lfloor z ^ { n + 1 } \right\rfloor ,$ Then $f - R _ { n } g = [ z ^ { n + 1 } ] ,$ and since ${ \pmb g } ( { \bf 0 } ) \neq { \bf 0 }$ we find that $f / g = R _ { n } + [ z ^ { n + 1 } ]$ The coefficients of $R _ { n }$ are the Taylor coefficients of $f ( z ) / g ( z )$ . They can be determined explicitly in determinant form, but the expressions are too complicated to be of essential help.

It is also important that we know how to form the development of a composite function $f ( g ( z ) )$ . In this case, if $g ( z )$ is developed around ${ \mathfrak { z } } _ { \mathbf { 0 } }$ the expansion of $f ( w )$ must be in powers of $w - g ( z _ { 0 } )$ To simplify, let us assume that ${ \mathfrak { z } } _ { 0 } = 0$ and $g ( 0 ) = 0$ . We can then set

$$
f ( w ) = a _ { 0 } + a _ { 1 } w + \cdot \cdot \cdot + a _ { n } w ^ { n } + \cdot \cdot \cdot \cdot
$$

and $g ( z ) = b _ { 1 } z + b _ { 2 } z ^ { 2 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + b _ { n } z ^ { n } + \cdot \cdot \cdot .$ . Using the same notations as before we write $f ( w ) = P _ { n } ( w ) + [ w ^ { n + 1 } ]$ and $g ( z ) = Q _ { n } ( z ) + \lfloor z ^ { n + 1 } \rfloor$ with $Q _ { n } ( 0 ) = 0$ Substituting $w = g ( z )$ we have to observe that

$$
P _ { n } ( Q _ { n } + [ z ^ { n + 1 } ] ) = P _ { n } ( Q _ { n } ( z ) ) + [ z ^ { n + 1 } ]
$$

and that any expression of the form $\{ w ^ { n + 1 } \}$ beco mes a $\{ z ^ { n + 1 } \}$ Thus we obtain $f ( g ( z ) ) = P _ { n } ( Q _ { n } ( z ) ) + [ z ^ { n + 1 } ]$ , and the Taylor coefficients of $f ( g ( z ) )$ are the coefficients of $P _ { \pi } ( Q _ { \pi } ( z ) )$ for powers $\lneq n$ .

Finally, we must be able to expand the inverse function of an analytic function ${ \pmb w } = { \pmb g } ( { \pmb z } )$ . Here we may suppose that $g ( \mathbf { 0 } ) = \mathbf { 0 } ;$ ,and we are looking for the branch of the inverse function $z = g ^ { - 1 } ( w )$ which is analytic in a neighborhood of the origin and vanishes for $w = 0$ For the existence of the inverse function it is necessary and sufficient that $g ^ { \prime } ( 0 ) \neq 0$ ; hence we assume that

$$
g ( z ) = a _ { 1 } z + a _ { 2 } z ^ { 2 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot = Q _ { n } ( z ) + [ z ^ { n + 1 } ]
$$

with $a _ { 1 } \neq 0$ Our problem is to determine a polynomial $P _ { \mathfrak { n } } ( w )$ such that $P _ { n } ( Q _ { n } ( z ) ) = z + [ z ^ { n + 1 } ]$ . In fact, under the assumption $a _ { 1 } \neq 0$ the notations $\textstyle { \left[ z ^ { n + 1 } \right] }$ and $[ w ^ { n + 1 } ]$ are interchangeable, and from $z = P _ { n } ( Q _ { n } ( z ) ) + [ z ^ { n + 1 } ]$ we obtain $z = P _ { n } ( g ( z ) + \left\{ z ^ { n + 1 } \right\} ) + \left\{ z ^ { n + 1 } \right\} = P _ { n } ( w ) + \left[ w ^ { n + 1 } \right]$ Hence $P _ { \pi } ( w )$ determines the coefficients of $g ^ { - 1 } ( w )$ .

In order to prove the existence of a polynomial $P _ { n }$ we proceed by induction. Clearly, we can take $P _ { 1 } ( w ) = w / a _ { 1 }$ If $P _ { n - 1 }$ is given, we set $P _ { n } = P _ { n - 1 } + b _ { n } w ^ { n }$ and obtain

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { P _ { n } ( Q _ { n } ( z ) ) = P _ { n - 1 } ( Q _ { n } ( z ) ) + b _ { n } a _ { 1 } ^ { n } z ^ { n } + [ z ^ { n + 1 } ] } \\ & { \qquad = P _ { n - 1 } ( Q _ { n - 1 } ( z ) + a _ { n } z ^ { n } ) + b _ { n } a _ { 1 } ^ { n } z ^ { n } + [ z ^ { n + 1 } ] } \\ & { \qquad = P _ { n - 1 } ( Q _ { n - 1 } ( z ) ) + P _ { n - 1 } ^ { \prime } ( Q _ { n - 1 } ( z ) ) a _ { \alpha } z ^ { n } + b _ { n } a _ { 1 } ^ { n } z ^ { n } + [ z ^ { n + 1 } ] . } \end{array}
$$

In the last member the first two terms form a known polynomial of the form $z + c _ { n } z ^ { n } + [ z ^ { n + 1 } ]$ , and we have only to take $b _ { n } = - c _ { n } { a _ { 1 } ^ { - n } }$ .

For practical purposes the development of the inverse function is found by successive substitutions. To illustrate the method we determine the expansion of tan $\boldsymbol { w }$ from the series

$$
w = \operatorname { a r c } \tan z = z - { \frac { z ^ { 3 } } { 3 } } + { \frac { z ^ { 5 } } { 5 } } - \cdot \cdot \cdot .
$$

If we want the development to include fifth powers, we write

$$
z = w + \frac { z ^ { 3 } } { 3 } - \frac { z ^ { 5 } } { 5 } + [ z ^ { 7 } ]
$$

and substitute this expression in the terms to the right. With appropriate remainders we obtain

$$
\begin{array} { c } { { z = w + \displaystyle \frac 1 3 \Big ( w + \displaystyle \frac { z ^ { 3 } } { 3 } + \{ w ^ { 5 } \} \Big ) ^ { 3 } - \displaystyle \frac 1 5 ( w + \displaystyle [ w ^ { 3 } ] ) ^ { 5 } + \{ w ^ { 7 } \} } } \\ { { = w + \displaystyle \frac 1 3 w ^ { 3 } + \displaystyle \frac 1 3 w ^ { 2 } z ^ { 3 } - \displaystyle \frac 1 5 w ^ { 5 } + [ w ^ { 7 } ] } } \\ { { = w + \displaystyle \frac 1 3 w ^ { 3 } + \displaystyle \frac 1 3 w ^ { 2 } ( w + \displaystyle [ w ^ { 3 } ] ) ^ { 3 } - \displaystyle \frac 1 5 w ^ { 5 } + [ w ^ { 7 } ] = w + \displaystyle \frac 1 3 w ^ { 3 } + \displaystyle \frac 2 { 1 5 } w ^ { 5 } + [ w ^ { 7 } ] . } } \end{array}
$$

Thus the development of tan $\boldsymbol { w }$ begins with the terms

$$
\tan w = w + \frac { 1 } { 3 } w ^ { 3 } + \frac { 2 } { 1 5 } w ^ { 5 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot .
$$

2 The Legendre polynomials are defined as the coefficients $P _ { n } ( \alpha )$ in the development

$$
( 1 - 2 \alpha z + z ^ { 2 } ) ^ { - \frac { 1 } { 4 } } = 1 + P _ { 1 } ( \alpha ) z + P _ { 2 } ( \alpha ) z ^ { 2 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot .
$$

Find $P _ { 1 } , P _ { 2 } , P _ { 3 } ,$ and $P _ { 4 }$

$\clubsuit$ Develop log $( \sin z / z )$ in powers of $\boldsymbol { z }$ up to the term $z ^ { 6 }$ .

${ \mathfrak { a } } _ { \bullet }$ What is the coefficient of $z ^ { 7 }$ in the Taylor development of tan $\Im \mathrm { \Psi }$

$$
c _ { n } = c _ { n - 1 } + c _ { n - 2 } .
$$

Show that the $c _ { n }$ are Taylor coefficients of a rational function, and determine a closed expression for $\pmb { c _ { n } }$ .

# 1.3. The Laurent Series. A series of the form

$$
b _ { 0 } + b _ { 1 } z ^ { - 1 } + b _ { 2 } z ^ { - 2 } + \cdot \cdot + b _ { n } z ^ { - n } + \cdot \cdot \cdot
$$

can be considered as an ordinary power series in the variable $1 / z$ .It will therefore converge outside of some circle $| z | = R$ except in the extreme case $R = \infty$ ; the convergence is uniform in every region $\left| z \right| \geq \rho$ ${ > } R$ , and hence the series represents an analytic function in the region $| z | > R$ . If the series (1) is combined with an ordinary power series, we get a more general series of the form

$$
\sum \limits _ { n = - \infty } ^ { + \infty } a _ { n } z ^ { n } .
$$

It will be termed convergent only if the parts consisting of nonnegative powers and negative powers are separately convergent. Since the first part converges in a disk $| z | < R _ { 2 }$ and the second series in a region $| z | > R _ { 1 } ,$ there is a common region of convergence only if $R _ { 1 } < R _ { 2 } ,$ and (2) represents an analytic function in the annulus $R _ { 1 } < | z | < R _ { 2 }$ .

Conversely, we may start from an analytic function $f ( z )$ whose region of definition contains an annulus $R _ { 1 } < | z | < R _ { 2 } ,$ or more generally an annulus $R _ { 1 } < | z - a | < R _ { 2 }$ . We shall show that such a function can always be developed in a general power series of the form

$$
f ( z ) = \sum _ { n = - \infty } ^ { + \infty } A _ { n } ( z - a ) ^ { n } .
$$

The proof is extremely simple. All we have to show is that $f ( z )$ can be written as a sum $f _ { 1 } ( z ) + f _ { 2 } ( z )$ where $f _ { 1 } ( z )$ is analytic for $| z - a | < R _ { 2 }$ and $f _ { 2 } ( z )$ is analytic for $| z - a | > R _ { 1 }$ with a removable singularity at $\infty$ . Under these circumstances $f _ { 1 } ( z )$ can be developed in nonnegative powers of ${ z - a }$ ,and $f _ { 2 } ( z )$ can be developed in nonnegative powers of $1 / ( z - a )$ .

To find the representation $f ( z ) = f _ { 1 } ( z ) + f _ { 2 } ( z )$ define $f _ { 1 } ( z )$ by

$$
f _ { 1 } ( z ) = { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } } \int _ { | \xi - a | = r } { \frac { f ( \xi ) \ d \xi } { \xi \ - \ z } }
$$

for $| z - a | < r < R _ { 2 }$ and $f _ { 2 } ( z )$ by

$$
f _ { 2 } ( z ) = - \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } \int _ { | \xi - a | = r } \frac { f ( \xi ) d \xi } { \xi - z }
$$

for $R _ { 1 } < r < | z - a |$ .In both integrals the value of $\pmb { r }$ is irrelevant as long as the inequality is fulfilled, for it is an immediate consequence of Cauchy's theorem that the value of the integral does not change with $\boldsymbol { r }$ provided that the circle does not pass over the point $\pmb { z }$ , For this reason $f _ { 1 } ( z )$ and $f _ { 2 } ( z )$ are uniquely defined and represent analytic functions in $| z - a | < R _ { 2 }$ and $| z - a | > R _ { 1 }$ respectively. Moreover, by Cauchy's integral theorem $f ( z ) = f _ { 1 } ( z ) + f _ { 2 } ( z )$ .

The Taylor development of $f _ { 1 } ( z )$ is

$$
f _ { 1 } ( z ) = \sum _ { n = 0 } ^ { \infty } A _ { n } ( z - a ) ^ { n }
$$

with

$$
A _ { n } = { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } } \int _ { | \xi - a | = r } { \frac { f ( \xi ) d \xi } { ( \xi - a ) ^ { n + 1 } } } \cdot
$$

In order to find the development of $f _ { 2 } ( z )$ we perform the transformation $\zeta = a + 1 / \zeta ^ { \prime }$ , $z = a + 1 / z ^ { \prime }$ . This transformation carries $| \zeta - a | = r$ into $| \zeta ^ { \prime } | = 1 / r$ with negative orientation, and by simple calculations we obtain

$$
f _ { 2 } \left( a + { \frac { 1 } { z ^ { \prime } } } \right) = { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } } \int _ { | \xi ^ { \prime } | = { \frac { z ^ { \prime } } { \gamma } } } { \frac { f \left( a + { \frac { 1 } { \xi ^ { \prime } } } \right) d \xi ^ { \prime } } { \xi ^ { \prime } - z ^ { \prime } } } = \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { \infty } B _ { n } z ^ { \prime n }
$$

with

$$
B _ { n } = { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } } \int _ { | \xi ^ { \prime } | = { \frac { 1 } { r } } } { \frac { f \left( a + { \frac { 1 } { \xi ^ { \prime } } } \right) d \xi ^ { \prime } } { \xi ^ { \prime } { } ^ { n + 1 } } } = { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } } \int _ { | \xi - a | = r } f ( \zeta ) ( \zeta - a ) ^ { n - 1 } d \xi .
$$

This formula shows that we can write

$$
f ( z ) = \sum _ { n = - \infty } ^ { + \infty } A _ { n } ( z - a ) ^ { n }
$$

where all the coefficients $A _ { n }$ are determined by (3). Observe that the integral in (3) is independent of $r$ as long as $R _ { 1 } < r < R _ { 2 }$ .

If $R _ { 1 } = 0$ the point $^ { a }$ is an isolated singularity and $A _ { - 1 } = B _ { 1 }$ is the residue at $\pmb { a }$ , for $f ( z ) - A _ { - 1 } ( z - a ) ^ { - 1 }$ is the derivative of a single-valued function in $0 < | z - a | < R _ { 2 }$ .

# EXERCISES

1. Prove that the Laurent development is unique.

2. Let $\Omega$ be a doubly connected region whose complement consists of the components $E _ { 1 } , E _ { 2 }$ Prove that every analytic function $f ( z )$ in $\Omega$ can be written in the form $f _ { 1 } ( z ) + f _ { 2 } ( z )$ where $f _ { 1 } ( z )$ is analytic outside of $E _ { 1 }$ and $f _ { 2 } ( z )$ is analytic outside of $E _ { 2 }$ (The precise proof requires a construction like the one in Chap. 4, Sec. 4.2.)

$\clubsuit$ The expression

$$
\{ f , z \} = \frac { f ^ { \prime \prime \prime } ( z ) } { f ^ { \prime } ( z ) } - \frac { 3 } { 2 } \biggl ( \frac { f ^ { \prime \prime } ( z ) } { f ^ { \prime } ( z ) } \biggr ) ^ { 2 }
$$

is called the Schwarzian derivative of $f .$ If $f$ has a multiple zero or pole, find the leading term in the Laurent development of $\{ f , z \}$ . Answer: If $f ( z ) = a ( z - z _ { 0 } ) ^ { m } + \cdot \cdot \cdot .$ , then $\{ f , z \} = { \scriptstyle \frac { 1 } { 2 } } ( 1 - m ^ { 2 } ) ( z - z _ { 0 } ) ^ { - 2 } +$

4 Show that the Laurent development of $( e ^ { z } - 1 ) ^ { - 1 }$ at the origin is of the form

$$
\frac { 1 } { z } - \frac { 1 } { 2 } + \sum _ { 1 } ^ { \infty } ( - 1 ) ^ { k - 1 } \frac { B _ { k } } { ( 2 k ) ! } z ^ { 2 k - 1 }
$$

where the numbers $B _ { k }$ , known as the Bernoulli numbers, are all positive.   
Calculate $B _ { 1 } , B _ { 2 } , B _ { 3 }$ .

5.Express the Taylor development of tan $z$ and the Laurent development of cot $z$ in terms of the Bernoulli numbers.

# 2. PARTIAL FRACTIONS AND FACTORIZATION

A rational function has two standard representations, one by partial fractions and the other by factorization of the numerator and the denominator. The present section is devoted to similar representations of arbitrary meromorphic functions.

2.1. Partial Fractions. If the function $f ( z )$ is meromorphic in a region $\mathfrak { a }$ , there corresponds to each pole $^ { b _ { \nu } }$ a singular part of $f ( z )$ consisting of the part of the Laurent development which contains the negative powers of $z - b ,$ ; it reduces to a polynomial $P _ { \nu } ( 1 / ( z - b _ { \nu } ) )$ . It is tempting to subtract all singular parts in order to obtain a representation

$$
f ( z ) = \sum _ { \nu } P _ { \nu } \left( { \frac { 1 } { z - b _ { \nu } } } \right) + g ( z )
$$

where $g ( z )$ would be analytic in $\Omega$ However, the sum on the right-hand side is in general infinite, and there is no guarantee that the series will converge. Nevertheless, there are many cases in which the series converges, and what is more, it is frequently possible to determine $g ( z )$ explicitly from general considerations. In such cases the result is very rewarding; we obtain a simple expansion which is likely to be very helpful.

If the series in (4) does not converge, the method needs to be modified. It is clear that nothing essential is lost if we subtract an analytic function $p _ { \nu } ( z )$ from each singular part $\scriptstyle P _ { \nu }$ By judicious choice of the functions $\pmb { p _ { v } }$ the series $\sum _ { p } ( P _ { p } - p _ { \nu } )$ can be made convergent. It is even possible to take the ${ \pmb { \mathscr { p } } } _ { \nu } ( z )$ to be polynomials.

We shall not prove the most general theorem to this effect. In the case where $\Omega$ is the whole plane we shall, however, prove that every meromorphic function has a development in partial fractions and, moreover, that the singular parts can be described arbitrarily. The theorem and its generalization to arbitrary regions are due to Mittag-Leffler.

Theorem 4. Let $\left\{ b _ { \nu } \right\}$ be a sequence of complex numbers with $\operatorname* { l i m } \boldsymbol { b } _ { \nu } = \infty$ , −→ ∞ and let $P _ { \nu } ( \xi )$ be polynomials without constant term. Then there are functions which are meromorphic in the whole plane with poles at the points $^ { b \ast }$ and the corresponding singular parts $P _ { \nu } ( 1 / ( z - b _ { \nu } ) )$ . Moreover, the most general meromorphic function of this kind can be written in the form

$$
f ( z ) = \sum _ { \nu } \left[ P _ { \nu } \left( \frac { 1 } { z - b _ { \nu } } \right) - p _ { \nu } ( z ) \right] + g ( z )
$$

where the $p _ { \nu } ( z )$ are suitably chosen fixed polynomials and $g ( z )$ is analytic in the whole plane.

Since the function $P , ( 1 / ( z - b _ { v } ) )$ is analytic for $| z | < | b _ { \nu } | ;$ ,it can be expanded in a Taylor series about the origin. $^ \dagger$ We choose for $p _ { \nu } ( z )$ a partial sum of this series, ending, say, with the term of degree $\scriptstyle { \pmb { n } } _ { \pmb { \nu } }$ The difference $P _ { \nu } - p _ { \nu }$ can be estimated by use of the explicit expression for the remainder given in Chap. 4, Sec. 3.1. If $| P _ { \nu } | \leq { M } ,$ for $| z | \leqq | b _ { \nu } | / 2 ,$ . we obtain, for instance,

$$
\Big | P _ { \nu } \Big ( \frac { 1 } { z - b _ { \nu } } \Big ) - p _ { \nu } ( z ) \Big | \leq M _ { \nu } \Big ( \frac { 4 | z | } { | b _ { \nu } | } \Big ) ^ { n _ { \nu } + 1 }
$$

for $| z | \leq | b _ { \nu } | / 4$ . Because of this estimate it is clear that the series in the right-hand member of (5) can be made convergent by choosing the $\scriptstyle \mathbf { \mathscr { n } } _ { \nu }$ large enough. Specifically, using the formula for the radius of convergence we find that the power series

$$
\sum _ { \nu } M _ { \nu } \left( \frac { 4 z } { b _ { \nu } } \right) ^ { n \nu + 1 }
$$

converges in the whole plane if $\mathrm { l i m } \ M _ { \nu } ^ { 1 / n _ { \nu } } / | b _ { \nu } | = 0$ ; this is assured by choosing, for instance, $n _ { \nu } > \log M _ { \nu }$ .

Coniderry c $| z | \leq R$ The series ∑ (P, - p) has only a finite number of terms which become infinite in $| z | \leq R$ ,and from a certain term on the inequality (6) will hold throughout the disk. If the terms with $\left| b _ { \nu } \right| \leqq R$ are omitted, it follows that the remaining series converges absolutely and uniformly in $| z | \leq R$ .Since $R$ is arbitrary, the series converges for all $z \neq b _ { \nu }$ and represents a meromorphic function in the whole plane. It is obvious that the singular parts are $P _ { \nu } ( 1 / ( z - b _ { \nu } ) )$ , and the rest of the theorem follows trivially.

As a first example we consider the function $\pi ^ { 2 } / \sin ^ { 2 } \pi z$ which has double poles at the points $z = n$ for integral $\pmb { n }$ . The singular part at the origin is $1 / z ^ { 2 }$ ,and since $\sin ^ { 2 } \pi ( z - n ) = \sin ^ { 2 } \pi z$ the singular part at $z = n$ is $1 / ( z - n ) ^ { 2 }$ The series

$$
\sum _ { n = - \infty } ^ { + \infty } \frac { 1 } { ( z - n ) ^ { 2 } }
$$

is convergent for $z \neq n$ , as seen by comparison with the familiar series $\sum _ { 1 } ^ { \infty } 1 / n ^ { 2 } .$

We suppose, for simplicity, that no $\scriptstyle \delta _ { \nu }$ equals zero.

the terms which become infinite on the set. For this reason we can write

$$
\frac { \pi ^ { 2 } } { \sin ^ { 2 } \pi z } = \displaystyle \sum _ { n = - \infty } ^ { + \infty } \frac { 1 } { ( z - n ) ^ { 2 } } + g ( z )
$$

where $g ( z )$ is analytic in the whole plane. We contend that $g ( z )$ is identically zero.

To prove this we observe that the function $\pi ^ { 2 } / \sin ^ { 2 } \pi z$ and the series (7) are both periodic with the period 1. Therefore the function $g ( z )$ has the same period. For $z = x + i y$ we have (Chap. 2, Sec. 3.2, Ex. 4)

$$
| \sin \pi z | ^ { 2 } = \cosh ^ { 2 } y - \cos ^ { 2 } x
$$

and hence $\pi ^ { 2 } / \sin ^ { 2 } \pi z$ tends uniformly to 0 as $| y | \to \infty$ . But it is easy to see that the function (7) has the same property. Indeed. the convergence is uniform for $| y | \geq 1$ , say, and the limit for $| y |  \infty$ can thus be obtained by taking the limit in each term. We conclude that $g ( z )$ tends uniformly to 0 for $| y |  \infty$ . This is sufficient to infer that $\vert g ( z ) \vert$ is bounded in a period strip $0 \leq x \leq 1$ , and because of the periodicity $| g ( z ) \}$ will be bounded in the whole plane. By Liouville's theorem $g ( z )$ must reduce to a constant, and since the limit is 0 the constant must vanish. We have thus proved the identity

$$
\frac { \pi ^ { 2 } } { \sin ^ { 2 } \pi z } = \sum _ { - \infty } ^ { \infty } \frac { 1 } { ( z - n ) ^ { 2 } } \cdot
$$

From this equation a related identity can be obtained by integration. The left-hand member is the derivative of $- \pi \cot \pi z$ , and the terms on the right are derivatives of $- 1 / ( z - n )$ . The series with the general term $1 / ( z - n )$ diverges, and a partial sum of the Taylor series must be subtracted from all the terms with $\varkappa \neq 0$ . As it happens it is sufficient to subtract the constant terms, for the series

$$
\displaystyle \sum _ { n \ne 0 } \left( \frac { 1 } { z - n } + \frac { 1 } { n } \right) = \sum _ { n \ne 0 } \frac { z } { n ( z - n ) }
$$

is comparable with $\sum _ { 1 } ^ { \infty } 1 / n ^ { 2 }$ and hence convergent. The convergence is uniform on every compact set, provided that we omit the terms which become infinite. For this reason termwise differentiation is permissible, and we obtain

$$
\pi \cot \pi z = { \frac { 1 } { z } } + \sum _ { n \neq 0 } \left( { \frac { 1 } { z - n } } + { \frac { 1 } { n } } \right)
$$

except for an additive constant. If the terms corresponding to $\pmb { n }$ and - are bracketed together, (10) can be written in the equivalent forms

$$
\pi \cot \pi z = \operatorname* { l i m } _ { m \to \infty } \sum _ { n = - m } ^ { m } { \frac { 1 } { z - n } } = { \frac { 1 } { z } } + \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { \infty } { \frac { 2 z } { z ^ { 2 } - n ^ { 2 } } } \cdotp
$$

With this way of writing it becomes evident that both members of $\mathbf { t }$ . equation are odd functions of $z _ { i }$ and for this reason the integration $\mathbf { c o n }$ . stant must vanish. The equations (10) and (11) are thus corrertl stated.

Let us now reverse the procedure and try to evaluate the analogou' sum

$$
\operatorname * { l i m } _ { m  \infty } \sum _ { - m } ^ { m } { \frac { ( - 1 ) ^ { n } } { z - n } } = { \frac { 1 } { z } } + \sum _ { 1 } ^ { \infty } ( - 1 ) ^ { n } { \frac { 2 z } { z ^ { 2 } - n ^ { 2 } } }
$$

which evidently represents a meromorphic function. It is very natur: to separate the odd and even terms and write

$$
\sum _ { - ( 2 k + 1 ) } ^ { 2 k + 1 } { \frac { ( - 1 ) ^ { n } } { z - n } } = \sum _ { n = - k } ^ { k } { \frac { 1 } { z - 2 n } } - \sum _ { n = - k - 1 } ^ { k } { \frac { 1 } { z - 1 - 2 n } } .
$$

By comparison with (11) we find that the limit is

$$
\frac { \pi } { 2 } \cot \frac { \pi z } { 2 } - \frac { \pi } { 2 } \cot \frac { \pi ( z - 1 ) } { 2 } = \frac { \pi } { \sin \pi z } ,
$$

and we have proved that

$$
{ \frac { \pi } { \sin \pi z } } = \operatorname* { l i m } _ { m  \infty } \sum _ { - m } ^ { m } ( - 1 ) ^ { n } { \frac { 1 } { z - n } } \cdot
$$

Give a complete justification of the steps that are needed.

$$
\sum _ { - \infty } ^ { \infty } \frac { 1 } { ( z + n ) ^ { 2 } + a ^ { 2 } } ?

2.2. Infinite Products. An infinite product of complex numbers

$$
p _ { 1 } p _ { 2 } \cdot \cdot \cdot p _ { n } \cdot \cdot \cdot = \prod _ { n = 1 } ^ { \infty } p _ { n }
$$

is evaluated by taking the limit of the partial products $P _ { n } = p _ { 1 } p _ { 2 } \cdots p _ { n } .$ It is said to converge to the value $P = \operatorname* { l i m } _ { n \to \infty } P _ { n }$ if this limit exists and is different from zero. There are good reasons for excluding the value zero. For one thing, if the value $P = 0$ were permitted, any infinite product with one factor 0 would converge, and the convergence would not depend on the whole sequence of factors. On the other hand, in certain connections this convention is too radical. In fact, we wish to express a function as an infinite product, and this must be possible even if the function has zeros. For this reason we make the following agreement: The infinite product (14) is said to converge if and only if at most a finite number of the factors are zero, and if the partial products formed by the nonvanishing factors tend to a finite limit which is different from zero.

In a convergent product the general factor ${ \pmb p } _ { \pmb n }$ tends to 1; this is clear by writing $p _ { n } = P _ { n } / P _ { n - 1 } ,$ the zero factors being omitted. In view of this fact it is preferable to write all infinite products in the form

$$
\prod _ { n = 1 } ^ { \infty } \left( 1 + a _ { n } \right)
$$

so that $\pmb { a } _ { n }  \mathbf { 0 }$ is a necessary condition for convergence.

If no factor is zero, it is natural to compare the product (15) with the infinite series

$$
\sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { \infty } \log { ( 1 + a _ { n } ) } .
$$

Since the $\scriptstyle a _ { n }$ are complex we must agree on a definite branch of the logarithms, and we decide to choose the principal branch in each term. Denote the partial sums of (16) by $S _ { n }$ Then $P _ { n } = e ^ { S _ { n } }$ , and if $S _ { n } \to S$ it follows that $P _ { n }$ tends to the limit $P = e ^ { s }$ which is $\neq { \bf 0 }$ In other words, the convergence of (16) is a sufficient condition for the convergence of (15).

In order to prove that the condition is also necessary, suppose that $P _ { n } \longrightarrow P \ne 0$ , and choose a fixed value of log $P$ , for instance the value of the principal branch. With the corresponding value of arg $P$ determine $\mathrm { a r g } P _ { n }$ by the condition $\arg P - \pi < \arg P _ { n } \leq \arg P + \pi ,$ and set log $P _ { n } = \log | P _ { n } | + i \arg P _ { n }$ We know that $S _ { n } = \log P _ { n } + h _ { n } \cdot 2 \pi i _ { 1 }$ . where $h _ { n }$ is a well-determined integer. For two consecutive terms we obtain

$$
( h _ { n + 1 } - h _ { n } ) 2 \pi i = \log { ( 1 + a _ { n + 1 } ) } + \log { P _ { n } } - \log { P _ { n + 1 } } .
$$

We need pay attention only to the imaginary parts. As $_ n$ is sufficiently large we have for instance $| \arg { ( 1 + a _ { n + 1 } ) } | < 2 \pi / 3$ a $\arg P _ { n } - \arg P |$ $< 2 \pi / 3 ,$ and $| \arg P _ { n + 1 } - \arg P | < 2 \pi / 3$ These inequalities imply $| h _ { n + 1 } - h _ { n } | < 1$ , and thus we conclude that $h _ { n + 1 } = h _ { n }$ for all sufficiently large $\pmb { n }$ Ultimately $h _ { n }$ is therefore constantly equal to a certain integer $h _ { i }$ ,and we find that $S _ { n }$ tends to the limit $S = \log P + h \cdot 2 \pi i$ We have proved:

Theorem 5. The infinite product $\prod _ { 1 } ^ { \infty } { ( 1 + a _ { n } ) }$ with $1 + a _ { n } \not \approx 0$ converges simultaneously with the series $\sum _ { 1 } ^ { \infty } \log { ( 1 + a _ { n } ) }$ whose terms represent the values of the principal branch of the logarithm.

The question of convergence of a product can thus be reduced to the more familiar question concerning the convergence of a series. It can be further reduced by observing that the series (16) converges absolutely at the same time as the simpler series $\Sigma \left| a _ { n } \right|$ . This is an immediate consequence of the fact that

$$
\operatorname* { l i m } _ { z \to 0 } { \frac { \log { ( 1 + z ) } } { z } } = 1 .
$$

If either the series (16) or $\sum _ { 1 } ^ { \infty } \left| a _ { n } \right|$ converges, we have $\pmb { a } _ { n }  \mathbf { 0 }$ , and for a given $\varepsilon > 0$ the double inequality

$$
( 1 - \mathfrak { s } ) | a _ { n } | < | \log { ( 1 + a _ { n } ) } | < ( 1 + \mathfrak { s } ) | a _ { n } |
$$

will hold for all sufficiently large $\mathbfit { \Delta } n$ It follows immediately that the two series are in fact simultaneously absolutely convergent.

An infinite product is said to be absolutely convergent if and only if the corresponding series (16) converges absolutely. With this terminology we can state our result in the following terms:

Theorem 6. A necessary and suficient condition for the absolute convergence of the product $\prod _ { 1 } ^ { \infty } { ( 1 + a _ { n } ) }$ is the conergence of the series $\sum _ { 1 } ^ { \infty } \left| a _ { n } \right.$ .

In the last theorem the emphasis is on absolute convergence. By simple examples it can be shown that the convergence of $\sum _ { 1 } ^ { \infty } a _ { n }$ is neither sufficient nor necessary for the convergence of the product $\prod _ { 1 } ^ { \infty } { \big ( } 1 + a _ { n } { \big ) } .$ .

It is clear what to understand by a uniformly convergent infinite product whose factors are functions of a variable. The presence of zeros may cause some slight difficulties which can usually be avoided by considering only sets on which at most a finite number of the factors can vanish. If these factors are omitted, it is sufficient to study the uniform convergence of the remaining product. Theorems 5 and 6 have obvious counterparts for uniform convergence. If we examine the proofs, we find that all estimates can be made uniform, and the conclusions lead to uniform convergence, at least on compact sets.

1. Show that

\prod _ { n = 2 } ^ { \infty } \left( 1 - { \frac { 1 } { n ^ { 2 } } } \right) = { \frac { 1 } { 2 } } .

Prove that for $| z | < 1$

$$
( 1 + z ) ( 1 + z ^ { 2 } ) ( 1 + z ^ { 4 } ) ( 1 + z ^ { 8 } ) \cdot \cdot \cdot = { \frac { 1 } { 1 - z } } .
$$

$$
\prod _ { 1 } ^ { \infty } \left( 1 + { \frac { z } { n } } \right) e ^ { - z / n }
$$

converges absolutely and uniformly on every compact set.

4Prove that the value of an absolutely convergent product does not change if the factors are reordered.

Show that the function

$$
\theta ( z ) = \prod _ { \scriptstyle 1 \atop \frac { 1 } { 2 } } ^ { \infty } ( 1 + h ^ { 2 n - 1 } e ^ { z } ) ( 1 + h ^ { 2 n - 1 } e ^ { - z } )
$$

where $| h | < 1$ is analytic in the whole plane and satisfies the functional equation

$$
\theta ( z + 2 \log h ) = h ^ { - 1 } e ^ { - z } \theta ( z ) .
$$

2.3. Canonical Products. A function which is analytic in the whole plane is said to be entire, or integral. The simplest entire functions which are not polynomials are $e ^ { z }$ , sin $\pmb { z }$ , and cos $\pmb { z }$ .

If $g ( z )$ is an entire function, then $f ( z ) = e ^ { \theta ( z ) }$ is entire and $\yen 0$ Conversely, if $f ( z )$ is any entire function which is never zero, let us show that $f ( z )$ is of the form $e ^ { \pmb { \sigma } ^ { ( z ) } }$ . To this end we observe that the function $f ^ { \prime } ( z ) / f ( z )$ , being analytic in the whole plane, is the derivative of an entire function $g ( z )$ From this fact we infer, by computation, that $f ( z ) e ^ { - \theta ( z ) }$ has the derivative zero, and hence $f ( z )$ is a constant multiple of $e ^ { \pmb { \sigma } ^ { ( z ) } }$ ; the constant can be absorbed in $g ( z )$ .

By this method we can also find the most general entire function with a finite number of zeros. Assume that $f ( z )$ has $\pmb { m }$ zeros at the origin $\mathbf { \dot { \mathbf { \psi } } } _ { m }$ may be zero), and denote the other zeros by $a _ { 1 } , a _ { 2 } , \ldots , a _ { N } ,$ ,multiple zeros being repeated. It is then plain that we can write

$$
f ( z ) = z ^ { m } e ^ { \theta ^ { ( z ) } } \prod _ { 1 } ^ { N } { \biggl ( } 1 - { \frac { z } { a _ { n } } } { \biggr ) } \cdotp
$$

If there are infinitely many zeros, we can try to obtain a similar representation by means of an infinite product. The obvious generalization would be

$$
f ( z ) = z ^ { m } e ^ { \theta ^ { ( z ) } } \prod _ { 1 } ^ { \infty } { \biggl ( } 1 - { \frac { z } { a _ { n } } } { \biggr ) } \cdotp
$$

This representation is valid if the infinite product converges uniformly on every compact set. In fact, if this is so the product represents an entire function with zeros at the same point (except for the origin) and with the same multiplicities as $f ( z )$ . It follows that the quotient can be written in the form $z ^ { m } e ^ { \pmb { \theta } ^ { ( z ) } }$ .

The product in (17) converges absolutely if and only f $\sum _ { 1 } ^ { \infty } 1 / | a _ { n } |$ is convergent, and in this case the convergence is also uniform in every closed disk $| z | \leq R$ It is only under this special condition that we can obtain a representation of the form (17).

In the general case convergence-producing factors must be introduced. We consider an arbitrary sequence of complex numbers $a _ { n } \neq 0$ with lim $a _ { n } = \infty$ , and prove the existence of polynomials $p _ { n } ( z )$ such that n→∞

$$
\prod _ { 1 } ^ { \infty } \left( 1 - { \frac { z } { a _ { n } } } \right) e ^ { p _ { n } ( z ) }
$$

converges to an entire function. The product converges together with the series with the general term

$$
r _ { n } ( z ) = \log \left( 1 - \frac { z } { a _ { n } } \right) + p _ { n } ( z )
$$

where the branch o the logarithm shall be chosen o that the magnary part of ${ \pmb r } _ { n } ( z )$ lies between $- \pi$ and $\pmb { \pi }$ (inclusive).

For a given $\pmb { R }$ we consider only the terms with $\left. a _ { n } \right. > R$ In the disk $| z | \leq R$ the principal branch of lo $\textrm { g } ( 1 - z / a _ { n } )$ can be developed in a Taylor series

$$
\log \left( 1 - { \frac { z } { a _ { n } } } \right) = - { \frac { z } { a _ { n } } } - { \frac { 1 } { 2 } } { \bigg ( } { \frac { z } { a _ { n } } } { \bigg ) } ^ { 2 } - { \frac { 1 } { 3 } } { \bigg ( } { \frac { z } { a _ { n } } } { \bigg ) } ^ { 3 } - \cdot \cdot \cdot .
$$

We reverse the signs and choose $p _ { n } ( z )$ as a partial sum

$$
p _ { n } ( z ) = { \frac { z } { a _ { n } } } + { \frac { 1 } { 2 } } \left( { \frac { z } { a _ { n } } } \right) ^ { 2 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + { \frac { 1 } { m _ { n } } } \left( { \frac { z } { a _ { n } } } \right) ^ { m _ { n } } .
$$

Then $\pmb { r _ { n } } ( z )$ has the representation

$$
r _ { n } ( z ) = \frac { 1 } { m _ { n } + 1 } \left( \frac { z } { a _ { n } } \right) ^ { m _ { n } + 1 } - \frac { 1 } { m _ { n } + 2 } \left( \frac { z } { a _ { n } } \right) ^ { m _ { n } + 2 } - \cdot \cdot \cdot
$$

and we obtain easily the estimate

$$
| r _ { n } ( z ) | \leq { \frac { 1 } { m _ { n } + 1 } } \left( { \frac { R } { | a _ { n } | } } \right) ^ { m _ { n } + 1 } \left( 1 - { \frac { R } { | a _ { n } | } } \right) ^ { - 1 } .
$$

Suppose now that the series

$$
\sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { \infty } { \frac { 1 } { m _ { n } + 1 } } \left( { \frac { R } { \left| a _ { n } \right| } } \right) ^ { m _ { n } + 1 }
$$

converges. By the estimate (19) it follows first that ${ r _ { n } ( z ) \to 0 }$ ,and hence ${ \pmb r } _ { { \pmb n } } ( z )$ has an imaginary part between $- \pi$ and $\pmb { \pi }$ as soon as $\pmb { n }$ is sufficiently large. Moreover, the comparison shows that the series $\Sigma r _ { n } ( z )$ is absolutely and uniformly convergent for $| z | \leq R _ { \mathrm { i } }$ , and thus the product (18) represents an analytic function in $| z | < R$ For the sake of the reasoning we had to exclude the values $\left| a _ { n } \right| \leqq R ,$ , but it is clear that the uniform convergence of (18) is not affected when the corresponding factors are again taken into account.

It remains only to show that the series (20) can be made convergent for all $\pmb { R }$ But this is obvious, for if we take ${ \mathfrak { m } } _ { n } = n$ , (20) becomes a power series with an infinite radius of convergence as seen either by use of the formula for the radius of convergence or by consideration of a majorant geometric series for any fixed value of $R$ .

Theorem 7. There exists an entire function with arbitrarily prescribed zeros ${ \pmb a } _ { n }$ provided that, in the case of infinitely many zeros, $a _ { n } \to \infty$ . Every entire function with these and no other zeros can be written in the form

$$
f ( z ) = z ^ { m } e ^ { v ( z ) } \prod _ { n = 1 } ^ { \infty } \bigg ( 1 - \frac { z } { a _ { n } } \bigg ) e ^ { \frac { z } { a _ { n } } + \frac { 1 } { 2 } \left( \frac { z } { a _ { n } } \right) ^ { 3 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + \frac { 1 } { m _ { n } } \left( \frac { z } { a _ { n } } \right) ^ { m } n }
$$

where the product is taken over all $a _ { n } \neq 0$ , the $m _ { n }$ are certain integers, and $g ( z )$ is an entire function.

This theorem is due to Weierstrass. It has the following important corollary:

Corollary. Every function which is meromorphic in the whole plane is the quotient of two entire functions.

In fact, if $F ( z )$ is meromorphic in the whole plane, we can find an entire function $g ( z )$ with the poles of $F ( z )$ for zeros. The product $F ( \mathfrak { z } ) g ( \mathfrak { z } )$ is then an entire function $f ( z )$ , and we obtain $F ( z ) = f ( z ) / g ( z )$ .

The representation (21) becomes considerably more interesting if it is possible to choose all the ${ \pmb m } _ { \pmb n }$ equal to each other. The preceding proof has shown that the product

$$
\prod _ { 1 } ^ { \infty } \left( 1 - { \frac { z } { a _ { n } } } \right) e ^ { { \frac { z } { a _ { n } } } + { \frac { 1 } { 2 } } \left( { \frac { z } { a _ { n } } } \right) ^ { 2 } + \dots + { \frac { 1 } { h } } \left( { \frac { z } { a _ { n } } } \right) ^ { h } }
$$

converges and represents an entire function provided that the series $\sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { \infty } \ ( R / | a _ { n } | ) ^ { h + 1 } / ( h + 1 )$ converges for all $R$ that i t t saovided that $\Sigma 1 / | a _ { n } | ^ { h + 1 } < \infty$ , Assume that $h$ is the smallest integer for which this series converges; the expression (22) is then called the canonical product associated with the sequence $\left\{ a _ { n } \right\}$ , and $\pmb { h }$ is the genus of the canonical product.

Whenever possible we use the canonical product in the representation (21), which is thereby uniquely determined. If in this representation $g ( z )$ reduces to a polynomial, the function $f ( z )$ is said to be of finite genus, and the genus of $f ( z )$ is by definition equal to the degree of this polynomial or to the genus of the canonical product, whichever is the larger. For instance, an entire function of genus zero is of the form

$$
C z ^ { m } \prod _ { 1 } ^ { \infty } \left( 1 - { \frac { z } { a _ { n } } } \right)
$$

with $\Sigma 1 / | a _ { n } | < \infty$ . The canonical representation of an entire function of genus 1 is either of the form

$$
C z ^ { m } e ^ { \alpha z } \prod _ { 1 } ^ { \infty } \left( 1 - \frac { z } { a _ { n } } \right) e ^ { z / a _ { n } }
$$

with $\Sigma 1 / | a _ { n } | ^ { 2 } < \infty , \Sigma 1 / | a _ { n } | = \infty$ , or of the form

$$
C z ^ { m } e ^ { \alpha z } \prod _ { 1 } ^ { \infty } \left( 1 - \frac { z } { a _ { n } } \right)
$$

with $\Sigma 1 / | a _ { n } | < \infty$ , $\alpha \neq 0$ .

As an application we consider the product representation of $\sin \pi z$ . The zeros are the integers $z = \pm n$ Since $\Sigma 1 / n$ diverges and $\scriptstyle \pmb { \Sigma } \mathbf { 1 } / n ^ { 2 }$ converges, we must take $h = 1$ and obtain a representation of the form

$$
\sin \pi z = z e ^ { \sigma ( z ) } \prod _ { n \ne 0 } \left( 1 - \frac { z } { n } \right) e ^ { \pi / n } .
$$

In order to determine $g ( z )$ we form the logarithmic derivatives on both sides. We find

$$
\pi \cot \pi z = { \frac { 1 } { z } } + g ^ { \prime } ( z ) + \sum _ { n \varkappa 0 } \left( { \frac { 1 } { z - n } } + { \frac { 1 } { n } } \right)
$$

where the procedure is easy to justify by uniform convergence on any compact set which does not contain the points $z = n$ By comparison with the previous formula (10) we conclude that $g ^ { \prime } ( z ) = 0$ Hence $g ( z )$ is a constant, and since lim sin $\pi z / z = \pi$ we must have $e ^ { \theta ^ { ( z ) } } = \pi ,$ , and thus z→0

$$
\sin \pi z = \pi z \prod _ { n \neq 0 } \left( 1 - { \frac { z } { n } } \right) e ^ { z / n } .
$$

In this representation the factors corresponding to $\boldsymbol { n }$ and $- n$ can be bracketed together, and we obtain the simple form

$$
\sin \pi z = \pi z \prod _ { 1 } ^ { \infty } \left( 1 - { \frac { z ^ { 2 } } { n ^ { 2 } } } \right) \cdot
$$

It follows from (23) that sin $\pmb { \pi } \pmb { \mathcal { z } }$ is an entire function of genus 1.

1. Suppose that $a _ { n } \to \infty$ and that the $A _ { n }$ are arbitrary complex numbers. Show that there exists an entire function $f ( z )$ which satisfies $f ( a _ { n } ) = A _ { n }$ .

Hint: Let $g ( z )$ be a function with simple zeros at the $a _ { n }$ Show that

$$
\sum _ { 1 } ^ { \infty } g ( z ) { \frac { e ^ { \gamma _ { n } ( z - a _ { n } ) } } { z - a _ { n } } } \cdot { \frac { A _ { n } } { g ^ { \prime } ( a _ { n } ) } }
$$

converges for some choice of the numbers $\gamma _ { n }$

. Prove that

$$
\sin \pi ( z + \alpha ) = e ^ { \pi z \cot \pi \alpha } \prod _ { - \infty } ^ { \infty } \left( 1 + { \frac { z } { n + \alpha } } \right) e ^ { - z / ( n + \alpha ) }
$$

whenever $\pmb { \alpha }$ is not an integer. Hint: Denote the factor in front of the canonical product by $g ( z )$ and determine $g ^ { \prime } ( z ) / g ( z )$ .

$\pmb { \hat { \mathrm { \dots } } }$ What is the genus of cos $\sqrt { z ! }$ $^ { 4 }$ If $f ( z )$ is of genus $\pmb { h }$ , how large and how small can the genus of $f ( z ^ { 2 } )$

2.4. The Gamma Function. The function sin $\pi z$ has all the integers for zeros, and it is the simplest function with this property. We shall now introduce functions which have only the positive or only the negative integers for zeros. The simplest function with, for instance, the negative integers for zeros is the corresponding canonical product

$$
G ( z ) = \prod _ { 1 } ^ { \infty } \left( 1 + { \frac { z } { n } } \right) e ^ { - z / n } .
$$

It is evident that $G ( - z )$ has then the positive integers for zeros, and by comparison with the product representation (23) of $\sin \pi z$ we find at once

$$
z G ( z ) G ( - z ) = { \frac { \sin \pi z } { \pi } } \cdotp
$$

Because of the manner in which $G ( z )$ has been constructed, it is bound to have other simple properties. We observe that $G ( z - 1 )$ has the same zeros as $G ( z )$ , and in addition a zero at the origin. It is therefore clear that we can write

$$
G ( z - 1 ) = z e ^ { \gamma ( z ) } { \cal G } ( z ) ,
$$

where ${ \pmb \gamma } ( z )$ is an entire function. In order to determine ${ \pmb \gamma } ( { \pmb z } )$ we take the logarithmic derivatives on both sides. This gives the equation

$$
\sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { \infty } { \biggl ( } { \frac { 1 } { z - 1 + n } } - { \frac { 1 } { n } } { \biggr ) } = { \frac { 1 } { z } } + \gamma ^ { \prime } ( z ) + \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { \infty } { \biggl ( } { \frac { 1 } { z + n } } - { \frac { 1 } { n } } { \biggr ) } .
$$

In the series to the left we can replace $\boldsymbol { n }$ by $n + 1$ By this change we obtain

$$
\begin{array} { c } { { \displaystyle \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { \infty } \left( \frac { 1 } { z - 1 + n } - \frac { 1 } { n } \right) = \frac { 1 } { \bar { z } } - 1 + \displaystyle \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { \infty } \left( \displaystyle \sum _ { z } \frac { 1 } { + n } - \frac { 1 } { n + 1 } \right) } } \\ { { = \displaystyle \frac { 1 } { z } - 1 + \displaystyle \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { \infty } \left( \frac { 1 } { z + n } - \frac { 1 } { n } \right) + \displaystyle \sum _ { n = 1 } ^ { \infty } \left( \displaystyle \frac { 1 } { n } - \frac { 1 } { n + 1 } \right) . } } \end{array}
$$

The last series has the sum 1, and hence equation (27) reduces to $\gamma ^ { \prime } ( z ) = 0$ . Thus $\gamma ( z )$ is a constant, which we denote by $\pmb { \gamma } _ { : }$ , and $G ( z )$ has the reproductive property $G ( z - 1 ) = e ^ { \gamma } z G ( z )$ . It is somewhat simpler to consider the function $H ( z ) = G ( z ) e ^ { \gamma z }$ which evidently satisfies the functional equation $H ( z - 1 ) = z H ( z )$ .

The value of $\pmb { \gamma }$ is easily determined. Taking $z = 1$ we have

$$
\mathbf { 1 } = G ( \mathbf { 0 } ) = e ^ { \boldsymbol { r } } G ( \mathbf { 1 } ) ,
$$

and hence

$$
e ^ { - \gamma } = \prod _ { n = 1 } ^ { \infty } \left( 1 + { \frac { 1 } { n } } \right) e ^ { - 1 / n } .
$$

Here the nth partial product can be written in the form

$$
( n + 1 ) e ^ { - ( 1 + \vdots + \vdots + \cdot \cdot + 1 / n ) } ,
$$

and we obtain

$$
\gamma = \operatorname* { l i m } _ { n \to \infty } { \biggl ( } 1 + { \frac { 1 } { 2 } } + { \frac { 1 } { 3 } } + \cdot \cdot + { \frac { 1 } { n } } - \log n { \biggr ) } .
$$

The constant $\pmb { \gamma }$ is called Euler's constant; its approximate value is .57722.

If $H ( z )$ satisfies $H ( z - 1 ) = z H ( z )$ ,then $\Gamma ( z ) = 1 / [ z H ( z ) ]$ satisfies $\mathrm { ~ I ~ } ( z - 1 ) = \Gamma ( z ) / ( z - 1 )$ , or

$$
\Gamma ( z + 1 ) = z \Gamma ( z ) .
$$

This is found to be a more useful relation, and for this reason it has become customary to implement the restricted stock of elementary functions by inclusion of $\Gamma ( z )$ under the name of Euler's gamma function.

Our definition leads to the explicit representation

$$
\Gamma ( z ) = { \frac { e ^ { - \tau z } } { z } } \prod _ { n = 1 } ^ { \infty } \left( 1 + { \frac { z } { n } } \right) ^ { - 1 } e ^ { \tau / n } ,
$$

and the formula (26) takes the form

$$
\Gamma ( z ) \Gamma ( 1 - z ) = \frac { \pi } { \sin \pi z } \cdot
$$

We observe that $\Gamma ( z )$ is a meromorphic function with poles at ${ \pmb z } = { \pmb 0 } _ { \mathrm { i } }$ $- 1 , - 2 ,$ . . . but without zeros.

We have $\mathbf { \Gamma } \Gamma ( 1 ) = \mathbf { 1 }$ , and by the functional equation we find $\mathbf { \Gamma } \mathbf { { r } } ( 2 ) = \mathbf { 1 } ,$ $\Gamma ( 3 ) = 1 \cdot 2$ $\Gamma ( 4 ) = { \bf 1 } \cdot { \bf 2 } \cdot { \bf 3 }$ and generally $\Gamma ( n ) = ( n - 1 ) !$ The $\mathbf { r }$ -function can thus be considered as a generalization of the factorial. From (30) we conclude that $\Gamma ( { \textstyle { \frac { 1 } { 2 } } } ) = { \mathcal { V } } \pi$ .

Other properties are most easily found by considering the second derivative of log $\Gamma ( z )$ for which we find, by (29), the very simple expression

$$
{ \frac { d } { d z } } \left( { \frac { \Gamma ^ { \prime } ( z ) } { \Gamma ( z ) } } \right) = \sum _ { n = 0 } ^ { \infty } { \frac { 1 } { ( z + n ) ^ { 2 } } } \cdot
$$

For instance, it is plain that $\Gamma ( z ) \ \Gamma ( z + { \frac { 1 } { 2 } } )$ and $\Gamma ( 2 z )$ have the same poles, and by use of (31) we find indeed that

$$
\begin{array} { r l r } {  { \frac { d } { d z } \Big ( \frac { \Gamma ^ { \prime } ( z ) } { \Gamma ( z ) } \Big ) + \frac { d } { d z } \Big ( \frac { \Gamma ^ { \prime } ( z + \frac { 1 } { z } ) } { \Gamma ( z + \frac { 1 } { z } ) } \Big ) = \displaystyle \sum _ { n = 0 } ^ { \infty } \frac { 1 } { ( z + n ) ^ { 2 } } + \sum _ { n = 0 } ^ { \infty } \frac { 1 } { ( z + n + \frac { 1 } { z } ) ^ { 2 } } } } \\ & { } & { = 4 \Big [ \sum _ { n = 0 } ^ { \infty } \frac { 1 } { ( 2 z + 2 n ) ^ { 2 } } + \sum _ { n = 0 } ^ { \infty } \frac { 1 } { ( 2 z + 2 n + 1 ) ^ { 2 } } \Big ] = 4 \sum _ { m = 0 } ^ { \infty } \frac { 1 } { ( 2 z + m ) ^ { 2 } } } \\ & { } & { = 2 \frac { d } { d z } \Big ( \frac { \Gamma ^ { \prime } ( 2 z ) } { \Gamma ( 2 z ) } \Big ) . } \end{array}
$$

By integration we obtain

$$
\Gamma ( z ) \Gamma ( z + \textstyle { \frac { 1 } { 2 } } ) = e ^ { a z + b } \Gamma ( 2 z ) ,
$$

where the constants $^ { a }$ and $^ { b }$ have yet to be determined. Substituting $z = { \textstyle \frac { 1 } { 2 } } \mathrm { a n d } z = 1$ we make use of the known values $\Gamma ( _ { \bar { z } } ^ { 1 } ) = \sqrt { \pi } , \Gamma ( 1 ) = 1 _ { : }$ $\Gamma ( 1 _ { \bar { 2 } } ^ { \bar { 1 } } ) = { \scriptstyle \frac { 1 } { 2 } } \Gamma ( { \scriptstyle \frac { 1 } { 2 } } ) = { \scriptstyle \frac { 1 } { 2 } } \sqrt { \pi } , \Gamma ( 2 ) = 1$ and are led to the relations

$$
{ \scriptstyle { \frac { 1 } { 2 } } } a + b = { \scriptstyle { \frac { 1 } { 2 } } } \log \pi , \quad \quad a + b = { \scriptstyle { \frac { 1 } { 2 } } } \log \pi - \log 2 .
$$

It follows that

$$
a = - 2 \log 2 \qquad { \mathrm { a n d } } \qquad b = { \textstyle { \frac { 1 } { 2 } } } \log \pi + \log 2 ;
$$

the final result is thus

$$
{ \sqrt { \pi } } \Gamma ( 2 z ) = 2 ^ { z z - 1 } \Gamma ( z ) \Gamma ( z + { \textstyle \frac { 1 } { 2 } } )
$$

which is known as Legendre's duplication formula.

1Prove the formula of Gauss:

$$
( 2 \pi ) ^ { \frac { n - 1 } { 2 } } \Gamma ( z ) = n ^ { z - 1 } \Gamma \left( { \frac { z } { n } } \right) \Gamma \left( { \frac { z + 1 } { n } } \right) \cdot \cdot \cdot \Gamma \left( { \frac { z + n - 1 } { n } } \right) .
$$

Show that

$$
\Gamma \left( { \frac { 1 } { 6 } } \right) = 2 ^ { - 1 } \left( { \frac { 3 } { \pi } } \right) ^ { \frac { 1 } { 3 } } \Gamma \left( { \frac { 1 } { 3 } } \right) ^ { 2 } .
$$

What are the residues of $\Gamma ( z )$ at the poles $z = - n ?$

2.5. Stirling's Formula. In most connections where the $\mathbf { r }$ function can be applied, it is of utmost importance to have some information on the behavior of $\Gamma ( z )$ for very large values of $\pmb { z }$ . Fortunately, it is possible to calculate $\Gamma ( z )$ with great precision and very little effort by means of a classical formula which goes under the name of Stirling's formula. There are many proofs of this formula. We choose to derive it by use of the residue calculus, following mainly the presentation of Lindelf in his classical book on the calculus of residues. This is a very simple and above all a very instructive proof inasmuch as it gives us an opportunity to use residues in less trivial cases than previously.

The starting point is the formula (31) for the second derivative of log $\Gamma ( z )$ , and our immediate task is to express the partial sum

$$
{ \frac { 1 } { z ^ { 2 } } } + { \frac { 1 } { ( z + 1 ) ^ { 2 } } } + { \frac { 1 } { ( z + 2 ) ^ { 2 } } } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + { \frac { 1 } { ( z + n ) ^ { 2 } } }
$$

as a convenient line integral. To this end we need a function with the residues $1 / ( z + \nu ) ^ { 2 }$ at the integral points $\pmb { \nu }$ ; a good choice is

$$
\Phi ( \xi ) = \frac { \pi \cot \pi \xi } { ( z + \xi ) ^ { 2 } } \cdot
$$

Here $\xi$ is the variable while $\pmb { z }$ enters only as a parameter, which in the first part of the derivation will be kept at a fixed value $z = x +$ iy with $x > 0$ .

We apply the residue formula to the rectangle whose vertical sides lie on ${ \boldsymbol { \xi } } = \mathbf { 0 }$ and $\xi = n + \textstyle { \frac { 1 } { 2 } }$ and with horizontal sides ${ \mathfrak { \eta } } = \pm { \mathfrak { Y } }$ ,with the intention of letting first $Y$ and then $\mathscr { n }$ tend to $\infty$ . This contour, which we denote by $\pmb { K }$ , passes through the pole at 0, but we know that the formula remains valid provided that we take the principal value of the integral and include one-half of the residue at the origin. Hence we obtain

$$
\operatorname { p r . v . } { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } } \int _ { K } \Phi ( \xi ) d \xi = - { \frac { 1 } { 2 z ^ { 2 } } } + \sum _ { \nu = 0 } ^ { n } { \frac { 1 } { ( z + \nu ) ^ { 2 } } } \cdotp
$$

On the horizontal sides of the rectangle cot $\pi \xi$ tends uniformly to ±i for $Y  \infty$ . Since the factor $1 / ( z + \zeta ) ^ { 2 }$ tends to zero, the corresponding integrals have the limit zero. We are now left with two integrals over infinite vertical lines. On each line $\begin{array} { r } { \pmb { \xi } = \pmb { n } + \frac { 1 } { 2 } , } \end{array}$ ,cot $\pmb { \pi } \pmb { \zeta }$ is bounded, and because of the periodicity the bound is independent of $\pmb { n }$ The integral over the line $\xi = n + \textstyle { \frac { 1 } { 2 } }$ is thus less than a constant times

$$
\int \limits _ { \xi = n + 1 } \frac { d \eta } { | \xi + z | ^ { 2 } }
$$

This integral can be evaluated, for on the line of integration

$$
\bar { \xi } = 2 n + 1 - \xi ,
$$

and we obtain by residues

$$
\frac { 1 } { i } \int \frac { d \xi } { | \xi + z | ^ { 2 } } = \frac { 1 } { i } \int \frac { d \xi } { ( \xi + z ) ( 2 n + 1 - \xi + \bar { z } ) } = \frac { 2 \pi } { 2 n + 1 + 2 x } .
$$

The limit for $n  \infty$ is thus zero.

Finally, the principal value of the integral over the imaginary axis from $- i \infty$ to $+ i \infty$ can be written in the form

$$
\frac { 1 } { 2 } \int _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } \cot \pi i \eta \left[ \frac { 1 } { ( i \eta + z ^ { 2 } ) } - \frac { 1 } { ( i \eta - z ) ^ { 2 } } \right] d \eta = - \int _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } \coth \pi \eta \cdot \frac { 2 \eta z } { ( \eta ^ { 2 } + z ^ { 2 } ) ^ { 2 } } d \eta .
$$

The sign has to be reversed, and we obtain the formula

$$
\frac { d } { d z } \left( \frac { \Gamma ^ { \prime } ( z ) } { \Gamma ( z ) } \right) = \frac { 1 } { 2 z ^ { 2 } } + \int _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } \coth \pi \eta \cdot \frac { 2 \eta z } { ( \eta ^ { 2 } + z ^ { 2 } ) ^ { 2 } } d \eta .
$$

It is preferable to write

$$
\coth \ \pi \eta = 1 + { \frac { 2 } { e ^ { 2 \pi \eta } - 1 } }
$$

and observe that the integral obtained from the term 1 has the value $1 / z$ . We can thus rewrite (32) in the form

$$
{ \frac { d } { d z } } \left( { \frac { \Gamma ^ { \prime } ( z ) } { \Gamma ( z ) } } \right) = { \frac { 1 } { z } } + { \frac { 1 } { 2 z ^ { 2 } } } + \int _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } { \frac { 4 \eta z } { ( \eta ^ { 2 } + z ^ { 2 } ) ^ { 2 } } } \cdot { \frac { d \eta } { e ^ { 2 \pi \eta } - 1 } }
$$

where the integral is now very strongly convergent.

For z restricted to the right half plane this formula can be integrated. We find

$$
{ \frac { \Gamma ^ { \prime } ( z ) } { \Gamma ( z ) } } = C + \log z - { \frac { 1 } { 2 z } } - \int _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } { \frac { 2 \eta } { \eta ^ { 2 } + z ^ { 2 } } } \cdot { \frac { d \eta } { e ^ { 2 \pi \eta } - 1 } } ,
$$

where log $\pmb { z }$ is the principal branch and $c$ is an integration constant. The integration of the last term needs some justification. We have to make sure that the integral in (34) can be differentiated under the sign of integration; this is so because the integral converges uniformly when $\pmb { z }$ is restricted to any compact set in the half plane $x > 0$ .

We wish to integrate (34) once more. This would obviously introduce arc tan $\left( z / \eta \right)$ in the integral, and although a single-valued branch could be defined we prefer to avoid the use of multiple-valued functions. That is possible if we first transform the integral in (34) by partial integration. We obtain

$$
\int _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } { \frac { 2 \eta } { \eta ^ { 2 } + z ^ { 2 } } } \cdot { \frac { d \eta } { e ^ { 2 \pi \eta } - 1 } } = { \frac { 1 } { \pi } } \int _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } { \frac { z ^ { 2 } - \eta ^ { 2 } } { ( \eta ^ { 2 } + z ^ { 2 } ) ^ { 2 } } } \log { ( 1 - e ^ { - 2 \pi \eta } ) } d \eta
$$

where the logarithm is of course real. Now we can integrate with respect to $\pmb { z }$ and obtain

(35)

$$
\begin{array} { l } { \displaystyle { \log \Gamma ( z ) } } \\ { = c ^ { \prime } + c z + \left( z - \frac { 1 } { 2 } \right) \log z + \frac { 1 } { \pi } \int _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } \frac { z } { \eta ^ { 2 } + z ^ { 2 } } \log \frac { 1 } { 1 - e ^ { - 2 \pi \eta } } d \eta } \end{array}
$$

where $c ^ { \prime }$ is a new integration constant and for convenience ${ \bf { \vec { C } } } - { \bf { 1 } }$ has been replaced by $c$ . The formula means that there exists, in the right half plane, a single-valued branch of log $\Gamma ( z )$ whose value is given by the right-hand member of the equation. By proper choice of $c ^ { \prime }$ we obtain the branch of log $\Gamma ( z )$ which is real for real ${ \pmb z }$

It remains to determine the constants $\pmb { c }$ and $c ^ { \prime }$ . To this end we must first study the behavior of the integral in (35) which we denote by

$$
J ( z ) = { \frac { 1 } { \pi } } \int _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } { \frac { z } { \eta ^ { 2 } + z ^ { 2 } } } \log { \frac { 1 } { 1 - e ^ { - 2 \pi \eta } } } d \eta .
$$

It is practically evident that $\boldsymbol { J } ( z ) \to \mathbf { 0 }$ for $z  \infty$ provided that $\pmb { z }$ keeps away from the imaginary axis. Suppose for instance that $\pmb { z }$ is restricted to the half plane $x \geq c > 0$ Breaking the integral into two parts we write

$$
J ( z ) = \int _ { 0 } ^ { \frac { | z | } { 2 } } + \int _ { \frac { | z | } { 2 } } ^ { \infty } = J _ { 1 } + J _ { 2 } .
$$

In the first integral $| \eta ^ { 2 } + z ^ { 2 } | \geq | z | ^ { 2 } - | z / 2 | ^ { 2 } = 3 | z | ^ { 3 } / 4 ,$ and hence

$$
| J _ { 1 } | \leq \frac { 4 } { 3 \pi | z | } \int _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } \log \frac { 1 } { 1 - e ^ { - 2 \pi \eta } } d \eta .
$$

In the second integral $| \eta ^ { 2 } + z ^ { 2 } | = | z - i \eta | \cdot | z + i \eta | > c | z |$ , and we find

$$
| J _ { 2 } | < \frac { 1 } { \pi c } \int _ { \frac { | z | } { 2 } } ^ { \infty } \log \frac { 1 } { 1 - e ^ { - 2 \kappa _ { 7 } } } d \eta .
$$

Since the integral of log $( 1 - e ^ { - 2 \pi \eta } )$ is obviously convergent, we conclude that $\scriptstyle J _ { 1 }$ and $\pmb { J _ { 2 } }$ tend to 0 as $z  \infty$ .

The value of $c$ is found by substituting (35) in the functional equation $\Gamma ( z + 1 ) = z \Gamma ( z )$ or log $\Gamma ( z + 1 ) = \log z + \log \Gamma ( z ) ;$ if we restrict $\pmb { z }$ to positive values, there is no hesitancy about the branch of the logarithm. The substitution yields

$$
\begin{array} { r } { C ^ { \prime } + C z + C + ( z + \frac { 1 } { 2 } ) \log { ( z + 1 ) } + J ( z + 1 ) \qquad } \\ { = C ^ { \prime } + C z + ( z + \frac { 1 } { 2 } ) \log { z } + J ( z ) , } \end{array}
$$

and this reduces to

$$
C = - \left( z + \frac { 1 } { 2 } \right) \log \left( 1 + \frac { 1 } { z } \right) + J ( z ) - J ( z + 1 ) .
$$

Letting $z  \infty$ we find that $c = - 1$

Next we apply (35) to the equation $\Gamma ( z ) \Gamma ( 1 - z ) = \pi / \sin \pi z$ ,choosing $z = { \textstyle \frac { 1 } { 2 } } + i y$ We obtain

$$
\begin{array} { r } { 2 C ^ { \prime } - 1 + i y \log { ( \frac { 1 } { 2 } + i y ) } - i y \log { ( \frac { 1 } { 2 } - i y ) } + J ( \frac { 1 } { 2 } + i y ) + J ( \frac { 1 } { 2 } - i y ) } \\ { = \log { \pi } - \log \cosh { \pi y } . } \end{array}
$$

This equation, in which the logarithms are to have their principal values, is so far proved only up to a constant multiple of $\scriptstyle 2 { \mathrm { \pi } } i$ But for $y = 0$ the equation is correct as it stands because (35) determines the real value of log $\Gamma ( \scriptstyle { \frac { 1 } { 2 } } )$ ; hence it holds for all ${ \pmb y }$ As $y  \infty$ we known that $J ( \mathbf { \phi } _ { 2 } ^ { 1 } + i y )$ and $J ( \mathbf { \phi } _ { 2 } ^ { 1 } - i y )$ tend to 0. Developing the logarithm in a Taylor series we find

$$
i y \log { \frac { { \frac { 1 } { 2 } } + i y } { { \frac { 1 } { 2 } } - i y } } = i y \left( \pi i + \log { \frac { 1 + { \frac { 1 } { 2 i y } } } { 1 - { \frac { 1 } { 2 i y } } } } \right) = - \pi y + 1 + { \mathfrak { e } } _ { 1 } ( y )
$$

while in the right-hand member

$$
\log \cosh \pi y = \pi y - \log 2 + \varepsilon _ { 2 } ( y )
$$

with $\mathfrak { e } _ { \mathbf { 1 } } ( y )$ and $\dot { \mathfrak { e } } _ { 2 } ( y )$ tending to 0. These considerations yield the value $C ^ { \prime } = { \frac { 1 } { 2 } } \log 2 \pi$ We have thus proved Stirling's formula in the form

$$
\log \Gamma ( z ) = { \textstyle { \frac { 1 } { 2 } } } \log 2 \pi - z + ( z - { \textstyle { \frac { 1 } { 2 } } } ) \log z + J ( z )
$$

or equivalently

$$
\Gamma ( z ) = \sqrt { 2 \pi } z ^ { z - 1 } e ^ { - \varepsilon } e ^ { J ( z ) }
$$

with the representation (36) of the remainder valid in the right half plane.   
We know that $\boldsymbol { J } ( z )$ tends to 0 when $z  \infty$ in a half plane $x \not \equiv { \pmb { c } } > { \pmb { 0 } }$ .

In the expression for $\boldsymbol { J } ( z )$ we can develop the integrand in powers of $1 / z$ and obtain

$$
J ( z ) = { \frac { C _ { 1 } } { z } } + { \frac { C _ { 2 } } { z ^ { 8 } } } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + { \frac { C _ { k } } { z ^ { 2 k - 1 } } } + J _ { k } ( z )
$$

with

$$
C _ { r } = ( - 1 ) ^ { \nu - 1 } { \frac { 1 } { \pi } } \int _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } \eta ^ { 2 \nu - 2 } \log { \frac { 1 } { 1 - e ^ { - 2 \pi \eta } } } d \eta
$$

and

$$
J _ { k } ( z ) = \frac { ( - 1 ) ^ { k } } { z ^ { 2 k + 1 } } \frac { 1 } { \pi } \int _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } \frac { \eta ^ { 2 k } } { 1 + ( \eta / z ) ^ { 2 } } \log \frac { 1 } { 1 - e ^ { - 2 \pi \eta } } d \eta .
$$

It can be proved (for instance by means of residues) that the coefficients $^ { c , }$ are connected with the Bernoulli numbers (cf. Ex. 4, Sec. 1.3) by

$$
C _ { \tau } = ( - 1 ) ^ { \nu - 1 } \frac { 1 } { ( 2 \nu - 1 ) 2 \nu } B _ { \nu } 
$$

Thus the development of $\boldsymbol { J } ( z )$ takes the form

$$
\begin{array} { c } { { J ( z ) = \displaystyle \frac { B _ { 1 } } { 1 \cdot 2 } \frac { 1 } { z } - \frac { B _ { 2 } } { 3 \cdot 4 } \cdot \frac { 1 } { z ^ { 3 } } + \cdot \cdot \cdot } } \\ { { + \ ( - 1 ) ^ { k - 1 } \displaystyle \frac { B _ { k } } { ( 2 k - 1 ) 2 k } \displaystyle \frac { 1 } { z ^ { 2 k - 1 } } + J _ { k } ( z ) . } } \end{array}
$$

The reader is warned not to confuse this with a Laurent development. The function $\boldsymbol { J } ( z )$ is not defined in a neighborhood of $\infty$ and, therefore, does not have a Laurent development; moreover, if $k  \infty$ , the series obtained from (41) does not converge. What we can say is that for a fixed $\boldsymbol { k }$ the expression $J _ { k } ( z ) z ^ { 2 k }$ tends to 0 for $z  \infty$ (in a half plane $x \ge c > 0$ ). This fact characterizes (41) as an asymptotic development. Such developments are very valuable when $\pmb { z }$ is large in comparison with $k ,$ but for fixed $\pmb { z }$ there is no advantage in letting $\boldsymbol { k }$ become very large.

Stirling's formula can be used to prove that

$$
\Gamma ( z ) = \int _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } e ^ { - t } t ^ { z - 1 } d t
$$

whenever the integral converges, that is to say for $\pmb { x } > \mathbf { 0 }$ Until the identity has been proved, let the integral in (42) be denoted by $\pmb { F } ( z )$ . Integrating by parts we find at once that

$$
F ( z + 1 ) = \int _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } e ^ { - t } t ^ { z } d t = z \int _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } e ^ { - t } t ^ { z - 1 } d t = z F ( z ) .
$$

Hence $F ( z )$ satisfies the same functional equation as $\Gamma ( z )$ ,and we find that $F ( z ) / \Gamma ( z ) = F ( z + 1 ) / \Gamma ( z + 1 )$ In other words $\mathbf { \mathit { F } } ( \mathfrak { z } ) / \mathbf { \mathit { T } } ( \mathfrak { z } )$ is periodic with the period 1. This shows, incidentally, that $F ( z )$ can be defined in the whole plane although the integral representation is valid only in a half plane.

In order to prove that $F ( z ) / \Gamma ( z )$ is constant we have to estimate $| F / \Gamma |$ in a period strip, for instance in the strip $\mathbf { 1 } \lessapprox x \lessapprox 2$ In the first place we have by (42)

$$
| F ( z ) | \leq \int _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } e ^ { - t } t ^ { x - 1 } d t = F ( x ) ,
$$

and hence $F ( z )$ is bounded in the strip. Next, we use Stirling's formula to find a lower bound of $| \Gamma ( z ) |$ for large $_ y$ From (37) we obtain

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \log | \Gamma ( z ) | = \frac { 1 } { 2 } \log 2 \pi - x + ( x - \frac { 1 } { 2 } ) \log | z | - y \arg z + \mathrm { R e } J ( z ) . } \end{array}
$$

Only the term $- y$ arg $z$ becomes negatively infinite, being comparable to $- \pi | y | / 2$ Thus $| F / T |$ does not grow much more rapidly than $e ^ { \star | y | / 2 }$ .

For an arbitrary function this would not suffice to conclude that the function must be constant, but for a function of period 1 it is more than enough. In fact, it is clear that $F / \mathbf { \varGamma }$ can be expressed as a single-valued function of the variable $\xi = e ^ { 2 \pi i z }$ ; to every value of $\boldsymbol { \xi } \neq \mathbf { 0 }$ there correspond infinitely many values of $\mathscr { z }$ which differ by multiples of 1, and thus a single value of $F / \Gamma$ . The function has isolated singularities at $\boldsymbol { \xi } = \mathbf { 0 }$ and $\qquad \vdots \ : = \ : \infty$ , and our estimate shows that $\vert F / \Gamma \vert$ grows at most like $| \xi | ^ { - \frac { 1 } { 2 } }$ for $\xi \to 0$ and $| \zeta | ^ { \frac { 3 } { 4 } }$ for $\zeta \to \infty$ . It follows that both singularities are removable, and hence $F / \Gamma$ must reduce to a constant. Finally, the fact that $F ( \bf { 1 } ) = \Gamma ( \bf { 1 } ) = \bf { 1 }$ shows that $F ( z ) = \Gamma ( z )$ .

# EXERCISES

1. Prove the development (41).

.For real ${ \boldsymbol { x } } > 0$ prove that

$$
\Gamma ( x ) = \sqrt { 2 \pi } x ^ { x - 1 } e ^ { - x } e ^ { \theta ( x ) / 1 2 x }
$$

with $0 < \theta ( x ) < 1$

The formula (42) permits us to evaluate the probability integral

$$
\int _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } e ^ { - t ^ { \ast } } d t = { \frac { \imath } { 2 } } \int _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } e ^ { - x } x ^ { - \frac { \imath } { 2 } } d x = { \frac { \imath } { 2 } } \Gamma ( { \frac { \imath } { 2 } } ) = { \frac { \imath } { 2 } } { \sqrt { \pi } } .
$$

Use this result together with Cauchy's theorem to compute the Fresnel integrals

$$
\int _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } \sin \left( x ^ { 2 } \right) d x , \qquad \int _ { 0 } ^ { \infty } \cos \left( x ^ { 2 } \right) d x .
$$

Answer: Both are equal to $\scriptstyle { \frac { 1 } { 2 } } \displaystyle { \sqrt { \pi / 2 } }$ .

# 3. ENTIRE FUNCTIONS

In Sec. 2.3 we have already considered the representation of entire functions as infinite products, and, in special cases, as canonical products. In this section we study the connection between the product representation and the rate of growth of the function. Such questions were first investigated by Hadamard who applied the results to his celebrated proof of the Prime Number Theorem. Space does not permit us to include this application, but the basic importance of Hadamard's factorization theorem will be quite evident.

3.1. Jensen's Formula. If $f ( z )$ is an analytic function, then log $\vert f ( z ) \vert$ is harmonic except at the zeros of $f ( z )$ Therefore, if $f ( z )$ is analytic and free from zeros in $| z | \leq \rho ;$ .

$$
\log | f ( 0 ) | = \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi } \int _ { 0 } ^ { 2 \pi } \log | f ( \rho e ^ { i \theta } ) | d \theta ,
$$

and log $\vert f ( z ) \vert$ can be expressed by Poisson's formula.

The equation (43) remains valid if $f ( z )$ has zeros on the circle $| z | = \pmb { \rho }$ . The simplest proof is by dividing $f ( z )$ with one factor $z - \rho e ^ { i \theta _ { 0 } }$ for each zero. It is sufficient to show that

$$
\log \rho = \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi } \int _ { 0 } ^ { 2 \pi } \log \left| \rho e ^ { i \theta } - \rho e ^ { i \theta _ { 0 } } \right| d \theta
$$

or

$$
\int _ { 0 } ^ { 2 \pi } \log \left| e ^ { i \theta } - e ^ { i \theta _ { 0 } } \right| d \theta = 0 .
$$

This integral is evidently independent of $\theta _ { 0 } ,$ and we have only to show that

$$
\int _ { 0 } ^ { 2 \pi } \log \left| 1 - e ^ { i \theta } \right| d \theta = 0 .
$$

But this is a consequence of the formula

$$
\int _ { 0 } ^ { \pi } \log \sin x d x = - \pi \log 2
$$

proved in Chap. 4, Sec, 5.3 (cf. Chap. 4, Sec. 6.4, Ex. 5).

We will now investigate what becomes of (43) in the presence of zeros in the interior $| z | < \rho$ Denote these zeros by $a _ { 1 } , ~ a _ { 2 } ,$ . .., ${ a } _ { n }$ multiple zeros being repeated, and assume first that ${ z } = 0 \cdot$ is not a zero. Then the function

$$
F ( z ) = f ( z ) \prod _ { i = 1 } ^ { n } { \frac { p ^ { 2 } - { \bar { a } } _ { i } z } { \rho ( z - a _ { i } ) } }
$$

is free from zeros in the disk, and $| F ( z ) | = | f ( z ) | \mathrm { o n } | z | = \rho .$ Consequently we obtain

$$
\log \left| F ( 0 ) \right| = { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi } } \int _ { 0 } ^ { 2 \pi } \log \left| f ( \rho e ^ { i \theta } ) \right| d \theta
$$

and, substituting the value of $F ( \mathbf { 0 } )$

$$
\log \left| f ( 0 ) \right| = - \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { n } \log \left( { \frac { \rho } { \left| a _ { i } \right| } } \right) + { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi } } \int _ { 0 } ^ { 2 \pi } \log \left| f ( { \rho e ^ { i \theta } } ) \right| d \theta .
$$

This is known as Jensen's formula. Its importance lies in the fact that it relates the modulus $\vert f ( z ) \}$ on a circle to the moduli of the zeros.

If $f ( { \bf 0 } ) = { \bf 0 }$ , the formula is somewhat more complicated. Writing $f ( z ) = c z ^ { h } + \cdot \cdot \cdot .$ we apply (44) to $f ( z ) ( \rho / z ) ^ { h }$ and find that the left-hand member must be replaced by log $| c | + h \log \rho$ .

There is a similar generalization of Poisson's formula. All that is needed is to apply the ordinary Poisson formula to log $| F ( z ) |$ We obtain

$$
\log \left| f ( z ) \right| = - \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { n } \log \left| \frac { \rho ^ { 2 } - \bar { a } _ { i } z } { \rho ( z - a _ { i } ) } \right| + \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi } \int _ { 0 } ^ { 2 \pi } \mathrm { R e } \frac { \rho e ^ { i \theta } + z } { \rho \epsilon ^ { i \theta } - z } \log \left| f ( \rho e ^ { i \theta } ) \right| d \theta ,
$$

provided that $f ( z ) \neq 0$ . Equation (45) is frequently referred to as the Poisson-Jensen formula.

Strictly speaking the proof is valid only if $\begin{array} { r } { f \neq 0 \ , \mathsf { o n } \left| z \right| = \rho \ } \end{array}$ But (44) shows that the integral on the right is a continuous function of $\pmb { \rho } _ { \mathrm { t } }$ ,and from there it is easy to infer that the integral in (45) is likewise continuous. In the general case (45) can therefore be derived by letting $\pmb { \rho }$ approach a limit.

The Jensen and Poisson-Jensen formulas have important applications in the theory of entire functions.

3.2. Hadamard's Theorem. Let $f ( z )$ be an entire function, and denote its zeros by $a _ { n }$ ; for the sake of simplicity we will assume that $f ( 0 ) \neq 0 ,$ .
