5. Formulate and prove a theorem to the effect that a uniform limit of subharmonic functions is subharmonic.

4.2. Solution of Dirichlet's Problem. The first to use subharmonic functions for the study of Dirichlet's problem was O. Perron. His method is characterized by extreme generality, and it is completely elementary.

We consider a bounded region $\Omega$ and a real-valued function $f ( \zeta )$ defined on its boundary $\mathbf { r }$ (for clarity, boundary points will be denoted by $\xi$ . To begin with, $f ( \xi )$ need not even be continuous, but for the sake of simplicity we assume that it is bounded, $| f ( \zeta ) | \leq { \cal M }$ With each $f$ we associate a harmonic function $u ( z )$ in $\Omega _ { i }$ , defined by a simple process which will be detailed below. If $f$ is continuous, and if $\Omega$ satisfies certain mild conditions, the corresponding function $\mathbf { \nabla } ^ { \mathcal { U } }$ will solve the Dirichlet problem for $\Omega$ with the boundary values $f$ .

We define the class $\mathfrak { B } ( f )$ of functions $v$ with the following properties:

$( a )$ $v$ is subharmonic in $\Omega$ ; (b) $\operatorname* { l i m } _ { z \to \xi } v ( z ) \ \leqq { f ( \xi ) }$ for all $\displaystyle \xi \in \mathbf { \mathbb { T } }$

The precise meaning of $( b )$ is this: given $\mathfrak { E } > \mathbf { 0 }$ and a point $\zeta \in \mathbf { \Gamma } ^ { \mathbf { \sigma } }$ there exists a neighborhood $\Delta$ of $\xi$ such that $v ( z ) < f ( \xi ) + \mathfrak { s }$ in $\Delta \cap \Omega$ The class $\mathfrak { B } ( f )$ is not empty, for it contains all constants $\leq - M$ We prove:

Lemma l. The function $u _ { ; }$ , defined as $u ( z ) = 1 . \mathrm { u . b . } \ v ( i $ $v ( z )$ for $v \in { \mathfrak { V } } ( f )$ , is harmonic in $\Omega ,$ .

In the first place, each $v$ is $\leq M$ in $\Omega$ This is a simple enough consequence of the maximum principle, but because of its importance we want to explain this point in some detail. For a given $\varepsilon > 0$ , let $E$ be the set of points $z \epsilon \Omega$ for which $v ( z ) \geq \bar { \lambda } I + \varepsilon$ The points $z$ in the complement ${ \sim } E$ are of three kinds: (1) points in the exterior of $\Omega _ { r }$ points on $\mathbf { r }$ , points in $\Omega$ with $v ( z ) < M + \varepsilon .$ In case (1) $z$ has a neighborhood contained in the exterior, in case (2) there is a neighborhood $\Delta$ with $v < M + \mathfrak { s i n } \Delta \cap \Omega _ { : }$ , by property $( b )$ , and in case (3) there exists, by continuity, a neighborhood in $\dot { \boldsymbol \Omega }$ with $v < M + \varepsilon .$ Hence ${ \sim } E$ is open, and $E$ is closed. Moreover, since $\Omega$ is bounded, $E$ is compact. If $E$ were not void, $v$ would have a maximum on $E$ , and this would also be a maximum in $\Omega .$ This is impossible, for because of $\left( b \right) v$ cannot be a constant $> M$ Hence $E$ is void for every e, and it follows that $v \leq M$ in $\Omega$ .

Consider a disk $\Delta$ whose closure is contained in $\Omega$ , and a point $z _ { 0 } \epsilon \Delta$

There exists a sequence of functions $v _ { n } \in \mathfrak { B } ( f )$ such that $\operatorname* { l i m } _ { n \to \infty } v _ { n } ( z _ { 0 } ) = u ( z _ { 0 } ) ,$ . Set $V _ { n } = \mathrm { m a x } ~ ( v _ { 1 } , v _ { 2 } , \ \cdot \ . ~ . ~ , v _ { n } )$ Then the $V _ { n }$ form a nondecreasing sequence of functions in $\mathfrak { B } ( f )$ We construct $V _ { n } ^ { \prime }$ equal to $V _ { n }$ outside of $\Delta$ and equal to the Poisson integral of $V _ { n }$ in $\pmb { \triangle }$ By property (4) of the preceding section the $V _ { n } ^ { \prime }$ are still in $\mathfrak { B } ( f )$ . They form a nondecreasing sequence, and the inequality $v _ { n } ( z _ { 0 } ) \leq V _ { n } ( z _ { 0 } ) \leq V _ { n } ^ { \prime } ( z _ { 0 } ) \leq u ( z _ { 0 } )$ shows that lim $V _ { n } ^ { \prime } ( z _ { 0 } ) = u ( z _ { 0 } )$ , By Harnack's principle the sequence $\{ V _ { n } ^ { \prime } \}$ conn_→∞   
verges to a harmonic limit function $\boldsymbol { U }$ in $\Delta$ which satisfies $U \leq u$ and $U ( z _ { 0 } ) = u ( z _ { 0 } )$ .

Suppose now that we start the same process from another point $z _ { 1 } \epsilon \Delta$ . We select $w _ { n } \epsilon \mathfrak { V } ( f )$ so that $\operatorname* { l i m } _ { n \to \infty } w _ { n } ( z _ { 1 } ) = u ( z _ { 1 } ) ,$ , but this time, before proceeding with the construction, we replace ${ \boldsymbol { w } } _ { n }$ by $\bar { w } _ { n } = \operatorname* { m a x } \left( v _ { n , } w _ { n } \right)$ . Setting $W _ { n } = \operatorname* { m a x } { ( \bar { w } _ { 1 } , \ . \ . \ , \bar { w } _ { n } ) }$ we construct the corresponding sequence $\{ W _ { n } ^ { \prime } \}$ with the aid of the Poisson integral and are led to a harmonic limit functiqn $U _ { 1 }$ which satisfies $U \leqq U _ { 1 } \leqq u$ and $U _ { 1 } ( z _ { 1 } ) = u ( z _ { 1 } )$ It follows that $U  U _ { 1 }$ has the maximum zero at ${ z _ { 0 } }$ Therefore $\boldsymbol { U }$ is identically equal to $U _ { 1 , }$ and we have proved that $u ( z _ { 1 } ) = U ( z _ { 1 } )$ for arbitrary $z _ { 1 } \epsilon \Delta$ . It follows that $\textbf { \em u }$ is harmonic in any disk $\Delta$ and, consequently, in all of $\Omega$ .

$\mathbf { W } \mathbf { \hat { \Sigma } }$ will now investigate the circumstances under which $\mathbf { \Delta } _ { \boldsymbol { u } }$ solves the Dirichlet problem for continuous $f .$ We note first that the Dirichlet probiem does not always have a solution. For instance, if $\Omega$ is the punctured disk $0 < | z | < 1 _ { : }$ , consider the boundary values $f ( 0 ) = { \bf 1 }$ and $f ( \zeta ) = 0$ for $| \boldsymbol { \gamma } | = 1$ A harmonic function with these boundary values would be bounded and would, hence, present a removable singularity at the origin. But then the maximum principle would imply that the function vanishes identically and thus could not have the boundary value 1 at the origin. It follows that no solution can exist.

It is also easy to see that a solution, if it exists, must be identical with u. In fact, if $U$ is a solution it is first of all clear that $U \in { \mathfrak { B } } ( f )$ , and hence $u \not \equiv U$ The opposite inequality $u \leq U$ follows by the maximum principle which implies $v \leq U$ for all $v \in { \mathfrak { B } } ( f )$

The existence of a solution can be asserted for a wide class of regions. Generally speaking, the solution exists if the complement of $\Omega$ is not too thin" in the neighborhood of any boundary point. We begin by proving a lemma which, on the surface, seems to have little to do with the notion of thinness.

Lemma 2. Suppose that there exists a harmonic function $w ( z )$ in $\Omega$ whose continuous boundary values $\omega ( \xi )$ are strictly positive except at one point $\xi _ { 0 }$ where $\omega ( \zeta _ { 0 } ) = 0$ , Then, if $f ( \xi )$ is continuous at $\boldsymbol { \xi } _ { 0 } ,$ the corresponding function u determined by Perron's method satisfies lim $u ( z ) = f ( \boldsymbol { \zeta } _ { 0 } )$ . 2→50

The lemma will be proved if we show that $\operatorname* { l i m } _ { z  5 0 } u ( z ) \le f ( \xi _ { 0 } ) + \varepsilon$ and $\operatorname* { l i m } _ { z  5 0 } u ( z ) \geq f ( \xi _ { 0 } ) - \varepsilon$ for all $\mathfrak { E } > \mathbf { 0 }$ We are still assuming that $\Omega$ is bounded and $| f ( \zeta ) | \leq { \cal M }$ .

Determine a neighborhood $\pmb { \Delta }$ of $\boldsymbol { \zeta } _ { 0 }$ such that $| f ( \zeta ) - f ( \zeta _ { 0 } ) | < \varepsilon$ for $\xi \in \Delta$ $\operatorname { I n } \Omega - \Delta \cap \Omega$ the function $\omega ( z )$ has a positive minimum $\pmb { \omega _ { 0 } }$ We consider the boundary values of the harmonic function

$$
\begin{array} { r } { W ( z ) = f ( \xi _ { 0 } ) + \varepsilon + \frac { \omega ( z ) } { \omega _ { 0 } } ( M - f ( \xi _ { 0 } ) ) . } \end{array}
$$

For $\zeta \epsilon \Delta$ we have $W ( \xi ) \geq f ( \xi _ { 0 } ) + \varepsilon > f ( \xi )$ ,and for $\xi$ outside of $\Delta$ we obtain $W ( \xi ) \geq M + \varepsilon > f ( \xi )$ By the maximum principle any function $v \in { \mathfrak { B } } ( f )$ must hence satisfy $\upsilon ( z ) < W ( z )$ . It follows that $u ( z ) \leq W ( z )$ and consequently $\varlimsup _ { z  \xi _ { 0 } } u ( z ) \leq W ( \xi _ { 0 } ) = f ( \xi _ { 0 } ) + \varepsilon ,$ which is the first inequality we set out to prove.

For the second inequality we need only show that the function

$$
V ( z ) = f ( \xi _ { 0 } ) - \mathtt { e } - \frac { \omega ( z ) } { \omega _ { 0 } } \left( M + f ( \xi _ { 0 } ) \right)
$$

is in $\mathfrak { B } ( \mathfrak { f } )$ For $\xi \in \Delta$ we have $V ( \xi ) \equiv f ( \xi _ { 0 } ) - \mathtt { e } < f ( \xi )$ , and at all other boundary points $V ( \xi ) \le - M - \varepsilon < f ( \xi )$ Since $V$ is harmonic it belongs to $\mathfrak { B } ( f )$ and we obtain $u ( z ) \geq V ( z ) , \operatorname* { l i m } _ { \bar { z } \to \bar { \xi } _ { 0 } } u ( z ) \geq \Gamma ^ { r } ( \xi _ { 0 } ) = f ( \xi _ { 0 } ) - \varepsilon .$ This completes the proof.

The function $\omega ( z )$ of Lemma 2 is sometimes called a barrier at the point $\boldsymbol { \xi } _ { 0 } .$ Clearly, we can now say that the Dirichlet problem is solvable provided that there is a barrier at each boundary point. It remains to formulate geometric conditions which imply the existence of a barrier. Actually, necessary and sufficient conditions of a purely geometric character are not known, but it is relatively easy to find sufficient conditions with a wide range of applicability.

To begin with the simplest case, suppose that $\Omega \cup \mathbf { r }$ is contained in an open half plane, except for a point $\xi _ { 0 }$ which lies on the boundary line. If the direction of this line is $\pmb { \alpha }$ (with the half plane to the left), then $\omega ( z ) = \operatorname { I m } e ^ { - i \alpha } ( z - \xi _ { 0 } )$ is a barrier at $\xi _ { 0 }$ .

More generally, suppose that $\boldsymbol { \xi } _ { 0 }$ is the end point of a line segment all of whose points, except $\xi _ { 0 } ,$ lie in the exterior of $\Omega$ If the other end point is denoted by $\displaystyle \xi _ { 1 , }$ we know that a single-valued branch of

$$
\sqrt { \frac { z - \xi _ { 0 } } { z - \xi _ { 1 } } }
$$

can be defined outside of the segment. With a proper determination of the angle $\pmb { \alpha }$ the function

$$
\operatorname { I m } \left[ e ^ { - i \alpha } \sqrt { \frac { z - \xi _ { 0 } } { z - \xi _ { 1 } } } \right]
$$

is easily seen to be a barrier at $\displaystyle { \boldsymbol { \zeta } } _ { 0 } .$

This is not the strongest result that can be obtained by these methods, but it is sufficient for most applications. We shall therefore be content with the following statement:

Theorem 9. The Dirichlet problem can be solved for any region $\Omega$ such that each boundary point is the end point of a line segment whose other points are exterior to $\Omega$ .

The hypothesis is fulflled if $\Omega$ and its complement have a common boundary consisting of a finite number of simple closed curves with a tangent at each point. Corners and certain types of cusps are also permissible. $^ \dagger$

# 5. CANONICAL MAPPINGS OF MULTIPLY CONNECTED REGIONS

Riemann's mapping theorem permits us to conclude that any two simply connected regions, with the exception of the whole plane, can be mapped conformally onto each other, or that they are conformally equivalent. For multiply connected regions of the same connectivity this is no longer true. Instead we must try to find a system of canonical regions with the property that each multiply connected region is conformally equivalent to one and only one canonical region. The choice of canonical regions is to a certain extent arbitrary, and there are several types with equally simple properties.

In order to stay on an elementary level we will limit ourselves to the study of regions of finite connectivity. We shall find that the basic step toward the construction of canonical mappings is the introduction of certain harmonic functions with a particularly simple behavior on the boundary. Of these the harmonic measures are related only to the region and one of its contours, while the Green's function is related to the region and an interior point.

$^ \dagger$ The best result that can be proved by essentially the same method is the following: The Dirichlet problem can be solved for any region whose complement is such that no component reduces to a point. From this proposition an independent proof of the Riemann mapping theorem can easily be derived.

5.1. Harmonic Measures. When studying the conformal mappings of a region $\Omega$ we can of course replace $\Omega$ by any region known to be conformally equivalent to $\Omega$ , that is to say, we can perform preliminary conformal mappings at will. Because of this freedom in the choice of the original region it turns out that it is never necessary to deal with the difficulties which may arise from a complicated structure of the boundary.

In the following $\Omega$ denotes a plane region of connectivity $n > 1$ The components of the complement are denoted by $E _ { 1 } , \ E _ { 2 } ,$ . . . , $E _ { n }$ ,and we take $E _ { n }$ to be the unbounded component. Without loss of generality we can and will assume that no $E _ { k }$ reduces to a point, for it is clear that a point component is a removable singularity of any mapping function, and consequently the mappings remain the same if this isolated boundary point is added to the region.

The complement of $E _ { n }$ is a simply connected region $\Omega ^ { \prime }$ . By Riemann's theorem, $\Omega ^ { \prime }$ can be mapped conformally onto the disk $| z | < 1$ ; under this mapping $\Omega$ is transformed into a new region, and the images of $E _ { 1 , }$ . . . , $E _ { n - 1 }$ are the bounded components of its complement. For the sake of convenience we agree to use the same notations as before the mapping; in particular, $E _ { n }$ is now the set $| z | \geq 1$ The unit circle $| z | = 1$ , traced in the positive direction, will be denoted by $C _ { n }$ and is called the outer contour of the new region $\Omega$ .

Consider now the complement of $E _ { 1 }$ with respect to the extended plane. This is again a simply connected region, and we map it onto the outside of the unit circle with $\infty$ corresponding to itself. The image of $C _ { n }$ is a directed closed analytic curve which we continue to denote by $C _ { n } ,$ just as we keep all the other notations. In addition we define the inner contour $c _ { 1 }$ to be the unit circle in the new plane, traced in the negative direction.

The process can evidently be repeated until we end up with a region $\Omega$ bounded by an outer contour $C _ { n }$ and $n - 1$ inner contours $C _ { 1 } , \ldots , C _ { n - 1 }$ (Fig. 31). It is important to note that the index of a contour with respect to an arbitrary point in the plane can be readily computed. For instance, at the stage where $C _ { k } , k < n _ { : }$ , is the unit circle, the index of $c _ { k }$ is $^ { - 1 }$ with respect to interior points of $E _ { k }$ and 0 with respect to all other points not on $c _ { k }$ . The subsequent mappings will not change this state of affairs. The fact is clear, and a formal proof based on the argument principle can easily be given. One shows in the same way that the outer contour $C _ { n }$ has the index 0 with respect to interior points of $E _ { n }$ and the index 1 with respect to all other points not on $\scriptstyle { C _ { n } }$ It follows that the cycle $C = C _ { 1 } + C _ { 2 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + C _ { n }$ bounds $\Omega$ in the sense of Chap. 4, Sec. 5.1, Definition 4. The distinction between outer and inner contours is coincidental, for evidently an inversion with respect to an interior point of $E _ { k }$ will make $c _ { k }$ the outer contour.

![](images/df9b5675087b9579e6b0b67f1facc7fb34ccbb4b00243fbc890d96d926f88c16.jpg)  
FIG. 31. Transformations of a multiply connected region.

It is clear that Theorem 9 applies to $\Omega _ { * }$ As a matter of fact the existence of a barrier is completely obvious since any contour can be transformed into a circle.

Suppose now that we solve the Dirichlet problem in $\Omega$ with the boundary values 1 on $C _ { k }$ and 0 on the other contours. The solution is denoted by $\omega _ { k } ( z )$ , and it is called the harmonic measure of $C _ { k }$ with respect to the region $\Omega$ . We have clearly $0 < \omega _ { k } ( z ) < 1$ in $\Omega$ and

$$
\omega _ { 1 } ( z ) + \omega _ { 2 } ( z ) + \cdot \cdot \cdot + \omega _ { n } ( z ) = 1 .
$$

If we map $\Omega$ so that $c _ { i }$ becomes a circle, then $\omega _ { k }$ can be continued across $C _ { i }$ according to the reflection principle. We conclude that $\omega _ { k }$ is harmonic in the closed region $\Omega$ in the sense that it can be extended to a larger region.

The contours $C _ { 1 } , \ldots , C _ { n - 1 }$ form a homology basis for the cycles in $\Omega$ homology being understood with respect to an unspecified larger region. The conjugate harmonic differential of $\omega _ { k }$ has periods

$$
\alpha _ { k j } = \int _ { C _ { j } } { \frac { \partial \omega _ { k } } { \partial n } } d s = \int _ { C _ { j } } { ^ { * } d \omega _ { k } }
$$

along $C _ { j } .$ We assert that no linear combination $\lambda _ { 1 } \omega _ { 1 } ( z ) \ + \lambda _ { 2 } \omega _ { 2 } ( z ) \ : + \ : \cdot \ : .$ $+ \lambda _ { n - 1 } \omega _ { n - 1 } ( z )$ with constant coeficients can have a single-valued conjugate function unless all the $\lambda _ { i }$ are zero. To see this, suppose that this expression were the real part of an analytic function $f ( z )$ . By the reflection principle, $f ( z )$ would have an analytic extension to the closure of $\pmb { \Omega }$ . The real part of $f ( z )$ would be constantly eqal to $\lambda _ { i }$ on $C _ { i _ { j } }$ $\dot { \iota } = 1 $ . . . , $n - 1$ , and zero on $C _ { n }$ . Consequently, each contour would be mapped onto a vertical line segment. If ${ \boldsymbol { w } } _ { \mathbf { 0 } }$ does not lie on any of these segments, a single-valued branch of arg $( f ( z ) - w _ { 0 } ) )$ can be defined on each contour. It follows by the argument principle that $f ( z )$ cannot take the value ${ \boldsymbol { w } } _ { 0 }$ in $\Omega .$ But then $f ( z )$ must reduce to a constant, for otherwise the image of $\pmb { \Omega }$ would certainly contain points not on the line segments. We conclude that the real part of $f ( z )$ is identically zero, and hence the boundary values $\lambda _ { i }$ must all vanish. $\blacktriangleright$

What we have proved is that the homogeneous system of linear equations

$$
\lambda _ { 1 } \alpha _ { 1 j } + \lambda _ { 2 } \alpha _ { 2 j } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + \lambda _ { n - 1 } \alpha _ { n - 1 , j } = 0 ( j = 1 , \cdot \cdot \cdot , n - 1 )
$$

has only the trivial solution $\lambda _ { i } = 0$ , for these are the conditions under which $\lambda _ { 1 } \omega _ { 1 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + \lambda _ { n - 1 } \omega _ { n - 1 }$ has a single-valued conjugate. By the theory of linear equations any inhomogeneous system of equations with the same coefficients as (13) must have a solution. In particular, we conclude that it is possible to solve the system

$$
\begin{array} { l } { { \lambda _ { 1 } \alpha _ { 1 1 } + \lambda _ { 2 } \alpha _ { 2 1 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + \lambda _ { n - 1 } \alpha _ { n - 1 , 1 } = 2 \pi } } \\ { { \lambda _ { 1 } \alpha _ { 1 2 } + \lambda _ { 2 } \alpha _ { 2 2 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot \cdot + \lambda _ { n - 1 } \alpha _ { n - 1 , 2 } = 0 } } \\ { { \cdot \ \cdot \cdot \ \cdot \ \cdot \ \cdot \ \cdot \ \cdot \ \cdot \ \ \cdot \ \cdot \ \ \cdot \ \cdot \ \ \cdot \ \ \cdot \ \ \cdot \ \ \cdot \ \ \cdot \ \ \cdot \ \ \cdot \ \ \cdot \ \ \cdot \ \ \cdot \ \ \cdot \ \ \ \cdot \ \ \cdot \ \ \ \cdot \ \ \cdot \ \ \ \cdot \ \ \ \cdot \ \ \ \cdot \ \ \ \cdot \ \ } } \\ { { \lambda _ { 1 } \alpha _ { 1 , n - 1 } + \lambda _ { 2 } \alpha _ { 2 , n - 1 } + \cdot \ \cdot \ \cdot \ \cdot \ + \lambda _ { n - 1 } \alpha _ { n - 1 , n - 1 } = 0 } } \\ { { \lambda _ { 1 } \alpha _ { 1 n } + \lambda _ { 2 } \alpha _ { 2 n } + \ \cdot \ \cdot \ \cdot \ \cdot \ + \lambda _ { n - 1 } \alpha _ { n - 1 , n } = - 2 \pi } } \end{array}
$$

where the last equation is a consequence of the $n - 1$ first (because $\alpha _ { k 1 } + \alpha _ { k 2 } \ + \ \cdot \ \cdot \ + \alpha _ { k n } = 0 )$ . In other words, we can find a multiplevalued integral $f ( z )$ with periods $\pm 2 \pi i$ along $c _ { 1 }$ and $C _ { n }$ and all other periods equal to zero, the real part being constantly equal to $\lambda _ { k }$ on $\scriptstyle { C _ { k } }$ (we set $\lambda _ { n } = 0$ . The function $F ( z ) = e ^ { f ( z ) }$ is then single-valued. We prove:

![](images/5b3bf57d95f6e074cc6d1ff76cc4d1cf8de9fc335cf1428d355dc1c8bdcd407a.jpg)  
FIG. 32. Concentric slit region.

Theorem 10. The function $F ( z )$ effects $\pmb { a }$ one-to-one conformal mapping of $\Omega$ onto the annulus $1 < | w | < e ^ { \lambda _ { 1 } }$ minus $n - 2$ concentric arcs situated on the circles $| w | = e ^ { \lambda _ { i } } ;$ $i = 2$ . . . , $n - 1$ .

The mapping is illustrated in Fig. 32. The contours $c _ { 1 }$ and $C _ { n }$ are in one-to-one correspondence with the full circles, while the other contours are flattened into circular slits. It should be imagined that each slit has two edges which together with the end points form a closed contour.

The proof is by use of the argument principle. We know that $F ( z )$ is analytic with a constant modulus on each contour. The number of roots of the equation $F ( z ) \ = w _ { 0 }$ is given by

$$
\begin{array} { r l r } {  { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } \int _ { C _ { 1 } } \frac { F ^ { \prime } ( z ) \ d z } { F ( z ) - w _ { 0 } } + \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } \int _ { C _ { 1 } } \frac { F ^ { \prime } ( z ) \ d z } { F ( z ) \ - \ w _ { 0 } } + \cdot \cdot \cdot } } \\ & { } & { + \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } \int _ { C _ { n } } \frac { F ^ { \prime } ( z ) \ d z } { F ( z ) \ - \ w _ { 0 } } , } \end{array}
$$

at any rate if $\scriptstyle w _ { 0 }$ is not taken on the boundary. For ${ \boldsymbol { w } } _ { 0 } = \mathbf { 0 }$ the terms in (15) are known, being equal to $\mathbf { l } , \mathbf { 0 } , \ldots , \mathbf { 0 } , - \mathbf { 1 }$ ,respectively. The integral over $c _ { 1 }$ remains constantly equal to 1 for $| w _ { 0 } | < e ^ { \lambda _ { 1 } } ;$ ,and it vanishes for $| w _ { 0 } | > e ^ { \lambda _ { I } }$ ; similarly, the last integral is $^ { - 1 }$ for $| w _ { 0 } | < 1$ and 0 for $| w _ { 0 } | > 1$ The integrals over $C _ { k } ,$ $1 < k < n$ vanish for all ${ \boldsymbol { w } } _ { 0 }$ with $\left. w _ { 0 } \right. \ne e ^ { \lambda _ { k } }$ Suppose now that the value $\pmb { w _ { 0 } }$ is actually taken by $F ( z )$ ; inasmuch as $\Omega$ must be mapped onto an open set, we can choose $| w _ { 0 } | \neq \mathrm { a l l } \ e ^ { \lambda _ { i } } ,$ . For this ${ \boldsymbol { w } } _ { \mathbf { 0 } }$ the expression (15) must be positive. But that is possible only if $1 < | w _ { 0 } | < e ^ { \lambda _ { 1 } }$ Thus ${ \bf \lambda } \lambda _ { 1 } > 0$ and, by continuity, $0 \leq \lambda _ { i } \leqq \lambda _ { 1 } .$

From here on the proof could be completed by means of a purely topological argument. It is more instructive, however, and in fact simpler, to draw the conclusion from the argument principle. When there are simple poles on the boundary, the residue theorem continues to hold provided that the contour integral is replaced by its Cauchy principal value, and provided that the sum of the residues includes one-half of the residues on the boundary. $^ \dagger$ In the present situation the second convention means that a value taken on the boundary is counted with half its multiplicity. The computation of the principal values causes no difficulty. If $| w _ { 0 } | = e ^ { \lambda _ { k } }$ , we find that

$$
\operatorname { p r . v . } \int _ { C _ { k } } { \frac { F ^ { \prime } ( z ) ~ d z } { F ( z ) ~ - ~ w _ { 0 } } } = { \frac { 1 } { 2 } } \int _ { C _ { k } } { \frac { F ^ { \prime } ( z ) ~ d z } { F ( z ) } } ,
$$

for by elementary geometry (or direct computation)

$$
d \arg \ ( F ( z ) - w _ { 0 } ) = { \frac { 1 } { 2 } } d \arg F ( z )
$$

Consequently, the principal values in (15) are $\scriptstyle { \frac { \mathbf { 1 } } { \mathbf { 2 } } }$ for $k = 1 , \ 0$ for $2 \ \leqq$ $k \ \cong n - 1 , - { \frac { 1 } { 2 } }$ for $k = n$ .

We conclude now that each value on the circle $| w _ { 0 } | = 1$ or $| w _ { 0 } | = e ^ { \lambda _ { 1 } }$ is taken one-half time, that is to say once on the boundary; this proves that $c _ { 1 , }$ and $C _ { n }$ are mapped in a one-to-one manner and that $0 < \lambda _ { i } < \lambda _ { 1 } ,$ $i \neq 1$ , $\boldsymbol { n }$ Next, if $1 < | w _ { 0 } | < e ^ { \lambda _ { 1 } }$ , it follows that $\boldsymbol { w } _ { 0 }$ is taken either once in the interior, twice on the boundary, or once on the boundary with the multiplicity 2. On each contour $C _ { 2 } ,$ . . . , $C _ { n - 1 }$ a single-valued branch of arg $F ( z )$ can be defined, and the values of multiplicity 2 correspond to relative maxima and minima of arg $F ( z )$ . There is at least one maximum and one minimum, and there cannot be more or else $F ( z )$ would pass more than twice through the same values. Furthermore, the difference between the maximum and the minimum must be $< 2 \pi$ which shows that each contour is mapped onto a proper arc. Finally, the arcs which correspond to different contours must be disjoint.

We have proved the complete Theorem 10, and in addition we have been able to describe the correspondence of the boundaries. The significance of the theorem is that we can map $\mathfrak { a }$ onto a canonical region bounded by two circles and $n - 2$ concentric circular slits; by way of normalization the radius of the inner circle is chosen equal to 1. For a given choice of $C _ { 1 }$ and $C _ { n }$ the canonical mapping is uniquely determined up to a rotation. This follows from the fact that the system (15) has only one solution.

The shape of a canonical region of connectivity $\boldsymbol { n }$ depends on $3 n - 6$ real constants. In fact, the position and size of each slit is determined

$^ \dagger$ In Chap. 4, Sec. 5.3, the Cauchy principal value was introduced in the case of an integral over a straight line. In the case of an arbitrary analytic arc it is simplest to define the principal value by means of an auxiliary conformal mapping which transforms a subarc into a line segment. The stated generalization of the residue thcorem follows quite easily and proves that the principal value is independent of the auxiliary conformal mapping.

by three numbers, a total of ${ \bf 3 } n - { \bf 6 }$ ; the thickness of the annulus gives one additional parameter, but another parameter must be discounted to allow for the arbitrary rotation.

# EXERCISES

1. Prove directly that two circular annuli are conformally equivalent if and only if the ratios of their radii are equal.

2. Prove that $\alpha _ { i j } = \alpha _ { j i }$ Hint: Apply Theorem 21, Chap. 4.

5.2. Green's Function. We suppose again that $\Omega$ is a region of finite connectivity, and inasmuch as preliminary conformal mappings will be permissible we can assume that $\Omega$ is bounded by analytic contours $C _ { 1 } ,$ . . . , $C _ { n }$ ; this time the case $\mathscr { n } = 1$ will be included.

We consider a point $z _ { 0 } \epsilon \Omega$ and solve the Dirichlet problem in $\Omega$ with the boundary values log $| \boldsymbol { \gamma } - \boldsymbol { \mu } _ { 0 } |$ . The solution is denoted by $\mathit { G } ( z )$ , but the main interest is attached to the function $g ( z ) = G ( z ) - \log | z - z _ { 0 } |$ known as the Green's function of $\Omega$ with pole at ${ \mathfrak { z } } _ { 0 }$ When the dependence on $\scriptstyle z _ { 0 }$ is emphasized, it is denoted by $g ( z , z _ { 0 } )$ .

The Green's function is harmonic in $\Omega$ except at $z _ { 0 } ,$ and it vanishes on the boundary. In a neighborhood of ${ \mathit { z } } _ { 0 }$ it differs from $- \log | z - z _ { 0 } |$ by a harmonic function. By these properties $g ( z )$ is uniquely determined. In fact, if $g _ { 1 } ( z )$ has the same properties, then $g - g _ { 1 }$ is harmonic throughout $\Omega$ and vanishes on the boundary. By the maximum principle it follows that $g _ { 1 }$ is identically equal to $g$ .

If two regions are conformally equivalent, then the Green's functions with corresponding poles are equal at points which correspond to each other. To be more explicit, let $z = z ( \zeta )$ define a one-to-one conformal mapping of a region $\pmb { \Omega } ^ { \prime }$ in the $\boldsymbol { \gamma }$ -plane onto a region $\mathfrak { a }$ in the $z$ plane. Choose a point $\zeta _ { 0 } \in \Omega ^ { \prime }$ and denote by $g ( z , z _ { 0 } )$ the Green's function of $\Omega$ with pole at $z _ { 0 } = z ( \zeta _ { 0 } )$ . It is claimed that $g ( z ( \zeta ) , z _ { 0 } )$ is the Green's function of $\Omega ^ { \prime }$ To begin with, if $\boldsymbol { \xi }$ tends to a boundary point, then $z ( \zeta )$ approaches the boundary of $\Omega _ { i }$ and hence $g ( z ( \zeta ) , z _ { 0 } )$ has the boundary values zero. As to the behavior at $\boldsymbol { \tau _ { 0 } }$ we know that $g ( z ( \zeta ) , z _ { 0 } )$ differs from $- \mathrm { l o g } \ \lvert z ( \zeta ) - z ( \zeta _ { 0 } ) \rvert$ by a harmonic function of $z ( \zeta )$ , and hence by a harmonic function of $\boldsymbol { \xi }$ But the difference log $| z ( \xi ) - z ( \xi _ { 0 } ) | - \log | \xi - \xi _ { 0 } |$ is also harmonic, and it follows that $g ( z ( \zeta ) , z _ { 0 } )$ has the desired behavior at $\boldsymbol { \zeta } _ { 0 }$ We have proved that the Green's function is invariant under conformal mappings, and it is in view of this invariance that preliminary conformal mappings can be performed at will.

In the case of a simply connected region there is a simple connection between Green's function and the Riemann mapping function. For the unit disk $\vert w \vert < 1$ the Green's function with respect to the origin is evidently $- \log \left| w \right|$ Therefore, if $\textit { w } = f ( z )$ maps $\pmb { \Omega }$ onto the unit disk with $z _ { 0 }$ going into the origin, we find by the invariance that

$$
g ( z , z _ { 0 } ) = - \log | f ( z ) | .
$$

Conversely, if $g ( z , z _ { 0 } )$ is known, the mapping function can be determined.

The Green's function has an important symmetry property. Given two points $z _ { 1 } , \ z _ { 2 } \in \Omega$ ,we write for short $g ( { \pmb { \mathscr { L } } } , z _ { 1 } ) = g _ { 1 } , g ( z , z _ { 2 } ) = g _ { 2 }$ By Theorem 21, Chap. 4, the differential $g _ { 1 } { ^ { * } d } g _ { 2 } - g _ { 2 } { ^ { * } d } g _ { 1 }$ is locally exact in the region obtained by omitting the points ${ \pmb z _ { 1 } }$ and ${ z _ { 2 } }$ from $\Omega$ If $\pmb { c _ { 1 } }$ and $c _ { 2 }$ are small circles about ${ \pmb z _ { 1 } }$ and $z _ { 2 } ,$ described in the positive sense, the cycle $C - c _ { 1 } - c _ { 2 }$ is homologous to zero (as before, $\begin{array} { r } { C = C _ { 1 } + \cdots + C _ { n } ) . } \end{array}$ . Since ${ \mathfrak { g } } _ { 1 }$ and ${ \pmb g } _ { 2 }$ vanish on $c$ , we conclude that

$$
\int _ { c _ { 1 } + c _ { 2 } } g _ { 1 } \ : { } ^ { * } d g _ { 2 } \ : - \ : g _ { 2 } \ : { } ^ { * } d g _ { 1 } = 0 .
$$

Introducing $G _ { 1 } = g _ { 1 } + \log | z - z _ { 1 } |$ we have ${ } ^ { * } d g _ { 1 } = { } ^ { * } d G _ { 1 } - d \arg \left( z - z _ { 1 } \right)$ and find

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \int _ { c _ { 1 } } g _ { 1 } { * d g _ { 2 } } - g _ { 2 } { * d g _ { 1 } } = \int _ { c _ { 1 } } G _ { 1 } { * d g _ { 2 } } - g _ { 2 } { * d G _ { 1 } } - \int _ { c _ { 1 } } \log | z - z _ { 1 } | { * d g _ { 2 } } \quad \quad } \\ { \qquad + \int _ { c _ { 1 } } g _ { 2 } d \arg ( z - z _ { 1 } ) . \quad } \end{array}
$$

On the right-hand side the first integral vanishes because $G _ { 1 }$ and ${ \pmb g } _ { 2 }$ are harmonic inside $\pmb { c _ { 1 } }$ , and the second integral vanishes because $| z - z _ { 1 } |$ is constant on $\pmb { c _ { 1 } }$ and $^ { * } d g _ { 2 }$ is an exact differential in a neighborhood of ${ \boldsymbol { z } } _ { 1 }$ . The last integral equals $2 \pi g _ { 2 } ( z _ { 1 } )$ by the mean-value property of harmonic functions. In a symmetric way the integral over $\pmb { c _ { 2 } }$ must equal $- 2 \pi g _ { 1 } ( z _ { 2 } )$ and it is proved that $g _ { 2 } ( z _ { 1 } ) - g _ { 1 } ( z _ { 2 } ) ~ = 0$ or

$$
g ( z _ { 1 } , z _ { 2 } ) = g ( z _ { 2 } , z _ { 1 } ) .
$$

Because of this symmetry property the Green's function $g ( z , z _ { 0 } )$ is harmonic also in the second variable.

The conjugate function of $g ( z , z _ { 0 } )$ , denoted by $h ( z , z _ { 0 } )$ , is of course multiple-valued. It has above all the period $2 \pi$ along a small circle $\pmb { c }$ about ${ z _ { 0 } }$ , In addition, it has the periods

$$
P _ { k } ( z _ { 0 } ) = \int _ { C _ { k } } d h \ ( z , z _ { 0 } ) = \int _ { C _ { k } } \ast _ { d g } \ ( z , z _ { 0 } ) \qquad ( k = 1 , \ . \ . \ . \ , n ) .
$$

Lemma 3. The period $P _ { k } ( z _ { 0 } )$ equals the harmonic measure $\omega _ { k } ( z _ { 0 } )$ multiplied by $2 \pi$ .

The proof is another application of Theorem 21, Chap. 4. We express the fact that the integral of $\omega _ { k } \ ^ { * } d g \_ { } - \ g \ ^ { * } d \omega _ { k }$ over $c - \pmb { c }$ must vanish. The integral over $c$ reduces to $P _ { k } ( z _ { 0 } )$ , and by the same computation as above the integral over $\pmb { c }$ equals $2 \pi \omega _ { k } ( c _ { 0 } )$ : Hence $P _ { k } ( z _ { 0 } ) = 2 \pi \omega _ { k } ( z _ { 0 } )$ .

5.3. Parallel Slit Regions. A little more explicitly than before, let us write

$$
g ( z , z _ { 0 } ) \ = G ( z , z _ { 0 } ) \ - \log | z - z _ { 0 } |
$$

with $z _ { 0 } = x _ { 0 } + i y _ { 0 } \epsilon \Omega .$ We know that $G ( z , z _ { 0 } )$ is symmetric, and harmonic in each variable; as a function of $\pmb { z }$ it has the boundary values $\log | \xi - z _ { 0 } |$

Consider the difference quotient $Q ( z , h ) = ( G ( z , z _ { 0 } + h ) - G ( z , z _ { 0 } ) ) / h$ where we choose $\pmb { h }$ real and so small that $z _ { 0 } + h$ is still in $\Omega$ ,This is a harmonic function of $\boldsymbol { z }$ with boundary values $( \log | \xi - z _ { 0 } - h | -$ $\log | \{ - z _ { 0 } | ) / h$ As $h \to 0$ these boundary values tend uniformly to $\partial / \partial x _ { 0 } \log | \xi _ { \mathrm { s } } - z _ { 0 } | = \mathrm { R e } \ 1 / ( \xi - z _ { 0 } )$ . It follows by the maximum-minimum principle that $Q ( z , h )$ tends to its limit $( \partial / \partial x _ { 0 } ) G ( z , z _ { 0 } )$ uniformly, not only on compact sets, but on all of $\Omega$ If we include the boundary values, we have thus uniform convergence on the closure $\mathfrak { Q } -$ , which is a compact set. The conclusion is that $( \partial / \partial x _ { 0 } ) G ( z , z _ { 0 } )$ is harmonic in $\mathfrak { a }$ , as a function of $z _ { \ast }$ , and that it has the boundary values $\mathrm { R e } 1 / ( \xi - z _ { 0 } )$ If we compare with (16) it follows that $u _ { 1 } ( z ) = ( \partial / \partial x _ { 0 } ) g ( z , z _ { 0 } )$ is harmonic for $z \neq z _ { 0 } ,$ continuously zero on the boundary, and differs from Re $1 / ( z - z _ { 0 } )$ by a harmonic function.

The conjugate differential of $u _ { 1 } ( z )$ has certain periods $\scriptstyle A _ { k }$ along the contours $c _ { k } ,$ But it is easy to construct a linear combination of $u _ { 1 } ( z )$ and the harmonic measures $\omega _ { j } ( z )$ whose conjugate differential is free from periods. Indeed, $u _ { 1 } + \lambda _ { 1 } \omega _ { 1 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + \lambda _ { n - 1 } \omega _ { n - 1 }$ has this property provided that

$$
\lambda _ { 1 } \alpha _ { 1 k } + \lambda _ { 2 } \alpha _ { 2 k } + { \bf \alpha } \cdot { \bf \alpha } \cdot { \bf \alpha } \cdot { \bf \alpha } \lambda _ { n - 1 , k } = - A _ { k } \qquad ( k = 1 , { \bf \alpha } \cdot { \bf \alpha } \cdot { \bf \alpha } , n - 1 ) .
$$

We know already that this inhomogeneous system of equations always has a solution. We have thus established the existence of a function $p ( z )$ which is single-valued and analytic in $\Omega$ , except for a simple pole with the residue 1 at $z _ { 0 } ,$ and whose real part is constant on each contour. By these requirements $p ( z )$ is uniquely determined up to an additive constant.

By differentiation with respect to ${ \mathfrak { y } } _ { \mathfrak { d } }$ we conclude quite similarly that $v _ { 2 } ( z ) = - ( \partial / \partial y _ { 0 } ) g ( z , z _ { 0 } )$ vanishes on the boundary and has the same singularity as I $\textbf { m } 1 / ( z - z _ { 0 } )$ . If a suitable linear combination of harmonic measures is added, the conjugate function becomes single-valued.

![](images/e7b3d5f7ebd337b96473a437a063183da80c123feb3dae374138e8b0cbe063b0.jpg)  
FIG. 33. Parallel slit regions.

Hence there exists a single-valued analytic function $q ( z )$ with the singular part $1 / ( z - z _ { 0 } )$ whose imaginary part is constant on each contour.

The functions $p ( z )$ and $q ( z )$ lead to simple canonical mappings

Theorem 1l. The mappings determined by $p ( z )$ and $q ( \tilde { z } )$ are one to one, and the image of $\Omega$ is a slit region whose complement consists of n vertical or horizontal segments, respectively (Fig. 33a, b).

The proof is quite similar to that of Theorem 10. This time the expression

$$
\sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { n } { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } } \int _ { C _ { k } } { \frac { p ^ { \prime } ( z ) \ d z } { p ( z ) \ - \ w _ { 0 } } }
$$

represents the number of zeros of $p ( z ) - w _ { 0 }$ minus the number of poles. But it is easy to see that (17) vanishes for all ${ \boldsymbol { w } } _ { \mathrm { { 0 } } } ,$ including boundary values. In the latter case the principal value must be formed, but if $\boldsymbol { w } _ { 0 }$ is taken on $C _ { k }$ the imaginary part of $p ^ { \prime } d z / ( p - w _ { 0 } )$ vanishes along $C _ { k }$ and there is no difficulty whatsoever. Since there is exactly one pole we conclude that $p ( z )$ takes every value once in the interior of $\mathfrak { a }$ , twice on the boundary, or once on the boundary with the multiplicity 2. The rest of the proof is an exact duplication of the earlier reasoning. The proof remains valid for $q ( z )$ without change.

Parallel slit regions may be thought of as canonical regions, but they are not all conformally inequivalent, even if it is required that the point at $\infty$ should correspond to itself. For instance, the mappings by $p ( z )$ and $i q ( z )$ lead to vertical slit regions which are different, but conformally equivalent. It is only for mappings with the same residue at ${ z _ { 0 } }$ that the slit mappings are uniquely determined, except for a parallel translation.

# EXERCISES

1. Prove that $g ( z , z _ { 0 } )$ is simultaneously continuous in both variables, for $z \neq z _ { 0 }$ Hint: Apply the maximum-minimum principle to $G ( z , z _ { 0 } )$ .

Show that the function $e ^ { - i \alpha } ( q \ \cos \ \alpha + i p \ \sin _ { \alpha } \alpha )$ maps $\Omega$ onto a region bounded by inclined slits.

$^ { * } { \mathfrak { s } } _ { \mathfrak { n } }$ Using Ex. 2, show that $p + q$ maps $\pmb { \Omega }$ in a one-to-one manner onto a region bounded by convex contours. Comments:

(i)A closed curve is said to be convex if it intersects every straight line at most twice.

(ii) To prove that the image of $C _ { k }$ under $p + q$ is convex we need only show that for every $_ { \alpha }$ the function Re $( p + q ) e ^ { i \alpha }$ takes no value more than twice on $\scriptstyle { C _ { k } }$ But Re $( p + q ) e ^ { i \alpha }$ differs from Re $( q \cos \alpha + i p \sin \alpha )$ only by a constant, and the desired conclusion follows by the properties of the mapping function in Ex. 2.

(iii) Finally, the argument principle can be used to show that the images of the contours $C _ { k }$ have winding number 0 with respect to all values of $p + q$ , This implies, in particular, that the convex curves lie outside of each other.

# 7 ELLIPTIC FUNCTIONS

# 1. SIMPLY PERIODIC FUNCTIONS

A function $\pmb { f } ( z )$ is said to be periodic with period $\omega \neq 0$ if

$$
f ( z + \omega ) = f ( z )
$$

for all $\pmb { z }$ For instance, $e ^ { z }$ has the period $2 \pi i$ and sin $_ { z }$ and cos $\pmb { z }$ have the period $2 \pi$ To be more precise, we are interested only in analytic or meromorphic functions $\pmb { f } ( z )$ , and they shall be considered in a region $\Omega$ which is mapped onto itself by the translation $z  z + \omega$ .

If $\pmb { \omega }$ is a period, so are all integral multiples nw. There may be other periods as well, but for the present we focus our attention exclusively on the periods nw. From this point of view we shall call $\pmb { f } ( z )$ a simply periodic function with period $\omega ,$ In particular, it is irrelevant whether $\pmb { \omega }$ is itself a multiple of another period.

1.1. Representation by Exponentials. The simplest function with period $\pmb { \omega }$ is the exponential $e ^ { 2 \pi i z / \omega }$ . It is a fundamental fact that any function with period $\pmb { \omega }$ can be expressed in terms of this particular function.

Let $\Omega$ be a region with the property that $z \in \Omega$ implies $z + \omega \epsilon \Omega$ and $z - \omega \epsilon \Omega$ We define $\Omega ^ { \prime }$ in the $\boldsymbol { \tau }$ -plane to be the image of $\Omega$ under the mapping $\xi = e ^ { 2 \pi i z / \omega }$ ; it is obviously a region. For instance, if $\Omega$ is the whole plane, then $\Omega ^ { \prime }$ is the plane punctured at O. If $\Omega$ is a parallel strip, defined by $a < \mathrm { I m } ( 2 \pi z / \omega ) < b _ { \mathrm { ( } }$ then $\pmb { \Omega } ^ { \prime }$ is the annulus $e ^ { - b } < | \xi | < e ^ { - a }$ .

Suppose that $f ( z )$ is meromorphic in $\Omega$ and has the period $\omega .$ Then there exists a unique function $\pmb { F }$ in $\Omega ^ { \prime }$ such that

$$
f ( z ) = F ( e ^ { 2 \pi i z / \omega } ) .
$$

Indeed, to determine $\pmb { F } ( \pmb { \zeta } )$ we write $\xi = e ^ { 2 \pi i z / \omega }$ $z$ is unique up to an additive multiple of $\pmb { \omega }$ and this multple ds not infuce the value $f ( z )$ . It is evident that $\pmb { F }$ is meromorphic. Conversely, if ${ \pmb F }$ is meromorphic in $\Omega ^ { \prime }$ , then (1) defines a meromorphic function $f$ with period $\boldsymbol { \omega }$ .

1.2. The Fourier Development. Assume that $\Omega ^ { \prime }$ contains an annulus $r _ { 1 } < | \boldsymbol { \xi } | < r _ { 2 }$ in which ${ \pmb F }$ has no poles. In this annulus $\pmb { F }$ has a Laurent development

$$
F ( \xi ) = \sum _ { n = - \infty } ^ { \infty } c _ { n } \xi ^ { n } ,
$$

and we obtain

$$
f ( z ) = \sum _ { - \infty } ^ { \infty } c _ { n } e ^ { 2 \pi i n z / \omega } .
$$

This is the complex Fourier development of $f ( z )$ , valid in the parallel srip that corresponds to the given annulus.

Th ce Chap.  Sec. 1are v y

$$
c _ { n } = \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } \int _ { | \xi | = r } F ( \xi ) \xi ^ { - n - 1 } d \xi , \qquad ( r _ { 1 } < r < r _ { 2 } ) ,
$$

and by change of variable this becomes

$$
c _ { n } = { \frac { 1 } { \omega } } \int _ { a } ^ { a + \omega } f ( z ) e ^ { - 2 \pi i n z / \omega } d z .
$$

Here $^ a$ is an arbitrary point in the parallel strip, and the integration is along any path from $^ a$ to $a + \omega$ which remains within the strip. If $f ( z )$ is analytic in the whole plane, the same Fourier development is valid everywhere.

1.3. Functions of Finite Order. When $\Omega$ is the whole plane $F ( \xi )$ has isolated singularities at $\xi = 0$ and $\xi = \infty$ . If both these singularities are inessential, that is, either removable singularities or poles, then ${ \pmb F }$ is a rational function. We say in this case that $f$ has finite order, equal to the order of $\pmb { F }$ .

We recall that a rational function assumes every complex value, including $\infty$ , the same number of times, provided that we observe the usual multiplicity convention. We obtain a similar result for simply periodic functions of finite order if we agree not to distinguish between $\pmb { \mathscr { z } }$ and $z + \omega$ For convenient terminology, let us say that $z + n \omega$ is equivalent to ${ \pmb z } ,$ If $f$ is of order $\pmb { m }$ we find that every complex value $c \neq F ( 0 )$ and $F ( \infty )$ is assumed at $\mathbf { \nabla } m$ inequivalent points, with due count of multiplicities. We observe further that $f ( z ) \to F ( 0 )$ when $\mathrm { I m } ~ ( z / \omega ) $ $- \infty$ and $f ( z ) \to F \left( \infty \right)$ when $\mathrm { I m } \langle z / \omega \rangle  \infty$ . If we are willing to agree that these values are also "assumed" (with proper multiplicity), we can maintain that all complex values are assumed exactly $_ m$ times.

For another interpretation we may consider the period strip, defined by $0 \leq \mathrm { I m } \ ( z / \omega ) < 2 \pi$ Since this strip contains only one representative from each equivalence class we find that $f ( z )$ assumes each complex value $\mathbf { \nabla } m$ times in the period strip, except that the values $F ( 0 )$ and $F ( \infty )$ require a special convention.

# 2. DOUBLY PERIODIC FUNCTIONS

The terms elliptic function and doubly periodic function are interchangeable; we have already met examples of such functions in connection with the conformal mapping of rectangles and certain triangles (Chap. 6, Sec. 2). Elliptic functions have been the object of very extensive study, partly because of their function theoretic properties and partly because of their importance in algebra and number theory. Our introduction to the topic covers only the most elementary aspects.

2.1. The Period Module. Let $f ( z )$ be meromorphic in the whole plane. We shall examine the set $\pmb { M }$ of all its periods. If $\omega$ is a period, so are all integral multiples nw, and if $\omega _ { 1 }$ and $\omega _ { 2 }$ belong to $M$ , so does $\omega _ { 1 } + \omega _ { 2 }$ ; as a consequence, all linear combinations $n _ { 1 } \omega _ { 1 } + n _ { 2 } \omega _ { 2 }$ are in $\mathscr { M }$ In algebra, a set with these properties is called a module (more precisely: a module over the integers), and we shall call $M$ the period module of $f .$

Apart from the trivial case of a constant function, $M$ has also a topological property: all its points are isolated. In fact, since $f ( \omega ) = f ( 0 )$ for all $\omega \in \textit { M }$ the existence of a finite accumulation point would immediately imply that $f$ is constant. A module with isolated points is said to be discrete.

Our first step is to determine all discrete modules.

Theorem l. A discrete module consists either of zero alone, of the integral multiples nw of a single complex number $\omega \neq 0$ , or of all linear combinations $n _ { 1 } \omega + n _ { 2 } \omega _ { 2 }$ with integral coefficients of two numbers $\omega _ { 1 }$ $\omega _ { 2 }$ with nonreal ratio $\omega _ { 2 } / \omega _ { 1 }$

As soon as $M$ contains a number $\omega \neq 0$ it also contains one, call it $\omega _ { 1 } ,$ whose absolute value is a minimum. Indeed, if $\boldsymbol { r }$ is large enough the disk $| z | \leq r$ contains a point from $M$ , other than 0. Because the points are isolated there are only a finite number of such points, and we choose $\omega _ { 1 }$ to be one closest to the origin (the reader may show that there are always 2, 4, or 6 closest points). The multiples $n \omega _ { 1 }$ are also in $M$ , and these may be all.

Suppose now that there exists an ω € $M$ which is not an integral multiple of $\omega _ { 1 }$ Among all such there is one, $\omega _ { 2 } ,$ whose absolute value is smallest. We claim that $\omega _ { 2 } / \omega _ { 1 }$ is not real. If it were, there would exist an integer $n$ such that $n < \omega _ { 2 } / \omega _ { 1 } < n + 1$ This would give $0 <$ $| n \omega _ { 1 } - \omega _ { 2 } | < | \omega _ { 1 } | ,$ an obvious contradiction.

It can now be concluded that all numbers in $M$ are of the form $\scriptstyle n _ { 1 } \omega _ { 1 } +$ $n _ { 2 } \omega _ { 2 }$ First of all, because $\omega _ { 2 } / \omega _ { 1 }$ is nonreal any complex number ω can be written in the form $\lambda _ { 1 } \omega _ { 1 } + \lambda _ { 2 } \omega _ { 2 }$ with real $\lambda _ { 1 }$ and $\lambda _ { 2 }$ To see this we need only solve the equations

$$
\begin{array} { l } { \omega = \lambda _ { 1 } \omega _ { 1 } + \lambda _ { 2 } \omega _ { 2 } } \\ { \bar { \omega } = \lambda _ { 1 } \bar { \omega } _ { 1 } + \lambda _ { 2 } \bar { \omega } _ { 2 } . } \end{array}
$$

Since the determinant $\omega _ { 1 } \bar { \omega } _ { 2 } - \omega _ { 2 } \bar { \omega } _ { 1 }$ is $\yen 0$ the system has a unique solution $( \lambda _ { 1 } , \lambda _ { 2 } )$ ; but $( \bar { \lambda } _ { 1 } , \bar { \lambda } _ { 2 } )$ is also a solution, and we conclude that $\bf { \lambda } _ { \bf { \lambda } } \lambda _ { 1 }$ and $\lambda _ { 2 }$ are real. To continue the proof, there exist integers $m _ { 1 } , ~ m _ { 2 }$ such that $\left| { \lambda } _ { 1 } - m _ { 1 } \right| \leqq { \frac { 1 } { 2 } }$ , $| \lambda _ { 2 } - m _ { 2 } | \leq \frac { 1 } { 2 } .$ If $\omega$ belongs to $M$ , so does

$$
\omega ^ { \prime } = \omega - m _ { 1 } \omega _ { 1 } - m _ { 2 } \omega _ { 2 } .
$$

We have $| \omega ^ { \prime } | < \frac { 1 } { 2 } | \omega _ { 1 } | + \frac { 1 } { 2 } | \omega _ { 2 } | \le | \omega _ { 2 } |$ where the first inequality is strict because $\omega _ { 2 }$ is not a real multiple of $\omega _ { 1 } ,$ By the way $\omega _ { 2 }$ was chosen it follows that $\omega ^ { \prime }$ must an integral multiple of $\omega _ { 1 } ,$ and hence ω has the asserted form.

2.2. Unimodular Transformations. We assume henceforth that it is the third alternative in Theorem 1 that occurs. The pair $( \omega _ { 1 } , \omega _ { 2 } )$ has the property that any $\omega \in M$ has a unique representation of the form $\omega = n _ { 1 } \omega _ { 1 } + n _ { 2 } \omega _ { 2 }$ Any pair with this property will be called a basis of $M$ (even if it is not determined by the construction in the proof of Theorem 1).

We investigate the relation between two bases $( \omega _ { 1 } , \omega _ { 2 } )$ and $( \omega _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } , \omega _ { 2 } ^ { \prime } )$ Because $( \omega _ { 1 } , \omega _ { 2 } )$ is a basis there exist integers $a , b , c , d$ such that

$$
\begin{array} { l l } { { \omega _ { \mathrm { 2 } } ^ { \prime } = a \omega _ { \mathrm { 2 } } + b \omega _ { \mathrm { 1 } } } } \\ { { \omega _ { \mathrm { 1 } } ^ { \prime } = c \omega _ { \mathrm { 2 } } + d \omega _ { \mathrm { 1 } } . } } \end{array}
$$

We prefer to write these equations in matrix form

$$
{ \binom { \omega _ { 2 } ^ { \prime } } { \omega _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } } } = { \binom { a } { c } } { \binom { b } { d } } { \binom { \omega _ { 2 } } { \omega _ { 1 } } } .
$$

$$
\binom { \omega _ { 2 } ^ { \prime } } { \omega _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } } \bar { \omega } _ { 2 } ^ { \prime } \biggr ) = \binom { a } { c } b \binom { \omega _ { 2 } } { \omega _ { 1 } } \bar { \omega } _ { 1 } \biggr ) \cdot
$$

Since $( \omega _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } , \omega _ { 2 } ^ { \prime } )$ is also a basis we have similarly

$$
\binom { \omega _ { 2 } } { \omega _ { 1 } } \quad \bar { \omega } _ { 2 } ^ { \prime } \biggr ) = \binom { a ^ { \prime } } { c ^ { \prime } } \quad b ^ { \prime } \biggr ) \binom { \omega _ { 2 } ^ { \prime } } { \omega _ { 2 } ^ { \prime } } \quad \tilde { \omega } _ { 2 } ^ { \prime } \biggr )
$$

with integral $a ^ { \prime } , b ^ { \prime } , c ^ { \prime } , d ^ { \prime }$ .

From (3) and (4) we obtain

$$
\binom { \omega _ { 2 } \quad \bar { \omega } _ { 2 } } { \omega _ { 1 } } = \binom { a ^ { \prime } \quad b ^ { \prime } } { c ^ { \prime } } \binom { a } { c } \binom { b } { d } \binom { \omega _ { 2 } \quad \bar { \omega } _ { 2 } } { \omega _ { 1 } } .
$$

Here the determinant $\omega _ { 2 } \bar { \omega } _ { 1 } - \omega _ { 1 } \bar { \omega } _ { 2 } \mathrm { i s } \neq 0$ , for otherwise any two numbers in the module woyld have a real ratio, contrary to assumption. A matrix with determinant $\neq \mathbf { 0 }$ has an inverse matrix, and if we multiply (5) by the inverse of $\binom { \omega _ { 2 } \bar { \omega } _ { 2 } } { \omega _ { 1 } \bar { \omega } _ { 1 } }$ we obtain

$$
{ \binom { a ^ { \prime } } { c ^ { \prime } } } \quad b ^ { \prime } \rangle { \binom { a } { c } } \quad b \rangle = { \binom { 1 } { 0 } } \quad 0 \rangle .
$$

The matrics ${ \left( \begin{array} { l l } { a } & { b } \\ { c } & { d } \end{array} \right) } { \mathrm { a n d } } \left( { a ^ { \prime } } \begin{array} { l l } { \phantom { a ^ { \prime } } } & { b ^ { \prime } } \\ { c ^ { \prime } } & { d ^ { \prime } } \end{array} \right)$ ne I ticular, their determinants must satisfy

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \left| \begin{array} { l l } { a ^ { \prime } } & { b ^ { \prime } } \\ { c ^ { \prime } } & { d ^ { \prime } } \end{array} \right| \cdot \left| \begin{array} { l l } { a } & { b } \\ { c } & { d } \end{array} \right| = 1 , } \end{array}
$$

and since both are integers we must have

$$
\left| \begin{array} { l l } { { a } } & { { b } } \\ { { c } } & { { d } } \end{array} \right| = \left| \begin{array} { l l } { { a ^ { \prime } } } & { { b ^ { \prime } } } \\ { { c ^ { \prime } } } & { { d ^ { \prime } } } \end{array} \right| = \pm { \bf \sigma } \pm { \bf 1 } .
$$

Linear transformations of the form (2) with integral coefficients and determinant $\pm 1$ are said to be unimodular. We have proved:

Any two bases of the same module are connected by a unimodular transformation.

Geometrically, it is natural to consider the parallelogram spanned by a basis $( \omega _ { 1 } , \omega _ { 2 } )$ in its relation to the lattice formed by all numbers in the module. Figure 34 shows two bases of the same module. Observe that the parallelograms have equal area.

We note here that the unimodular matrices, or the corresponding linear transformations, form a group, the modular group.

![](images/613a3c3cb5cd7ae154dc1b15cfecffd107b38b081c0733816fa39adc790ac460.jpg)  
FIG. 34. Period module.

2.3. The Canonical Basis. Among all possible bases of $\tilde { M }$ it is possible to single out one, almost uniquely, to be called the canonical basis. It will not always be necessary , or even desirable, to use such a special basis, but it is important to know that one exists. Except for minor adjustments it will be the basis introduced in the course of the proof of Theorem 1.

Theorem 2. There exists a basis $( \omega _ { 1 } , \omega _ { 2 } )$ such that the ratio $\tau = \omega _ { 2 } / \omega _ { 1 }$ satisfies the following conditions: (i) Im $\tau > 0$ (ii) $- { \scriptstyle { \frac { 1 } { 2 } } } < \mathrm { R e } ~ \tau \leq { \frac { 1 } { 2 } } ,$ (iii) $| \tau | \geq 1$ , (iv) $ { \mathrm { R e } } \tau \geq 0$ if $| \tau | = 1$ .The ratio $\pmb { \tau }$ is uniquely determined by these conditions, and there is a choice of two, four, or sir corresponding bases.

Proof. If we select $\omega _ { 1 }$ and $\omega _ { 2 }$ as in the proof of Theorem 1, then $| \omega _ { 1 } | \leqq$ $| \omega _ { 2 } | , \ | \omega _ { 2 } | \ \leq \ | \omega _ { 1 } + \omega _ { 2 } |$ ,and $\left| \omega _ { 2 } \right| \le \left| \omega _ { 1 } - \omega _ { 2 } \right|$ In terms of $\pmb { \tau }$ these conditions are equivalent to $| \tau | \geq 1$ and $| \mathrm { R e } \ \tau | \le \frac { 1 } { 2 }$ If $\mathrm { I m } \tau < 0$ we replace $( \omega _ { 1 } , \omega _ { 2 } )$ by $( - \omega _ { 1 } , \omega _ { 2 } )$ ; this makes $ { \mathrm { I m } } \tau > 0$ without changing the condition on Re $\pmb { \tau }$ If Re $\tau = - { \frac { 1 } { 2 } }$ we replace the basis by $( \omega _ { 1 } , \omega _ { 1 } + \omega _ { 2 } )$ , and if $| \tau | = 1$ , $ { \mathrm { R e } } \tau < 0$ we replace it by $( - \omega _ { 2 } , \omega _ { 1 } )$ . After these minor changes all the conditions are satisfied.

We have seen that the most general change of basis is by a uninodular transformation. If the new ratio is $\scriptstyle { \tau ^ { \prime } }$ we obtain

$$
\tau ^ { \prime } = { \frac { a \tau + b } { c \tau + d } }
$$

with $\ a d - b c = \pm \textbf { 1 }$ Simple computation gives

$$
\operatorname { I m } \tau ^ { \prime } = { \frac { \pm \operatorname { I m } \tau } { | c \tau + d | ^ { 2 } } }
$$

where the sign is the same as that of $a d - b c ,$

Suppose that both $\pmb { \tau }$ and $\scriptstyle { \tau ^ { \prime } }$ satisfy conditions (i) to (iv). We shall show that they must then be equal. Our first remark is that by condition (i) it is the upper sign that is valid in (7), and hence $a d - b c = 1$ . Second, because $\pmb { \tau }$ and ${ \boldsymbol { \tau } } ^ { \prime }$ play symmetric roles we are free to assume that $\operatorname { I m } \tau ^ { \prime } \leq \operatorname { I m } \tau$ It then follows from (7) that $\{ c \tau + d \} \lessgtr 1$ Because $c$ and $^ d$ are integers there are very few possibilities for this inequality to hold.

One such possibility is to have $\pmb { c } = \mathbf { 0 }$ $d = \pm 1$ The relation $a d - b c = 1$ reduces to ${ a d = 1 }$ , and because $^ { a }$ and $d$ are integers we have either $a = d = \mathbf { i } { \widetilde { \ } }$ or $a = d = - 1$ . Equation (6) becomes $\tau ^ { \prime } = \tau \pm b _ { \mathrm { { i } } }$ and by use of condition (ii) it follows that $| b | = | \mathrm { R e } \ \tau ^ { \prime } - \mathrm { R e } \ \tau | < 1$ . Therefore, and because $^ { b }$ is an integer, $\mathbf { \nabla } b = \mathbf { 0 }$ and $\tau ^ { \prime } = \tau$ .

A second possibility is to have $d = \mathbf { 0 }$ . Then $b c = - 1 \mathrm { \ s o }$ that either $\delta = { \bf 1 } , c - - { \bf - 1 }$ o1 $\mathbf { \partial } _ { t } ^ { t } = - \mathbf { i } , c = \mathbf { i }$ The inequality $| c \tau + d | \le 1$ becomes $| \pmb { \tau } | \leq \mathbf { 1 }$ , and according to condition (iii) we have thus $| \tau | = 1$ Equation reads $\tau ^ { \prime } = \pm a - 1 / \tau ,$ which we may write as ${ \pmb \tau } ^ { \prime } = \pm { \pmb a } - { \bar { \tau } } .$ It follows by (ii) that $| a | = | \mathrm { R e } \tau ^ { \prime } + \mathrm { R e } \tau | < 1$ ,except when $\tau = \tau ^ { \prime } = e ^ { \pi i / 3 }$ . Save for this exception we have thus $a = 0 , \tau ^ { \prime } = - \bar { \tau } .$ But by condition (iv) this can hold only if $\tau = \tau ^ { \prime } = i$ .

There remains only the possibility that $c$ and $d$ are both $\yen 0$ ,in which case $| c d | \geq 1$ We conclude by use of (ii) and (ii) that

$$
\begin{array} { r } { | c \tau + d | ^ { 2 } = c ^ { 2 } | \tau | ^ { 2 } + d ^ { 2 } + 2 c d \mathrm { ~ R e ~ } \tau \geq c ^ { 2 } + d ^ { 2 } - | c d | \eqno = ~ ( | c | - | d | ) ^ { 2 } + | c d | \geq 1 . } \\ { = ~ ( | c | - | d | ) ^ { 2 } + | c d | \geq 1 . } \end{array}
$$

Because of the opposite inequality $| c \tau + d | \le 1$ the equality sign must hold in all places.This is possible only $| \tau | = 1$ and $\mathrm { R e } \tau = { \frac { 1 } { 2 } }$ , that is for $\tau = e ^ { \pi i / 3 }$ . However, under the restrictions (i) to (iv) this value corresponds to a strict minimum of $\operatorname { I m } \tau$ Therefore the assumption ${ \mathbf { I m } } \tau ^ { \prime } \leqq$ $\operatorname { I m } \tau$ gives again $\scriptstyle \tau ^ { \prime } = \tau$ .

We have proved that $\pmb { \tau }$ is unique, and Fig. 35 shows the part of the plane to which a canonical $\pmb { \tau }$ must belong. The canonical basis $( \omega _ { 1 } , \omega _ { 2 } )$ can always be replaced by $( - \omega _ { 1 } , - \omega _ { 2 } )$ . There are other bases with the same $\pmb { \tau }$ if and only if $\pmb { \tau }$ is a fixpoint of a unimodular transformation (6). This happens only for $\tau = i \mathrm { a n d } \tau = e ^ { \pi i / 3 }$ ; the former is a fixpoint of $- 1 / \tau ,$ the latter of $- ( \tau + 1 ) / \tau$ and of $- 1 / ( \tau + 1 )$ . These are the multiple shoices referred to in the theorem.

FIG. 35. 7-plane.

2.4. General Properties of Elliptic Functions. In the following $f ( z )$ will denote a meromorphic function which admits all numbers in the module $\mathscr { M }$ with basis $\left( \omega _ { 1 } , \omega _ { 2 } \right)$ as pcriods. Wc shall not assume that the basis is canonical, and it will not be required that $M$ comprise all the periods.

It is convenient to say that $z _ { 1 }$ is congruent to $z _ { 2 } ,$ $z _ { 1 } \equiv z _ { 2 }$ (mod $M$ , if the difference $z _ { 1 } - z _ { 2 }$ belongs to $M$ ,i.e., $z _ { 1 } = z _ { 2 } + n _ { 1 } \omega _ { 1 } + n _ { 2 } \omega _ { 2 }$ The function $f$ takes the same values at congruent points, and may thus be regarded as a function on the congruence classes. A concrete way to make use of this property is to restrict the function to a parallelogram $P _ { \mathbf { \lambda _ { 0 } } }$ with vertices $a$ $a + \omega _ { 1 } , a + \omega _ { 2 } , a + \omega _ { 1 } + \omega _ { 2 } ,$ By including part of the boundary we may represent each congruence class by exactly one point in $P _ { a } ,$ and then $f$ is completely determined by its values on $P _ { \pmb { a } }$ The choice of $^ { a }$ is irrelevant, and we leave it free in order to attain, for instance, that $f$ has no poles on the boundary of $P _ { \alpha }$ .

# Theorem 3. An elliptic function without poles is a constant.

If $f ( z )$ has no poles, it is bounded on the closure of $P _ { a } ,$ and hence in the whole plane. By Liouville's theorem (Chap. 4, Sec. 2.3) it must reduce to a constant.

Because the poles have no accumulation point there are only finitely many poles in $P _ { \alpha }$ When we speak of the poles of an elliptic function we mean a full set of mutually incongruent poles. Multiplicities are counted in the usual manner.

Theorm .The sum of he ues of n eliptunction

We may choose $^ a$ so that none of the poles fall on the boundary of $P _ { \alpha }$ If the boundary $\partial P _ { \alpha }$ is traced in the positive sense, the sum of the residues at the poles in $P _ { \pmb { \mathscr { a } } }$ equals

$$
\frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } \int _ { \partial P _ { 0 } } f ( z ) \mathop { } { d { z } } .
$$

Because $\pmb { f }$ has periods $\omega _ { 1 } , \ \omega _ { 2 }$ the integral vanishes, for the integrals over opposite sides of the parallelogram cancel against each other.

As a consequence of the theorem there does not exist an elliptic function with a single simple pole.

Theorem 5. A nonconstant elliptic function has equally many poles as it has zeros.

The poles and zeros of $\pmb { f }$ are simple poles of $f ^ { \prime } / f ,$ which is itself an elliptic function. The multiplicities are the residues of $f ^ { \prime } / f ,$ counted positive for zeros and negative for poles. The theorem now follows from Theorem 4.

If $c$ is any constant, $\pmb { f } ( z ) - \pmb { c }$ has the same poles as $\pmb { f } ( z )$ Therefore, all values are assumed equally many times. The number of incongruent roots of the equations $\mathbf { \boldsymbol { f } } ( \mathbf { \boldsymbol { z } } ) = \mathbf { \boldsymbol { c } }$ is called the order of the elliptic function.

Theorem 6. The zeros ${ { a } _ { 1 } }$ , . . . , $a _ { n }$ and poles $^ { b _ { 1 } , }$ . . . , $b _ { n }$ of an elliptic function satisfy $a _ { 1 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + a _ { n } \equiv b _ { 1 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + b _ { n }$ (mod M).

This is proved by considering the integral

$$
\frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } \int _ { \partial P _ { 0 } } \frac { z f ^ { \prime } ( z ) } { f ( z ) } d z
$$

where we may again assume that there are no zeros or poles on the boundary. By the calculus of residues the integral equals $a _ { 1 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot$ $+ a _ { n } - b _ { 1 } - \cdot \cdot \cdot - b _ { n }$ provided that we choose the representative zeros and poles inside $P _ { \pmb { a } }$ Consider the sides from $^ a$ to $a + \omega _ { 1 }$ and from $a + \omega _ { 2 }$ to $a + \omega _ { 1 } + \omega _ { 2 }$ The corresponding part of the integral may be written

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } \left( \int _ { a } ^ { a + \omega _ { 1 } } - \int _ { a + \omega _ { 2 } } ^ { a + \omega _ { 1 } + \omega _ { 2 } } \right) \frac { z f ^ { \prime } ( z ) } { f ( z ) } d z = - \frac { \omega _ { 2 } } { 2 \pi i } \int _ { a } ^ { a + \omega _ { 1 } } \frac { f ^ { \prime } ( z ) } { f ( z ) } d z . } \end{array}
$$

Except for the factor $- \omega _ { 2 }$ the right-hand member represents the winding number around the origin of the closed curve described by $\pmb { f } ( z )$ when $\boldsymbol { z }$

varies from $\pmb { a }$ to $a + \omega _ { 1 } .$ It is consequently an integer. The sameapplies the oher pair  osite sidesTheeore the value is form $n _ { 1 } \omega _ { 1 } + n _ { 2 } \omega _ { 2 }$ , and the theorem is proved.

# 3. THE WEIERSTRASS THEORY

The simplest elliptic functions are of order 2, and such functions have either a double pole with residue zero, or two simple poles with opposite residues. We shall follow the classical example of Weierstrass, who chose a function with a double pole as the starting point of a systematic theory.

3.1. The Weierstrass $\wp$ -function. We may as well place the pole at : the origin, and since multiplication with a constant factor is clearly irrelevant, we may require that the singular part is $z ^ { - 2 }$ If f is elliptic and has only this singularity at the origin and its congruent points, it is easy ' to see that $f$ must be an even function. Indeed, $f ( z ) - f ( - z )$ has the . same periods and no singularity. Therefore it must reduce to a constant, and on setting $z = \omega _ { 1 } / 2$ we conclude that the constant is zero.

A constant can be added at will, and we can therefore choose the constant term in the Laurent development about the origin to be zero. With this additional normalization $\pmb { f } ( z )$ is uniquely determined, and it is . traditionally denoted by a special typographical symbol $\wp ( z )$ . The Laurent development has the form

$$
\wp ( z ) = z ^ { - 2 } + a _ { 1 } z ^ { 2 } + a _ { 2 } z ^ { 4 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot .
$$

So far all this is hypothetical, for we have not yet shown the existence : of an elliptic function with this development. We shall follow the usual procedure in such cases, namely to postulate the existence and derive an explicit expression. The clue is to develop in partial fractions by the $\vdots$ method in Chap. 5, Sec. 2. Our aim is to prove the formula

$$
\wp ( z ) = { \frac { 1 } { z ^ { 2 } } } + \sum _ { \omega \neq 0 } \left( { \frac { 1 } { ( z - \omega ) ^ { 2 } } } - { \frac { 1 } { \omega ^ { 2 } } } \right)
$$

where the sum ranges over all $\omega = n _ { 1 } \omega _ { 1 } + n _ { 2 } \omega _ { 2 }$ except 0. Observe that $( z - \omega ) ^ { - 2 }$ is the singular part at $\boldsymbol { \omega }$ , and that we have subtracted $\omega ^ { - 2 }$ in order to produce convergence.

Our first task is to verify that the series converges. If $| \omega | > 2 | z | ,$ . say, an immediate estimate gives

$$
\left| \frac { 1 } { ( z - \omega ) ^ { 2 } } - \frac { 1 } { \omega ^ { 2 } } \right| = \left| \frac { z ( 2 \omega - z ) } { \omega ^ { 2 } ( z - \omega ) ^ { 2 } } \right| \leq \frac { 1 0 | z | } { | \omega | ^ { 3 } } .
$$

vided that

$$
\sum _ { \omega \neq 0 } { \frac { 1 } { | \omega | ^ { 3 } } } < \infty .
$$

This is indeed the case. Because $\omega _ { 2 } / \omega _ { 1 }$ is nonreal, there exists a $k > 0$ such that $| n _ { 1 } \omega _ { 1 } + n _ { 2 } \omega _ { 2 } | \geq k ( | n _ { 1 } | + | n _ { 2 } | )$ for all real pairs $( n _ { 1 } , n _ { 2 } )$ . If we consider only integers there are $_ { 4 n }$ pairs $( n _ { 1 } , n _ { 2 } )$ with $| n _ { 1 } | + | n _ { 2 } | = n$ . This gives

$$
\sum _ { \omega \neq 0 } | \omega | ^ { - 3 } \leq 4 k ^ { - 3 } \sum _ { 1 } ^ { \infty } n ^ { - 2 } < \infty .
$$

The next step is to prove that the right-hand side of (9) has periods $\omega _ { 1 }$ and $\omega _ { 2 }$ Direct verification is relatively cumbersome. Instead we write, temporarily,

$$
f ( z ) = \frac { 1 } { z ^ { 2 } } + \sum _ { \omega \ne 0 } \left( \frac { 1 } { ( z - \omega ) ^ { 2 } } - \frac { 1 } { \omega ^ { 2 } } \right)
$$

and obtain by termwise differentiation

$$
f ^ { \prime } ( z ) = - \frac { 2 } { z ^ { 3 } } - \sum _ { \omega \ne 0 } \frac { 2 } { ( z - \omega ) ^ { 3 } } = - 2 \sum _ { \omega } \frac { 1 } { ( z - \omega ) ^ { 3 } } .
$$

The last sum is obviously doubly periodic. Therefore $f ( z + \omega _ { 1 } ) - f ( z )$ and $f ( z + \omega _ { 2 } ) - f ( z )$ are constants. Because $f ( z )$ is even (as seen from (10)), it suffices to choose $z = - \omega _ { 1 } / 2$ and $z = - \omega _ { 2 } / 2$ to conclude that the constants are zero. We have thus proved that $\pmb { f }$ has the asserted periods.

It follows now that $\wp ( z ) - f ( z )$ is a constant, and by the form of the development at the origin the constant is zero. We have thereby proved the existence of $\wp ( z )$ , and also that it can be represented by the series (9). For convenient reference we display the important formula

$$
\wp ^ { \prime } ( z ) = - 2 \sum _ { \omega } { \frac { 1 } { ( z - \omega ) ^ { 3 } } } \nonumber
$$

3.2. The Functions $\boldsymbol { \mathfrak { Y } } ( z )$ and $\sigma ( z )$ . Because $\wp ( z )$ has zero residues, it is the derivative of a single-valued function. It is traditional to denote the antiderivative of $\wp ( z )$ by $- \boldsymbol { \gamma } ( z )$ , and to normalize it so that it is odd. By use of (9) we are led to the explicit expression

$$
\xi ( z ) = { \frac { 1 } { z } } + \sum _ { \omega \ne 0 } \Big ( { \frac { 1 } { z - \omega } } + { \frac { 1 } { \omega } } + { \frac { z } { \omega ^ { 2 } } } \Big ) \cdot
$$

The convergence is obvious, for apart from the term $1 / z$ we obtain the new series by integration from 0 to $\boldsymbol { z }$ along any path that does not pass through the poles.

It is clear that $\xi ( z )$ satisfies conditions $\xi ( z + \omega _ { 1 } ) = \xi ( z ) + \eta _ { 1 } ,$ $\xi ( z + \omega _ { 2 } ) = \xi ( z ) + \eta _ { 2 } ,$ where $\pmb { \eta _ { 1 } }$ and $\eta _ { 2 }$ are constants. They are connected with $\omega _ { 1 } , \omega _ { 2 }$ by a very simple relation. To derive it we choose any $a \neq 0$ and observe that

$$
\frac { 1 } { 2 \pi i } \int _ { \partial P _ { a } } \xi ( z ) \mathop { } { d { z } } = 1 ,
$$

by the residue theorem. The integral is easy to evaluate by adding the contributions from opposite sides of the parallelogram, and we obtain the equation

$$
\eta _ { 1 } \omega _ { 2 } - \eta _ { 2 } \omega _ { 1 } = 2 \pi i ,
$$

known as Legendre's relation.

The integration can be carried one step further provided that we use an exponential to eliminate the multiple-valuedness. Just as easily we can verify directly that the product

$$
\sigma ( z ) = z \prod _ { \omega \neq 0 } \left( 1 - { \frac { z } { \omega } } \right) e ^ { z / \omega + { \frac { 1 } { 2 } } ( z / \omega ) ^ { 2 } }
$$

converges and represents an entire function which satisfies

$$
\sigma ^ { \prime } ( z ) / \sigma ( z ) = \xi ( z ) .
$$

The formula (13) is a canonical product representation of $\sigma ( z )$ .

How does $\sigma ( z )$ change when $z$ is replaced by $z + \omega _ { 1 } \mathrm { o r } z + \omega _ { 2 } \}$ Fron

$$
\frac { \sigma ^ { \prime } ( z + \omega _ { 1 } ) } { \sigma ( z + \omega _ { 1 } ) } = \frac { \sigma ^ { \prime } ( z ) } { \sigma ( z ) } + \eta _ { 1 }
$$

it follows at once that

$$
\sigma ( z + \omega _ { 1 } ) = C _ { 1 } \sigma ( z ) e ^ { \eta _ { 1 } z }
$$

with constant $\displaystyle \mathrm { C } _ { \perp }$ To determine the constant we observe that $\sigma ( z )$ is an odd function. On setting $z = - \omega _ { 1 } / 2$ the value of $C _ { 1 }$ can be determined, and we find that $\sigma ( z )$ satisfies

$$
\begin{array} { l } { { \sigma ( z + \omega _ { 1 } ) = - \sigma ( z ) e ^ { \eta _ { 1 } ( z + \omega _ { 1 } / 2 ) } } } \\ { { \sigma ( z + \omega _ { 2 } ) = - \sigma ( z ) e ^ { \eta _ { 2 } ( z + \omega _ { 2 } / 2 ) } . } } \end{array}
$$
