1. Show that any even elliptic function with periods $\omega _ { 1 } , \ \omega _ { 2 }$ can bo expressed in the form

$$
C \prod _ { k = 1 } ^ { n } { \frac { \wp ( z ) - \wp ( a _ { k } ) } { \wp ( z ) - \wp ( b _ { k } ) } } \qquad ( C = { \mathrm { c o n s t . } } )
$$

provided that 0 is neither a zero nor a pole. What is the corresponding form if the function either vanishes or becomes infinite at the origin?

Show that any elliptic function with periods $\omega _ { 1 } , \omega *$ can be written as

$$
C \prod _ { k = 1 } ^ { n } { \frac { \sigma ( z - a _ { k } ) } { \sigma ( z - b _ { k } ) } } \qquad ( C = \mathrm { c o n s t . } ) .
$$

Hint: Use (14) and Theorem 6.

3.3. The Differential Equation. By use of formula (12) it is easy to derive the Laurent expansion of $\xi ( z )$ about the origin, and differentiation will then yield the corresponding expansion of $\wp ( z )$ . We have first

$$
{ \frac { 1 } { z - \omega } } + { \frac { 1 } { \omega } } + { \frac { z } { \omega ^ { 2 } } } = - { \frac { z ^ { 2 } } { \omega ^ { 3 } } } - { \frac { z ^ { 3 } } { \omega ^ { 4 } } } - \cdot \cdot \cdot
$$

and when we sum over all periods we obtain

$$
\xi ( z ) = \frac { 1 } { z } - \sum _ { k = 2 } ^ { \infty } G _ { k } z ^ { 2 k - 1 }
$$

where we have written

$$
G _ { k } = \sum _ { \omega \ne 0 } \frac { 1 } { \omega ^ { 2 k } } \cdot
$$

Observe that the corresponding sums of odd powers of the periods are zero, as was to be expected since $\xi$ is an odd function. Because

we obtain further

$$
\wp ( z ) = - \zeta ^ { \prime } ( z )
$$

$$
\wp ( z ) = { \frac { 1 } { z ^ { 2 } } } + \sum _ { k = 2 } ^ { \infty } ( 2 k - 1 ) G _ { k } z ^ { 2 k - 2 } .
$$

In the following computation we write down only the significant terms, since it is understood that the omitted terms are of higher order:

$$
\begin{array} { l } { { \wp ( z ) = \displaystyle \frac { 1 } { z ^ { 2 } } + 3 G _ { z } z ^ { 2 } + 5 G _ { z } z ^ { 4 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot } } \\ { { \qquad \quad } } \\ { { \wp ^ { \prime } ( z ) = - \displaystyle \frac { 2 } { z ^ { 3 } } + 6 G _ { 2 } z + 2 0 G _ { 3 } z ^ { 6 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot } } \\ { { \qquad \downarrow \wp ^ { \prime } ( z ) ^ { 2 } = \displaystyle \frac { 4 } { z ^ { 6 } } - \frac { 2 4 G _ { 2 } } { z ^ { 2 } } - 8 0 G _ { 3 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot } } \\ { { \qquad \quad } } \\ { { \displaystyle 4 \wp ( z ) ^ { 3 } = \displaystyle \frac { 4 } { z ^ { 6 } } + \frac { 3 6 G _ { 2 } } { z ^ { 2 } } + 6 0 G _ { 3 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot } } \\ { { \qquad \quad } } \\ { { \displaystyle 6 0 G _ { 2 } \wp ( z ) = \frac { 6 0 G _ { 2 } } { z ^ { 2 } } + 0 + \cdot \cdot \cdot \cdot } } \end{array}
$$

The last three lines yield

$$
\wp ^ { \prime } ( z ) ^ { 2 } - 4 \wp ( z ) ^ { 3 } + 6 0 G _ { 2 } \wp ( z ) = - 1 4 0 G _ { 3 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot .
$$

Here the left-hand side is a doubly periodic function, and the right-hand side has no poles. We may therefore conclude that

$$
\wp ^ { \prime } ( z ) ^ { 2 } = 4 \wp ( z ) ^ { 3 } - 6 0 G _ { 2 } \wp ( z ) - 1 4 0 G _ { 3 } .
$$

It is customary to set $g _ { 2 } = 6 0 G _ { 2 } , g _ { 3 } = 1 4 0 G _ { 3 } \mathrm { s o }$ that the equation becomes

$$
\wp ^ { \prime } ( z ) ^ { 2 } = 4 \wp ( z ) ^ { 3 } - g _ { 2 } \wp ( z ) - g _ { 3 } .
$$

This is a first-order differential equation for $w = \wp ( z )$ . It can be solved explicitly, namely, by the formula

$$
z = \int ^ { w } \frac { d w } { \sqrt { 4 w ^ { 3 } - g _ { 2 } w - g _ { 3 } } } + \mathrm { c o n s t a n t } ,
$$

which shows that $\wp ( z )$ is the inverse of an elliptic integral. More accurately, this connection is expressed by the identity

$$
z - z _ { 0 } = \int _ { \wp ( z _ { 0 } ) } ^ { \wp ( z ) } \frac { d w } { \sqrt { 4 w ^ { 3 } - g _ { 2 } w - g _ { 3 } } }
$$

where the path of integration is the image under $\wp$ of a path from ${ z _ { 0 } }$ to $z$ that avoids the zeros and poles of $\wp ^ { \prime } ( z )$ , and where the sign of the square oot must be chosen so that it actually equals $\wp ^ { \prime } ( z )$ .

We recall that we encountered the relationship between elliptic functions and elliptic integrals already in connection with the conformal mapping of rectangles and certain triangles (Chap. 6, Sec. 2).

# \*EXERCISES

The Weierstrass functions satisfy numerous identities which are best dealt with in an exercise section. They can be proved either by comparing two elliptic functions with the same zeros and poles (when ${ \pmb \sigma } \cdot { \pmb \sigma } \cdot $ -functions are involved), or by comparing elliptic functions with the same singular parts (when only $\wp$ and $\boldsymbol { \xi }$ -functions are involved). The following sequence of formulas is so arranged that we need to resort to this method only once.

1.

$$
\wp ( z ) - \wp ( u ) = - \frac { \sigma ( z - u ) \sigma ( z + u ) } { \sigma ( z ) ^ { 2 } \sigma ( u ) ^ { 2 } }
$$

(Use (14) to show that the right-hand member is a periodic function of ${ \boldsymbol { z } } ,$ Find the multiplicative constant by comparing the Laurent evelopments.)

2.

$$
\frac { \wp ^ { \prime } ( z ) } { \wp ( z ) - \wp ( u ) } = \mathfrak { t } ( z - u ) + \mathfrak { t } ( z + u ) - 2 \mathfrak { t } ( z ) .
$$

(Follows from (16) by taking logarithmic derivatives.)

3.

$$
\xi ( z + u ) = \xi ( z ) + \xi ( u ) + \frac { 1 } { \bar { \cdot } } \frac { \wp ^ { \prime } ( z ) - \wp ^ { \prime } ( u ) } { \wp ( z ) - \wp ( u ) } \cdot
$$

(This is a symmetrized version of (17).)

4. The addition theorem for the $\wp .$ -function:

$$
\wp ( z + u ) = - \wp ( z ) - \wp ( u ) + { \frac { 1 } { 4 } } { \Bigg ( } { \frac { \wp ^ { \prime } ( z ) - \wp ^ { \prime } ( u ) } { \wp ( z ) - \wp ( u ) } } { \Bigg ) } ^ { 2 } .
$$

(Differentiation of (18) leads to a formula which contains $\wp \prime \prime ( z )$ . I can be eliminated by (15) which gives $\wp \prime \prime = 6 \wp ^ { 2 } - \frac { 1 } { 2 } g _ { 2 }$ Symmetrization yields (19). Observe that this is an algebraic addition theorem, for $\wp \prime ( z )$ and $\wp \prime ( u )$ can be expressed algebraically through $\wp ( z )$ and $\wp ( u ) . \mathinner { \rangle }$

5. Prove

$$
\wp ( 2 z ) = { \frac { 1 } { 4 } } \left( { \frac { \wp ^ { \prime \prime } ( z ) } { \wp ^ { \prime } ( z ) } } \right) ^ { 2 } - 2 \wp ( z ) .
$$

. Prove $\wp ^ { \prime } ( z ) = - \sigma ( 2 z ) / \sigma ( z ) ^ { 4 }$

.Prove that

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \left| \begin{array} { c c c } { \wp ( z ) } & { \wp ^ { \prime } ( z ) } & { 1 } \\ { \wp ( u ) } & { \wp ^ { \prime } ( u ) } & { 1 } \\ { \wp ( u + z ) } & { - \wp ^ { \prime } ( u + z ) } & { 1 } \end{array} \right| = 0 . } \end{array}
$$

3.4. The Modular Function $\lambda ( \tau )$ . The differential equation (15) car also be written as

$$
\wp ^ { \prime } ( z ) ^ { 2 } = 4 ( \wp ( z ) - e _ { 1 } ) ( \wp ( z ) - e _ { 2 } ) ( \wp ( z ) - e _ { 3 } ) ,
$$

where $e _ { 1 } , e _ { 2 } , e _ { 3 }$ are the roots of the polynomial $4 w ^ { 3 } - g _ { 2 } w - g _ { 3 } ,$

To find the values of the $e _ { k }$ we determine the zeros of $\wp ^ { \prime } ( z )$ . The symmetry and periodicity of $\wp ( z )$ imply $\wp ( \omega _ { 1 } - z ) = \wp ( z )$ Hence $\wp \prime ( \omega _ { 1 } - z ) = - \wp \prime ( z )$ , from which it follows that $\wp ^ { \prime } ( \omega _ { \bf { 1 } } / 2 ) = 0$ Similarly $\wp \prime ( \omega _ { 2 } / 2 ) = 0$ ,and also $\wp ^ { \prime } ( ( \omega _ { 1 } + \omega _ { 2 } ) / 2 ) = 0$ The numbers $\omega _ { 1 } / 2 ,$ $\omega _ { 2 } / 2$ and $( \omega _ { 1 } + \omega _ { 2 } ) / 2$ are mutually incongruent modulo the periods. Therefore they are precisely the three zeros of $\wp \prime$ , which is of order 3, and all the zeros are simple. When we compare with (20) it follows that we can set

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \boldsymbol { e } _ { 1 } = \mathcal { O } ( \omega _ { 1 } / 2 ) , \qquad \boldsymbol { e } _ { 2 } = \mathcal { O } ( \omega _ { 2 } / 2 ) , \qquad \boldsymbol { e } _ { \circ } = \mathcal { O } ( ( \omega _ { 1 } + \omega _ { 2 } ) / 2 ) , } \end{array}
$$

It follows, moreover, and this is very important, that these roots are all distinct. Indeed, $\wp ( z )$ assumes each value $e _ { k }$ with multiplicity 2, and if two of them were equal that value would be assumed four times in contradiction with the fact that $\wp$ is of order 2.

If we substitute $z = \omega _ { 1 } / 2 , \omega _ { 2 } / 2$ and $( \omega _ { 1 } + \omega _ { 2 } ) / 2$ in the definition (9) of $\wp ( z )$ it is seen at once that the $\scriptstyle e _ { k }$ are homogeneous of order $- 2$ in $\omega _ { 1 } , \ \omega _ { 2 }$ (in other words, if the periods are multiplied by $t _ { i }$ then the $e _ { k }$ are multiplied by $t ^ { - 2 }$ ). We conclude that the quantity

$$
\lambda ( \tau ) = { \frac { e _ { 3 } - e _ { 2 } } { e _ { 1 } - e _ { 2 } } }
$$

depends only on the ratio ${ \tau } = { \omega _ { 2 } } / { \omega _ { 1 } } ,$ as indicated by our notation. It is quite clear from (9) that $\lambda ( \tau )$ is the quotient of two analytic functions in the upper half plane $ { \mathrm { I m } } \tau > 0$ Because $e _ { 1 } \neq e _ { 2 }$ it is actually analytic, rather than meromorphic; because $e _ { 2 } \neq e _ { 3 }$ it is never equal to 0, and because $e _ { 1 } \neq e _ { 3 }$ it is never equal to 1.

We shall study the dependence on $\pmb { \tau }$ in greater detail. If the periods are subjected to the unimodular transformation

$$
\begin{array} { l } { \omega _ { 2 } ^ { \prime } = a \omega _ { 2 } + b \omega _ { 1 } } \\ { \omega _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } = c \omega _ { 2 } + d \omega _ { 1 } } \end{array}
$$

then, first of all, the $\wp$ -function does not change. Therefore, by looking at (20), the roots $e _ { k }$ can at most be permuted. Let us see what actually happens. It is clear from (23) that $\omega _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } / 2 \equiv \omega _ { 1 } / 2$ and $\omega _ { 2 } ^ { \prime } / 2 \equiv \omega _ { 2 } / 2$ if ${ a \equiv d \equiv 1 }$ (mod 2) and $b \equiv c \equiv 0$ (mod 2). Under this condition the $e _ { k }$ do not change, and we have shown that

$$
\lambda \left( { \frac { a \tau + b } { c \tau + d } } \right) = \lambda ( \tau ) \qquad { \mathrm { f o r } } \qquad \left( { \begin{array} { l l } { a } & { b } \\ { c } & { d } \end{array} } \right) \equiv \left( { \begin{array} { l l } { 1 } & { 0 } \\ { 0 } & { 1 } \end{array} } \right) ( { \mathrm { m o d ~ } } 2 ) .
$$

The transformations which satisfy the congruence relation in (24) form a subgroup of the modular group (cf. Sec. 2.2), known as the congruence subgroup mod 2. Equation (24) asserts that $\lambda ( \tau )$ is invariant under this subgroup. Quite generally, when an analytic or meromorphic function is invariant under a group of linear transformations, we call it an automorphic function. More specifically, a function which is automorphic with respect to a subgroup of the modular group is called a modular function (or an elliptic modular function).

We still have to determine the behavior of $\lambda ( \tau )$ under a modular transformation that does not belong to the congruence subgroup. $\mathbf { I } _ { \mathbf { \bar { v } } }$ is sientiic ent $2 \ { \tan } \left( { \begin{array} { c c } { 1 } & { 1 } \\ { 0 } & { 1 } \end{array} } \right) \ { \mathrm { a n d } } \left( { \begin{array} { c c } { 0 } & { 1 } \\ { 1 } & { 0 } \end{array} } \right)$ respectively, for all other types can be composed from these. In the first case we obtain $\omega _ { 2 } ^ { \prime } / 2 = ( \omega _ { 1 } + \omega _ { 2 } ) / 2$ and $\omega _ { \mathbf { 1 } } ^ { \prime } / 2 = \omega _ { \mathbf { 1 } } / 2$ ; this means that $\mathbf { \epsilon } _ { e \mathbf { 2 } }$ and $\scriptstyle { e _ { 3 } }$ are interchanged, while $e _ { 1 }$ remains fixed, and hence $\lambda$ goes over into $( e _ { 2 } - e _ { 3 } ) / ( e _ { 1 } - e _ { 3 } ) = \lambda / ( \lambda - 1 )$ . In the second case $\omega _ { 2 } ^ { \prime } / 2 = \omega _ { 1 } / 2 , \omega _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } / 2 =$ $\omega _ { 2 } / 2$ , so that $e _ { 1 }$ and $\scriptstyle { e _ { 2 } }$ are interchanged, and $\lambda$ goes over in to $1 - \lambda$ Sample transformations are $\tau \to \tau + 1$ and $\tau \to - 1 / \tau$ We find that $\lambda ( \tau )$ satisfies the functional equations

$$
\lambda ( \tau + 1 ) = \frac { \lambda ( \tau ) } { \lambda ( \tau ) - 1 } , \qquad \lambda \left( - \frac { 1 } { \tau } \right) = 1 - \lambda ( \tau ) .
$$

3.5. The Conformal Mapping by $\lambda ( \tau )$ . For convenience we shall henceforth use the normalization $\omega _ { 1 } = 1$ $\omega _ { 2 } = \tau$ With this choice of periods we obtain from (9) and (21)

$$
\begin{array} { l } { { e _ { 3 } - e _ { 2 } = \displaystyle \sum _ { m , n = - \infty } ^ { \infty } \left[ \frac { 1 } { ( m - \frac { 1 } { 2 } + ( n + \frac { 1 } { 2 } ) \tau ) ^ { 2 } } - \frac { 1 } { ( m + ( n - \frac { 1 } { 2 } ) \tau ) ^ { 2 } } \right] } } \\ { { e _ { 1 } - e _ { 2 } = \displaystyle \sum _ { m , n = - \infty } ^ { \infty } \left[ \frac { 1 } { ( m - \frac { 1 } { 2 } + n \tau ) ^ { 2 } } - \frac { 1 } { ( m + ( n - \frac { 1 } { 2 } ) \tau ) ^ { 2 } } \right] } } \end{array}
$$

where the double series are absolutely convergent. Our first observation is that these quantities are real when $\pmb { \tau }$ is purely imaginary (this is also true of the individual $\boldsymbol { e } _ { k _ { \mathrm { } } }$ ). Indeed, when we replace $\pmb { \tau }$ by $- \tau$ the sums remain the same, except for a rearrangement of the terms. We conclude that $\lambda ( \tau )$ is real on the imaginary axis.

Because $\left( { \begin{array} { l l } { 1 } & { 2 } \\ { 0 } & { 1 } \end{array} } \right)$ $\lambda ( \tau + 2 ) = \lambda ( \dot { \tau } )$ In other words, $\lambda$ has period 2. As we have seen in Sec. 2, this means that $\lambda ( \tau )$ can be expressed as a function of $e ^ { \pi i \tau }$ It would not be difficult to determine the Fourier development, but we shall be content to show that $\lambda ( \tau ) \to 0$ as $\operatorname { I m } \tau \to \infty$ .

To evaluate (26) we sum first with respect to $_ m$ This summation can be carried out explicitly by use of the formula

$$
{ \frac { \pi ^ { 2 } } { \sin ^ { 2 } \pi z } } = \sum _ { - \infty } ^ { \infty } { \frac { 1 } { ( z - m ) ^ { 2 } } }
$$

(Chap. 5, Sec. 2.1, (9)). We obtain at once

$$
\begin{array} { l } { { \displaystyle e _ { 3 } - e _ { 2 } = \pi ^ { 2 } \sum _ { n = - \infty } ^ { \infty } \bigg ( \frac { 1 } { \cos ^ { 2 } \pi ( n - \frac { 1 } { 2 } ) \tau } - \frac { 1 } { \sin ^ { 2 } \pi ( n - \frac { 1 } { 2 } ) \tau } \bigg ) } } \\ { { \displaystyle e _ { 1 } - e _ { 2 } = \pi ^ { 2 } \sum _ { n = - \infty } ^ { \infty } \bigg ( \frac { 1 } { \cos ^ { 2 } \pi n \tau } - \frac { 1 } { \sin ^ { 2 } \pi ( n - \frac { 1 } { 2 } ) \tau } \bigg ) . } } \end{array}
$$

The series are strongly convergent, both for $n \to + \infty$ and $n \to - \infty$ , for $| \cos n \pi \tau |$ and $| \sin n \pi \tau |$ are comparable to $e ^ { | n | \pi \mathbf { I } \cdot \mathbf { m } \tau }$ ; the convergence is uniform for $\operatorname { I m } \tau \geq \delta > 0$ .

The limits can now be taken termwise, and we find that $e _ { 3 } - e _ { 2 } \to 0$ . $e _ { 1 } - e _ { 2 } \to \pi ^ { 2 }$ (from the term $n = 0 ,$ Hence $\lambda ( \tau ) \to 0$ as In $_ { 1 } \ \tau \to \infty$ , uniformly with respect to the real part of $\pmb { \tau }$ It follows further by the second equation (25) that $\lambda ( \tau ) \to 1$ when $\pmb { \tau }$ approaches 0 along the imaginary axis.

We need one more piece of information, namely the order to which $\lambda ( \tau )$ vanishes together with $e ^ { \pi i \tau }$ From (2) the leading terms in $e _ { 3 } \sim e _ { 2 }$ are the ones corresponding to $\mathbf { \nabla } _ { n } = \mathbf { 0 }$ and $\pmb { n } = \pmb { 1 }$ . The sum of these terms is

$$
2 \pi ^ { 2 } \left[ \frac { 4 e ^ { \pi i \tau } } { ( 1 + e ^ { \pi i \tau } ) ^ { 2 } } + \frac { 4 e ^ { \pi i \tau } } { ( 1 - e ^ { \pi i \tau } ) ^ { 2 } } \right]
$$

and we conclude that

$$
\lambda ( \tau ) e ^ { - \pi i \tau } \to 1 6
$$

for $\operatorname { I m } \tau \to \infty$

In Fig. 36 the region $\Omega$ is bounded by the imaginary axis, the line $ { \mathrm { R e } } { } \tau = 1$ , and the circle $\begin{array} { r } { | \pmb { \tau } - \frac { 1 } { 2 } | = \frac { 1 } { 2 } , } \end{array}$ The transformation $\tau + 1$ maps the imaginary axis on $\mathrm { R e } \boldsymbol { \tau } = \mathbf { 1 }$ ,and $1 - 1 / \tau$ maps $\begin{array} { r } { \mathrm { R e } \tau = 1 0 \mathrm { n } | \tau - \frac { 1 } { 2 } | = \frac { 1 } { 2 } } \end{array}$ . Since $\lambda ( \tau )$ is real on the imaginary axis, it follows by virtue of the relations (25) that it is real on the whole boundary of $\Omega _ { * }$ Furthermore, $\lambda ( \tau ) \to 1$ as $\boldsymbol { \tau }$ tends to 0 and $\lambda ( \tau ) \to \infty$ as $\pmb { \tau }$ tends to 1 inside $\Omega$ .

We apply the argument principle to determine the number of times $\lambda ( \tau )$ takes a nonreal value ${ \boldsymbol { w } } _ { 0 }$ in $\Omega _ { e }$ Cut off the corners of $\mathfrak { a }$ by means of a horizontal line segment $\mathbf { I m } \pmb { \tau } = \pmb { t } _ { 0 }$ and its images under the transformations $- 1 / \tau$ and $1 - 1 / \tau$ (these images are circles tangent to the real axis). For sufficiently large $\scriptstyle t _ { 0 }$ it is clear that $\lambda ( \tau ) \neq w _ { 0 }$ in the portions that have been cut off. The circle near $\tau = 1$ is mapped by $\lambda ( \tau )$ on a curve $\lambda = \lambda ( 1 - 1 / \tau ) = 1 - 1 / \lambda ( \tau )$ ;where ${ \boldsymbol { \tau } } = s + i { \boldsymbol { t } } _ { 0 } ,$ . $0 \leq s \leq 1$ ; in view of (28) this is approximately a large semicircle in the upper half plane. It is now evident that the image of the contour of the truncated region $\Omega$ has winding number I about $w _ { 0 }$ if Im $w _ { 0 } > 0$ , and winding number 0 if Im $w _ { 0 } < 0$ As a result $\lambda ( \tau )$ takes every value in the upper half plane exactly once in $\mathfrak { Q }$ , and no value in the lower half plane. This is also sufficient to guarantee that $\lambda ( \tau )$ is monotone on the boundary of $\Omega$ Indeed, if it were not, the derivative $\lambda ^ { \prime } ( \tau )$ would vanish at a boundary point, and it would be impossible for a full semicircular neighborhood of that boundary point to be mapped onto the upper half plane.

Theorem 7. The modular function $\lambda ( \tau )$ effects a one-to-one conformal mapping of the region $\Omega$ onto the upper half plane. The mapping extends continuously to the boundary in such a way that $\mathbf { \tau } _ { \pmb { \tau } } = \mathbf { 0 }$ ,1, $\infty$ correspond to $\lambda = 1 , \infty , 0$ .

By reflection the region $\Omega ^ { \prime }$ that is symmetric to $\Omega$ with respect to the imaginary axis is mapped onto the lower half plane, and thus both regions together correspond to the whole plane, except for the points O and 1.

We shall also prove:

Theorem 8. Every point $\pmb { \tau }$ in the upper half plane is equivalent under the congruence subgroup mod 2 to exactly one point in $\bar { \Omega } \cup \Omega ^ { \prime }$ .

We refer to Fig. 37. The reader is asked to verify that the region $\pmb { \triangle }$ is mapped on the shaded regions in the figure by means of the linear transformations $\pmb { \tau }$ , $- 1 / \tau , \tau - 1 , 1 / ( 1 - \tau ) , ( \tau - 1 ) / \tau , \tau / ( 1 - \tau )$ which we shall denote by $s _ { 1 } , s _ { 2 } , \ldots , s _ { 6 } .$ The matrices of the inverse transformations $S _ { k } ^ { - 1 } \left( k = 1 , \cdot \cdot \cdot , 6 \right)$ are in order

$$
{ \binom { 1 } { 0 } } , \ 0 _ { 1 } ^ { 0 } \bigg ) , \ { \binom { 0 } { 1 } } , \ { \binom { - 1 } { 0 } } , \ { \binom { 1 } { 0 } } , \ { \binom { 1 } { 1 } } , \ { \binom { 1 } { 1 } } , \ { \binom { - 1 } { 0 } } , \ { \binom { 0 } { - 1 } } , \ { \binom { 1 } { 1 } } , \ { \binom { 1 } { 1 } } .
$$

One recognizes readily that these matrices form a complete set of mutually incongruent matrices in the sense that every unimodular matrix is congruent mod 2 to exactly one of them. Precisely the same can be shown for the transformations $S _ { k } ^ { \prime }$ ${ \bf \Sigma } _ { k } ^ { \prime } \left( k = 1 , \cdot \cdot \cdot , 6 \right)$ which map $\Delta ^ { \prime }$ on the unshaded regions in the figure (the task of writing them down is left to the reader).

Together the 12 images of $\bar { \Delta }$ and $\bar { \Delta } ^ { \prime }$ cover the set $\hat { \Omega } \setminus \Omega ^ { \prime }$ (closures should be taken with respect to the open half plane).

Let $\pmb { \tau }$ be any point in the upper half plane. The set $\bar { \Delta } \cup \bar { \Delta } ^ { \prime }$ can be identified with the closure of the shaded region in Fig. 34. Therefore, according to Theorem 2 there exists a modular transformation $s$ such that $\&$ lies in $\bar { \Delta } \cup \bar { \Delta } ^ { \prime }$ Suppose first that $\mathit { s } _ { \tau }$ is in $\bar { \Delta } .$ We know that the matrix of $s$ is congruent mod 2 to the matrix of an $S _ { k } ^ { - 1 }$ It follows that the matrix of $T = S _ { k } S$ is congruent to the identity matrix; in other words, $\boldsymbol { T }$ belongs to the congruence subgroup. Since $\mathit { s } _ { \tau }$ lies in $\bar { \Delta }$ we know further that $T _ { \overline { { \tau } } } = S _ { k } ( S \tau )$ lies in $\Re \cup \\hat { \Omega } ^ { \prime }$ The same reasoning applies if $\bar { S _ { \tau } ^ { - } } \bar { \epsilon } \bar { \Delta } ^ { \prime }$ Thus there is always a $\mathcal { T } _ { \tau }$ in $\boldsymbol { \mathfrak { Q } } \cup \boldsymbol { \mathfrak { Q } } ^ { \prime }$ , and a trivial consideration shows that it can be chosen in $\Re \cup \mathfrak { a } ^ { \prime }$ .

The uniqueness follows readily from the fact that the $S _ { k }$ as well as the ${ \cal S } _ { k } ^ { \prime }$ are mutually incongruent. We shall leave it to the reader to work out the details.

is automorphic with respect to the full modular group. Where does it take the values O and 1, and with what multiplicities? Show that

# 8 GLOBAL ANALYTIC FUNCTIONS

# 1. ANALYTIC CONTINUATION

In the preceding chapters we have stressed that all functions must be well defined and, therefore, single-valued. In the case of functions like log $\pmb { z }$ or $\hat { \mathbf { \Omega } } _ { \mathbf { \Omega } } \sqrt { z }$ which are not uniquely determined by their analytic expression, a special effort was needed to show that, under favorable circumstances, a single-valued branch can be selected. While this point of view answers the need for logical clarity, it does not do justice to the fact that the ambiguity of the logarithm or the square root is an essential characteristic which cannot be ignored. There is thus a clear need for a concept that emphasizes rather than circumvents multiplevaluedness.

1.1. General Analytic Functions. An analytic function $f ( z )$ defined in a region $\Omega$ will constitute a function element, denoted by $( f , \Omega )$ , and a global analytic function will appear as a collection of function elements which are related to each other in a prescribed manner.

Two function elements $( f _ { 1 } , \Omega _ { 1 } )$ and $( f _ { 2 } , \Omega _ { 2 } )$ are said to be direct analytic continuations of each other if $\Omega _ { 1 } \cap \Omega _ { 2 }$ is nonempty and $f _ { 1 } ( z ) = f _ { 2 } ( z )$ in $\Omega _ { 1 } \cap \Omega _ { 2 }$ More specifically, $( f _ { 2 } , \Omega _ { 2 } )$ is called a direct analytic continuation of $( f _ { 1 } , \Omega _ { 1 } )$ to the region $\Omega _ { 2 }$ There need not exist any direct analytic continuation to $\Omega _ { 2 } ,$ but if there is one it is uniquely determined. For suppose that $( f _ { 2 } , \Omega _ { 2 } )$

and $( g _ { 2 } , \Omega _ { 2 } )$ are two direct analytic continuations of $( f _ { 1 } , \Omega _ { 1 } )$ ; then $f _ { 2 } = g _ { 2 }$ in $\Omega _ { 1 } \cap \Omega _ { 2 } ,$ and this implies $f _ { 2 } = g _ { 2 }$ throughout $\Omega _ { 2 }$ We note that if $\Omega _ { 2 } \subset \Omega _ { 1 } ,$ then the direct analytic continuation of $( f _ { 1 } , \Omega _ { 1 } )$ is $( f _ { 1 } , \Omega _ { 2 } )$ .

If $( f _ { 1 } , \Omega _ { 1 } )$ and $( f _ { 2 } , \Omega _ { 2 } )$ are direct analytic continuations of each other, it is evident that an analytic function $f$ can be defined in $\Omega _ { 1 } \cup \Omega _ { 2 }$ by setting $f = f _ { 1 }$ in $\Omega _ { 1 }$ and $f = f _ { 2 }$ in $\Omega _ { 2 }$ Since it had been possible to consider the function element $( f , \Omega _ { 1 } \cup \Omega _ { 2 } )$ from the beginning, it would seem that nothing has been gained. Consider, however, a third function element $( f _ { 3 } , \Omega _ { 3 } )$ which we assume to be a direct analytic continuation o $( f _ { 2 } , \Omega _ { 2 } )$ . Then it may well happen that $\Omega _ { 3 }$ overlaps $\Omega _ { 1 _ { - } }$ , but that $( f _ { 3 } , \Omega _ { 3 } )$ is nevertheless not a direct analytic continuation of $( f _ { 1 } , \Omega _ { 1 } )$ . In this situation the collection $( f _ { 1 } , \Omega _ { 1 } ) , ( f _ { 2 } , \Omega _ { 2 } ) , ( f _ { 3 } , \Omega _ { 3 } )$ cannot be replaced by a single function element. We are thus led to a genuine broadening of the concept of function.

More generally, we shall consider chains of function elements $( f _ { \mathbf { 1 } } , \Omega _ { \mathbf { 1 } } )$ , $( f _ { 2 } , \Omega _ { 2 } )$ , . . . , $( f _ { n } , \Omega _ { n } )$ such that $( f _ { k } , \Omega _ { k } )$ is a direct analytic continuation of $( f _ { k - 1 } , \Omega _ { k - 1 } )$ . The elements in such a chain are said to be analytic continuations of each other. We adopt the following definition:

Definition l. A global analytic function is a nonvoid collection f of function eiements $( f , \Omega )$ which is such that any two elements in f are analytic continuations of each other by way of a chain whose links are members of f.

A complete analytic function is a global analytic function which contains all analytic continuations of any one of its elements.

A complete analytic function is evidently maximal in the sense that it cannot be further extended, and it is clear that every function element belongs to a unique complete analytic function. The incomplete global analytic functions are more arbitrary, and there are many cases in which two different collections of function elements should be regarded as defining the same function. For instance, a single-valued function $f ( z )$ , defined in $\mathfrak { a }$ , can be identified either with the collection which consists of the single function element $( f , \Omega )$ , or with the collection of all $( f , \Omega ^ { \prime } )$ with $\Omega ^ { \prime } \subset \Omega$ .

A global analytic function f has a uniquely determined derivative $\mathbf { f ^ { \prime } }$ defined by the function elements $( f ^ { \prime } , \Omega )$ Indeed, if $( f _ { 1 } , \Omega _ { 1 } )$ and $( f _ { 2 } , \Omega _ { 2 } )$ are direct analytic continuations of each other, so are $( f _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } , \Omega _ { 1 } )$ and $( f _ { 2 } ^ { \prime } , \Omega _ { 2 } )$ . The higher derivatives $\mathbf { f } ^ { \prime \prime }$ , $\mathbf { f } ^ { \prime \prime \prime }$ ,. . . can be defined in the same way.

A similar relationship may exist between any two global analytic functions f and $\mathbf { g }$ We assume that there is given a correspondence which to every $( f , \Omega )$ f assigns a unique function element $( g , \Omega ) \in \mathbf { g }$ in such a way that direct analytic continuations go over in direct analytic continuations. In these circumstances we agree to say that f is subordinate to $\mathbf { g }$ , and it is possible to define $\mathbf { f } + \mathbf { g }$ and fg as collections consisting of the elements $( f + g , \Omega )$ , $( f g , \Omega )$ which correspond to the elements $( f , \Omega )$ of f. For instance, f is subordinate to any entire function $\mathbf { b }$ , from which it follows that $\mathbf { f } + \mathbf { h }$ and $\mathbf { f h }$ are well defined. $^ \dagger$

We can now formulate a classical principle known as the permanence of functional relations. Suppose that certain global analytic functions f, g, . . . are given and that, for instance, f is subordinate to all the others. Let it be known that a set of corresponding function elements $( f , \Omega ) , ( g , \Omega ) , \ldots ,$ satisfy a relation of the form $G ( f , g , \ . \ . \ . ) = 0$ ,where the expression $\pmb { G }$ is a polynomial in several variables (the proof is valid much more generally). If $( f _ { 1 } , \Omega _ { 1 } ) , ( g _ { 1 } , \Omega _ { 1 } ) ,$ , . . . is a set of direct analytic continuations, it follows at once that $G ( f _ { 1 } , g _ { 1 } , \dots . ) = 0$ in $\Omega _ { 1 }$ for the simple reason that the composite function $G ( f _ { 1 } ( z ) , g _ { 1 } ( z ) , \textrm { \hdots } . )$ is analytic in $\Omega _ { 1 }$ and vanishes in $\Omega \cap \Omega _ { 1 }$ . We are thus able to conclude that the relation $G ( f , g , \ . \ . \ . ) = 0$ holds for all sets of corresponding function elements, a fact which may also be expressed through the equation $G ( \mathbf { f } , \mathbf { g } , \mathbf { \sigma } \cdot \cdot \mathbf { \sigma } \cdot ) = 0 .$ .

# EXERCISES

1. Prove that a function element $( f , \mathbf { C } )$ , where $\mathbf { c }$ is the whole plane, determines a complete analytic function consisting of all function elements of the form $( f , \Omega )$ . Define $\mathcal { N } ^ { \top }$ as a global analytic function by means of a finite number of function elements. $\clubsuit .$ Suppose that $( f , \Omega )$ satisfies a differential equation of the form $P ( f , f ^ { \prime } , f ^ { \prime \prime } , \cdot \cdot \cdot ) = 0$ where $P$ is a polynomial whose coefficients are entire functions. Prove that all function elements of the complete analytic function determined by $( f , \Omega )$ satisfy the same differential equation.

1.2. The Riemann Surface of a Function. In order to study the multiple-valued nature of a global analytic function it is convenient to introduce the notion of a branch.‡ Two function elements $( f _ { 1 } , \Omega _ { 1 } )$ and $( f _ { 2 } , \Omega _ { 2 } )$ are said to determine the same branch at a point $z _ { 0 } \epsilon \Omega _ { 1 } \cap \Omega _ { 2 }$ whenever $f _ { 1 } = f _ { 2 }$ in a neighborhood of ${ \mathit { z } } _ { 0 }$ , In order that this happen it is sufficient, but not necessary, that the function elements are direct analytic continuations of each other. They are always, however, analytic continuations of each other, for they are both direct analytic continuations of their common restriction to a neighborhood of ${ z _ { 0 } } .$ We note that two

$^ \dagger$ We make only transient use of this notion of subordinacy which is related to but not identical with one in rather common use.

\$ The word germ is also commonly used.

function elements determine the same branch at ${ \mathit { z } } _ { 0 }$ if and only if they have the same Taylor development about ${ \boldsymbol { z } } _ { 0 }$ .

The relation between function elements which we have just introduced is evidently an equivalence relation. With respect to this equivalence relation the totality of function elements $( f , \Omega )$ with $z _ { 0 } \epsilon \Omega$ falls into welldefined equivalence classes which we call the analytic branches at ${ \mathit { z } } _ { 0 }$ It is easy to see that they can be identified with all power series in $z - z _ { 0 }$ with a positive radius of convergence. We will denote the branch at ${ \mathfrak { z } } _ { 0 }$ determined by the function element $( f , \Omega )$ as $( f , z _ { 0 } )$ .

For a global analytic function f we pick out the branches $( f , z _ { 0 } )$ determined by function elements $( f , \Omega )$ e f and call them the branches of f at ${ z _ { 0 } } .$ To every branch there corresponds a unique function value $f ( z _ { 0 } )$ as well as unique values of the derivatives $f ^ { \prime } ( z _ { 0 } ) , f ^ { \prime \prime } ( z _ { 0 } )$ . . . . In analogy to the construction of Riemann surfaces of elementary multiple-valued functions we introduce a set $\mathfrak { F }$ (the Riemann surface) whose elements $\pmb { \lambda }$ (the points) are the branches $( f , z )$ of f. We are then in a position to consider f as a single-valued function $\mathbf { f } ( \pmb { \imath } _ { 3 } )$ on $\mathfrak { F }$ The function ${ z } = \pmb { p } ( \pmb { \imath } )$ which to every $\ z = \ ( f , z )$ assigns the uniquely determined value $_ { z }$ is called the projection of $\mathfrak { F }$ into the complex plane, and $\pmb { z }$ is the trace of $\pmb { \delta } \cdot$

The consideration of the Riemann surface is not of much use unless we can say when a function is continuous on $\mathfrak { F }$ Since continuity can be expressed in terms of neighborhoods, it is sufficient to define neighborhoods on $\mathfrak { F }$ Given ${ \mathfrak { z o } } = ( f _ { 0 } , z _ { 0 } )$ , determined by the function element $( f _ { 0 } , \Omega _ { 0 } )$ f, we choose a neighborhood $V \subset \Omega _ { 0 }$ of ${ \boldsymbol { z } } _ { 0 }$ and consider the set $\mathfrak { B }$ of all branches $( f _ { 0 } , z )$ with $z \in V$ By definition, $\mathfrak { B }$ will be a neighborhood of ${ \pmb \xi } _ { 0 } .$ It follows readily that $\mathbf f ( \pmb \lambda )$ and $p ( \mathfrak { z } )$ are continuous functions in the sense that there exists, for any given $\varepsilon > 0$ , a neighborhood  with the property that $| \mathbf { f } ( _ { \widetilde { \delta } } ) - \mathbf { f } ( _ { \widetilde { \delta } 0 } ) | < \varepsilon$ . $| p ( \mathfrak { z } ) - p ( \mathfrak { z } _ { 0 } ) | < \mathfrak { s }$ for all $\pmb { \mathscr { z } } \in \mathfrak { V }$ .

By the introduction of Riemann surfaces we gain a very simple interpretation of subordination. Let $\mathfrak { F }$ and $\mathfrak { G }$ be the Riemann surfaces of f and g. Then f is subordinate to $\pmb { \mathrm { g } }$ if and only if there exists a continuous mapping $\pmb { \sigma }$ of $\mathfrak { F }$ into $\mathfrak { G }$ such that $\pmb { \delta }$ and $\sigma ( \mathfrak { z } )$ have the same projection $z$ ; the proof is immediate. We observe that the mapping $\pmb { \sigma }$ is not necessarily unique, which means that f may be subordinate to $\mathbf { g }$ in different ways. In a language which appeals to the imagination the existence of a projection preserving mapping implies that the surface $\mathfrak { F }$ can be spread out over $\mathfrak { G }$ , or that $\mathfrak { F }$ may be considered as a Riemann surface relative to $\mathfrak { G }$ .

We note finally that our definition of Riemann surfaces is provisional in as far as it does not yet include the case of branch points.

1.3. Analytic Continuation Along Arcs. We consider a global analytic function f with the Riemann surface $\mathfrak { F }$ and an arc $\pmb { \gamma }$ in the complex plane with the equation $z \equiv z ( t ) , \alpha \\\cong t \cong \beta$ , Suppose that there exists on $\mathfrak { F }$ an arc $\bar { \pmb { \gamma } }$ whose projection is $\pmb { \gamma }$ : we mean by this that $\pmb { \bar { \gamma } }$ has an equation $\begin{array} { r } { \ z = \ z ( t ) } \end{array}$ with $p ( \mathfrak { z } ( t ) ) = z ( t )$ for all $\pmb { t } ,$ The fundamental assumption that $\bar { \pmb { \gamma } }$ is an arc means of course that $\pmb { \lambda } ( t )$ is continuous with respect to the neighborhoods introduced on $\mathfrak { F }$ .

It is desirable to give a parallel interpretation which does not refer explicitly to the Riemann surface. To each $\pmb { t }$ there corresponds a $\pmb { \delta }$ with the projection $z ( t )$ and, therefore, a branch of the form $( f , z ( t ) )$ . For a given $t _ { 0 }$ this branch is determined by a function element $( \boldsymbol { f } _ { 0 } , \Omega _ { 0 } )$ with $z ( t _ { 0 } ) \in \Omega _ { 0 } .$ A neighborhood consists of branches $( f _ { 0 } , z )$ , and the continuity of $z ( t )$ evidently implies the existence of a $\delta > 0$ such that for $\vert t - t _ { 0 } \vert < \delta$ the branch $( f , z ( t ) )$ is determined by the function element $( f _ { 0 } , \Omega _ { 0 } )$ . When this is the case, we shall say that the branch $( f , z ( t ) )$ and any one of the corresponding function elements have been obtained by continuation along the arc $\pmb { \gamma }$ According to this terminology there is complete equivalence between continuations along $\pmb { \gamma }$ and arcs $\bar { \pmb { \gamma } }$ on $\mathfrak { F }$ which project into $\pmb { \gamma }$ .

The continuation along an arc corresponds to the intuitive notion of a continuously changing branch. The existence of a continuation is not guaranteed, but the following important uniqueness theorem is valid:

Theorem I. Two continuations $( f _ { 1 } , z ( t ) )$ and $( f _ { 2 } , z ( t ) )$ of a global analytic function f along the same arc $\pmb { \gamma }$ are either identical, or else they differ for all t.

Consider the subset $\pmb { { \cal E } }$ of the closed interval $\mathbf { \Gamma } ( \alpha , \beta )$ in which

$$
( f _ { 1 } , z ( t ) ) \ : = \ : ( f _ { 2 } , z ( t ) ) .
$$

Choose $t _ { 0 } \in E$ and suppose that the corresponding branches are determined by function elements $( f _ { 1 } ^ { 0 } , \Omega _ { 1 } ) , ~ ( f _ { 2 } ^ { 0 } , \Omega _ { 2 } )$ By assumption $f _ { 1 } ^ { 0 } = f _ { 2 } ^ { 0 }$ in a neighborhood of ${ \boldsymbol { z } } ( t _ { 0 } )$ . If $\pmb { t }$ is sufficiently near to $t _ { 0 } ,$ the point $z ( t )$ lies in this neighborhood; moreover, we can choose $f _ { 1 } = f _ { 1 } ^ { 0 } , f _ { 2 } = f _ { 2 } ^ { 0 } ,$ and it follows that the branches $( f _ { 1 } , z ( t ) )$ , $( f _ { 2 } , z ( t ) )$ are identical. This result shows that the complement of $\mathbfcal { E }$ is closed. Suppose now that $t _ { 0 }$ is not in $\mathbfcal { E }$ With the same notations, $f _ { 1 } ^ { 0 } ( z )$ and $f _ { 2 } ^ { 0 } ( z )$ are not identical in any neighborhood of $z ( t _ { 0 } )$ . Consequently there exists a neighborhood $\pmb { \Delta }$ of $z ( t _ { 0 } )$ in which $f _ { 1 } ^ { 0 } ( z ) \ne f _ { 2 } ^ { 0 } ( z )$ , except perhaps for $z = z ( t _ { 0 } )$ . For $\pmb { t }$ sufficiently near to $t _ { 0 } ~ z ( t ) \in \Delta$ , and we may take $f _ { 1 } = f _ { 1 } ^ { 0 } , f _ { 2 } = f _ { 2 } ^ { 0 }$ But if $z ( t ) \neq z ( t _ { 0 } )$ the branches $( f _ { 1 } ^ { 0 } , z ( t ) )$ and $( f _ { 2 } ^ { 0 } , z ( t ) )$ are different for the simple reason that $f _ { 1 } ^ { 0 } ( z ( t ) ) \neq f _ { 2 } ^ { 0 } ( z ( t ) )$ and if $z ( t ) = z ( t _ { 0 } )$ they are different by assumption. It follows that $E$ itself is closed, and since the interval is connected the theorem is proved.

By virtue of this theorem a continuation is uniquely determined for instance by its initial branch $( f _ { 0 } , z ( \alpha ) )$ . We may therefore speak of the continuation of f along $\pmb { \gamma }$ from the initial branch $\scriptstyle ( f _ { 0 } , z ( \alpha ) )$ , provided only that such a continuation exists.

If $\pmb { \gamma }$ is an arbitrary arc and f a global analytic function, it may well happen that f does not have any continuation along $\pmb { \gamma } ,$ or that a continuation exists for some initial branches, but not for all. Let us investigate the case of an initial branch $( f _ { 0 } , z ( \alpha ) )$ which cannot be continued along $\pmb { \gamma }$ If $t _ { 0 } > \alpha$ is sufficiently near to $\pmb { \alpha }$ , there will exist a continuation of the initial branch along the subarc corresponding to the interval $( \alpha , t _ { 0 } )$ ; indeed, this is trivially the case if the subare is contained in the region ${ \mathfrak { a } } _ { 0 }$ of the function element $( f _ { 0 } , \Omega _ { 0 } )$ . The least upper bound of all such $t _ { 0 }$ is a number $\pmb { \tau }$ which satisfies $\alpha < \tau < \beta$ , and it is easily seen that the continuation will be possible for $t _ { 0 } < \tau$ ,impossible for $\qquad t _ { 0 } \equiv \tau$ In a certain sense the subarc corresponding to $( \alpha , \tau )$ may be said to lead to a point at which f ceases to be defined. In particular, if f is a complete analytic function, the subarc is called a singular path from the given initial branch; less precisely, it is said to lead to a singular point of f. The term singular point should be used only when the corresponding path is clearly indicated.

The connection between continuation along arcs and stepwise continuation by means of a chain of direct analytic continuations requires further illumination. In the first place, $( f _ { 1 } , \Omega _ { 1 } )$ , $( f _ { 2 } , \Omega _ { 2 } )$ , . . . , $( f _ { n } , \Omega _ { n } )$ is a chain of direct analytic continuations, it is always possible to connect a point $z _ { 1 } \in \Omega _ { 1 }$ to a point $z _ { n } \epsilon \Omega _ { n }$ by means of an arc $\pmb { \gamma }$ such that f has a continuation along $\gamma$ with the initial branch $( f _ { 1 } , z _ { 1 } )$ and the terminal branch $( f _ { n } , z _ { n } )$ . Indeed, it is sufficient to let $\pmb { \gamma }$ be composed of a subarc $\gamma _ { 1 } \subset \Omega _ { 1 }$ from $z _ { 1 }$ to a point $z _ { 2 } \in \Omega _ { 1 } \cup \Omega _ { 2 } ,$ a second subarc $\gamma _ { 2 } \subset \Omega _ { 2 }$ from $z _ { 2 }$ to $z _ { 3 } \epsilon$ $\Omega _ { 2 } \cup \Omega _ { 3 } ,$ and so on. The continuation along $\pmb { \gamma }$ is completely defined by setting $\mathfrak { z } ( t ) = ( f _ { k } , z ( t ) )$ on $\gamma _ { k }$ .

Conversely, if a continuation $\smash { \hat { \delta } ^ { ( t ) } }$ is given, we can find a chain of direct analytic continuations which follows the arc $\pmb { \gamma }$ in the same way as in the preceding construction, provided merely that f is defined by means of a sufficiently large collection of function elements. By means of Heine-Borel's lemma it is shown that the parametric interval can be subdivided into $( \alpha , t _ { 1 } ) , ~ ( t _ { 1 } , t _ { 2 } )$ , . . . , $( t _ { n - 1 } , \beta )$ such that $\gimel ( t ) \ = \ ( f _ { k } , z ( t ) )$ in $( t _ { k - 1 } , t _ { k } )$ for suitably chosen function elements $( f _ { k } , \Omega _ { k } )$ Although $( f _ { k - 1 } , \Omega _ { k - 1 } )$ and $( f _ { k } , \Omega _ { k } )$ need not be direct analytic continuations of each other, they are at least direct analytic continuations of their common restriction to a neighborhood of $z ( t _ { k - 1 } )$ . If these restrictions are contained in the collection f, and this is certainly the case if f is a complete analytic function, then we can find a chain of direct analytic continuations with the desired properties.

In order to illustrate the use of continuations along arcs we will give a definition of log $\pmb { z }$ as a complete analytic function. We define it as the collection of all function elements $( f , \Omega )$ such that $e ^ { f ( z ) } = z$ in $\Omega _ { * }$ It must be proved that this collection is complete.

We have to show that any two function elements $( f _ { 1 } , \Omega _ { 1 } ) , ( f _ { 2 } , \Omega _ { 2 } )$ in the collection can be joined by a chain of direct analytic continuations. Because of the permanence of functional relations it is clear that the intermediate function elements will belong to the same collection.

Choose points $z _ { 1 } \epsilon \Omega _ { 1 } , z _ { 2 } \epsilon \Omega _ { 2 } ,$ and join them by a differentiable ar $\pmb { \gamma }$ which does not pass through the origin. This is possible since neither ${ \pmb z _ { 1 } }$ nor $z _ { 2 }$ can be O. Consider the function

$$
\varphi ( t ) = f _ { 1 } ( z _ { 1 } ) + \int _ { \alpha } ^ { t } \frac { z ^ { \prime } ( t ) } { z ( t ) } d t .
$$

By differentiation, $z ( t ) e ^ { - \varphi ( t ) }$ is constant; for $t = \alpha$ the value is 1, and hence $e ^ { \varphi ( t ) } = z ( t )$ For a given $\pmb { t }$ there exists, in any neighborhood of $z ( t )$ which does not include the origin, a uniquely determined branch $f ( z )$ of log $z$ which takes the value $\varphi ( t )$ for $z = z ( t )$ . It is clear that $( f , z ( t ) )$ defines a continuation along $\pmb { \gamma }$ The terminal branch may not coincide with $f _ { 2 } ,$ but its value must differ from $f _ { 2 } ( z _ { 2 } )$ by a multiple of $\scriptstyle 2 \pi i ,$ In order to obtain the right value at $z _ { 2 }$ all that remains is to continue the terminal branch along a closed curve which circles the origin a suitable number of times. Finally, the arcwise continuation can be replaced by a finite chain of direct analytic continuations, and it is proved that log $\pmb { z }$ is a complete analytic function.

2. If a function element is defined by a power series inside of its circle of convergence, prove that the corresponding complete analytic function has necessarily a singular path in the circle of convergence which leads to a point on the circumference. ("A power series has at least one singular point on its circle of convergence.")

1.4. Homotopic Curves. We must now study the topological properties of closed curves in a region from a point of view which is fundamental for the theory of analytic continuations. The question which interests us is the behavior of an arc under continuous deformations. From an intuitive standpoint this is an extremely simple notion. If $\pmb { \gamma _ { 1 } }$ and $\pmb { \gamma _ { 2 } }$ are two arcs with common end points, contained in a region $\Omega$ it is very natural to ask whether $\pmb { \gamma _ { 1 } }$ can be continuously deformed into $\gamma _ { 2 }$ when the end points are kept fixed and the moving arc is confined to $\Omega ,$ . For instance, in Fig. 38 the arc $\pmb { \gamma _ { 1 } }$ can be deformed into $\gamma _ { 2 } ,$ but not into γa. Two arcs which can be deformed into each other are said to be homotopic with respect to $\mathfrak { Q }$ This is evidently an equivalence relation.

![](images/31c16638d879dbe8512e3f0f14aaaeb28cf8e68a7c501014762e1a5d95181b38.jpg)  
FIG. 38. Homotopic arcs.

A precise definition must of course be given. Fortunately, the physical conception of deformation has an almost immediate interpretation in mathematical terms. It is indeed clear that a deformation of an arc can be described by means of a continuous function $z = z ( t , u )$ of two variables where the point $^ { ( t , u ) }$ ranges over a rectangle $\alpha \leq t \leq \beta _ { \mathrm { { i } } }$ $\mathbf { 0 } \leq u \leq \mathbf { 1 } .$ To every fixed value $u \ : = \ : u _ { 0 }$ there corresponds an arc $z = z ( t , u _ { 0 } )$ , and the effect of the deformation is to change the initial arc $z = z ( t , 0 )$ into $z = z ( t , 1 )$ . The deformation takes place within $\Omega$ if $z ( t , u ) \in \Omega$ for all $( t , u )$ , and it is a deformation with fixed end points if $z ( \alpha , u )$ and $_ { z ( \beta , u ) }$ are constant. To every fixed value $t = t _ { 0 }$ there corresponds an arc $z = z ( t _ { 0 } , u )$ with $\boldsymbol { u }$ as parameter which may be called the deformation path of the point corresponding to $t _ { 0 }$ Figure 39 illustrates the effect of a deformation.

We are led to the following definition of homotopy:

Two arcs $\pmb { \gamma _ { 1 } }$ and $\curvearrowleft$ defined by equations $z = z _ { 1 } ( t )$ and $z = z _ { 2 } ( t )$ over the same parametric interval $\alpha \leq t \leq \beta$ , are said to be homotopic in $\Omega$ if there exists a continuous function $z ( t , u )$ of two variables, defined for $\alpha \leq t \leq \beta$ $\mathbf { 0 } \leq u \leq \mathbf { 1 }$ , with the following properties:

1. $z ( t , u ) \in \Omega$ for all $^ { ( t , u ) }$ .   
2. $z ( t , 0 ) = z _ { 1 } ( t ) , z ( t , 1 ) = z _ { 2 } ( t ) f o r \ a l l \ t .$ .   
3. $z ( \alpha , u ) = z _ { 1 } ( \alpha ) = z _ { 2 } ( \alpha )$ , $z ( \beta , u ) = z _ { 1 } ( \beta ) = z _ { 2 } ( \beta )$ for all u.

It is only for the sake of convenience that we have required the parametric intervals to be the same. If this is not the case we transform the intervals into each other by a linear change of parameter, and agree to consider the original arcs as homotopic if they are homotopic in the new parametrization.

Simple formal proofs which the reader can easily supply show that the relation of homotopy, as defined above, is an equivalence relation. We can thus divide all arcs into equivalence classes, called homotopy classes; the arcs in a homotopy class have common endpoints and can be deformed into each other within $\Omega$ It deserves to be pointed out that different parametric representations of the same arc are always homotopic. Indeed, $z = z _ { 1 } ( t )$ is a reparametrization of $z = z _ { 2 } ( t )$ if and only if there is a nondecreasing function $\tau ( t )$ such that $z _ { 1 } ( t ) = z _ { 2 } ( \tau ( t ) )$ The function

$$
z ( t , u ) = z _ { 1 } ( ( 1 - u ) t + u \tau ( t ) )
$$

has all its values on the arc under consideration, and therefore in $\Omega$ For ${ \boldsymbol { u } } = \mathbf { 0 }$ and $u = 1$ we obtain respectively $z ( t , 0 ) = z _ { 1 } ( t )$ and

$$
z ( t , 1 ) = z _ { 1 } ( \tau ( t ) ) = z _ { 2 } ( t )
$$

as required, and the end points are evidently kept fixed.

If two arcs $\pmb { \gamma _ { 1 } }$ and $\gamma _ { 2 }$ are traced in succession, with $\gamma _ { 2 }$ beginning at the terminal point of $\pmb { \gamma _ { 1 } }$ , they form a new arc which we will now denote by $\pmb { \gamma _ { 1 } \gamma _ { 2 } }$ in contrast to the notation $\gamma _ { 1 } + \gamma _ { 2 }$ preferred in homology theory. The parametrization of $\yen 12$ is not uniquely determined, but for the determination of the homotopy class this is of no importance. Very simple reasoning shows, moreover, that the homotopy class of $\yen 12$ depends only on the homotopy classes of $\pmb { \gamma _ { 1 } }$ and $\gamma _ { 2 }$ By virtue of this fundamental fact we may consider the operation which leads to the homotopy class of $\gamma _ { 1 } \gamma _ { 2 }$ as a multiplication of homotopy classes. It is defined only when the initial point of $\curvearrowleft$ coincides with the terminal point of $\pmb { \gamma _ { 1 } }$ If we restrict our attention to the homotopy classes of closed curves which begin and end at a fixed point $z _ { 0 } ,$ the product is always defined and is represerited by a curve in the same family. What is more, with this definition of product the homotopy classes of closed curves from $z _ { 0 } ,$ with respect to the region $\Omega _ { i }$ form a group. In order to prove this assertion we must establish:

![](images/f2972208fedc9359a661aa838649d54a9c05c2e8a9eb378dbc4b4e5763557bca.jpg)  
FIG. 39. Deformation.

1. The associative law: $( \gamma _ { 1 } \gamma _ { 2 } ) \gamma _ { 3 }$ is homotopic to $\gamma _ { 1 } ( \gamma _ { 2 } \gamma _ { 3 } )$ . Existence of a unit curve 1 such that ${ \pmb \gamma } { \pmb 1 }$ and $_ { 1 \gamma }$ are homotopic to $\pmb { \gamma }$ . 3. Existence of an inverse $\pmb { \gamma } ^ { - 1 }$ such that $\pmb { \gamma } \pmb { \gamma } ^ { - 1 }$ and $\pmb { \gamma } ^ { - 1 } \pmb { \gamma }$ are homotopic o 1.

The associative law is trivial since $( \gamma _ { 1 } \gamma _ { 2 } ) \gamma _ { 3 }$ is at most a reparametrization of $\pmb { \gamma _ { 1 } } ( \pmb { \gamma _ { 2 } \gamma _ { 3 } } )$ . For a unit curve we can choose the constant $z = z _ { 0 } ,$ actually, the symbol 1 may represent any closed curve which can be shrunk to the point $z _ { 0 }$ Finally, the inverse $\pmb { \gamma } ^ { - 1 }$ is the curve $\pmb { \gamma }$ traced in the opposite direction. If $\pmb { \gamma }$ is represented by $z = z ( t ) , \alpha \le t \le \beta , \gamma ^ { - 1 }$ can be represented by $z = z ( 2 \beta - t )$ , $\beta \leq t \leq 2 \beta - \alpha$ ,The equation of $\gamma \gamma ^ { - 1 }$ is thus

$$
\begin{array} { l } { z = z ( t ) \qquad \mathrm { f o r } \ \alpha \leq t \leq \beta } \\ { z = z ( 2 \beta - t ) \qquad \mathrm { f o r } \ \beta \leq t \leq 2 \beta - \alpha . } \end{array}
$$

The curve can be shrunk to a point by means of the deformation

$$
\begin{array} { l } { z ( t , u ) = z ( t ) \qquad \mathrm { f o r ~ } \alpha \leq t \leq u \alpha + ( 1 - u ) \beta } \\ { z ( t , u ) = z ( u \alpha + ( 1 - u ) \beta ) \qquad \mathrm { f o r ~ } u \alpha + ( 1 - u ) \beta \leq t \leq u ( \beta - \alpha ) + \beta } \\ { z ( t , u ) = z ( 2 \beta - t ) \qquad \mathrm { f o r ~ } u ( \beta - \alpha ) + \beta \leq t \leq 2 \beta - \alpha . } \end{array}
$$

The interpretation is clear: we are letting the turning point recede from $z ( \beta )$ to ${ \boldsymbol { z } } ( \alpha )$ . Since $z ( t , 1 ) = z ( \alpha ) = z _ { 0 } ,$ we have proved that $\gamma \gamma ^ { - 1 }$ is homotopic to 1. The proof is independent of the hypothesis that $\pmb { \gamma }$ be a closed curve; thus $\gamma \gamma ^ { - 1 }$ is homotopic to 1 for any arc $\pmb { \gamma }$ from ${ z _ { 0 } }$ .

The group which we have constructed is called the homotopy group, or the fundamental group, of the region $\Omega$ with respect to the point ${ \boldsymbol { \varepsilon } } _ { 0 }$ As an abstract group it does not depend on the point ${ \mathit { z } } _ { 0 }$ If $ \tilde { \varepsilon } _ { \boldsymbol { \Theta } } ^ { \prime }$ is another point in $\Omega$ ,we join ${ \boldsymbol { z } } _ { 0 }$ to $ z _ { 0 } ^ { \prime }$ by an arc c in $\mathfrak { a }$ To every closed curve $\pmb { \gamma } ^ { \prime }$ from $ z _ { 0 } ^ { \prime }$ corresponds a closed curve $\gamma = c \gamma ^ { \prime } c ^ { - 1 }$ from $z _ { 0 }$ This correspondence is homotopy preserving and may thus be regarded as a correspondence between homotopy classes. As such it is product preserving, for $( c \gamma _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } c ^ { - 1 } ) ( c \gamma _ { 2 } ^ { \prime } c ^ { - 1 } )$ is homotopic to $c ( \gamma _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } \gamma _ { 2 } ^ { \prime } ) c ^ { - 1 }$ ,by cancellation of $_ { c ^ { - 1 } c }$ . Finally, the correspondence is one to one, for if $\pmb { \gamma }$ is given we can choose $\gamma ^ { \prime } = c ^ { - 1 } \gamma c$ and find that the corresponding curve $c \gamma ^ { \prime } c ^ { - 1 } = ( c c ^ { - 1 } ) \gamma ( c c ^ { - 1 } )$ is homotopic to $\pmb { \gamma }$ . It is thus proved that the homotopy groups with respect to ${ \boldsymbol { z } } _ { 0 }$ and $ z _ { 0 } ^ { \prime }$ are isomorphic.

If $\gamma _ { 1 } , ~ \gamma _ { 2 }$ are any two arcs with the initial point $z _ { 0 }$ and a common terminal point, then $_ { \gamma _ { 1 } }$ is homotopic to $\gamma _ { 2 }$ if and only if $\pmb { \gamma _ { 1 } \gamma _ { 2 } ^ { - 1 } }$ is homotopic to 1. For if $\pmb { \gamma _ { 1 } }$ is homotopic to $\curvearrowright$ then $\pmb { \gamma _ { 1 } \pmb { \gamma _ { 2 } ^ { - 1 } } }$ is homotopic to $\pmb { \gamma _ { 2 } \gamma _ { 2 } ^ { - 1 } }$ , and hence to 1. Conversely, $\pmb { \gamma _ { 1 } \gamma _ { 2 } ^ { - 1 } }$ is homotopic to 1, then

$$
( \gamma _ { 1 } \gamma _ { 2 } ^ { - 1 } ) \gamma _ { 2 } = \gamma _ { 1 } ( \gamma _ { 2 } ^ { - 1 } \gamma _ { 2 } )
$$

is simultaneously homotopic to $\pmb { \gamma _ { 1 } }$ and $\curvearrowright$ proving that $\pmb { \gamma _ { 1 } }$ is homotopic to $\pmb { \gamma _ { 2 } }$ For this reason it is sufficient to study the homotopy of closed curves.

The explicit determination of homotopy groups is simplified by the fact that the homotopy group is obviously a topological invariant. Indeed, by a topological mapping of $\Omega$ onto $\Omega ^ { \prime }$ any deformation in $\Omega$ can be carried over to $\Omega ^ { \prime }$ and is seen to determine a product preserving oneto-one correspondence between the homotopy classes. Topologically equivalent regions have therefore isomorphic homotopy groups.

The homotopy group of a disk reduces to the unit element; this means that any two arcs with common end points are homotopic. The proof makes use of the convexity of the disk: the arc $z = z _ { 1 } ( t )$ can be deformed into $z = z _ { 2 } ( t )$ by means of the deformation

$$
z ( t , u ) = ( 1 - u ) z _ { 1 } ( t ) + u z _ { 2 } ( t )
$$

whose deformation paths are line segments. The same proof would be valid for any convex region. In particular, the whole plane has likewise a homotopy group which reduces to the unit element.

We proved in Chap. 6, Sec. 1, that any simply connected region which is not the whole plane can be mapped conformally onto a disk. In this connection the conformality is not important, but the fact that the mapping is topological permits us to conclude that any simply connected region has a fundamental group which reduces to its unit element. We shall find that the converse is also true.

1.5. The Monodromy Theorem. Let $\Omega$ be a fixed region in the $\pmb { z }$ plane. We consider the case of a global analytic function f which can be continued along all arcs $\pmb { \gamma }$ contained in $\Omega ,$ starting with any branch defined at the initial point of $\pmb { \gamma }$ More precisely, to any arc $z = z ( t )$ , $\alpha \leq t \leq \beta$ , contained in $\Omega _ { i }$ , and for every function element $( f _ { \mathbf { 0 } } , \Omega _ { 0 } )$ ef with $z ( \alpha ) \in \Omega _ { 0 }$ there shall exist a continuation $\mathfrak { z } ( t ) = ( f , z ( t ) )$ whose initial branch is the one defined by $( f _ { 0 } , \Omega _ { 0 } )$ .

When two arcs $\gamma _ { 1 } , ~ \gamma _ { 2 }$ with common end points are given, we are interested to know whether a common initial branch, continued along $\pmb { \gamma _ { 1 } }$ and $_ { \gamma _ { 2 } , }$ will lead to the same terminal branch. The basic theorem, known as the monodromy theorem, is the following:

Theorem 2. If the arcs $\pmb { \gamma _ { 1 } }$ and $\pmb { \gamma _ { 2 } }$ are homotopic with respect to $\mathfrak { Q }$ , and if an initial branch of f can be continued along all arcs contained in $\Omega _ { s }$ then the continuations of this initial branch along $\pmb { \gamma _ { 1 } }$ and $\gamma _ { 2 }$ must lead to the same terminal branch.

To begin with we note that continuation along an arc of the form $\pmb { \gamma } \pmb { \gamma } ^ { - 1 }$ will evidently lead back to the initial branch. Similarly, continuation along an arc of the form $\sigma _ { 1 } ( \gamma \gamma ^ { - 1 } ) \sigma _ { 2 }$ will have the same effect as continuation along $\sigma _ { 1 } \sigma _ { 2 }$ , For this reason, to say that the continuations along $\pmb { \gamma _ { 1 } }$ and $\pmb { \gamma _ { 2 } }$ lead to the same terminal branch is equivalent to saying that continuation along $\pmb { \gamma _ { 1 } \gamma _ { 2 } ^ { - 1 } }$ leads back to the initial element.

According to the assumption there exists a deformation $_ { z ( t , u ) }$ of $\pmb { \gamma _ { 1 } }$ into $\pmb { \gamma _ { 2 } }$ Every arc $\pmb { \sigma }$ in the deformation rectangle $\scriptstyle { R }$ is carried by $\tilde { \mathbf { \Lambda } } _ { \mathcal { Z } } ( t , u )$ into an arc ${ \pmb \sigma } ^ { \prime } \in \Omega _ { \mathrm { i } }$ ,and if ${ \pmb { \sigma } } ^ { \prime }$ begins at the initial point of $\pmb { \gamma _ { 1 } }$ and $\pmb { \gamma _ { 2 } }$ there exists a unique continuation of the given initial branch along ${ \pmb \sigma } ^ { \prime }$ For the sake of simplicity it will be called a continuation along $\pmb { \sigma }$ The theorem asserts that the continuation along the perimeter $\mathbf { r }$ of $\scriptstyle { \mathcal { R } }$ leads back to the initial element. The sense in which $\mathbf { r }$ is described is immaterial, but should be fixed once and for all.

A simple proof can be based on the method of bisection. We begin by bisecting $\scriptstyle { \pmb R }$ horizontally, and denote by $\pmb { \pi _ { 1 } }$ the perimeter of the lower " half $\scriptstyle R _ { 1 } ,$ described from the lower left-hand corner 0 and in the direction : which coincides with the direction of $\mathbf { r }$ along the common side. With" the upper half $R _ { 2 }$ we associate a curve $\pi _ { 2 }$ which begins at 0, leads vertically to the lower left-hand corner of $R _ { 2 }$ , describes the perimeter of $\scriptstyle { R _ { 2 } }$ in the sense which coincides with that of $\mathbf { \Gamma } ^ { \mathbf { \Gamma } }$ along the common side, and returns vertically to 0 (Fig. 40). We recognize that the curve $\pi _ { 1 } \pi _ { 2 }$ differs from $\mathbf { r }$ only by an intermediate arc of the form $\sigma { \pmb \sigma } ^ { - 1 }$ , For this reason the" effect of continuing along $\pi _ { 1 } \pi _ { 2 }$ is the same as if we continue along $\mathbf { r . } \overset { * * } { \underset { \ast } { } }$ Consequently, if $\pi _ { 1 }$ and $\pi _ { 2 }$ both lead back to the initial branch, so does F. We make now the opposite assumption that $\mathbf { r }$ does not lead back to the ; initial branch. Then either $\pmb { \pi } _ { \pmb { 1 } }$ or $\pmb { \pi _ { 2 } }$ has the same property. The corresponding rectangle is bisected vertically, and the same reasoning is applied. When the process is repeated, we obtain a sequence of rectangles $R \supset R ^ { ( 1 ) } \supset R ^ { ( 2 ) } \supset \cdot \cdot \cdot \supset R ^ { ( n ) } \supset \cdot \cdot \cdot$ and corresponding closed curves $\pi ^ { ( n ) }$ such that the continuation of the initial branch along $\pi ^ { ( n ) }$ does not lead back to the same branch. Each $\pi ^ { ( n ) }$ is of the form $\sigma _ { n } \Gamma _ { n } \sigma _ { n } ^ { - 1 }$ where $\sigma _ { n }$ is a well-determined polygon leading from 0 to the lower left-hand corner of $\mathcal { R } ^ { ( n ) }$ and $\mathbf { { r } } _ { n }$ denotes the perimeter of ${ \pmb R } ^ { ( n ) }$ ; moreover, $\sigma _ { n }$ is a subarc of $\sigma _ { \mathscr { n } + 1 }$ .

![](images/c453982d6fdc0ae7417f6958e7fd177d985fc2bb9f53dbb3d47f1fc84965feaa.jpg)  
FIG. 40. The monodromy theorem.

As $n  \infty$ the rectangles $\mathcal { R } ^ { ( n ) }$ converge to a point $P _ { \infty _ { \mathrm { . } } }$ ,and the polygons $\sigma _ { n }$ form, in the limit, a continuous curve $\sigma _ { \infty }$ ending at $P _ { \infty }$ There exists a continuation of the initial branch along $\sigma _ { \infty }$ which terminates with a branch $( f _ { \infty } , z ( P _ { \infty } ) )$ at the point corresponding to $P _ { \infty }$ For sufficiently large $\pmb { n }$ the image of $\mathbf { r } _ { n }$ will be contained in a neighborhood $\mathbf { \vec { r } }$ of $z ( P _ { \infty } )$ , and the branch obtained at the terminal point of $\sigma _ { n }$ must be determined by the function element $( f _ { \infty } , \Delta )$ . When this is the case, the element $( f _ { \infty } , \Delta )$ can be used to construct a continuation along. $\pi ^ { ( n ) }$ which leads back to the initial branch. This contradicts the property by which $\pi ^ { ( n ) }$ was defined, and we have proved that the continuation along $\mathbf { r }$ must end with the initial branch.

The monodromy theorem implies, above all, that any global analytic function which can be continued along all arcs in a simply connected region determines one single-valued analytic function for each choice of the initial branch. This fact can also be expressed by saying that a Riemann surface (without branch points) over a simply connected region must consist of a single sheet.

We can further draw the consequence, already announced, that a region whose homotopy group reduces to the unit element must necessarily be simply connected. For suppose that $\Omega$ is multiply connected. Then there exists a bounded component $E _ { 0 }$ of the complement of $\mathfrak { Q }$ ,and if ${ z _ { 0 } \in E _ { 0 } }$ we know that log $( z - z _ { 0 } )$ is not single-valued in $\Omega ,$ By the monodromy theorem it follows that the homotopy group of $\Omega$ cannot reduce to the unit element.

This is the last step toward proving the equivalence of the following three characterizations of simply connected regions: (1) $\Omega$ is simply connected if its complement is connected; (2) $\Omega$ is simply connected if it is homeomorphic with a disk; (3) $\Omega$ is simply connected if its fundamental group reduces to the unit element.

1.6. Branch Points. For a closer study of the singularities of multiplevalued functions it is necessary to determine, explicitly, the fundamental group of the punctured disk. Let the punctured disk be represented by $0 < | z | < 1$ , and consider a fixed point, for instance the point $z _ { 0 } = r$ on the positive radius. By means of a central projection, given by

$$
z ( t , u ) = ( 1 - u ) z ( t ) + u r \frac { z ( t ) } { | z ( t ) | }
$$

any closed curve $z = z ( t )$ from ${ \boldsymbol { z } } _ { 0 }$ can be deformed into a curve which lies on the circle $| z | = r$ It is thus sufficient to consider curves $\pmb { \gamma }$ on this circle. The equation of $\gamma$ will again be written as $z = z ( t )$ .

By continuity every $t _ { 0 }$ has a neighborhood in which $| z ( t ) ~ - ~ z ( t _ { 0 } ) | ~ <$ $r / 2$ ; in such a neighborhood $z ( t )$ cannot take both the values $\pmb { r }$ and $- \pmb { \gamma } _ { }$ . It follows easily, by use of Heine-Borel's lemma or the method of bisection, that it is possible to write $\gamma = \gamma _ { 1 } \gamma _ { 2 } \cdot \cdot \cdot \gamma _ { n }$ where each $\gamma _ { k }$ either does not pass through $\boldsymbol { r }$ or does not pass through $- \boldsymbol { r }$ For simplicity, let us refer to the points $\pmb { r }$ and $- \boldsymbol { r }$ by letters $P _ { 0 }$ and $P _ { \mathrm { ~ 0 ~ } } ^ { \prime }$ (Fig. 41), and let the end points of $\gamma _ { k }$ be denoted by $P _ { k }$ and $P _ { k + 1 }$ Since $\gamma _ { k }$ is contained in the simply connected region obtained by deleting either the positive or negative radius, it can be deformed into one of the two arcs $P _ { k } P _ { k + 1 } .$ As a result $\gamma$ can be deformed into a product of simple arcs with the successive end points $P _ { 0 } P _ { 1 } P _ { 2 } \cdots P _ { n } P _ { 0 } ,$ This path may in turn be replaced by $P _ { 0 } P _ { 1 } P _ { 2 } P _ { 0 } P _ { 2 } P _ { 3 } P _ { 0 } \cdot \cdot \cdot P _ { 0 } P _ { n - 1 } P _ { n } P _ { 0 }$ where each arc $P _ { k } P _ { 0 }$ and $P _ { 0 } P _ { k }$ is, for definiteness, the one which does not contain $P _ { 0 } ^ { \prime }$ In fact, the new path is obtained by inserting the doubly traced arcs $P _ { k } P _ { 0 } P _ { k }$ which we know to be homotopic to 1.

We have shown that each $\pmb { \gamma }$ is homotopic to a product of closed curves of the form $P _ { 0 } P _ { k } P _ { k + 1 } P _ { 0 }$ If $P _ { k } P _ { k + 1 }$ does not contain $P _ { 0 } ^ { \prime } ,$ this curve is homotopic to 1. If, on the other hand, $P _ { k } P _ { k + 1 }$ contains $P _ { 0 } ^ { \prime }$ it is seen by enumeration of the possible cases that the curve is homotopic to $c$ or $c ^ { - 1 } { \mathrm { : } }$ . where $c$ is the full circle. Consequently, every closed curve is homotopic to a power of $c$ .

![](images/e4afe700ec183a6fbf221582ae7c3779f4198afd06ecf3bd807d9062a5243cba.jpg)  
FIG. 41.

Finally, we observe that $c ^ { m }$ is homotopic to 1 only if $\mathbf { \nabla } m = 0$ .This is seen by the fact that

$$
\int _ { { \cal C } ^ { m } } \frac { d z } { z } = m \cdot 2 \pi i ,
$$

while if the curve were homotopic to 1 the integral would have to vanish. From our results we conclude that the fundamental group of the punctured disk is isomorphic to the additive group of integers. Evidently, an arbitrary annulus has the same fundamental group.

We consider now a global analytic function f which can be continued along all arcs in the punctured disk $0 < | z | < 1$ . We choose an initial branch at $z _ { 0 } = r$ and continue it along the curves $c ^ { m }$ Either the continuation never returns to the initial branch, or there exists a smallest positive integer $h$ such that $C ^ { h }$ leads back to the initial branch. In the latter case, set $m = q h + r$ with $0 \leq r < h$ If $c ^ { m }$ leads back to the initial branch, so does $c ^ { \star }$ But since $r < h$ that is possible only if $\mathbf { \nabla } r = 0$ , and we find that $c ^ { m }$ leads to the initial branch if and only if $_ m$ is a multiple of $\boldsymbol { h }$ .

Consider the mapping $z = \zeta ^ { h }$ of $0 < | \zeta | < 1$ onto $0 < | z | < 1$ We claim that f can be expressed as a single-valued analytic function $F ( \xi )$ in the punctured disk of the $\boldsymbol { \xi } .$ -plane. The precise sense of this statement is that there exists, for every ξ1, $0 < | \boldsymbol { \xi } _ { 1 } | < 1$ , a function element $( f , \Omega )$ f with $\{ _ { 1 } ^ { h } \in \Omega _ { , }$ , such that $F ( \{ \} ) = f ( \{ \} ^ { h } )$ in a neighborhood of $\boldsymbol { \zeta _ { 1 } }$ in particular, it is required that the function element which corresponds to the point $\displaystyle \xi _ { 0 } = r ^ { 1 / h }$ determines the initial branch at ${ \pmb z } _ { 0 } .$ .

In order to construct $F ( \xi )$ , we join $\boldsymbol { \xi } _ { 0 }$ to $\xi$ by an arc $\pmb { \gamma } ^ { \prime }$ and continue the initial branch of f along the image of $\gamma ^ { \prime }$ under the mapping $z = \zeta ^ { h }$ we define $F ( \xi )$ as the terminal value obtained through this continuation. It must be proved that $F ( \xi )$ is uniquely determined. If $\pmb { \gamma _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } }$ and $\pmb { \gamma _ { 2 } ^ { \prime } }$ are two paths from $\boldsymbol { \xi } _ { 0 }$ to $\boldsymbol { \xi } _ { : }$ , then $\gamma _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } \gamma _ { 2 } ^ { \prime - 1 }$ can be deformed into a power $c ^ { \prime } { \mathfrak { n } }$ of the circle through $\boldsymbol { \zeta } _ { 0 }$ Consequently, the image curve $\gamma _ { 1 } \gamma _ { 2 } ^ { - 1 }$ can be deformed into the image of $C ^ { \prime } { \boldsymbol { \mathfrak { n } } }$ ,which is $C ^ { n h }$ But $c ^ { n h }$ leads back to the initial branch, and therefore $\gamma _ { 1 }$ and $\gamma _ { 2 }$ determine the same terminal branch. Finally, if $\boldsymbol { \xi }$ is in a neighborhood of $\boldsymbol { \zeta } _ { 1 , }$ we can first follow an arc $\gamma _ { 1 } ^ { \prime }$ from $\boldsymbol { \zeta } _ { 0 }$ to $\boldsymbol { \xi } _ { 1 }$ and then a variable arc $\gamma ^ { \prime }$ from $\boldsymbol { \xi } _ { 1 }$ to $\xi$ which stays within the neighborhood. If the neighborhood is sufficiently restricted, the continuation along the image of $\gamma ^ { \prime }$ is determined by a single function element $( f , \Omega )$ , and we have $F ( \{ \} ) = f ( \{ \xi ^ { h } \}$ in that neighborhood.

Since $F ( \xi )$ is single-valued and analytic in a punctured neighborhood of the origin, it has a convergent Laurent development of the form

$$
F ( \xi ) = \sum _ { - \infty } ^ { \infty } A _ { n } \xi ^ { n } .
$$

The corresponding multiple-valued function of $\pmb { \varepsilon }$ may be said to possess the development

$$
f ( z ) = \sum _ { - \infty } ^ { \infty } A _ { n } z ^ { n / \hbar } .
$$

It must be observed that this development depends on the choice of the initial branch. Different initial branches may yield entirely different developments and, in particular, different values of $\boldsymbol { h }$ The series(1) yields a total of $h$ related developments obtained by choosing different initial values of $z ^ { 1 / h }$ If $\omega = e ^ { 2 \pi i / h }$ , these developments are represented by

$$
f _ { \nu } ( z ) = \sum _ { - \infty } ^ { \infty } A _ { n } \omega ^ { \nu n } z ^ { n / h } \qquad ( \nu = 0 , 1 , . ~ . ~ . ~ , h - 1 ) .
$$

When the branch $( f _ { \nu } , z _ { 0 } )$ is continued along $c$ , it leads to the branch $( f _ { \nu + 1 } , z _ { 0 } )$ with the understanding that the subscript $\pmb { h }$ is identified with 0.

In special cases the Laurent development may contain only a finite number of negative powers. Then $F ( \zeta )$ has either a removable singularity or a pole, and the multiple-valued function $f ( z )$ (or, more correctly, the global analytic function obtained by continuing the given initial branch within a punctured disk) is said to have an algebraic singularity or branch point at ${ z = 0 }$ , provided of course that $h > 1$ If $F ( \boldsymbol { \zeta } )$ has a removable singularity, the branch point is an ordinary algebraic singularity, in the opposite case it is an algebraic pole. In either case $f ( z )$ tends to a definite limit $A _ { 0 }$ or $\infty$ as $_ { z }$ tends to 0 along an arbitrary arc.

Clearly, we could just as well have studied an isolated singularity at an arbitrary point $\pmb { a }$ or $\infty$ , and the radius of the punctured disk can be as small as we wish. In the case of a finite $\pmb { h }$ the correspondence between $w = f ( z )$ and the independent variable $\pmb { \mathscr { z } }$ can be expressed through equations of the form

$$
\begin{array} { l } { { w = \displaystyle \sum _ { - \infty } ^ { \infty } A _ { n } \xi ^ { n } } } \\ { { z = a + \xi ^ { h } \qquad \mathrm { o r } \qquad z = \xi ^ { - h } . } } \end{array}
$$

The variable $\boldsymbol { \xi }$ takes the name of local uniformizing variable.

In the case of an algebraic singularity it is desirable to complete the Riemann surface of f by the inclusion of a corresponding branch point on the surface. The projection of this point will be $\pmb { a }$ , and the point itself is not determined by a branch of f, but by developments similar to (2). Finally, a neighborhood is formed by the branches which correspond to points $\boldsymbol { \zeta }$ in a neighborhood of $\xi = 0$ From now on it will bo assumed that the Riemann surface of a global analytic function includes all such branch points.

# 2. ALGEBRAIC FUNCTIONS

An equation of the form $P ( w , z ) = \mathbf { 0 }$ ,where $P$ is a polynomial in two variables, has for each $\pmb { z }$ a finite number of solutions $w _ { 1 } ( z )$ , . . . , ${ w } _ { m } ( z )$ . We wish to show that these roots can be interpreted as values of a global analytic function $\mathbf f ( z )$ which is then called an algebraic function. Conversely, if a global analytic function is given, we want to be able to tell whether it does or does not satisfy a polynomial equation.

2.1. The Resultant of Two Polynomials. A polynomial $P ( w , z )$ in two variables is irreducible if it cannot be expressed as the product of two polynomials none of which is constant. Two polynomials $P$ and $Q$ are relatively prime if they have no common factor except for constants.

The following theorem is algebraic in character. Because of its fundamental importance for the theory of algebraic functions we will nevertheless reproduce its proof.

Theorem 3. If $P ( w , z )$ and $Q ( w , z )$ are relatively prime polynomials, there are only a finite number of values ${ z _ { 0 } }$ for which the equations $P ( w , z _ { 0 } ) = 0$ and $Q ( w , z _ { 0 } ) = \mathbf { 0 }$ have a common root.

We suppose that $P$ and $Q$ are ordered according to decreasing powers of $\boldsymbol { w }$ and set $Q ( w , z ) = b _ { 0 } ( z ) w ^ { m } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + b _ { m } ( z )$ where $\scriptstyle b _ { 0 } ( z )$ is not identically zero. If $P$ is divided by $Q ,$ the division algorithm yields a quotient and remainder which are polynomials in $\pmb { w }$ and rational functions in $\pmb { z }$ . We set up a Euclidean algorithm of the form

$$
\begin{array} { l } { { c _ { 0 } P = q _ { 0 } Q + R _ { 1 } } } \\ { { c _ { 1 } Q = q _ { 1 } R _ { 1 } + R _ { 2 } } } \\ { { c _ { 2 } R _ { 1 } = q _ { 2 } R _ { 2 } + R _ { 3 } } } \\ { { . ~ . ~ . ~ . ~ . ~ . ~ . ~ . ~ . ~ . ~ . ~ . ~ . ~ . ~ . ~ . ~ . ~ . } } \\ { { c _ { n - 1 } R _ { n - 2 } = q _ { n - 1 } R _ { n - 1 } + R _ { n } } } \end{array}
$$

where the $Q _ { k }$ and $\scriptstyle { R _ { k } }$ are polynomials in $\boldsymbol { w }$ and $\pmb { z }$ while the $\mathbf { c } _ { k }$ are polynomials in $\boldsymbol { z }$ used to clear the fractions. The degrees in $\boldsymbol { w }$ of the $\scriptstyle { R _ { k } }$ are decreasing, and $\scriptstyle { R _ { n } }$ is a polynomial in $z$ alone. If $\scriptstyle R _ { n } ( z )$ were identically zero, the unique factorization theorem implies, by the last relation in (3), that $\scriptstyle { R _ { n - 2 } }$ would be divisible by any irreducible factor of $R _ { n - 1 }$ which is of positive degree in $\boldsymbol { w }$ . The same reasoning shows, step by step, that all the $\scriptstyle { R _ { k } }$ as well as $Q$ and $P$ would be divisible by the same factor. This is contrary to the assumption, for $R _ { n - 1 }$ is of positive degree in $\mathbf { \boldsymbol { w } }$ and must therefore have an irreducible factor which contains $\boldsymbol { w }$

Suppose now that $P ( w _ { 0 } , z _ { 0 } ) = 0$ and $Q ( w _ { 0 } , z _ { 0 } ) = 0$ Substituting these values in (3) we obtain $R _ { 1 } ( w _ { 0 } , z _ { 0 } ) = 0 _ { : }$ . . . , $R _ { n - 1 } ( w _ { 0 } , z _ { 0 } ) = 0$ and finally $\begin{array} { r } { R _ { n } ( z _ { 0 } ) = 0 } \end{array}$ But since $\scriptstyle { R _ { n } }$ is not identically zero, there are only a finite number of $z _ { 0 }$ which satisfy this condition, and the theorem follows.

The polynomial $R _ { n } ( z )$ is called the resultant of $P$ and $Q$ More precisely, if we wish the resultant to be uniquely determined, we should require that the exponents $c _ { k }$ in (3) are of the lowest degree possible. We are not so interested in the resultant as in the statement of Theorem 3. The theorem will be applied to an irreducible polynomial $P ( w , z )$ and its partial derivative $P _ { w } ( w , z )$ with respect to $\boldsymbol { w }$ These polynomials are relatively prime as soon as $P$ has positive degree in $w _ { \mathrm { a } }$ , and the resultant of $P$ and $P _ { \ast }$ is called the discriminant of $P$ The zeros of the discriminant are the values $z _ { 0 }$ for which the equation $P ( w , z _ { 0 } ) = 0$ has multiple roots.

We note, finally, that the resultant $\boldsymbol { R } ( z )$ of any two relatively prime polynomials $P$ and $Q$ can be written in the form $R = p P + q Q$ where $\pmb { p }$ and $q$ are polynomials. This follows immediately from (3).

# 2.2. Definition and Properties of Algebraic Functions. We begin by formulating a precise definition:

Definition 2. A complete analytic function f is called an algebraic function if all its function elements $( f , \Omega )$ satisfy a relation $P ( f ( z ) , z ) = 0$ in $\mathfrak { Q }$ where $P ( w , z )$ is a polynomial which does not vanish identically.

Because of the permanence of functional relations it is sufficient to assume that one function element satisfies the equation $P ( f ( z ) , z ) = 0$ . The others will then automatically satisfy the same relation. Moreover, it may be assumed that $P ( w , z )$ is an irreducible polynomial. Suppose indeed that $P ( w , z )$ has the factorization $P = P _ { 1 } P _ { 2 }$ . . . $P _ { n }$ in irreducible factors. For any fixed point ${ \boldsymbol { z } } \in \Omega$ one of the equations $P _ { k } ( f ( z ) , z ) = 0$ must hold. If we consider a sequence of different points $z _ { n } \epsilon \Omega$ which tend to a limit in $\mathfrak { Q }$ ,then one of the relations $P _ { k } ( f ( z _ { n } ) , z _ { n } )$ must hold infinitely often.
